# 古文参同契集解

**Preface to the *Collected Commentaries on the Ancient Text of the Cantong qi* in the *Siku Quanshu***

We, your ministers, respectfully note: The *Collected Commentaries on the Ancient Text of the Cantong qi* in three juan was compiled by Jiang Yibiao of the Ming dynasty. Yibiao styled himself Fuyangzi and was a native of Yuyao. Wei Boyang’s original *Cantong qi* comprised three pian, which Peng Xiao later divided into chapters and annotated. Though subsequent commentators repeatedly rearranged and subdivided the text, the sequence of the upper, middle, and lower pian remained unchanged until the Ming dynasty, when Yang Shen produced a separate edition. He claimed to have unearthed a stone casket in the south containing the *Ancient Text of the Cantong qi*—three pian of the main text, one pian of preface, three pian of Xu Jingxiu’s commentary, one pian of postscript, two pian of Chunyu Shutong’s *Three Phases Diagram*, and one pian of postscript—totaling eleven pian. He boasted of having seen what even Master Zhu Xi had not. Yibiao’s commentary is based entirely on Shen’s edition, hence the title *Ancient Text*. He disassembled the annotations of Peng Xiao, Chen Xianwei, Chen Zhixu, and Yu Yan, appending them piecemeal beneath each section, thus calling it a *Collected Commentary*. Upon examination, we find that his work frequently inverts the original text: a eulogy present in the original is reclassified as Jingxiu’s postscript, while a chapter originally titled *Shusai’s Omissions* is extracted and recast as Shutong’s postscript.

The *Cantong qi* has seldom been cited by other works. Apart from Yu Fan’s commentary on the *Book of Changes*, which quotes its line “the sun and moon are the *Changes*,” no other references are found. Historical records of its transmission are also scant. The only reliable source is Peng Xiao’s preface, which merely states that “Master Wei showed it to Xu, an Attendant in Qingzhou, whose given name was Yue, and he annotated it.” Zheng Qiao’s *Tongzhi: Bibliography* lists *Xu’s Attendant’s Commentary on the Yin-Yang Unification of the Cantong qi* in three juan but does not specify whether this Xu was Jingxiu. How, then, did Jingxiu’s name suddenly emerge after two thousand years? Zhu Xi once remarked that the eulogy in the text seemed to be a postscript to a commentary, though the commentary itself was no longer extant. This edition coincidentally aligns with Zhu’s observation—could it be that Shen fabricated his claims by misappropriating Zhu’s words? As for Chunyu Shutong, he was merely a transmitter of the text. An old saying among Daoists mentions “Xu’s Attendant and Chunyu Shutong each writing a preface.” Peng Xiao, citing Liu Zhigu’s *Discourse on the Origin of the Sun and Moon*, thoroughly refuted this error. Yet Shen again attributed the *Three Phases Diagram* to Shutong, using the very argument Peng had debunked to prove its antiquity—a baseless assertion.

The *Cantong qi* is a relic of the Han and Wei dynasties, though no direct evidence confirms this. From the Jin dynasty onward, Ge Hong’s *Biographies of Divine Transcendents* states that “Boyang wrote the *Cantong qi* and *Five Phases Diagram*, totaling three juan.” In the Tang dynasty, the *Old唐书: Bibliography* (based on the *Kaiyuan Catalog of Inner and Outer Classics*) lists *The Zhouyi Cantong qi* in two juan by Wei Boyang and *The Zhouyi Five Phases Diagram* in one juan by Wei Boyang. What era, then, does Shen’s so-called *Ancient Text* hail from? Yibiao’s edition further alters all references to “Master Wei” in earlier commentaries to “Master Xu” to conform to Shen’s claims, violating the principle of suspending judgment where doubt exists. Nevertheless, since Shen’s edition, this version has gained currency, and later commentators have often followed it, rendering it ineradicable. We now provisionally adopt its chapter divisions, subdividing it into sub-juan, and include it alongside Peng Xiao’s edition in the catalog. This is to expose the origins of its fabrication and distortion, lest future scholars be misled.

Respectfully submitted after collation in the tenth month of the forty-sixth year of the Qianlong reign.

**Chief Compilers:**
Your Minister Ji Yun
Your Minister Lu Xixiong
Your Minister Sun Shiyi

**Chief Collator:**
Your Minister Lu Feichi

**Original Preface**

The *Cantong qi* stands as the progenitor of all alchemical scriptures. Yet, upon examining the bibliographic records of the Sui and Tang dynasties, one finds no trace of its title. Only in the *Biographies of Divine Transcendents* is it recorded that Wei Boyang of Shangyu, a man well-versed in poetry, prosody, and literary arts, cultivated the Dao and nurtured his aspirations, distilling the *Zhouyi* to compose the *Cantong qi*. Xu Jingxiu of the same prefecture provided annotations, and during the reign of Emperor Huan, it was transmitted to Chunyu Shutong, thereafter circulating in the world. By the Five Dynasties period, Peng Xiao, a Daoist of Yongkang in Shu, divided it into ninety chapters to correspond with the nine revolutions of the firing process, appending a poem on the tripod and cauldron to align with the attainment of the One in true lead. His interpretations, however, were forced and contrary to Master Wei’s original intent.

The text had long been scattered and disordered, leaving later readers unable to distinguish between the classic and its commentaries, or between the words of Wei, Xu, and Chunyu—this confusion began with Peng. Master Zhu Xi composed his *Examination of Discrepancies* and commentary based on Peng’s edition, while Yu Yuwu of the Yuan dynasty, in his own annotations, relied on Zhu’s version. Yuwu sought to separate the three-character, four-character, and five-character lines into distinct sections but did not complete the task. Though he recognized the sequence’s disorder and its deviation from Master Wei’s original text, he, like others, lacked a definitive basis.

I once read Zhang Pingshu’s *Wuzhen pian*, which states: “Shutong received instruction from Wei Boyang, leaving behind the *Cantong qi* as the eternal alchemical classic.” I surmised that Pingshu must have seen the ancient text, for after years of searching, he never obtained it. Recently, I met Yang Qionglai, Vice Censor of Hongya, who mentioned that in the southern regions, a stone casket unearthed from the earth contained an ancient version of the *Cantong qi* by Wei Boyang, comprising three main chapters, one preface, three chapters of Xu Jingxiu’s annotations, one postscript, three supplementary chapters on the Three Phases by Chunyu Shutong, two chapters of later postscripts, and one final postscript—eleven chapters in total. This version had not been tampered with by later hands.

Eagerly, I sought to transcribe it, but before I could, someone arrived from Wu with a printed edition, falsely claiming it was the fruit of arduous contemplation, a sudden enlightenment where tangled chapters and disordered passages dissolved like mist and ice. Such claims deceive both self and others, and upon examining its separate preface, I found it further asserted that someone from Guiji had presented a superior ancient text, instantly correcting all spurious versions. Yet within a leaf or half a slip, the deception was already evident—like covering one’s ears to steal a bell or hiding one’s head while exposing one’s feet. Truly lamentable!

Having rejoiced in the reemergence of the ancient text and witnessed what Master Zhu had never seen, I considered it a once-in-a-millennium delight. Thus, I composed a preface and preserved it. Alas! Ancient texts from the Eastern Han surviving to this day are exceedingly rare. This book, like a broken jade tablet restored or a flawed disk rejoined, is truly precious.

Yet, some interpret its metaphors and analogies as pertaining to bedroom arts or mistake them for furnace alchemy, leading to the ruin of lives, the squandering of fortunes, and the dissipation of families. Success is rare—one in ten thousand—while failure claims eight or nine out of ten. As Ban Gu observed, the pursuit of transcendence is meant to preserve the authenticity of life and seek nothing external. It should serve to calm the mind and align with the great transformation, freeing the heart from fear. However, those who fixate solely on such practices produce ever more bizarre and abstruse writings, deviating from the teachings of the sages. Beware!

These words are appended herewith.

Written on the tenth day after the Winter Solstice in the bingwu year of the Jiajing reign by Dongtian Zhenyi of Chengdu, Yang Shen.

---

In the dingyou year of the Jiajing reign, during my convalescence, I immersed myself in quietude and perused various esoteric texts, seeking resources for inner cultivation. Upon reading the *Cantong qi*, I found the annotated versions utterly incomprehensible and set them aside. Eighteen years have since passed, to this jiayin year, and my mind has often returned to the text. As the progenitor of alchemical scriptures, why is it so obscure and disordered, rendering it nearly unintelligible?

Yesterday, while sorting through the posthumous works of my late father, Master Yunlong, I chanced upon an ancient version of the *Cantong qi*—Wei Boyang’s original text in three chapters with one preface, Xu Jingxiu’s annotations in three chapters with one postscript, and Chunyu Shutong’s *Three Phases* supplement in two chapters with one final postscript, totaling eleven chapters. This version, devoid of commentary, had not been tampered with by later hands and was the very edition prefaced by Yang Shen of Sheng’an of Chengdu. Upon reading it, I realized that the versions I had encountered years prior had interspersed Wei’s preface and the annotations of Jingxiu and Shutong into the main text, creating profound confusion. Later commentators, unaware of the origins of these errors, perpetuated them, embellishing the text with forced interpretations, leading to further distortions. No wonder later readers, encountering repeated phrases and obscure meanings, accepted these corruptions wholesale, compounding the disorder.

Unbeknownst to them, the chaos originated with Peng Xiao. Now, I have corrected these falsifications, taking the ancient text as the standard. I extracted Wei’s original chapters and compiled excerpts from the commentaries of Peng Xiao, Chen Xianwei, Chen Zhixu, and Yu Yan beneath each section to elucidate their meanings. Yu’s commentary, in particular, was so fragmented and disordered that selecting coherent passages required considerable effort. Some passages, though seemingly disjointed, were likely misplaced due to the intermingling of Xu and Chunyu’s annotations and postscripts. Where continuity was lacking, I marked the breaks with a circle. Given the limitations of this edition, which includes only these four commentaries, I could not incorporate more.

Xu Jingxiu’s annotations and Chunyu Shutong’s *Three Phases* were transcribed from the ancient version, with the four commentaries appended below. Their respective postscripts were included as plain text, separate from the annotations. Divided into two juan, Wei’s *Cantong qi* forms the upper volume, while Xu and Chunyu’s annotations and *Three Phases* comprise the lower volume, completing this definitive edition. Thus, a millennium of errors are at once rectified, and we behold what Peng, Chen, and others never saw—a truly exhilarating achievement!

May future readers clearly discern Master Wei’s original intent and no longer confuse the classic with its commentaries.

As for the discovery of the ancient text, Yang Shen attributed it to a stone casket unearthed in the south. His preface provides ample detail, so I will not reiterate it here. However, upon close examination, much of the *Cantong qi*’s language and meaning resembles that of the *Longhu jing*, suggesting that Master Wei drew upon the latter to expound its principles rather than merely imitating it.

The *Book of Changes* stands as the earliest work on the Dragon and Tiger, while the *Cantong qi* models itself upon it. Xu and Chunyu, in turn, each modeled their own works upon it, expressing their individual insights. Since ancient times, scholars have delved into these texts only to conflate them into one, leading to grave errors.

I once encountered a perfected being who transmitted the elixir formula—so simple and concise that a mere one or two phrases sufficed, with no need for further elaboration. If this is so, then the composition of the *Cantong qi* might seem superfluous. Yet this is not the case. To guide later learners, the text employs myriad analogies and metaphors, veiling its discourse not to expose the truth directly but to lead readers to ponder and grasp its essence beyond the words themselves. For such a treasure, the teachings cannot but be conveyed in roundabout and guarded language. To distill it into a single phrase would be to reduce it to mere oral instruction. The Dao cannot be lightly divulged in its entirety; if it were, people would trivialize its words, growing careless and neglectful in their practice. Without unwavering resolve and singular focus, the great Dao would remain forever unattainable. This is why Master Wei borrowed the language of the *Changes* to expound the alchemical method in the *Cantong qi*.

Its lower chapter states: *"Exposing the branches while concealing the root, adopting various names to obscure the multitude of texts."* This is precisely the intent. Though I dare not claim this compilation as a definitive aid to Master Wei’s work, it may yet offer some assistance to Peng, Chen, and others. For the arduous efforts of exploration in those early years have not been in vain, culminating in this present achievement.

Written as a preface on the full moon of the fourth lunar month in the *jiayin* year of the Wanli reign [1614], by Fu Yangzi Jiang Yibiao of Dongyue Yuyao.

*Imperially Commissioned Complete Library of the Four Treasuries*
*Collected Explications of the Ancient Text Cantong qi*, Volume One [Part One]
By Jiang Yibiao of the Ming Dynasty
*Cantong qi* by Wei Boyang of the Eastern Han

**Upper Chapter** [This speaks of the three sages riding the dragon to ascend to heaven, the Dao of the *Great Changes*]

Heaven is firm, Earth is yielding; their union and embrace.
Yang endows, yin receives; male and female depend on each other to engender the essence of creation.
Qi unfurls, Kan and Li crown the head, their radiance spreading—
Mysterious and profound, beyond depiction.
The sage measures and weighs, aligning the primal foundations.
The four elements merge into chaos, entering the realm of emptiness.
The sixty-four hexagrams unfold like a chariot’s canopy,
The dragon [or: horse] harnessed, the enlightened ruler at the reins.
When harmony prevails, the path is smooth, free from deviation.
The perilous way is steep and treacherous, endangering the state.
The noble one dwells in his chamber; if his words are virtuous,
A thousand *li* away, they resonate—such is the lord of ten thousand chariots,
Abiding in the ninefold palace, issuing decrees in accord with yin and yang.
He stores his instruments, awaiting the season, never transgressing the hexagram’s time.
*Zhun* begins at *zi* and *shen*, employing *yin* and *xu*.
The remaining sixty hexagrams each have their appointed days.
I briefly present two images, though I cannot exhaust their depths.
Establish righteousness, enact punishments, bestow virtue where due.
To oppose them brings misfortune; to follow them brings fortune.
Enforce the laws with utmost sincerity, meticulous and precise.
Observe the celestial signs, scrutinize the waxing and waning—
Even the slightest deviation brings regret and harm.
At the solstices, the measures change; errors lead to disorder.
Twisting paths in midwinter’s scorching heat,
Frost and snow at midsummer’s peak,
Equinoxes out of alignment, timekeeping awry—
Drought and flood contend, wind and rain fail to harmonize.
Locusts surge forth, omens multiply,
Heaven displays its portents, mountains crumble, earth splits.
The filial son’s devotion moves the imperial pole,
Nearby, it emerges from his own mouth;
Far off, it flows to distant lands—
Now summoning calamity, now bringing fortune,
Now ushering in peace, now inciting war.
These four outcomes arise from within.

The heart’s stirrings follow constant principles,
Adhering to the plumb line, aligning with the seasons and qi.
Hard and soft are determined, never encroaching upon each other.
The Five Phases guard their boundaries, never recklessly waxing or waning.
The *Changes* circulate, flowing and bending, contracting and extending,
Concealing and emerging, transforming within.
Encompassing the myriad things, it governs the Dao’s framework.
From nonbeing, it shapes being; the vessel’s use lies in its emptiness.
Thus, it infers the waxing and waning of Kan and Li, their rise and fall.
Words are not idly crafted; theories are not baselessly born.
Citing evidence, verifying effects, calibrating measures,
Divine clarity infers the patterns, structuring characters to reveal the principle.
Kan is the moon’s essence, *wu*; Li is the sun’s radiance, *si*.
Sun and moon form the *Changes*, hard and soft in balance.
Earth flourishes in the four seasons, weaving the beginning and end.
Blue, red, white, black—each resides in its quadrant,
All receiving the power of the central palace, *wu* and *si*.
Between the dark and new moons, their signs converge, moving within.
Chaos and vastness, female and male unite,
Their fluids moistening and nourishing, their transformations flowing.
Heaven and earth, spirits and numina—beyond measure.
Their utility lies in securing the body, refining form, and concealing the origin.
Beginning in the northeast, the region of *Ji* and *Dou*,
It revolves rightward, spewing forth sprouts, hidden depths revealing signs.
Emerging in the clarity of *Mao* and *Bi*,
*Zhen* arises as the omen, yang qi’s inception.
The first nine: the hidden dragon.
Yang is established by threes, yin by eights.
Three days, *Zhen* stirs; eight days, *Dui* moves.
The second nine: the dragon appears, peaceful and luminous.
The third five virtues coalesce, forming the body of *Qian*.
The third nine: vigilant at dusk, the divine tally wanes and waxes, gradually transforming, returning to its origin.
*Xun* continues the lineage, consolidating and upholding.
The fourth nine: leaping or retreating, the path is perilous.
*Gen* governs advance and retreat, not overstepping the time—
Twenty-three days, holding to the appointed term.
The fifth nine: the flying dragon, in the celestial position, rejoices.
The sixth five: *Kun* bears and binds, completing the cycle, nurturing the multitude of offspring, ever the mother of the world.
The ninth: the exalted dragon battles in the wild.
Using the nine: soaring as the Dao’s compass.
Yang’s numbers conclude, then begin anew.
Pushing emotions to align with nature, revolving in mutual succession,
The jade pivot ascends and descends, circulating above and below.
The six lines are difficult to discern, thus they have no fixed position—
This is the ancestral origin of the *Changes*.
At dawn of the new moon, *Fu* begins, yang’s inception.
Entering and exiting without hindrance, it establishes the standard of firmness.
The *Huangzhong* pitch is set at *zi*, its signs flourishing.
Spreading warmth, the gentle qi steams the black millet, attaining constancy.
Approaching the furnace, the path opens, the light of rectification [or: generation] shines,
Growing brighter day by day.
*Chou*’s *Dalü* binds and adjusts, rising and falling to form *Tai*.
Hard and soft unite, yin and yang intertwine—
The small departs, the great arrives, converging at *yin*, advancing with the season.
Passing through *Dazhuang*, arrayed at the gate of *mao*,
Elm pods fall, returning to their root.
Punishment and virtue contend; day and night begin to divide.
*Guai*: yin retreats, yang ascends, advancing.
Washing and cleansing the wings, shaking off the dust of the past,
Vigorous and bright, its radiance spreads to the four neighbors.
Yang concludes at the center of *si*, then *Gou* begins the sequence.
Treading frost appears first; the well’s chill lies deepest.
*Wu* is the guest, submitting to yin; yin becomes the host.
Retreating from the worldly position, gathering its essence,
Cherishing virtue, awaiting the time, lingering in obscurity.
Dark and blocked, *Pi* is impassable; sprouts do not grow.
Yin extends, yang contracts, yang is lost [or: injured, wounded].
---

When injury is leveled, it is thus.
Observe names and examine their measures of power and balance,
Discern the sentiments of mid-autumn:
Entrusting accumulation, guarding the young and old,
Withering and reviving, shepherd’s purse and wheat sprout anew.
They emerge under cover, yet when *Bo* decays,
Limbs rot, form dissolves,
Qi transforms and exhausts, vanishing without trace.
When the divine Dao reaches its limit, it returns to *Kun*’s origin,
Eternally compliant with earth’s principle,
Upholding heaven’s decree, spreading the profound and distant,
Obscure and remote, yet linked in resonance.
Nurturing the seed of yin and yang’s origin,
Vast and indistinct, none know its beginning.
First lost, then straying from the path; later, becoming the sovereign lord.
No plain without slope—such is the Dao’s natural course.
Flourishing and decline alternate, waxing and waning follow one another.
*Kun* ends, *Fu* begins, as if tracing an endless ring.
Emperors and kings, in succession for a thousand autumns, endure,
Governing at the helm, renewing the old, grasping the subtle and precise,
Unfolding and disseminating the precious essential Dao.
The Dipper’s handle unifies transformation’s guiding threads.
Hexagrams stir within, fortune and misfortune arise without.
The five planets interweave, responding to time,
Moving the four seasons and seven regulators.
When discordant, they separate and scatter;
When harmonious, they bow and rise.
*Wenchang* governs and records, interrogating and holding accountable.
The Three Dukes and all officials, each overseeing their domain,
Trace beginnings to ends, preserving or losing the thread.
If the ruler grows arrogant and indulgent, exceeding the Dao,
Or if ministers turn treacherous, acting against the proper path,
Hopes and expectations waver, fortunes shift, misfortune strikes.
The law enforces reproach, interrogating faults,
Imposing consequences upon the sovereign’s celestial pole.
Yet a wise ruler, governing with clarity, leaves the state free of harm.

When *Chen Xianwei* says:
*Qian* and *Kun*, hard and soft, pair and envelop one another,
Naturally, yang bestows while yin receives and conceals.
For yang, being masculine, scatters and sows;
Yin, being feminine, contains and receives.
Solitary yang does not generate; solitary yin does not nurture.
Masculine and feminine depend on each other,
Dispersing and spreading essence to complete creation.
As with human conception, none arise without yin and yang’s union—
Thus, creation’s workings begin with *Qian* and *Kun*.
Yet now *Kan* and *Li* are placed at the forefront,
For *Qian* and *Kun* are heaven and earth,
While *Kan* and *Li* are sun and moon.
Heaven and earth, though fixed in place, cannot merge into one,
Yet within their midst, sun and moon unite.
Thus, *Qian* and *Kun* form the elixir’s body,
While *Kan* and *Li* serve as its function.
Speaking only of *Kan* and *Li* at the outset
Emphasizes that the great elixir’s efficacy lies entirely in *Kan* and *Li*.
Yet *Kan* and *Li*’s function operates between *Qian* and *Kun*’s two bodies,
Rising and descending, soaring and hiding, ceaselessly shifting—
How easily could this be depicted?
The sage, wishing to gauge its profound subtleties for learners,
Observes that among the sixty miscellaneous hexagrams,
A single yang line and a single yin line,
Whether above or below, remain elusive and hard to fathom.
One should act like a wise ruler governing the times,
Using the sixty hexagrams as a chariot,
Abiding in effortless action to harness them.
Then, one may proceed serenely, step by step,
Advancing along the Dao with grace, harmony, and ease,
The path smooth and steady from beginning to end,
Circling through all hexagrams without peril.
Thus it is said: In harmony, one follows the path,
The road level, without deviation.
If one fails to attain this and recklessly takes a crooked path,
There will be the worry of overturning, the fault of peril.
*Liu Haichan* said: "Do not let the purple river chariot overturn"—
This carries the same meaning.
Though the road may be level, one must guard against danger,
Proceeding with utmost care.

The noble one, dwelling in his chamber,
Each has his appointed day.
For those cultivating the elixir, a year in seclusion is especially arduous.
Though their movements may be slight, their influence is profound.
Like a ruler of ten thousand chariots, dwelling deep within the ninefold gates,
Every action, word, or silence affects celestial harmony.
As the *Yi* says: "The noble one, dwelling in his chamber,
Resonates a thousand *li* away"—this serves as a fitting comparison.
One must follow yin and yang’s ascent and descent,
Advancing or halting with punishment and virtue,
Cherishing the supreme treasure,
Guarding the pupil of one’s eye,
Nurturing an infant,
Holding a full vessel,
Awaiting the appointed time without transgressing the hexagram’s month.
*Zhun* spans from *zi* to *shen*;
*Lin* spans from *yin* to *xu*.
The remaining hexagrams each have their appointed time.
For in a single day, two hexagrams unfold;
In one hour, six lines shift.
To discern yin and yang, one must distinguish day and night;
To know day and night, one must separate black and white.
Once black and white are distinct, hexagrams and lines are grasped.
As the *Yi* says: "Understand the Dao of day and night, and you will know"—
This is its meaning.

I briefly outline the two images—*Zhun* and *Lin*—
Yet cannot exhaust their depths, daring not fully reveal the celestial mechanism of fire.
When scholars encounter a teacher and receive guidance,
Timing their actions to congeal the elixir’s foundation,
They must adhere to the calendar’s principles,
Utterly sincere, meticulous, and vigilant,
Observing day and night, scrutinizing waxing and waning.
When yang arises, advance, as one bestows virtue when benevolence is due;
When yin arises, retreat, as one enacts punishment when justice demands.
Compliance brings fortune; defiance brings misfortune.
If even a hair’s breadth strays from this,
Regret and adversity lurk within, becoming thieves and harms—
Like weeds among seedlings, dust on a mirror.
Moreover, if people refining cinnabar fear errors in water and fire,
How much more so for this golden liquor, the great elixir?

At the two solstices, the measure changes.
The two solstices mark yin and yang’s inception and emergence.
If their function errs, celestial anomalies follow:
Summer snow, winter thunder, calamities and omens turn grim.
The two equinoxes mark yin and yang’s division and union.
If their function errs, celestial anomalies likewise arise—
Mountains crumble, earth splits, floods and droughts wreak havoc beyond measure.
This occurs when those who regulate fire miss the time, erring,
And the true embryo is harmed, the world collapses.
The filial son is likened to the true embryo,
The imperial standard to fortune and misfortune.
Thus, some achieve great peace, others bring war;
Some invite misfortune, others invite blessing—
All are summoned by the fire-regulator’s heart and mind.

The method of refining the elixir differs not from worldly methods.
Yet the consequences of governing the world are like echoes and shadows—
How much more so when seizing heaven and earth’s creation,
Aligning one’s virtue with heaven and earth, synchronizing with their movement?
Movement and stillness must be constant,
Following the inked line without deviation,
Adapting to the four seasons’ rhythms,
Naturally harmonizing with the two qi.
The hard does not overstep; the soft does not retreat.
Whether bending or stretching, returning or overturning,
The five phases circulate, each guarding its bounds—
Only then can one be free from worry.

Profound and unfathomable, hidden and elusive,
Yet within, transformation gives birth to the supreme treasure—
Like yin and yang’s union, generating the ten thousand things,
Serving as the Dao’s guiding threads.

To wield the formless in shaping the formed—
The *Jinbi Jing* states: "Form and formless restrain each other."
The Vermilion Bird flames through empty space,
Amid purple blossoms the sun’s radiance shines—
Yet cinnabar and mercury vanish away.

Master Wei’s words but elucidate the *Jinbi Jing*’s safeguard:
That safeguard lies in using the fire of true emptiness—
The nature’s fire—to govern the water of life,
The supreme treasure.
Fire’s essence is emptiness; it manifests only when meeting things.
All that is luminous and selfless belongs to fire’s virtue.
Yet when fire is moved within the crucible of the great void,
Purple flames fill the heavens, red light suffuses the golden palace,
Jade towers transform in myriad ways,
Divine beasts and spirit dragons soar in ten thousand forms—
These are lead and mercury,
Also the Kan and Li trigrams.
They are not nothingness,
Yet once their function is complete, where do they abide?
Thus it is said: "Trace the waxing and waning—Kan and Li vanish away."
Words are not idly spoken.

As for the workings of Wu and Ji in the *Yi*’s system of hexagram-nucleus correspondence:
Kan embraces the six Wu; Li embraces the six Ji.
Kan is the moon; Li is the sun.
Hence it is said: "Kan’s Wu is the moon’s essence; Li’s Ji is the sun’s light."
The characters for "sun" and "moon" combine to form *yi* (change).
Thus it is said: "Trace the turning to knit the character."
All these are grounded in principle as proof, not empty conjecture.

Since the *Yi* does not transcend sun and moon,
How could the elixir not originate in Kan and Li?
Yet Kan and Li both preserve Wu and Ji.
As the ancients said: "It is only because each holds true earth
That the golden elixir achieves return and restoration."
Moreover, earth flourishes in all four seasons,
Weaving through the beginning and end.
Though water, fire, metal, and wood each dwell in their own quadrant,
All receive the virtue of earth from the central palace.

Zhang Ziyang’s poem says:
"The four images and five phases wholly rely on earth—
Great indeed is earth’s virtue!"
For earth is the mother of metal.

Knowing that the five phases all return to earth, one then knows that the five phases all transform into metal. Only then can one guard the creative process within the central palace and sow the yellow sprout in the posterior earth.

Between the dark and new moons, when radiant essence scatters forth, one must align and act within the center, like the primordial chaos of Hongmeng—immeasurable. For this is the time when yin and yang unite, fluids moisten, and transformative circulation flows. How could one apply effort here? Thus it is fitting to rest the body, conceal the form, and withdraw to the region of Jidou, where the wheel of emergence may emit its luminous glow. As Hanshanzi’s poem says: "Do not expose the root—if the root is hollow, the fruit will fall." For substance and function differ, and so too does the application of effort.

Above Mao and Bi, turning and interacting, Master Wei likens the waxing and waning of the moon within a month to the advance and retreat of hexagram images. From the third day, one yang arises; by the eighth day, two yangs; by the fifteenth, three yangs are complete, and the Qian trigram is formed. On the sixteenth, one yin is born; by the twenty-third, two yins; by the thirtieth, three yins are complete, and the Kun trigram is formed. Mao and Bi reside in the west at the Geng position. On the third day of the month, the moon appears faintly in the west at the Geng position, corresponding to Zhen’s initial yang, and in the *Zhouyi*’s method of hexagram-nourishing, Zhen is assigned six Geng. The creative principle aligns thus. By the eighth day, the moon appears in the upper half of the southern sky at the Ding position, corresponding to Dui’s two yangs, and in the nourishing method, Dui is assigned six Ding. By the fifteenth, the moon is full in the east at the Jia position, and the Qian hexagram is assigned six Jia. At this time, the three yangs of the hexagram are complete, the Way of three and five has culminated, and what was full begins to wane, what extended retracts, what was high descends, and what ascended now falls. On the sixteenth, one yin is born, and yin begins its dominion. At dawn, the moon appears in the west at the Xin position, corresponding to Xun, which is assigned Xin. By the twenty-third, at dawn, the moon appears in the south at the Bing position, corresponding to Gen, which is assigned Bing. By the thirtieth, the moon sets in the east at the Yi position, corresponding to Kun, which is assigned Yi. When the cycle ends, it yields to yang once more, and the revolving jade mechanism turns endlessly.

As the ancestral foundation of the *Yi*, within a single day, the fire-phase revolves like the turning of the jade mechanism: ascending from Zi to Wu, descending thereafter, traversing the six lines. Though no visible traces are observed, the creative process is silently guarded within the heart. At this time, the numinous medicine follows the sun’s movement, ascending and descending, rising and falling, never pausing, never fixed in position. Thus it aligns with the *Yi*’s principle of yang ascending and yin descending.

That it is called the ancestral foundation of the *Yi* means that the sages first awakened to the principle of the golden elixir, cultivating themselves to transcend the ordinary and enter sagehood. Thereafter, they expounded this principle in the *Yi* to instruct later generations. Thus we know that the *Yi* was composed based on the great elixir, and the Way of the great elixir is the ancestral foundation of the *Yi*.

At the new moon’s dawn, *Fu* (Return) begins: at midnight, the Zi hour, the fire-phase commences. Before one yang arises, the fire-phase is still; the multitude of yins gather like the masses without governance. When the fire-phase moves, yang qi begins to flow, spreading warmth, steaming and fumigating the crucible. Once the light appears, it becomes the master of all transformations. At this time, the cultivator silently guards the mechanism of advancing the fire, using subtle firmness as the standard, moving in and out, gathering and releasing without haste. From here onward, cultivation gradually increases until pure Qian is achieved, and the treasure is forged. In the initial stages, one must be especially cautious. As the poem of the predecessors says: "On cold nights, tend to the fire; guard against the crowd until dawn." This is the meaning of "spreading gentle warmth, steaming to attain constancy."

Before the furnace, applying the twigs: at the Chou hour, advancing the two yangs’ fire-phase. By this time, radiance gradually increases, opening the mysterious path, and the light is true. Yang arises from below, so one should congeal it below. At this time, the effort is properly low. After passing this line, one gradually releases and lifts, following the transformative mechanism. Thus it is said: "Congeal properly in low and high, lifting to form *Tai*."

Moving and hastening with the time: at the Yin hour, advancing the three yangs’ fire-phase. By this time, firm and yielding separate, yin and yang each occupy half. Above is water, below is fire. Thus essence-water ascends, spirit-fire descends. What lifts desires to rise, what descends desires to fall. Yin and yang unite, the small departs and the great arrives, and the creative process converges here.

Day and night begin to divide: at the Mao hour, advancing the four yangs’ bathing phase. At this time, day and night begin to separate, life and death are balanced, and all things flourish in spring. Only the elm’s pods fall, for they correspond to the stars. At dawn, the golden elixir aligns with the myriad phenomena, and all phenomena arise from the sun and moon.

*Guai* (Decisiveness) retreats yin: at the Chen hour, advancing the five yangs’ fire-phase. By this time, the five yangs ascend, and the water source is clear and limpid, like a bird emerging from water, shaking its wings to wash away the dust of the past, preparing to soar into the heavens.

Qian’s vigor and brightness: at the Si hour, advancing the six yangs’ fire-phase. By this time, the pure Qian body is complete, the moon is full, the water is abundant, the light is radiant, the spirit is full, centered and unbiased, round and unbroken. Its light illumines the four directions, pervading the ten quarters.

*Gou* (Encounter) begins its threads: at the Wu hour, retreating one yin’s tally-phase. By this time, yin is the host and yang the guest. If one does not recognize the host within the guest or the guest within the host, a hair’s breadth of error brings no small harm. Though one yin has just sprouted and seems harmless, how can one ignore the cold spring at the well’s bottom or the frost preceding ice? Thus the predecessors urgently discuss the distinction between host and guest.

Withdrawing from the world, relinquishing position: at the Wei hour, retreating two yins’ tally-phase. Blocked and impassable, nothing flows.

Concealing yang’s name: at the Shen hour, retreating three yins’ tally-phase.

Observing its measure: at the You hour, retreating four yins’ bathing phase. Shepherd’s purse and wheat correspond to the sun, so by the eighth month, their sprouts emerge. The sun’s spirit is conceived in You. Wood reaches this point and conceals its embryo; earth reaches this point and bathes. Thus You is the bathing phase.

*Bo* (Splitting) disintegrates the limbs: at the Xu hour, retreating five yins’ tally-phase. When the Way is exhausted, it returns.

Bearing heaven and spreading proclamation: at the Hai hour, retreating six yins’ tally-phase.

The profound and distant mystery endures through a thousand ages. The lodestone draws iron, the yang-brush ignites fire, the square-vessel gathers water—all exemplify the principle of yin and yang mutually resonating, barriers yet communicating. Who can fathom its beginnings or ends?

The work of refining the elixir begins in obscurity and confusion. Only when the firing times are complete and the elixir’s power matures does one realize the body as the World-Honored One, like an emperor mounting his chariot. Is this Way anything other than the mechanism of "Kun’s end and Fu’s beginning"?

At the outset of governance, each office attends to its domain. The initial effort in cultivation is like the start of governance: one must suppress and sever all perverse excess, cast aside desires, and restrain the primal qi, making the guard firm and unyielding, with no fear of leakage. Naturally, treasure spreads within the golden womb, and spirit is born in the royal chamber. If even a hair’s breadth deviates, it is like governance gone awry, provoking timely responses and celestial omens, disrupting the harmony. The crux lies in managing the firing times with utmost precision, embodying the turning of the Dipper’s handle and the movement within the hexagrams. Auspicious and ominous signs arise externally: the five planets may misalign, or the constellations may stray from their paths.

The elixir resides in the spirit-chamber as a ruler establishes a state. The ruler’s founding of a state draws from celestial patterns: there are the Three Terraces, the positions of the ducal ministers; there is the Literary Prosperity office, which oversees records. The duty of the ducal ministers is to sit in counsel, harmonizing yin and yang, ensuring each official fulfills their role—thus accountability rests with the ducal ministers. The office of records measures talent, demoting or promoting the worthy and unworthy, ensuring each official maximizes their ability—thus oversight lies with Literary Prosperity. When all offices perform their duties, the people and things are at peace, and the realm enjoys great tranquility. When the hexagrams’ fire-signs do not lose their measure, the myriad transformations flow smoothly, and the sacred embryo grows. Yet governing a state depends on the summons of one person; refining the elixir depends on the resonance of one heart.

Tracing origins to essential ends: if a state suffers no harm, its ruler may grow arrogant, overbearing, or excessive, deviating from the Way—this is when the earthly number is excessive, and the balance is disrupted. Or ministers may be treacherous and perverse, acting contrary to proper order—this is when the watery weight is unstable. When weights and measures wax or wane, discord and misfortune arise; if blame is misplaced, fire’s excess lies with earth. The *Jinbi Jing* states, "It is not fire’s fault; the reprimand belongs to earth," referring precisely to this.

The elixir resides in the spirit-chamber as the North Star stands above, aligning the host of stars. The ruler governs by overseeing the myriad states; centered and unmoving, with reverent composure and non-action, the realm attains peace. Thus governance models itself on heaven, and the elixir models itself on governance.

Peng Xiao said: Qian’s hardness and Kun’s softness unite and embrace. In refining the golden liquid and returning elixir, one first establishes the cauldron of Qian and Kun in the state of *jiji* (hexagram 63). Then yin and yang conjoin their essence and qi within it. Next, water and fire, Kan and Li, follow in succession, uniting sun and moon, dragon and tiger. Thus the cauldron’s interior shines with profound brilliance, its depths visible. This is achieved by measuring the sacred texts, investigating the "mother of medicines," so that Qian and Kun, Li and Kan, merge and align, traversing the six voids, unfolding the five qi, cycling through the sixty hexagrams, passing the twelve earthly branches, forming a chariot to welcome the dragon-horse. Within lies the Golden Mother of the Central Palace, like a ruler governing the state, harmonizing the seasonal qi to follow, practicing non-action to achieve great compliance. Thus the path is level, without incline, and "there is no going that does not return."

If, however, the firing times are misaligned and the timing lacks precision, the path becomes perilous and obstructed, the state topples, and the Golden Consort within the spirit-chamber has no support, leading to rampant production. The essence of the golden elixir lies entirely in the two words: lead and fire. Lead and fire are water and fire, the ancestors of return. The remaining five phases and qi-times all assist in its completion. The Golden Consort is the Golden Mother.

The noble one dwells in their chamber: each has its appointed day. The *Yi* says, "When the noble one dwells in their chamber and speaks good words, there is response from a thousand *li* away." This illustrates the Metal Elder residing in the spirit-chamber, like a ruler in the ninefold palace. In managing the morning and evening water and fire, issuing commands is like proclaiming decrees, ensuring all qi are properly placed. One must also remain tranquil and non-active, storing the vessel until the time is right, following the hexagram lines, observing the clepsydra’s marks, and carefully reviewing each. The "morning and evening tally-signs" refer to the sixty hexagrams corresponding to a month’s cycle, with day and night each receiving one hexagram, cycling endlessly. *Yin* and *shen* are the positions where yin and yang begin and end.

I briefly present the two images: regret and remorse become thieves. The "two images" refer to water and fire, yin and yang. From *zi* to *si* belongs to spring and summer, when fire governs the phase of generation. Contained within the vessel, it warms and nurtures the spirit—this is the image of enacting benevolence. From *wu* to *hai* belongs to autumn and winter, when the tally-sign governs the phase of restraint. Descending into the womb, it coagulates the numinous mercury—this is the image of establishing justice and punishment.

Master Wei enables later generations to understand the movements of the sun and moon, the rise and fall of yin and yang, following the hexagrams and lines, with cold and heat as warnings. By carefully attending to waxing and waning, ensuring no regret or remorse arises within the cauldron, no thieves emerge from the images, the red essence descends as qi, the white mercury takes form, and the golden liquid returning elixir is swiftly achieved.

The solstices and equinoxes alter their measures. The summer solstice, winter solstice, spring equinox, and autumn equinox—these four refer to *zi*, *wu*, *mao*, and *you* among the twelve earthly branches, dividing the four seasons’ boundaries. Additionally, the virtue of earth traverses the ends of the four seasons, giving rise to the forms of dragon and tiger, metal and wood. Not only are the four seasons marked, but within the twelve branches, the twenty-four qi and seventy-two hou further symbolize the yearly qi-number.

If the clepsydra’s marks are off by a hair’s breadth, the sun and moon lose their measure, the new and full moons become uneven, day and night unequal. If the yang fire exceeds its mark, water and drought become unbalanced, and midwinter transforms into scorching summer. If the yin tally-sign loses its rhythm, cold and warmth clash, and midsummer reverses into thick frost. In the golden palace, cinnabar and mercury fail to sprout; in the jade cauldron, insects and pests arise. In major cases, mountains collapse and the earth splits, the metal tiger and wood dragon boil over. In minor cases, violent rains and fierce winds arise, the Kan man and Li woman flee, the metal tiger and wood dragon—east and west’s souls and spirits—scatter. The Kan man and Li woman are the husband and wife of south and north. If the filial child scatters, the true mercury within the womb is disrupted by misaligned firing times, flying away without rest, even causing the imperial cauldron’s mouth to burst forth in distant lands.

Whether auspicious or ominous, rising or falling, these and the above transformations all stem from the cultivator’s own heart. If one can model the celestial mechanism, weigh the sun and moon, carefully consider harmonization, and with utmost sincerity manage the firing times, summoning harmonious qi within the cauldron to produce pure yang, enabling the true master within the womb to generate pure Kun—this is called true mercury.

When movement and stillness follow their constant principles, bending and stretching, reversing and repeating—all in the operation of water and fire—movement and stillness must adhere to their proper times, enabling the qi of dragon and tiger to depend on one another. Like mother and child, they cherish and long for each other; the Five Phases guard their boundaries, circulating through the four seasons without deficit or excess, returning and reversing without loss—thus success may be witnessed daily.

Hidden and submerged, concealed and obscure, they serve as the guiding principles of the Dao. Whether concealed or manifest, active or latent, they form the root of all transformations and the mother of all generation and completion. Hence, they are called the guiding principles of the Dao.

From non-being, one governs being. When Kan and Li vanish, that which is "non-being" is the dragon; that which is "being" is the tiger. "Non-being" is the yang qi of mercury; "being" is the yin essence of lead. Lead and mercury reside within the empty vessel yet cannot spontaneously generate transformation. Through the ascent and descent of Kan and Li, the revolving of the four seasons, generation and completion become manifest—this is using the empty vessel to govern being with non-being.

The *Dragon-Tiger Classic* states: "Being and non-being restrain each other. When the Vermilion Bird flames in emptiness, yin arises and yang retreats; when yang stirs, yin conceals. As one wanes and the other waxes, Li and Kan vanish and reappear with the seasons."

Words are not carelessly fabricated. The workings of wu and ji are not idly invented by the sage to delude later generations. Thus, he cites the verification of the sun and moon, the efficacy of spirit and numen, the division of hardness and softness, the explication of metal and water, the analogy of dragon and tiger, the operation of yin and yang to gather essence, the completion of the work through the Five Soils, and the culmination in the four seasons—thereby obtaining the cyclical succession of blue, red, white, and black, all governed by wu and ji.

Kan wu is the essence of the moon—lunar yin. Wu is yang, representing yin containing yang, like metal born within water: the tiger. Li ji is the light of the sun—solar yang. Ji is yin, representing yang containing yin, like mercury born within fire: the dragon. Thus, in refining the elixir, one gathers the essence of the sun and moon, embraces the numinous qi of yin and yang, and when the cycle of stellar revolutions is complete and the movement of yin and yang concludes, all returns in merit to the virtue of earth, and spirit and essence are perfected.

The character *guan* (觀) is structured to resemble the sun and moon. Between the dark and new moon, at the moment when metal and water unite—after *gen* and before *yin*—yin and yang converge. As the yang tally retreats, metal and water share a common origin, merging in primordial chaos, male and female harmonizing, flowing metal moistening, fluids nourishing the mother’s womb, giving birth to the numinous and unfathomable. This enables the body to be at peace, the essence to be concealed, the form to be hidden, the substance to be stored, and the radiance of spirit to gradually emerge.

From the northeast, turning right, it encounters the new moon and revives, rising from the Dipper and Winnowing Basket, revolving to the west, spewing forth sprouts, gradually striving for brightness—though still within the concealment of yin—preparing to restore its former fullness. Thus, in the northeast, the *gen* region, it follows the position of inception and completion, the reawakening of the hidden dragon.

By analogy, when the tally reaches between *xu* and *hai*, the metal and water within the cauldron resemble the dark and new moon, nearing the *zi* tally’s inception. The *gen* position is not far from *yin*, hence it is said to "spew forth sprouts." Like the metal mother during the dark and new moon, metal and water flourish. At the first day of the month, encountering the *zi* tally’s single yang fire, metal meets fire and regains brilliance, revolving left to sprout and gradually generate the dragon’s body.

Thus, the following text states: "Gradual transformation in prosperity and decline ultimately returns to the origin."

Above the Pleiades and Hyades, the arousal of *zhen* serves as evidence. When the moon first emerges in the west above the Pleiades and Hyades, it proceeds with the yang fire. At *zi* and *chou*, the circulation of qi, completing one revolution every five days, begins here. The first yang line is born, the initial stirring of *zhen* qi, the inception within the cauldron. From this, yang qi gradually arises, and after three cycles of five days—fifteen days in total—pure *qian* is completed, and the body is fully formed.

"Yang is established in three" refers to the fire proceeding for three days, the initial spread of yang qi within the cauldron—hence "three days of *zhen* arousal." "Yin communicates in eight" means that after two five-day periods, two yang lines are born. By the eighth day, *dui* metal is activated, the qi of metal and water stabilizes, belonging to the upper trigram *kan*, with metal and water communicating—hence "eight days of *dui* progression."

"At nine-two, the dragon appears, peaceful and luminous" refers to the moon reaching the upper *kan*, its form half-complete, like the metal and water within the cauldron receiving the *yin* and *mao* tallies, with yin and yang each occupying half.

"Three and five, virtue is accomplished" refers to *qian* progressing to nine-three, reaching *chen* and *si*, precisely three five-day periods—fifteen days in total—with all three lines transformed into pure yang, the *qian* body fully formed. Thereafter, yin and yang divide, hence it is said: "Gradual transformation in prosperity and decline ultimately returns to the origin," cycling endlessly.

*Xun* succeeds in governing yin. At nine-four, with the yang line retreating and one yin line transforming, *xun* thus receives and leads the yin tally, proceeding for five days above *wu* and *wei*. The soft line gradually advances, yin qi revolving and arising, enveloping the yang essence, preventing any reckless movement. After four five-day periods, two yin lines are born. *Gen* proceeds for five periods, the tally reaching *shen* and *you*, encountering the lower *kan*—the twenty-third day—when metal and water are again balanced.

At nine-five, the flying dragon ascends to the celestial position, adding joy. At six five-day periods—thirty days—*kun* governs the yin tally, reaching *xu* and *hai*, when the qi of *qian* and *kun* are complete, the yin and yang of dragon and tiger, and the revolutions of the myriad things. Hence, it is said: "Nine-five adds joy."

At six-five, *kun* bears and binds, encompassing the beginning and end, nurturing all offspring as the mother of *guan*. When *kun* first transforms one line into yang, it becomes *zhen*, the eldest son of the *qian* household. When all three lines transform into pure yang, *qian* is formed. When yang reaches its extreme, yin is born, returning to the *qian* body, transforming one line into yin to become *xun*, the eldest daughter of the *kun* household. When all three lines transform into pure yin, *kun* is formed. From beginning to end, emergence and concealment all proceed from the *kun* body, nurturing the myriad things as the eternal mother of *guan*. All offspring are the various hexagrams.

Thus, six-five *kun* bears and binds, completing the cyclical return of the elixir, serving as the compass and square of the Dao.

"Using nine, fluttering and light" means that when yang reaches its extreme, it reveals its mandate and retreats—thus, having exhausted its number, it turns and interacts.

The revolving jade armillary follows the celestial mechanism, serving as the ancestral origin of the *Yi*. As stated above, *qian* and *kun* are the compass and square of the Dao. To follow the principles of yin and yang, one must adhere to the movements of the Dipper, with transformations fully expressed in the six lines, circulating without fixed position—this is the ancestral origin of the *Yi* Dao. It analogizes the operation of Kan and Li’s qi within the cauldron, circulating through the six voids within the image, its profound subtleties concealed and latent—this is the ancestral origin of the medicine.

At the new moon’s dawn, it is *Fu*. Five yin and one yang: yin qi has reached its extreme, and yang qi returns upward. The six soft lines of the lower trigram first transform into one hard line—this is the omen of the birth of one yang, hence the yang fire begins at the initial *zi*.

The common people receive constancy. At the new moon’s dawn, it is *Fu*: five yin lines and one yang line, with yin qi at its extreme and yang qi returning. This is the hexagram of the six soft lines’ lower trigram first transforming into one hard line—the sign of the birth of one yang—hence the yang fire begins at the initial *zi*.

At the inception of yang qi, the pitch corresponds to *Huangzhong*; within the cauldron, it receives the protective yang qi, laying the foundation for cinnabar and mercury. With five yin and one yang, yang as the master disperses qi to flow without obstruction—this is called "entering and exiting without hindrance."

The *Yi* says: *Fu* returns; returning is its Way. Is this not the manifestation of Heaven and Earth’s heart? It illustrates that within the cauldron of Heaven and Earth, the generation of the myriad things begins here, corresponding to the advance of yang fire in the eleventh month, *zi*.

Approaching the furnace to apply the firing regimen, adjusting the balance—*Lin* is the hexagram of four yin and two yang, symbolizing the progression of *chou* fire as yang virtue gradually advances, illuminating the cauldron. After the winter solstice, the days lengthen, and yang qi increasingly spreads, engendering spirit and essence. The correspondence with *Dalü* and the adjustment of balance refer to the gradual coagulation of metal and water, with flowing pearls forming above—this aligns with the advance of yang fire in the twelfth month, *chou*.

Raising it to form *Tai*, moving in accord with the season—*Tai* is the hexagram of three yin and three yang, where yin and yang qi balance, and husband and wife unite, gradually foreshadowing the essence of dragon and tiger, flourishing gold and mercury. Thus it is said: "The small departs, the great arrives." The *Yi* says: "There is no level that does not slope, no going that does not return"—this is the Way of yin and yang’s mutual succession, corresponding to the advance of yang fire in the first month, *yin*.

Progressing through *Dazhuang*, day and night begin to divide—*Dazhuang* is the hexagram of two yin and four yang, signifying that in mid-spring, though yang qi is abundant, it still contains yin qi, and the path of yin is about to depart. Hence, the elm pods fall following the yang. "Punishment and virtue contend" refers to the second and eighth months, the times when yin and yang divide their positions: yang is virtue, and virtue engenders the myriad things; yin is punishment, and punishment causes the myriad things to perish. Thus, in the second month, yang contains yin, and yin qi assaults things, so in mid-spring, the elm pods fall—this symbolizes that gold and sand, stirred by residual yin qi, descend into the womb. Hence, it is called "returning to the root." In the eighth month, yin contains yang, and yang qi arises, so in mid-autumn, the shepherd’s purse and wheat regenerate—this symbolizes that metal and water, nourished by residual yang qi, fill the chamber with fluid. Hence, it is called "renewed flourishing." The following *Guan* hexagram states: "At the juncture where punishment and virtue contend, yin and yang balance"—this corresponds to the cessation of the tally in the second month, *mao*.

*Guai*: yin retreats, shaking off the dust of the past—*Guai* is the hexagram of one yin and five yang, where yang ascends and yin retreats. Though yang qi is abundant, residual protective yin qi remains within the cauldron, which the *chen* fire scours, allowing the gold and sand to be cleansed, their wings shaking off the dust of the past. This corresponds to the advance of yang fire in the third month, *chen*.

*Qian*’s vigorous brilliance, centered and mutually engaging—The *Yi* says: "Great indeed is *Qian*’s primal power—firm, strong, centered, upright, pure, and refined." *Qian*’s primal power is the resource from which the myriad things begin. From the winter solstice’s birth of one yang to pure yang, clouds drift and rain falls, and the forms of things flow forth. This illustrates that within the cauldron, from the eleventh month’s reception of the tally to the fourth month, when all six lines transform into pure *Qian*, the resource is complete. Thus, the metal embryo encounters the fire of true yang, and metal, meeting fire, becomes a vessel, increasingly radiant, widely spreading to the four neighbors, filling the cauldron’s chamber. This corresponds to the advance of yang fire in the fourth month, *si*. "Centered and mutually engaging" means that when yang reaches its extreme, yin is born—referring to the following text’s "yin *Qian* and yang virtue."

*Gou* begins the sequence, with yin as the master—The *Yi* says: *Gou* is encounter. With five yang and one yin, yin arises and yang retreats; yin and yang divide their boundaries, and dragon and tiger mate, hence their encounter. It also says: "Tied to the metal *ni*, steadfast and auspicious"—the one yin first sings, tethered to the *wu* hour, transforming until extreme yin. "Treading frost first" means that when one yin is born, it must reach extreme yin; just as where there is protective frost, there must be solid ice. The "cold spring at the well’s bottom" refers to five yang below and one yin, where yin qi has not yet spread, and though the five yang are many, yin is now in charge. In all hexagrams, six lines with five yin and one yang take yang as master; five yang and one yin take yin as master. The many are mastered by the few, hence it is said: "yin is the master." This means that within the cauldron, the metal mother is originally the essence of supreme yin water; when it first receives yin qi’s protective signal, metal and water briefly express their nature, and one must know that solid ice and extreme yin are approaching. This corresponds to the retreat of the yin tally in the fifth month, *wu*.

Withdrawing from the world and relinquishing position, dwelling in obscurity—*Dun* is the hexagram of four yang and two yin, where yin qi gradually strengthens and yang qi gradually weakens. Yang withdraws from its position, gathering true essence to await what is to come. Residual yang dwells in yin, gradually nearing obstruction, hence it dwells in obscurity. The *Yi* says: "Things cannot forever withdraw, hence they await their time." This illustrates that within the cauldron, the essence of the red dragon is governed by yin, gradually merging with metal and water to nurture the maiden, then gathering true essence to follow the transformations of yin and yang. This corresponds to the retreat of the yin tally in the sixth month, *wei*.

*Pi*: blocked and impassable, the name of yang submerged—*Pi* is the hexagram of three yin and three yang, where Heaven and Earth both rest, yin and yang do not unite, and the myriad things do not sprout. The metal mother in the central palace naturally coils and unfurls in tranquility. This corresponds to the retreat of the yin tally in the seventh month, *shen*.

Observing its measure, born through covering—*Guan* is the hexagram of two yang and four yin, where yin qi is already abundant, akin to mid-autumn’s sentiment. Within the cauldron, metal, receiving yin qi, unites with water, transforming and engendering the maiden—quicksilver. Yet yin qi, though in use, still leaves residual yang harmony within the cauldron, which in mid-autumn revives the shepherd’s purse and wheat. Hence, it is said: "The old and withered flourish again." "Born through covering" means born everywhere. This illustrates that metal and water, bearing the yin tally in mid-autumn, nurture the numinous mercury to fill the womb. The second and eighth months, when yin and yang’s punishment and virtue contend, were annotated in the above *Dazhuang* hexagram. This corresponds to the cessation of the tally in the eighth month, *you*.

*Bo*: decaying limbs, the utmost spirit lost—The *Yi* says: *Bo* is flaying. With one yang and five yin, yin flourishes and yang declines; the soft encroaches upon the hard, and both metal and water rest. The limbs dissolve, and their merit returns to earth’s virtue. This corresponds to the retreat of the yin tally in the ninth month, *xu*. "The utmost spirit lost" [erroneously interpreted as *Kun*, not recorded].

When the Way is exhausted, it returns—*Qian* begins with *Kun*’s six yielding lines; beneath the nine earths, one firm line transforms—this is called the birth of one yang. Yang qi begins here, transforming until six changes yield pure *Qian*, its body complete. When pure yang qi is sufficient, yang reaches its extreme, and yin is born. Beneath *Qian*’s six firm lines, one yielding line transforms—this is called the birth of one yin, transforming until six changes yield pure *Kun* again. Hence, it is said: "When the Way is exhausted, it returns"—to *Kun*’s primal power. *Kun* is the Earth Mother.

The yin is so, and *Qian* is the Heavenly Father; the yang is so. Thus, yin attains yang and gives birth, yang attains yin and achieves completion—this is what is called the Dao of one yin and one yang. It bends to form the myriad things without omission, hence it is said: "Eternally following earth’s principle while bearing Heaven’s proclamation." This is likened to one year’s twelve months, one day’s twelve hours, the movement of yin and yang’s advance and retreat, the fire tally aligning with the essential qi of *Qian*, *Kun*, *Kan*, and *Li*, cycling endlessly with inexhaustible subtlety. Thus, the sacred maiden and numinous youth conjoin yin and yang within the divine chamber, soaring dragons and crouching tigers unite *gou*-soul and *po*-soul within the mother’s womb. Thereby, divine transformations know no bounds, and what is born is pure and perfect.

Though the profound and distant stretch across a thousand ages, ever abiding, Heaven and Earth may seem remote, yet when the sun and moon exchange qi in accordance with their celestial motions, they remain interconnected, nurturing the seeds of the myriad things as the progenitors of yin and yang. When yin and yang unite their essences in the vast expanse of Heaven and Earth, amidst the boundless and indistinct, none can discern their comings and goings.

This is likened to the cauldron and vessel, which model *Qian* and *Kun* as father and mother, turning the axle of *Kan* and *Li* as male and female within the cauldron’s boundless and indistinct space—none can perceive their transformations or traces. Metal is the root of the elixir within the cauldron. Initially, it is profound yellow, undifferentiated, its path lost and confused. Next, yin and yang divide and judge. Finally, the benevolent ruler and the five phases interact, returning without fail. *Pi* and *Tai* alternate, yin and yang mutually depend, beginning with *Fu* and ending with *Kun*, rising at the new moon and ceasing at the dark moon. Entering the tally, exiting the fire, augmenting water, pacifying metal, moving fire, halting water—this principle resembles a cycle. Within, the images of metal and water nurture one another, just as the ruler of men does with the four seasons. The tally fire increases and decreases without missing its proper time, and through a thousand autumns, dragon and tiger endure.

The chief of governance begins with the one who directs the tally fire, establishing the initial sequence. Once metal is cast and the cauldron vessel is renewed, the old is transformed—then the seals are secured and the defenses made tight. To unfold and spread the treasure is to allow the inner metal to flow freely, its fluids abundant, metal and water interdependent.

Again, it follows the handle of the Dipper, traversing the twelve hours, ascending in harmony with the five planets amid the four sevens (the twenty-eight lunar mansions). If the weft of the seasons becomes disordered and the tally’s rhythm does not align, fortune and misfortune arise from the hexagrams’ images, and the tally fire falters at dawn or dusk. Thus, the outer five planets fall into disarray, and the inner five viscera lose harmony. If the four sevens deviate, the circumpolar stars scatter and disperse, and within the cauldron, nothing comes to fruition. Though the outer fire moves, if the inner tally remains still and unresponsive, the celestial soul and earthly soul do not conjoin. Thereby, the stars and planets fall into chaos, the sun and moon diverge, the four seasons lose order, and the myriad things fail to grow—all due to the tally fire’s mismanagement in summer and autumn, causing the cauldron’s interior to suffer irregular drought and flood.

The *Wenchang* governs the records: the six stars of the Dipper’s bowl, called the *Wenchang* Palace—first is the Supreme General, second the Secondary General, third the Noble Minister, fourth the Director of Destinies, fifth the Director of Emoluments, and sixth the Director of Calamities. The supporting stars are the six beneath the bowl, paired two by two, called the Three Terraces, which oversee the world. Above, they assist the Son of Heaven in regulating yin and yang and harmonizing the four seasons; below, they ensure the myriad things attain their proper state, enabling ministers and grandees to fulfill their duties. This mirrors the cauldron’s reception of Heaven and Earth’s qi of the myriad things, giving rise to transformations and generation.

When yin and yang are discordant and the four seasons lose their measure, it is like the one who directs the tally fire failing to adjust properly—hence, it is called reproof. The secret of the golden liquor’s return to cinnabar lies in the two words "lead" and "fire," which mark its beginning and end. Though true lead is obtained, true fire is difficult to attain—how can it be lightly discussed?

Thus, Master Wei broadly illustrated it, yet still feared later generations would be misled.

At the origin and essential end, the state follows the harm-free Dao. *[The two sentences "At the origin..." are misinterpreted and thus not recorded.]*

The divine chamber’s yin essence resides in the Central Palace, abiding in the virtue of Earth and symbolizing the ruler. Like the cauldron’s inner response, it does not act independently. If the ruler grows arrogant and indolent, tribute from the four directions will fail to arrive; if ministers turn treacherous and deceitful, the timing of celestial movements will falter—eclipses, waxing and waning, new and full moons, all omens and misfortunes will be blamed on the ruler. The ruler is the golden essence and Earth’s virtue, the divine chamber itself. Thus, the alchemical maiden flees, the Red Dragon bolts, and the divine essence departs—what gold liquor can then be sought?

The "Pole Star receiving its proper position, at ease and yielding to those below" refers to the divine embryo dwelling in the Central Palace, likening the ruler to the Pole Star in the Bright Hall. The qi of yin and yang and the Five Agents are the ministers. When ruler and ministers govern within as the Pole Star stands at heaven’s center, the qi of yin and yang and the Five Agents harmonize, and the gold and water liquors within the cauldron naturally generate transformations.

Chen Zhixu said: This passage alone clarifies the four—yin, yang, essence, and *qi*. What are they? *Qian* is firm, *Kun* is yielding. The *Yi* says: *Qian* is the yang thing; *Kun* is the yin thing. Yin and yang unite in virtue, and firmness and softness take form, embodying heaven and earth’s design and penetrating the numinous power of the gods. Thus, *Qian*’s virtue is firm, strong, centered, upright, pure, and unadulterated; *Kun*’s virtue is soft, yielding, beneficial, and steadfast—where the noble person’s conduct lies. The union of *Qian* and *Kun* is the Dao of the golden elixir.

Therefore, *Qian*’s yang virtue governs bestowal, while *Kun*’s yin virtue specializes in containment and reception. Male and female rely on each other, depending on essence and *qi*. Essence and *qi* spread and extend. The *Yi* says: Essence and *qi* as things are the wandering soul and transformation. As things, they follow the natural course to generate humans and creatures; as transformation, they reverse their use to achieve Buddhahood or immortality.

What are *Kan* and *Li* at the head? When *Qian*’s firmness interacts with *Kun*, *Qian* becomes hollow within and forms *Li*; when *Kun*’s softness receives *Qian*, *Kun* becomes solid within and forms *Kan*. Thus, *Kan* and *Li* follow *Qian* and *Kun* to head the yin and yang, attaining the proper balance of firmness and softness. *Li*’s center holds the sun’s light; *Kan*’s center holds the moon’s radiance, casting their brilliance below. Profound and subtle, they are difficult to fathom, impossible to depict. Only the sage can measure and assist, narrating their primordial foundation.

What are the four? The primordial chaos—yin, yang, essence, and *qi*—encompassed within the void’s aperture, unfolding as the sixty-four hexagrams to form the chariot. *Kun* is the ox, the chariot; *Qian* is the dragon, the horse. When *Qian* and *Kun* unite in virtue, dragon and horse are harnessed, the world is at peace, and the enlightened ruler governs the times. The golden liquor’s return to cinnabar follows the same Dao—why? The dragon is the wood of the east, mercury; the horse is the cinnabar fire of the south. Dragon and horse obtain the west’s metal tiger to generate the north’s *Xuanwu* water. Thus, essence and *qi* congeal and unite to form the body, completing the harness.

When harmonious, they follow the level path without deviation. The enlightened ruler’s governance is like traveling the great road, not burdening the people. Harmonious *qi* responds to the times. If even slightly off the proper path, or if treacherous ministers obscure the worthy and envy the capable, the state will be imperiled. In cultivation, harmony comes first. When harmonious, all affairs follow the heart’s intent. The *Yi* says: Harmony is *Dui*’s auspicious conduct, without doubt. Once affairs are harmonious, the mind must be rectified, intentions made sincere, and vigilance maintained—then no peril or ruin will occur. Can one not be cautious of even the slightest deviation in the elixir?

The noble person in his chamber... *[The passage "The noble person in his chamber..." to "repeated reversals" is the most critical initial safeguard in entering the chamber, with meticulous refinement of the elixir’s difficulty, and so on.]* The sage uses the noble person as a metaphor. The *Yi*’s striking words say: When the noble person in his chamber speaks well, those a thousand *li* away respond—how much more those nearby? The Dao is fundamentally wordless; without words, how can it be revealed? The wordless are those of virtue, whose speech cannot be careless. How much more so for those who practice the Dao and refine the elixir?

The immortal elder cites the ancient sage’s words and expands upon them: The lord of ten thousand chariots in his nine-layered chamber issues decrees and commands, aligning with yin and yang’s seasons. Seeking the elixir must follow yin and yang’s seasonal nodes—when the first yang returns, store the vessel and await the time, not violating the hexagram’s month. Gathering the medicine must wait for the moon’s appearance: *Zhen*’s birth is the third day’s *geng* emergence; *Zhun*’s *zi* and *shen* are water’s flourishing positions; *Yong*’s *yin* and *xu* are fire’s storage positions. Each of the sixty-four hexagrams has its day—here, only *Zhun* and *Yong* are mentioned as examples. Those unable to exhaustively comprehend are the immortal elder’s admission of incomplete mastery, for the profound mysteries require oral transmission. The scripture says: These are not ordinary words of the world; only the perfected sage who has attained the Way and penetrated the subtle can fully grasp their meaning. Those who cannot exhaustively comprehend lack the innate capacity of the supreme sage—how can they practice this Dao and become perfected?

Establishing laws and punishments prevents deceit; applying benevolence and virtue entices joy. Resistance brings misfortune; compliance brings fortune. Following the regulations, decrees must be issued with utmost sincerity, and actions must be focused and discreet.

Vigilantly awaiting the days and hours: a year depends on one month, a month on one day, a day on one hour, an hour on one tally. Carefully observing the waxing and waning: one must know the water’s clarity and turbidity generated by *Dui*’s metal, its proportions. If even the slightest deviation or impurity occurs, blame and misfortune will harm the elixir crucible. Such errors cause yin and yang to diverge, the solstices to shift—midwinter becomes scorching summer, midsummer turns to bitter frost, the spring and autumn equinoxes lose their balance, day and night’s timekeeping fails, rain and drought go awry, and strange omens multiply. All these portents, like the above misfortunes, are metaphors for a single error in tending the furnace leading to a hundred mistakes, all stemming from insufficient self-cultivation. The foolish do not blame themselves but resent the elixir scriptures. Only those with great roots, a sincere heart, and true remorse, who redouble their efforts with diligence and unwavering focus, will naturally gain celestial aid. In all undertakings, they will succeed.

This is like the filial son whose sincerity moves the imperial pole. The heart is the spirit’s dwelling; when the heart is true, the spirit naturally arrives. Words are the heart’s voice; when words are trustworthy, emotions and nature resonate. Though speech emerges from one’s mouth, its sound spreads far. Failure brings calamity; success brings fortune. Affairs gone awry may lead to war; affairs accomplished bring joy and great peace. Success and failure, order and chaos—all arise from the human heart. In movement and stillness, one must not act recklessly but follow the plumb line. Then the four seasons respond, *qi* seeks its counterpart, firmness and softness harmonize, and the Five Agents align. The Dao of the *Yi* circulates and reverses without discord.

The hidden and submerged, the concealed and secluded—this speaks of the workings of *wu* and *ji*. Here is discussed the Dao of yin and yang, their *qi*: sometimes yin lies hidden within the body, sometimes yang sinks into concealment within the limbs. They transform and reside at the center, enveloping the myriad things, or vigorously bestow life and death to serve as the guiding principles of the Dao. If not for the dual functions of Qian and Kun, the subtle principles of the Dao, the Dao might well be obstructed and cease to flow.

The regulation of being by non-being, the utility of the formless—why do emptiness and being stand as two? The division of the Supreme Polarity yields precelestial and postcelestial. What is precelestial? That which is above form is called the Dao: it enters being from non-being. What is postcelestial? That which is below form is called the vessel: it enters being from non-being.

Laozi said: “The nameless is the beginning of heaven and earth; the named is the mother of the myriad things.” Hai Chan said: “From non-being to being is ever thus; from being to non-being, one may guard against it.” By inferring the waxing and waning of Kan and Li, all postcelestial things become vessels—formed, turbid, and subject to arising and perishing. They are not like the precelestial, which possesses the enduring Dao that allows one to ascend to the realm of the sages.

Confucius said: “Preceding heaven, heaven does not oppose; following heaven, one accords with the seasons.” Ordinary people only follow the postcelestial Dao, thus they are born and die in an unending cycle. The sage skillfully employs the precelestial Dao in reverse, hence through extending knowledge, investigating things, rectifying the heart, and cultivating the self, one attains everlasting existence without extinction. Counting what has passed is following; knowing what is to come is reversing. Thus the Dao of the *Yi* is the reversal of numbers.

“Words are not carelessly composed”: Among the books of the various masters and hundred schools, some are skillfully written, others clumsily—what does this matter?
