This is the ancestral book of the Dao; one must not recklessly insert a single word, lest later generations be misled. This is what is meant by “words are not carelessly composed.”

“Discourses are not idly produced”: The immortal elders modeled themselves on the Three Sages, aligning with yin and yang and emulating the sun and moon. Moreover, they jointly composed this book as a ladder and raft for the world. This is what is meant by “discourses are not idly produced.”

What is meant by “citing verification and seeing effects”? In ancient times, the Yellow Emperor ascended to heaven; Chao and Xu withdrew to the mountains; Laozi transformed the Hu and became a Buddha; in Huainan, even the chickens and dogs became immortals. These are all examples of “citing verification and seeing effects.”

What is meant by “measuring and discerning the numinous”? It is like the sun and moon conjoining as one, their brilliance manifesting in *gengzi*. “Making it numinous and bright depends on the person.” The structuring of graphs—such as *dan* (丹) arising from *yue* (月), the water image of the Kan trigram, and the sun and moon as the head of the *Yi*’s completion of the Dao—this is the structuring of graphs.

What is meant by “tracing principles as evidence”? When yin reaches its extreme, yang arises; when yang reaches its extreme, yin arises. Following yin and yang produces humans and living things; reversing their use necessarily completes the golden elixir. This is the original principle.

Kan *wu* is the essence of the moon: the northern position is Kan, within which lies true earth—this is yang earth. The Woman constellation presides over it, with yin concealed and yang essence. *Wu* serves as its gate, the lunar hub’s domain, where the hornless hare dwells—white within, black without. This is yang within yin, outwardly female yet inwardly male.

Li *ji* is the light of the sun: the southern position is Li, within which lies true earth—this is yin earth. The Willow constellation presides over it, with yang submerged and yin light. *Ji* serves as its door, the solar wheel’s abode, where the three-legged crow resides—black within, white without. This is yin within yang, inwardly female yet outwardly male.

The ancients took the sun and moon as the character *yi* (易), for *yi* is precisely yin and yang. Yet when one speaks of yin and yang, *yi* is not seen; when one speaks of *yi*, yin and yang become visible.

Moreover, without earth, the myriad things cannot sprout and grow, and the sun and moon especially rely on earth to gestate. Thus, when the sun moves east, it passes through Di earth; when it moves west, it passes through Stomach earth; when it travels south, it passes through Willow earth; when it nurtures in the north, it passes through Woman earth. The sun and moon obtain earth and thus endure in brightness; earth borrows the sun and moon to enrich its virtue. The earth’s dominion cycles through the four seasons: spring’s birth and summer’s growth are earth’s work; autumn’s gathering and winter’s closure are earth’s power. Therefore, each of the four seasons has its ruling days, and earth abides at the center, beginning and ending, weaving its functions. Though green, red, white, and black each reside in the east, west, south, and north, they all rely on the two earths of *wu* and *ji* to jointly perfect their virtue and enact numinous transformations.

This speaks of yin and yang’s positions, each residing in its place. All their uses must borrow from earth. There is no talk of dragon and tiger, lead and mercury, or the gathering of golden water. Thus, earlier the text spoke of arranging yin and yang’s paired positions, and later it states that each resides in its own direction—this is the marvelous order of its language.

From the dark moon to the new moon: This section takes the dark and new moons, waxing and full moons of a single month to compare the practice of elixir refinement, where lines and tally marks align. In a year, there are twelve dark and new moons, waxing and full moons. In heaven, the Greater Yin has twelve conjunctions with the sun; in the human realm, the Lesser Yin has twelve conjunctions with the physical form—this is the norm of yin and yang.

Only the Lesser Yin—profound, boundless, and immeasurable—can the sage measure, roaming leisurely within the Supreme Polarity before attempting to align the tally marks and begin the practice at the center. Thus it is called precelestial. Heaven’s Greater Yin each time conjoins with the sun: when the moon is below the sun and the sun above the moon, the moon receives the sun’s essence and light, its light facing heaven, unseen by people. This is like the union of man and woman, the man above and the woman below, the woman covered by the man and thus unseen. At this dark moon, the moon is above the sun, its radiance undivided—comparable to the human realm’s Lesser Yin.

At the time of the Supreme Polarity’s chaos, within the precelestial primordial vastness, when the tally marks are exhausted and the third day’s *geng* arrives, yang is about to be born. Yang male and yin female follow one another, uniting within, their fluids moistening and naturally bestowing transformation, flowing and circulating. Heaven and earth’s numinousness cannot be measured.

As for the great Dao, without the resources of sages and worthies, one cannot put it into motion. The *Yi* says: “Match its virtue with heaven and earth, its brightness with the sun and moon, its sequence with the four seasons, its auspiciousness and inauspiciousness with gods and spirits.” This is the numinous marvel of the golden elixir. Though heaven and earth cannot fathom it, gods and spirits cannot guess its measure—why? The golden elixir is the *qi* of the precelestial. The sage skillfully matches virtue with heaven and earth, reverses the workings of creation to generate this *qi*. The sage can match brightness with the sun and moon, invert the workings to preserve this *qi*. The sage cleverly matches the sequence of the four seasons, employing this *qi* so that cold and heat do not constrain one another. The sage subtly matches the auspiciousness and inauspiciousness of gods and spirits to complete this *qi*, so that ghosts and spirits reveal their forms. Thus, even heaven and earth’s numinousness cannot be measured—only the sage is capable.

What does it mean to “utilize it to secure the body and conceal the form”? It is the Dao of Kun. The function of Kun: within Kun’s palace lies earth, which governs Kan water. It is good at benefiting the myriad things without contention—this is the use of water.

The sage, by inverting its use, thereby attains water’s benefit—the *Yi*’s sixfold utility in perpetually upholding steadfastness is Kun’s virtue. It begins in the northeast, in the region of Jidou. What does this mean? Each new moon necessarily conjoins with the sun in the region of Jidou. Jidou belongs to Gen; Gen as a hexagram is yin encroaching upon yang. It is called the “spirit-path.” The moon, whenever it reaches this region, loses its brightness—hence it is said, “losing its companion.” This resembles the worldly cycle of the Five Phases: birth, aging, sickness, death, and the alternation of cold and heat.

When revolving rightward, it is the Way of Heaven: leftward motion. The sun, moon, and stars all revolve rightward. When the moon reaches this region, it must darken and conceal itself, appearing like a jade tablet or a jade scepter. On the first and second days, revolving rightward to the geng direction, its essence and light begin to emerge, and its *po* is born. When the moon reaches the Bi and Mao constellations, the moon borrows the sun’s light, which shines forth below like the initial yang of Qian. Beneath Kun lies the image of Zhen. On the evening of the third day, one yang and two yins—Qian’s eldest son—attains its time and enacts the Way, hence the third day is “Zhen’s movement.” On the eighth day, the upper *kan* is one yin and two yangs—Kun’s youngest daughter, Dui, receives the fire of ding and acts in Kun’s stead to govern the affair. Yin and yang harmonize, and the numinous is born—hence it is said, “on the eighth day, Dui acts.”

At the fifteenth day, it is the full moon, fully yang and radiant—hence it is said, “on the fifteenth, virtue is accomplished, and the Qian body is complete.” This resembles the upper line of the hexagram, akin to the golden elixir within the crucible, where metal and water, the Great Yin, reflect the sun to generate essence and *po*. The human body mirrors the moon to generate the golden elixir. Within the crucible lies the elixir; one must remain vigilant as if facing peril. When full, be cautious of overflow; when abundant, fear decline. Only the sage can maintain vigilance from beginning to end. Xun continues its lineage, firmly securing and carefully nurturing it, slowly circulating the yin tally to envelop the yang qi. Gen governs advance and cessation; when the tally is full, it descends to the lower *kan*, gradually congealing and solidifying. The celestial position is attained with joy. Lead and mercury are drawn forth; when lead is exhausted, mercury is dried. At six-five, Kun receives and binds, encompassing beginning and end. Lead and mercury are both transformed—the golden elixir is complete. Though one month illustrates its practice, the essential work requires ten months to approximate completion. When the work is fulfilled, three years suffice; when the merit is lofty, twelve years are needed. It depends on one’s accumulated practice.

As for the great sage who re-creates yin and yang, aligning emotion with nature, inverting and uniting them to establish a new crucible and forge the Nine Cauldrons’ great elixir—it is like the celestial pivot being re-established at zi. Comparing the twelve lines to a month’s cycle: from darkness to the new moon, the new moon reaches dawn, and the initial yang stirs again in the Fu hexagram. Hence it is said, “Thus, there is no constant position—this is the ancestral root of the *Yi*.”

At the dawn of the new moon, it is Fu, enduring for a thousand ages. The preceding speaks of the moon’s phases from darkness to fullness, illustrating the refinement of the elixir. This section compares it to the twelve months’ work and practice. The initial attainment of the elixir corresponds to the Fu hexagram—Fu is one yang concealed beneath five yins. When sages and worthies practice this Way, they transcend the mundane and enter sagehood; if wicked persons practice it, they lose their lives and forfeit their bodies. Thus, King Wen said: “When companions come, there is no blame. Returning and reversing the Way, in seven days it returns—beneficial to have somewhere to go.” “Companions coming” means companions arriving from afar. “No blame” means benefit without harm. “Returning and reversing the Way” refers to the practice of the *Yi*’s Way—acting in inversion. “In seven days it returns” means the moon conceals its light for seven days before shining again, just like a person. After seven days of movement, yang is initially born—“beneficial to have somewhere to go” means advancing auspiciously without loss. The *Eastern Capital Rhapsody* says, “The sun and moon conceal themselves in Longdou,” referring to yang’s imminent return. Confucius said, “Fu reveals the heart of Heaven and Earth.” The world does not know the heart of Heaven and Earth; Laozi called this heart the “gate of the Mysterious Female.” Master Fang referred to it as the “gate of life” and the “door of death.” The *Yi* says: “Thunder is within the earth—Fu. The former kings, on reaching this day, closed the passes; merchants and travelers did not proceed, and the lords did not inspect their domains.” Thunder is the firm yang; earth is the yielding yin. With firm yang within yielding yin, the primordial qi is restored. The former kings, practicing this Way on this day, closed the passes and did not inspect their domains, focusing their minds and dedicating their wills. Merchants and travelers represent miscellaneous distractions; not proceeding means cutting off such distractions to focus solely on the Way. Hence the *Xiang* says: “Not far off is the return—cultivate the self.” It also says: “The one in the middle acts alone in returning—to follow the Way.”

At the dawn of the new moon, it is Fu—the yang qi begins to flow. The sage’s heart is one. Fu means first concealing and then being able to return. The initial qi of yang enters and exits, communicating without harm. Establishing a marker, the subtle firmness—Qian moves directly. The Huangzhong pitch corresponds to the yang month, establishing zi. The masses are the multitude; all things begin to grow with this yang qi, all flourishing and manifest, spreading warmth. The common people, receiving this warmth, return to constancy. The multitude of the common people, receiving the warmth of the initial yang qi, all return to their constant Way. The marvel of elixir-making lies precisely here—do not treat it lightly.

Having received the master’s instructions in the Way, one must understand the Three Passes and Three Signs. Why? First, prepare the altar and platform, then gather the medicinal substances. Once obtained, the substances enter and exit, communicating. After refining, the work becomes gentle and warm, spreading and permeating warmth, penetrating directly—this is the First Pass and First Sign. Approaching and controlling the elixir crucible, applying the firing times, opening the path with intent, neither excessive nor reckless, harmonizing brilliance and light, becoming ever more humble and lowly. The Dalü pitch responds to the chou day, and the light gradually lengthens—this is the Middle Pass and Second Sign.

The Taicu pitch arrives, looking upward to complete Tai. Tai as a hexagram is earth above heaven. If yin is above, water can moisten below; if yang is below, fire can illuminate above. Thus, the *Xiang* of Xian says: “The yielding above and the firm below—these two qi respond and unite, resting in joy. The male descends, the female ascends—hence it is beneficial to be steadfast.” The Five Phases are inverted, and the seven treasures of heaven and earth: the yielding acts first, making others the host; the firm acts afterward, making oneself the guest. The male initially retreats slightly, while the female greatly advances. Metal qi requires each other; when yang is complete, the Qian body is whole. This is called “firm and yielding both flourishing, yin and yang uniting”—this is the Lower Pass and Third Sign.

Gradually passing through Dazhuang, the elixir congeals and returns. Flanking the mao gate, the elm pods return to the root. Punishment and virtue oppose each other; day and night begin to divide. The elixir’s sign falls within the Yellow Court—guard and nurture it with care and focus. Guai as a hexagram is yin deciding and separating; the yang qi has returned. The golden elixir is conceived; Qian is vigorous and radiant. The metal qi is now pure yang. At the end of si, the yin tally circulates. Gou receives and suddenly envelops the yang, preventing its escape. Yang regains yin, and yin becomes the host. Kun’s transformation: the first six, “treading frost,” is the cold spring at the well’s bottom—the yin qi descends. The sixth month is Dun, gathering essence and awaiting the time. Pi arrives in the seventh month—yin and yang do not communicate; yin extends while yang withdraws, the yang qi inwardly bright. The eighth month is Guan, observing the image and assessing autumn’s conditions, nurturing the young and tender. Like wheat sprouts, old and withered, they flourish again; like shepherd’s purse, the sprouts transform. When the qi is exhausted, the form is eroded. When the Way is exhausted, it returns to its origin.

The primal power of Kun moves in cycles of coming and going, ever compliant and receptive to Heaven. This work is composed with profound method: some sections begin with an introductory preface, others conclude with a summarizing closure; those lacking an introduction are encapsulated by their closing remarks, while those without a conclusion are framed by their opening lines. This particular passage, for instance, has no introductory preface but is instead encompassed by its closing summary. Its opening line states: "At the new moon’s dawn, it is Fu; Fu gives rise to Lin, Lin to Tai, Tai to Great Strength, Great Strength to Guai, Guai to Qian, Qian to Gou, Gou to Dun, Dun to Pi, Pi to Guan, Guan to Bo, and Bo to Kun." The conclusion, however, binds it thus: "profoundly obscure and boundlessly distant, yet separated yet interconnected." These two phrases are most succinct.

"Profoundly obscure" and "boundlessly distant" refer to the two entities of yin and yang: the former so profoundly deep as to elude grasp, the latter so immeasurably far as to defy conception. Yet their transformative efficacy is such that, though Heaven and Earth are sundered by vast distance, they remain linked as one. Between these two entities lies an interval—spanning myriad leagues in an instant—yet if the Yellow Matchmaker mediates their union, even the most distant becomes proximate. Thus, when these two entities align with the celestial measures, they nurture life and become the primal sages of yin and yang. The sage employs them to enact the Dao, though its vastness and elusiveness render it seemingly beyond grasp.

Those who first lose the track and later become sovereign lords do so through yielding compliance, smooth progress, and steadfast integrity—such is the path the noble person follows. First straying from the Way, then attaining constancy: these are the words of the Ten Wings. The immortal elder elaborates upon this, noting that yin becomes the host, and later, "becomes the sovereign lord"—both are the advantageous terrain of Kun, the Way of Earth.

There is no level ground without a slope: this is the natural principle of the Dao. Water, in its utmost levelness, pools to form a deep abyss; at the sloping banks, it does not flow. Yet if the slope is filled to its brim, the water naturally overflows. This analogy of water elucidates the natural principle of the Dao: transformation and renewal ever flourish, returning to Qian in due course. The waxing and waning follow one another, as Gou gives way to Kun. Thus it is said: "Kun ends and Fu begins," like tracing an unbroken ring.

If emperors and kings could uphold and govern the Dao of Qian and Kun, both in its reversal and compliance, then a lifespan of a thousand years would not be excessive. Should their merit tower and conduct shine, ascending to Heaven in broad daylight would be but their due. The foremost task of governance is to ensure the realm suffers no harm through the Dao. The immortal elder, using the difficulty of refining the elixir as a metaphor for governance, reveals the arduousness of quelling a rebellious populace. Just as refining the elixir makes one aware of the difficulty in tethering the "horse of the mind," so too does governance require shedding old stains and worldly defilements to renew all things—this is the primacy of governance. This is called "renewing the cauldron."

To curb anger and restrain desire, to emulate goodness and cultivate improvement—these are the foundations of self-cultivation.

This is called "transforming the old." Whether in governance or self-cultivation, one must first begin with oneself, exercising the utmost vigilance and meticulousness. By first regulating and thoroughly ordering oneself, governance will succeed and the body will be perfected. Confucius said: "Govern through virtue, and you will be like the North Star, abiding in its place while all other stars pay homage." Virtue is not merely a matter of governance—it is the source of all conduct. Can those who refine the elixir be without virtue?

What, then, is virtue? Benevolence, compassion, and love; clarity and sincerity—these are the marks of one of supreme virtue. Reverence, magnanimity, trustworthiness, diligence, and kindness—these are the gates to entering the Dao. For those who wish to refine the elixir, kindness and diligence must come first. Kindness suffices to employ others, and the immortal elder says: "To unfold and distribute treasure is to win the people’s joy externally while preserving one’s true qi internally." This is the foremost handle of the essential Dao.

If one repeatedly distributes treasure, one can unify and transform the fundamental principles. The "fundamental principles" are the masters of enforcing the law. When governance is skillfully unified and transformed, those who enforce the law will not find it burdensome. Just as when the lines of a hexagram move internally, fortune and misfortune manifest externally—this is akin to the five planets moving in disarray or harmony, with fortune and misfortune responding in due time. The same principle applies to *chǐ* and *lí*: *chǐ* means to alter, while *lí* means to shift or move.

The twenty-eight constellations, when in disarray, all bow and rise in accordance with the Literary Star of Great Vigilance, which is the chief star within the *Kui* constellation. The six stars of the Southern Dipper, known as the "Star of Unification," govern the Vermilion Bird’s spirit in the human body and record one’s name in the register of long life. The star of empty essence is the guiding principle of the Three Terraces, while the star that records and unifies is the leader of the Three Terraces. In the human body, it governs the Hall of Light and unfolds the virtue that enlightens the world. The *Dongfang Mingguang* star is the Purple Palace’s assistant, the two stars of the August Emperor. In the human body, they are the spirit of the Dark Warrior. If one encounters them, one’s lifespan may reach a thousand years. The remaining countless spirits and deities each preside over their respective domains.

Refining the elixir is a matter intimately tied to the workings of creation, and thus it is even more difficult than governance. To trace things back to their origin and grasp their end: by investigating the beginning, one can achieve enduring existence; by grasping the end, one can avoid perishing. The ruler is the heart; the ministers are the body.

The heart is the self; the body is the external world. If the self’s heart grows arrogant or self-satisfied, or if the body and external world contend against one another, refusing to follow the proper order, then the celestial patterns will deviate, fortunes will shift between fullness and decline, and misfortune will arise. Those who uphold the law will then reproach and question the ruler’s faults.

The *Chenji* is the aforementioned *Wenchang* star, also called the *Nanji Chenji*. It governs with rectitude, moving with ease while entrusting matters to its subordinates. The *Hall of Light* is the *Taifu*, diligently enacting good governance to ensure the state remains free from disorder. The state, too, is a body. When the body is at peace and its *qi* harmonious, the Dao is unharmed.

This passage illustrates that cultivating the self and refining the elixir is akin to a ruler governing the state: if the right people are chosen and proper policies enacted, the realm will be at peace. If one cultivates the self, exhausts the heart, and establishes destiny, the elixir path will be accomplished.

Yu Yan said: The *Zhengyi* states that when a person breathes, the interplay of hardness and softness mirrors the opening and closing of Heaven and Earth. When refining the elixir, the dry *yang* descends to unite with the *yin* of *Kun*, allowing breath to intermingle, hardness and softness to balance, and husband and wife to become one. Then, spirit and *qi* return to their root, nature and life merge, and the supreme medicine is produced.

The mutual dependence of endowment and reception is the principle of union. Heaven and Earth generate beings through the union of *yin* and *yang*; the elixir method produces medicine through the same union. The transformations within the human body correspond to those of Heaven and Earth—no creation can occur without union. If one can empty the heart and concentrate the spirit, aligning with the mechanisms of Heaven and Earth, then the workings of creation lie within one’s grasp.

*Kan* and *Li* are the sun and moon. When the sun and moon illuminate Heaven and Earth, the conch and clam absorb their light to produce pearls, and stubborn stones store it to become jade. How much more so for the human body, which possesses its own sun and moon—can it not reflect the light inward to form true pearls and yield true jade?

Yet this is but the external world—silent and vast, unfathomable, impossible to depict or plan. Only the heart can know it, the mind can conceive it. This is why the sages employ broad analogies and layered metaphors to expound the primordial foundation of the golden elixir, and why the *Cantong Qi* takes the *Yi* as its standard.

When *Qian*, *Kun*, *Kan*, and *Li* unite as one, spirit and *qi* return to their root, profound and subtle, beyond comprehension. Using *Qian* and *Kun* as the crucible and *Kan* and *Li* as the medicinal ingredients, the remaining sixty hexagrams—from *Zhun* and *Meng* to *Jiji* and *Weiji*—are arranged around the crucible as the *zhou tian* fire phases. Thus, all sixty hexagrams serve one’s purpose, and the dragon and horse submit to one’s control.

The body is like the state; the heart is like the ruler.

When the heart is settled, spirit congeals and *qi* harmonizes; the Three Palaces naturally ascend and descend, and the hundred vessels flow unimpeded. Thus, in the moment of elixir-making, there is no other path—only empty the heart, still the breath, and concentrate the spirit to enter the *qi*-cavern, following its ebb and flow with unbroken continuity. Over time, spirit will naturally congeal, breath will naturally settle, breath settled and *qi* gathers, *qi* gathered and the elixir forms. There is no need for massage, guiding and pulling, exhalation and inhalation, or meditative visualization—these are all heterodox ways.

The noble one dwells in his chamber, yet *Kan* and *Li* vanish without trace. The lord, exalted within the ninefold palace, speaks a word of goodness, and a thousand *li* away it is answered. The cultivator, containing his light and dwelling in silence, turns his reflection inward. When emptiness reaches its extreme and stillness its depth, the *qi* of Heaven and Earth naturally comes to him—this is the principle of mutual resonance and response.

The ancient sage-kings, in the first month of spring, commanded their ministers to spread virtue and harmonize decrees; in the first month of autumn, they ordered generals to select soldiers and sharpen weapons—all in accord with the seasons and the rhythms of Heaven and Earth’s yin and yang. The method of elixir refinement must first recognize the proper time, and even more, await its arrival. If the time has not yet come, one can only contain their light, dwell in silence, and wait with emptiness—never forcing it prematurely.

The hours of *zi*, *shen*, *yin*, and *xu*—like *zi*, *wu*, *mao*, and *you*—are all allegorical. Viewed through the sixty hexagrams, beginning with *Zhun* and ending with *Weiji*, yin and yang lines interweave and alternate. Though each differs, with opposing and corresponding bodies, every hexagram has its own resonance—truly, hexagram by hexagram, all are the same. If the scholar observes the two images of *Zhun* and grasps their pattern, they may extend this insight to silently comprehend the meanings of all hexagrams.

The West represents righteousness, the East benevolence. Punishment governs execution and subjugation, thus forging the lead of the West. Virtue governs generation and arousal, thus refining the mercury of the East. Lead belongs to metal, its nature utterly firm, concealed within *Kan*—without fierce firing and extreme forging, it cannot ascend. Thus, the martial fire is used to compel it, but the civil fire must not be applied. Mercury belongs to wood, its nature utterly yielding, concealed within *Li*—upon encountering true lead, it naturally stills. Thus, the civil fire is used to refine it, but the martial fire must not be applied. These two operations, when followed, bring fortune; when reversed, they bring misfortune. In drawing and circulating, one must guard against danger—even the slightest deviation is impermissible. Proceeding naturally brings fortune; forcing it in reverse brings misfortune. Therefore, one must follow the almanac to know the oral instructions for the proper moment. With utmost sincerity and unwavering focus, without the slightest stray thought, Heaven and Earth’s waxing and waning correspond to the ebb and flow of time. The human body models itself on Heaven and mirrors Earth—its blood and *qi* wax and wane in perfect accord with Heaven and Earth. Thus, the elixir method takes Heaven as the crucible, Earth as the furnace, the moon as the medicinal agent (its waxing and waning dictating the timing of gathering), and the sun as the fire’s rhythm (its rising and setting determining movement and stillness). Every act of drawing, circulating, bathing, and conjoining follows the patterns of Heaven and Earth—this is why it is called the Supreme Way. To seek other methods is but a side door, a petty art, the mere conjecture of idle speculation.

The so-called "fire" is not the fire of visualization, but the body’s innate true fire. The so-called "timing" is not the timing of hours and minutes, but the body’s innate *qi*-rhythms. Fire’s nature flares when met with the wind of *Xun*, and hides when met with the earth of *Kun*. Its use must neither exceed nor fall short—all depends on regulating its civil and martial aspects, discerning its warmth and coolness, ensuring balance. Then, great and small suffer no harm, both realms remain whole, and in an instant, the elixir’s first signs appear. This is what is meant by "harmonizing and circulating."

If even the slightest deviation occurs, the solstices shift their positions between north and south, the equinoxes diverge between east and west. Midwinter transforms into scorching summer, midsummer flips into sleet and snow—and lead and mercury scatter, a host of anomalies erupting. The human body takes *Qian* and *Kun* as father and mother, with the Six Children operating between them, moving up and down, ever obedient to their parents. Thus, they are called "filial children." The Imperial Ultimate is the central, upright position, the place to which the eight directions converge. If the filial child applies their heart and moves it, the tiger roars once, the dragon emerges from its cave, phoenixes dance and simurghs soar, entering the golden city. But if their actions are rebellious and discordant, failing to align with the Ultimate, the dark pearl scatters, the numinous mercury flees, and the Six Children become not filial children but rebels against the elixir path.

When medicine is present and fire phases are applied, metal, pressed by fire, surges and ascends to the *Li* Palace, transforming into water, which in turn restrains fire—thus fire is spared the disaster of flaring upward. But without medicine, applying fire only drives the empty yang to attack upward, burning the body itself—this is how fortune and misfortune diverge. True yang is born in the *Kan* Palace, turbid and unmoving. To capture and restrain *Li*’s true mercury, one must use the martial fire for fierce forging and extreme firing, enabling it to ascend. Once it reaches the *Li* Palace and conjoins with true mercury, one should guard the city and bathe—fire must no longer be added. This is the stark difference between peace and upheaval.

These four principles—where do they come from? They arise from returning to the heart alone. When still and unmoving, returning to the root and restoring stillness—this is the time of *Kun*. I then wait in stillness. When stillness reaches its extreme and movement stirs, yang *qi* secretly germinates—this is the time of *Fu*. I then move in response. This is the constant method. Otherwise, if one practices in the morning and abandons it by evening, it is not wholehearted devotion. When movement is called for, stillness is mixed in; when stillness is called for, movement interrupts. Sometimes one forces progress prematurely, sometimes one forgets and loses the proper sequence. Such movement is merely the movement of circulating *qi*; such stillness is merely the stillness of dhyāna. How can this be called following the method and adhering to the rule?

When applying effort, the four seasons must be skillfully harmonized and followed according to their proper times. Then warmth, coolness, cold, and heat each find their place, and hardness and softness are decisively distinguished. They do not encroach upon one another: when yang’s hardness is at work, the mind’s intent remains below, never encroaching above; when yin’s softness is at work, the mind’s intent remains above, never encroaching below.

The *Wang Liang* and *Qi Tian Tong Jing Song* say: "Metal, wood, center, water, fire—these five guard the single elixir field." When these five scatter, the whole body becomes *qi*; when they gather, the elixir field forms a treasure. Now, if one steadfastly and single-mindedly guards the elixir field without reckless expansion or contraction, they will blend and fuse, transforming into a single *qi*, congealing into golden liquid. Expansion is excess; contraction is insufficiency. Excess harms things; insufficiency fails to generate them. Only when one maintains unbroken continuity, as if present, and guards it with intention—over time, when the moment arrives and *qi* transforms, the crucible fills and must overflow, and the numinous and bright will naturally come.

---

The *Yi* speaks of the sun and moon. The sun and moon traverse the Yellow Way, circulating day and night in ceaseless revolution, cycling and returning without end. The human body—head and trunk as *qian*, abdomen as *kun*—resembles heaven and earth. Its two *qi* ascend and descend just like the sun and moon. Thus, one emulates the function of the sun and moon to achieve the same work as heaven and earth. The essential lies in unblocking the *Ren* and *Du* meridians. These two meridians are the sea of yin and yang for the entire body, the mechanism and barrier of the five *qi* and true origin.

The *Ren* meridian arises below the *Zhongji* point, ascends through the pubic hair, follows the inner abdomen up to *Guanyuan*, reaches the throat, and belongs to the sea of yin meridians. The *Du* meridian arises from the *Xiaji* point, runs alongside the spine, ascends to *Fengfu*, enters the brain, reaches the crown, follows the forehead to the bridge of the nose, and belongs to the sea of yang meridians. It is called the *Ren* meridian because women rely on it for conception and nourishment. It is called the *Du* meridian because it governs and leads the sea of meridians. If one can unblock these two meridians, all meridians will flow freely, naturally circulating throughout the body without stagnation or obstruction, and the Dao of longevity and enduring vision is decisively here.

Those who dwell in obscurity and concealment allow spirit to enter *qi*, *qi* to enter the navel, and sink to return to the sea’s depths. Those who transform within wait for the time when *qi* naturally transforms; when stillness reaches its limit, the mechanism spontaneously activates, and the sun emerges from within. The mother of the golden elixir is nothing but the primordial singular *qi*. Split into two, divided into three, scattered into the myriad things—all arise from this singular *qi*. Thus it is said: "It enfolds the ten thousand things as the guiding principle of the Dao."

The *Daodejing* states: "Thirty spokes share one hub; it is the center’s emptiness that makes the cart useful. Clay is shaped into a vessel; it is the hollow within that makes the vessel useful. Doors and windows are cut to make a room; it is the emptiness within that makes the room useful." These three are metaphors for the function of the void. Thus it is said: "Existence provides the advantage; nonexistence provides the function." Carts, vessels, and rooms are all tangible things with practical uses, yet what makes them carts, vessels, and rooms is the function of their emptiness.

Now, Master Wei speaks of using the void to control the existent, employing emptiness as the vessel’s function—this is Laozi’s teaching. In elixir methods, the Great Void serves as the crucible and furnace, and within the Great Void, there is naturally a wondrous function. *Guanyinzi* says: "Shaking clothes in emptiness yields wind; exhaling on things yields fire." Chen Baoyi’s commentary states: "Knowing that shaking emptiness yields wind, one can drum the bellows to generate *qi*. Knowing that exhaling on things yields fire, one can refine one’s *qi* into essence." If one is ignorant of this and seeks elsewhere for the crucible and vessel, it is not the Dao of emptiness and nothingness.

*Xi* (rest) is the sign for advancing fire, as *kun* transforms thrice into *qian*. *Xiao* (dissolution) is the sign for withdrawing the tally, as *qian* transforms thrice into *kun*.

From the first day of the lunar month when the *Zhen* trigram governs, passing through *Dui* to *Qian*, and from the full moon when the *Xun* trigram governs, passing through *Gen* to *Kun*—in this interval, the positions of *Kan* and *Li* are not seen. Is this what is meant by the disappearance of *Kan* and *Li*? No, *Kan* and *Li* have not disappeared; they move within the Six Voids, coming and going without fixed abode.

The words here are not carelessly fabricated. Wei Gong himself stated that he composed this book to reveal the secrets of this Dao, refusing to fabricate baseless words to deceive later generations. All his explanations are drawn from verified effects and personal, authentic attainment. Fearing his teachings might not suffice to convince posterity, he further calibrated the numinous movements of the sun and moon as a method and traced the principles of the *Yi* trigrams as evidence. Truly, he labored diligently for others.

*Kan* is yin without and yang within—this is the image of the moon. *Li* is yang without and yin within—this is the image of the sun. *Kan* receives *wu* (戊), the yang earth; *Li* receives *ji* (己), the yin earth. When the two earths unite, yin and yang harmonize, hardness and softness balance—just as the characters for "sun" (日) and "moon" (月) combine to form the character for "change" (易). Green, red, white, and black correspond to wood, fire, metal, and water, each occupying one direction, while earth resides at the center, dividing its vigor among *chen*, *xu*, *chou*, and *wei*, weaving through the beginning and end of the year. Thus, wood draws its flourishing from earth, fire its waning, metal its birth, and water its cessation—all depend on its power.

This earth pervades the universe, having no fixed position: in *Kan*, it is *wu* earth; in *Li*, it is *ji* earth; in the Central Palace, it is *wu-ji* earth; in the four seasons, it is *chen-xu-chou-wei* earth—all the same substance. Without the true earth to harmonize them, yin and yang would be blocked, hardness and softness divided, and the elixir could not form. As the *Wuzhen Pian* says: "The Four Symbols and Five Phases rely entirely on earth." How critical this is!

Between the dark moon and the new moon—this is the time of transformation and circulation. In elixir methods, where months are reckoned as days, this corresponds to the transition between *hai* and *zi* each day. Within the human body, yin and yang converge at this moment. If one practices the elixir at this time, the inner truth and outer response align like tallying tokens, neither early nor late, precisely at the center—this is the supreme wonder of heaven, earth, and humanity.

Before the primordial *qi* divides, there is chaos; when yin and yang mingle, they follow one another. In elixir terms, this is the state of utter stillness, where spirit congeals and *qi* gathers, unaware of the world’s existence, of one’s own body, of heaven as canopy or earth as carriage. All concerns vanish, and the Dao is one. After a while, the Three Palaces fill with *qi*, the mechanism stirs, and the flute sounds—with one sword-stroke, chaos is split open; with two hands, the primal mist is torn apart. This is called "generating being from non-being." The immortals’ refinement consists solely of gathering the primordial *qi* to serve as the mother of the golden elixir. "Gathering" means gathering without gathering; "taking" means taking without taking—all is attained within stillness and quiescence, without the slightest thought intruding.

Before the Supreme Ultimate divides and yin and yang separate, this is the primordial state before heaven and earth. In elixir terms, this is the moment of returning to the root and restoring stillness. When utterly still, the mind communes with heaven, and the workings of creation may be seized. The *Cuixu Pian* says: "Do not seek the workings of creation in the kidneys; rather, seek the primordial in the heart." Formerly, the Yellow Emperor lost his mysterious pearl and sent Li Zhu and Qi Gou to search for it, but they could not find it. Only Xuan Xiang obtained it—Xuan Xiang means "forgetting form." Only by forgetting form can the primordial *qi* be regained. The *Huánjīn Piān* says: "Where ghosts and spirits do not see, dragon and tiger surely meet." This is profoundly clear.

When the time arrives and *qi* transforms, yin and yang interact—this is the heaven and earth within the body, the intertwining of male and female within. Suddenly, the fire-wheel scorches the earthly veins, the spirit surges in astonishment, and the mountain springs gush forth. Between heaven and earth, all is moistened and greatly abundant. Without personal, authentic attainment, how could one know such true scenery exists?

The numinous brightness of heaven and earth cannot be measured. When yin and yang unite, the spirit and *sha* conceal themselves—this is the numinous moment of heaven and earth. When dragon and tiger merge, the *gui* spirit is unaware—this is the numinous moment of one’s body. Aligning the numinous spirit of one’s body with that of heaven and earth, the inner truth and outer response coincide. At the *Xun* gate, in the midst of stillness, both sides ascend and unite as one. If this could be attained through contemplation, calculation, or measurement, it would not be called the numinous.

"Exploiting the use to secure the body" and "waging virtue in the wild"—in elixir methods, where months are reckoned as days, this occurs at midnight, at the transition between *Kun* and *Fu*. Cross the legs, sit upright and unmoving as a mountain stone, seal the mouth and restrain the breath like a hibernating snake—this is "exploiting the use to secure the body" and concealing the form. At this time, contain the light, silently reflect inward, and let the breath return gently to the life-stem. After a while, one feels profound and indistinct, as if facing an unfathomable abyss—this is the moment when spirit and *qi* return to the root, body and mind revert to destiny, and the golden liquid congeals.

The fire-timing of elixir refinement mirrors the waxing and waning of the moon. On the third day of the month, the moon begins to brighten, starting in the northeast, in the region of *Ji* and *Dou*, then turning right until evening, when it rises like a wheel, sprouting in the southwest, above *Mao* and *Bi*. This image corresponds to *Zhen*; in terms of the *Qian* hexagram, it aligns with the first nine. This illustrates the initial stirring of the yang fire within the body. On the eighth day of the month, the moon’s light is half-bright, corresponding to *Dui*; in terms of *Qian*, it aligns with the second nine, illustrating the halfway point of the yang fire’s work. On the fifteenth day, the moon is full, its spirit abundant and light complete, corresponding to *Qian*; in terms of *Qian*, it aligns with the third nine, illustrating the fullness of the yang fire. On the sixteenth day, the moon begins to wane, corresponding to *Xun*; in terms of *Qian*, it aligns with the fourth nine, illustrating the beginning of the yin tally’s succession. On the twenty-third day, the moon’s light is half-diminished, corresponding to *Gen*; in terms of *Qian*, it aligns with the fifth nine, illustrating the halfway point of the yin tally’s work. On the thirtieth day, the light vanishes entirely, corresponding to *Kun*; in terms of *Qian*, it aligns with the top nine, illustrating the conclusion of the yin tally.

Wei Gong used the six trigrams to illustrate the fire-timing, matching them to the six lines of *Qian*, for the elixir is a treasure of pure yang, and *Qian* is the hexagram of pure yang.

Within the six trigrams, *Qian* is called the "nine in the third place," where vigilance at dusk guards against loss and the divine tally’s fracture; *Kun* is called the "nine at the top," where the dragon battles virtue in the wild. For in defending the city with *Qian*, civil strategy is employed, while in field combat with *Kun*, martial force is wielded—both demand vigilance against peril and foresight of danger.

The "Use of Nine" soars as the ancestral principle of the *Yijing*, for nine is the culmination of yang numbers. The elixir Way models itself on *Qian* and returns to pure yang, thus the "Use of Nine" also illustrates the nine revolutions of the fire-timing within the crucible. Each line of the fire-timing, once traversed, gives way to the next, without the slightest deviation—this is the compass and square of the Dao, ceaselessly renewed.

Metal’s nature and wood’s disposition are one thing divided into two, separated east and west. Yet if the Dipper’s handle is grasped to turn the mechanism, wood’s nature will yearn for metal, and metal’s nature will long for wood. With *Kan* and *Li* ascending and descending, metal and wood naturally cease to be divided.

The immortal’s returning elixir is the *Yijing* within the body; the nine revolutions of the fire-timing are the *Qian* within the body. The Dao circulates throughout the self—no line can be observed, no image or number sought. You may pursue it yet not see its head; follow it yet not see its tail. How could it possess form or substance to be perceived?

Only when heaven moves in concealment does heaven move, and when earth moves in concealment does earth move. The six lines shift and transform—these are the six lines of *Qian*. From this, extension and contact generate the sixty-four hexagrams, all tracing their ancestry here. Gradually, the great is traversed.

When *Dazhuang* reaches its peak, day and night first divide—*Dazhuang* is the hexagram of four yang lines. Its musical note corresponds to *Jiazhong*; in a day, it is dawn at *mao*; in a month, it spans from the eighth to the eleventh day; in a year, it is the month when *mao* is established. At this time, yin assists yang, and qi gathers to bring forth things. This illustrates that within the body, the yang fire is halfway complete, and the qi-timing is balanced. Thus, it is said that punishment and virtue carry each other, and day and night first divide. Yet all things should be sprouting in spring—why do the elm pods fall at this time? It is because yang contains yin.

*Guai* is when yin retreats and yang ascends, washing and cleansing the wings to shake off the dust of the past—*Guai* is the hexagram of five yang lines. Its musical note corresponds to *Guxi*; in a day, it is the *chen* hour of the morning meal; in a month, it spans from the eleventh to the thirteenth day; in a year, it is the month when *chen* is established. At this time, yang qi is already abundant, pressing near the edge of heaven. This illustrates that within the body, the yang fire ascends. Thus, it is said that yang ascends forward, and it is also said to wash and cleanse the wings, shaking off the dust of the past—like the great roc about to migrate to the celestial pond, striking the water to rise, its momentum must be vigorous.

*Qian* is strong and flourishing, its brightness spreading to the four neighbors—*Qian* is the hexagram of six yang lines. Its musical note corresponds to *Zhonglü*; in a day, it is the *si* hour of mid-morning; in a month, it spans from the thirteenth to the fifteenth day; in a year, it is the month when *si* is established. At this time, yang qi is at its peak, pervading the universe. This illustrates that within the body, the yang fire is complete, and the radiance of the elixir appears. The spirit of the head disperses into four streams, pouring into the mountains below, governing a nation without omission. Thus, it is said to spread to the four neighbors, and it is also said that yang ends at the center of *si* and interacts with *Gan*—here, the count of yang fire is complete, and the yin tally begins its work.

*Gou* begins to weave the threads, treading frost first—*Gou* is the hexagram of one yin line, where yin is the master. Its musical note corresponds to *Rui Bin*; in a day, it is noon at *wu*; in a month, it spans from the sixteenth to the eighteenth day; in a year, it is the month when *wu* is established. At this time, yin qi first arises, illustrating the ground where the yin tally begins within the body. Once the numinous elixir enters the mouth and returns, it re-enters the cold spring. One must gently guide it back to the cinnabar field, not rushing or hastening. Thus, it is said to tread frost first, the cold spring at the well’s bottom. It is also said that the guest serves yin, for yin is the master—here, one yin takes charge, and the many yang become guests. *Bin* means respect, a warning to guard against danger and consider risks.

*Dun* retreats from worldly position, gathering its essence and cherishing virtue, awaiting the time in secluded obscurity—*Dun* is the hexagram of two yin lines. Its musical note corresponds to *Linzhong*; in a day, it is the *wei* hour of dusk; in a month, it spans from the eighteenth to the twenty-first day; in a year, it is the month when *wei* is established. At this time, yin qi gradually grows, illustrating that within the body, the yin tally departs from the *wu* position, gathering and descending—like a worthy one retreating to a secluded valley. Thus, it is said to cherish virtue and dwell in seclusion.

*Pi* is blocked and impassable, leading to the ruin of name and reputation—*Pi* is the hexagram of three yin lines. Its musical note corresponds to *Yize*; in a day, it is the *shen* hour of the afternoon meal; in a month, it spans from the twenty-first to the twenty-third day; in a year, it is the month when *shen* is established. At this time, yang qi gradually wanes, illustrating that within the body, the yin tally descends further—like the three yin lines in the season of withering, when grasses and trees yellow and fall. Thus, it is said that yin stretches and yang bends, ruining name and reputation.

*Guan* weighs the scales, examining the sentiments of mid-autumn—*Guan* is the hexagram of four yin lines. Its musical note corresponds to *Nanlü*; in a day, it is dusk at *you*; in a month, it spans from the twenty-third to the twenty-fifth day; in a year, it is the month when *you* is established. At this time, yin assists yang’s work, and all things contract and mature. This illustrates that within the body, the yin tally is more than halfway descended, entering the cinnabar field—like trees withdrawing their blossoms to bear fruit. Thus, it is said to nurture the tender and young. Yet why do all things wither in autumn, while shepherd’s purse and wheat sprout at this time? It is because yin contains yang.

*Bo* rots the limbs, leading to the loss of the numinous—*Bo* is the hexagram of five yin lines. Its musical note corresponds to *Wuyi*; in a day, it is twilight at *xu*; in a month, it spans from the twenty-sixth to the twenty-eighth day; in a year, it is the month when *xu* is established. At this time, yang qi declines and perishes; the fruits on the branches all rot and fall to the ground. This illustrates that within the body, the yin tally is nearly exhausted, and the spirit’s work has nowhere to manifest. Thus, it is said that the transforming qi is spent, and the numinous is lost.

When the Dao is exhausted, it returns to *Kun Yuan*—*Kun* is the hexagram of six yin lines. Its musical note corresponds to *Yingzhong*; in a day, it is the *hai* hour of human rest; in a month, it spans from the twenty-eighth to the thirty-first day; in a year, it is the month when *hai* is established. At this time, pure yin takes charge, and all things return to their roots to renew their lives. This illustrates that within the body, when the yin tally is exhausted, it becomes still and motionless, returning to the origin and restoring tranquility. In this state, yin and yang interact no differently than in heaven and earth. Heaven’s qi descends to earth, and the things born within earth are all heaven’s qi. This is what is meant by returning the spirit to qi—the inner elixir’s Dao naturally completes itself. Between *hai* and *zi*, at the boundary of yin and yang, when the six yin lines are exhausted and the one yang has yet to arise, in this silence and emptiness, the immortal forges the elixir. Blocking *dui* and lowering the curtain, the spirit’s light shines downward into the *kan* palace. At first, it is dim and guarded, like the moon submerged in a cold pond. Then, spirit and qi unite, barriers silently dissolve—like a magnet drawing iron.

When forging the elixir, it is like the *hai* month: though the work is secret and hidden, the year’s nurturing begins its gestation here. When the night’s qi is not yet scattered, one gathers the spirit and collects the qi, sitting upright for a moment. Soon, spirit and qi return to the root, naturally giving birth to being from non-being. Gradually, they condense and accumulate, forming a measure of golden essence. At this time of the body and heart’s renewal, the spirit enters the vastness, becoming one with the great void. After long stillness, when the flower of the heart blooms, the three palaces fill with qi. One feels only a trance-like obscurity, not knowing how it comes to be. Then, in an instant, yin reaches its extreme and yang is born; stillness reaches its limit and the mechanism stirs. The gates of the face open wide, the empty sky shatters, and a burst of radiance breaks the silence, placing the power of transformation in one’s hands.

This chapter clearly reveals the source of the medicine’s production, thoroughly discussing the secrets of existence and non-existence, host and guest, before and after. There is no level ground that does not slope, no slope that does not return—this is the natural Dao. The elixir’s method, where stillness reaches its limit and moves, and movement reaches its limit and stills, with each movement and stillness mutually rooting the other, also arises from nature. The golden elixir is the *Yijing* within the body, where flourishing and decline alternate without end, and change is never obstructed. Thus, it is said that flourishing and decline follow one another; yang begins at *Fu* and ends at *Kun*, with head and tail connected. Thus, it is said that beginning and end form a ring. Those who study the Dao, working diligently all day in step with the times, may endure for a thousand years and remain eternal.

At the helm of governance—when the first yang stirs to forge the elixir—it is as when a sovereign takes the throne to govern. Renewing the old and reforming the obsolete, with no embellishment, the adept seals every aperture with vigilant care: the eyes enfold their light, the ears condense their resonance, the nose regulates its breath, the tongue seals its qi, the legs cross in stillness, and the body sits upright, the spirit withdrawn in inner guard—no thought turned outward even for an instant.

For when the eyes do not see, the *hun*-soul naturally returns to the liver and does not leak through the eyes; when the ears do not hear, the essence naturally returns to the kidneys and does not leak through the ears; when the tongue makes no sound, the spirit naturally returns to the heart and does not leak through the mouth; when the nose detects no scent, the *po*-soul naturally returns to the lungs and does not leak through the nose; when the four limbs do not move, the intent naturally returns to the spleen and does not leak through the limbs’ pores. With these five unbreached, essence, spirit, *hun*, *po*, and intent merge and fuse, transmuting into a single qi that gathers in the cinnabar field, congealing into the supreme treasure.

The crux of the elixir’s method lies entirely in the Dipper, which steers the yin and yang of the entire body and governs the myriad transformations within, just as the rope draws the net or the clasp fastens the garment. The three fields of the human body are divided in three locations; when the Dipper’s handle turns to steer them, true qi circulates up and down like the flowing of the Celestial River—this is called the essential Dao.

When the hexagram’s lines stir within, the first yang arises within the body—this is the moment of receiving qi. Though easily attained, at this instant one must act, for fortune and misfortune, regret and humiliation arise from movement, and not a hair’s breadth of deviation is permissible. The five phases must be gathered, metal and fire must share the same crucible—all hinges on a single thought’s return to the source. Otherwise, the constellations within the crucible will all be misaligned, and heaven and earth will stand far apart.

How well Lü Chunyang, the Old Immortal, said: “When water and fire are evenly balanced, the medicine is formed; when yin and yang are askew, the elixir fails—beware!” The *Records of the Grand Historian: Treatise on the Celestial Offices* states that the six stars of the Dipper’s bowl form the Palace of Literary Flourishing, and there are also the Three Terraces and Four Assistants, around which the stars of the celestial divisions revolve. Within the human body, the central palace is the sovereign who governs heaven and earth, and there are also the Three Dragons, Four Tigers, and the myriad hexagrams of the fire tally—let none neglect the cyclical signs: let *jia* and *yi* not lose *geng* and *xin*, and let no deviation occur.

The canonical departments regulate the twenty-four solar terms, arranging the twenty-eight lunar lodges across the celestial circuit, while the crucible’s fire times are meticulously calibrated. The beginning is the new moon, the end the dark moon.

The thread of preservation and dissolution lies within the dark and new moons—yin nears its end yet remains unfinished, yang approaches its birth yet has not yet arisen. Within this interval, the withdrawal and advance, the bathing and conjoining, must not err even slightly, for preservation and dissolution hinge upon this most profound and mysterious continuity.

The sovereign is the spirit; the minister is the qi. When forging the elixir, lead and mercury return to the earthen crucible, while body and mind remain still and unmoving. If the body stirs, qi scatters; if the mind stirs, spirit scatters. Only by congealing the spirit and gathering the qi, with mind and breath interdependent, may the numinous embryo be formed. Otherwise, the celestial luminance within the body will wax and wane in discord, bringing misfortune and calamity.

Tracing the cause, it arises from the mind’s indulgence and deviation from the Dao, whereupon qi turns devious and perverse, moving contrary to its proper course—thus it too suffers reproach. The *Fu Ming* chapter states: "Drive back the Northern Dipper, turn the celestial net; grasp the Southern Culmen, enter the chamber of depths." One may see that at the pole of the celestial pivot, its majesty resides in the moment of containing radiance and silent stillness, continuous yet as if present, allowing it to unfold naturally—one must not weary the spirit.

The *Treatise on the Celestial Offices* states: "The Eastern Palace’s Azure Dragon includes the Chamber and Heart constellations; the Heart is the Hall of Light." The *Yellow Court Classic* says: "The Hall of Light, the Golden Casket, the Purple Chamber—where are they? Between the brows, whence radiance descends to illuminate, just as the sovereign sits in the Hall of Light to govern and extend the Dao to the nine barbarians and eight wild tribes."

*Collected Explications of the Ancient Text "Can Tong Qi"*, Scroll One (First Part)
*Imperially Commissioned Four Library Collection*

**Collected Explications of the Ancient Text "Can Tong Qi", Scroll One (Second Part)**
*Composed by Jiang Yibiao of the Ming Dynasty*
*Can Tong Qi by Wei Boyang of the Eastern Han*

**Middle Scroll** *(This speaks of the Dao of the Yellow Emperor and Laozi for extending life, nurturing nature, and attaining longevity.)*

To nurture nature and extend life, one must first reflect deeply—consider what comes after, but first ponder what preceded it. The body one inherits is originally formless, a primordial essence, as Master Yu’s commentary states: *"Flowing forth, it relies on qi to lodge itself; yin and yang serve as its measure."* The soul and spirit reside within: the yang spirit is the sun-soul, the yin spirit the moon-soul. Soul and spirit dwell in mutual abodes. Nature governs the inner, establishing boundaries; emotion governs the outer, constructing walls. When walls are complete, all beings find peace.

At this time, when emotion harmonizes with heaven and earth: *Qian* moves and straightens, its qi spreading as clear streams; *Kun* is still and receptive, becoming the dwelling of the Dao. The firm retreats when applied; the yielding flourishes and nourishes. Nine returns, seven reversions, eight reunions, six residences—male white, female red. Metal and fire restrain one another, then water settles; fire is the inception of the Five Phases. *"The highest good is like water,"* pure and flawless, the formless image of the Dao. The True One is difficult to depict, yet its divisions are distributed, each dwelling alone. Like the egg’s white and black, they correspond—length and breadth but an inch, the beginning of limbs, five viscera, sinews, and bones all complete. After ten months, it emerges from the womb: bones supple enough to coil, flesh smooth as maltose.

The *Kan* male is the moon; the *Li* female is the sun. The sun bestows virtue; the moon unfurls its light. The moon receives the sun’s transformation—its body unharmed, its yang unbroken in covenant, its yin unencroaching upon its brightness. When darkness and new moon press close, eclipses veil and obscure, each overwhelming the other. Yang’s form dissipates; yin’s calamity arises. Male and female mutually contain and exhale to nourish; yin and yang intermingle, seeking one another. When metal transforms into water, water’s nature circulates without restraint; when fire transforms into earth, water cannot flow. The male moves and extends outward; the female is still and stores within. If overflow exceeds measure, the female restrains it—the po controls the hun, preventing excess. Neither cold nor heat, advancing and retreating in timely accord, each attains harmony. Together they exhale, verifying the tally:

*"Guan guan go the ospreys, on the isle in the river.
The modest, retiring, virtuous young lady—
for our prince a good mate."*
Male does not dwell alone; female does not live in solitude. The dark warrior, coiled dragon—intertwined, they support one another, clarifying the union of yin and yang. If two women share a chamber, though their beauty be striking, though Su Qin’s eloquence and Zhang Yi’s persuasiveness serve as matchmakers, though tongues loosen in clever speech and honeyed words pour forth to reconcile hearts—though they become husband and wife, hair may rot and teeth fall out, yet they will never truly know one another.

If medicinal substances are not of the same kind, their properties differ; if weights and measures are unequal, their principles diverge. Though the Yellow Emperor tend the furnace, Taiyi grasp the flame, the Eight Lords of Huainan refine and blend, though they erect lofty altars with jade steps, offer unicorn jerky and phoenix delicacies, grasp tallies and kneel in prayer, beseeching spirits with lamentations—though they bathe, fast, and purify in hope of some attainment—it is like mending a cauldron with glue, plastering a wound with salt, applying ice to dispel cold, or using hot water to remove frost. Flying phoenixes and dancing serpents only appear more perverse.

The highest virtue takes no action and does not seek through scrutiny; the lesser virtue acts without ceasing. When the upper is closed, it is called *having*; when the lower is closed, it is called *nothingness*. *Nothingness* serves the *having*; the *having* possesses divine virtue. Within these two apertures, the method of metal and qi also requires mutual dependence. Know the white to guard the black; the numinous will come of itself. White is the essence of metal; black is the foundation of water. Water is the pivot of the Dao; its number is one—the beginning and end of yin and yang. It contains the Yellow Sprout, the master of the Five Metals. The Northern River Cart is lead: outwardly black, inwardly harboring yellow. Cloaked in coarse cloth, it conceals jade. Outwardly a madman, inwardly gold is the mother of water; the mother gives birth to the child. Water is the child of gold; the child resides in the mother’s womb. The True Person’s subtlety is as if present, as if absent—vague as the great abyss, now sinking, now floating. Advancing and retreating, they disperse yet each guards its own domain.

Gather the white; refine it to vermilion. Forge it into a protective exterior, with white as the inner lining, steadfast within. Square and round, a mere inch in diameter—intermingled, they support one another. Born before heaven and earth, majestic and lofty, surrounded by walls and gates, it resembles Penglai. Encircled, sealed, yet four pathways converge. Hesitant, one guards and defends, tightly securing it to bar all wickedness. Curved chambers connect to form a bulwark against unforeseen threats. No need for anxious thought; no cause for wearying the spirit. When qi fills the chamber, none can detain it. Those who guard it prosper; those who lose it perish. In movement and stillness, rest and activity, it ever accompanies the person. Internally, it nurtures the self; in tranquility and emptiness, it returns to the original luminosity, illuminating the body within. Seal the orifices, fortify the spirit-root. The Three Lights sink into obscurity; warmly nurture the child-pearl. Though unseen to the eye, it is near and easily sought.

Chen Xianwei says: *"All that pertains to blood and qi has its nature, and that nature never deviates from the correct. All that pertains to blood and qi has its destiny, and that destiny never departs from emotion."*

Yet this nature is fundamentally correct—yet when one becomes deluded, loses the true nature, and sinks into deviant paths, even to the point of becoming a villain, a serpent, a scorpion, or some monstrous thing, there are those who transform true nature into perverse nature. This destiny is rooted in emotion—yet when one awakens the mind, perceives the nature, and comprehends that all sentient beings establish their destinies through lust, one may refine essence into qi, ascend to sagehood, transcend the mundane, and transmute destiny into rightful destiny. Only by knowing the root of nature can one nurture it; only by understanding the correctness of destiny can one prolong it.

Consider this: when a person first receives their bodily form, it is fundamentally a single correct nature. Yet when father and mother unite in essence, clouds of primordial qi spread forth—does this arise from correctness, or from emotion? The *Yuanjue Jing* states that destiny arises from desire, which aligns with this principle. Regulating yin and yang serves as the measure, each dwelling in solitude.

Emotion and nature arise from the hun and po souls; the hun and po arise from brightness and darkness; brightness and darkness arise from the sun and moon; the sun and moon arise from yin and yang. The sage, observing the mechanisms of Qian and Kun—firmness and softness, movement and stillness, opening and closing—deduces from them the Way of the Great Elixir, which accords with the *Great Change*. Its essential principle does not depart from regulating yin and yang.

Thus, when the sun emerges at *mao*, the sky brightens and the hun soul flourishes; when the sun sets at *you*, the sky darkens and the po soul prevails. The hun is the yang spirit; the po is the yin spirit. The hun makes the day its chamber; the po makes the night its dwelling—yet in truth, they do not depart from the two mechanisms of brightness and darkness.

When a person’s day is bright, they employ the hun and the spirit, for the hun’s essence is nature. When night is dark, they return to essence and the po, for essence and po are the root of destiny. Destiny generates emotion, so essence and po serve as the city walls. Nature generates the mind, so preserving the mind and spirit serves as the inner and outer ramparts. When the city walls are firm, people and things are at peace.

At this time, if one directs emotion outward, then Qian and Kun unite, and the principles of firmness and softness, movement and stillness, opening and closing are attained. Thereupon, a single circulation of qi pervades the great void, ascending and descending, merging and transforming. True earth, nine metals, eight woods, seven fires, and six waters all return to their abode—all become earth within earth. When earth is returned to, the Five Phases are complete, and the myriad things are born within it. The man appears in white form; the woman manifests in red hue. For when the Five Phases gather and coalesce, metal and fire restrain each other. Fire refines metal, and the metal coffer holds water like boiling soup in a cauldron; the jade cauldron seethes as if in a furnace, the golden furnace blazes. It is said that water, metal, and fire—these three are all within earth. When fire is mentioned, wood naturally exists. Once the transformations are complete, lead congeals and mercury coagulates within the cauldron—water’s achievement is the most excellent of all.

The supreme treasure is flawless; the utmost reality is difficult to grasp. When the fire-timing is sufficient and the Five Phases are distributed, each then dwells in solitude without encroaching upon the other. It is like a chicken’s egg—smooth as maltose, an inch in breadth, slightly narrower in width—within it resides the Dharma-body.

The ancients said: *"How much better to leap beyond the radiance of the pearl, surging forth until one reaches the Purple Tenuity Palace!"* The Kan male becomes the moon; with mutual seeking, heaven and earth endure long. The reason heaven and earth can endure is that the sun and moon move in alternation, yin and yang exchange and support each other to emit radiance. If this measure is ever lost, there arises the calamity of eclipse. The Kan and Li of humans are like the sun and moon of heaven and earth—can one steal the mechanism of heaven and earth?

Metal transforms into water; both emit verifying signs. This speaks of the marvel of metal and wood as husband and wife, water and fire as mates. Metal generates water; water’s nature is moist. Without earth to restrain it, there would inevitably be the peril of overflow. Water generates fire; fire generates earth. Once earth is generated, water cannot flow freely—it is restrained, stored, and paired with fire as its mate. Thus, metal and wood, though mutually destructive, become husband and wife through the power of fire. Water and fire, though mutually destructive, become mates through the power of earth. Thereafter, advance and retreat align with the seasons, each attaining harmony and manifesting in timely transformations, all emitting verifying signs.

*"Guan guan go the ospreys..."*—this speaks of the Way as the unity of one yin and one yang. Excessive yin or excessive yang is called illness. If one attains their proper match, no further effort is needed—naturally, they commune and conjoin to form the sacred embryo. If one fails to attain their proper match, then all efforts are in vain, and mental exertions are wasted—ultimately, nothing is achieved, and one only becomes more perverse.

The superior virtue takes no action; the inferior virtue cannot be without action. The Way of heaven descends to illuminate; the Way of earth ascends to humble itself. The Way of Kun ascends, yet its body is fundamentally still—stillness means non-action, and it cannot be sought through examination. The Way of Qian descends, yet its body is perpetually in motion—movement means action, and it ceaselessly strives. When the upper is closed and called "having," this refers to Kun’s Way ascending—closing it yields water, and water has form. When the lower is closed and called "non-having," this refers to Qian’s Way descending—closing it yields fire, and fire leaves no trace.

"Non-having" serves the upper: Qian can seek Kun to ascend. The upper possesses Kun’s virtue and harbors the treasure of True Unity. When Qian descends, it can generate Kun’s qi; when Kun ascends, it can generate Qian’s metal. The essential point lies in the two orifices of Qian and Kun mutually employing each other. When upper and lower commune in harmony, metal abounds and qi naturally arises; qi abounds and metal naturally flourishes. Thus, metal and qi truly depend on each other—this is the wondrous function of Qian and Kun.

*"Know the white, guard the black"*—the white is silver; the black is lead. "Know the white, guard the black" means refining silver within lead. When silver is refined within lead, spiritual clarity naturally arises. Yin is the essence of metal; lead is the foundation of water. Among the Five Phases, only water’s number is one, aligning with the pivot of the Dao and serving as the beginning of yin and yang. Though it is not the True One itself, it fulfills the function of the True One. Thus, the Way of the True One first takes golden sprout as the root of the yellow bud—golden sprout is water. To unite the myriad differences into one, one must first seek the one within the myriad differences as the foundation.

This is why the method of the golden elixir takes lead as its material—its principle is thus. Therefore, water is called the pivot of the Dao. Only at this point does it become clear that water fulfills the function of the True One, though it is not the True One itself. Water’s generating number is one; water’s completing number is six. In terms of the completing number six, water contains one part water and five parts earth. Thus, lead appears black externally yet contains golden splendor within, possessing the wondrous function of the Yellow Bud.

Water is the beginning of the Five Phases; lead is the master of the five metals. Water originally resides in the north; when transported to the south, it ascends from below, bearing the treasure upward—like the river chariot in motion. Hence, it is called the river chariot. Because yellow is mixed with black, it is called "clad in coarse cloth"—for lead’s substance is inherently base.

And because white silver lies within, it is called "concealing jade"—meaning the supreme treasure is guarded in secret. Generally, the principles of creation and transformation are such that the base guards the noble, obscurity nurtures clarity, the lowly preserves the exalted, and madness conceals sagacity. Though outwardly appearing as a madman, within he harbors the supreme treasure. Thus, the mechanism remains sealed and unexposed; the wise merchant hides his wealth deeply. How can this be spoken of in the same breath as those who hastily seek recognition from others?

Master Wei’s secret, though fundamentally rooted in lead, also extends its meaning to encompass both. Gold is the mother of water, each guarding its own domain. Silver is metal; lead is water. Metal generates water, hence gold is the mother of water. Water is the child of metal, and lead conceals silver, so the mother is within the child’s womb, and the child is within the mother’s placenta. The golden embryo resides within lead—this is the True Person within the abyss, now visible, now hidden, now sinking, now floating. Yet when lead is employed and the water recedes, the abyss clears, and the True Person emerges, each then returning to its own domain.

The *Jinbi Jing* states: "In the fiery crucible, lead sinks and silver floats"—its secret is the same. When one gathers the white, it becomes supremely exalted. Gathering the white means collecting the floating silver, the supreme treasure of the west. When refined, the vermilion congeals, and the sacred embryo of the Golden Elixir forms in the southern chamber. Once the Elixir is complete, its manifestation resembles the primordial chaos of Master Hundun, or the square and round, inch-wide True Lord of White Within—intermingled and mutually sustaining. This is Master Wei’s revelation of the precelestial symbolic method.

Having arisen before heaven and earth, its exalted supremacy is beyond all thought or discussion. Surrounding it are walls and gates, ever present with humanity. Within the divine chamber, the symbolic form is complete, and the world is established. The golden wheel encircles it like a surrounding wall, while the world resides at its center, as beautiful as Penglai. With the firmness of silver mountains and iron walls, it blocks all evil and ensures security. Its fourfold openness, like towers of jade and pavilions of pearl, allows for unobstructed spiritual play.

At this time, one may be free from anxious thoughts, untroubled by sorrow or toil, preserving the Great Harmony as if holding a full vessel. A single thought of movement or stillness, and the Dharmakāya follows. When the spirit and qi fill the body, it is most difficult to preserve. Guard it, and one flourishes; lose it, and one perishes. Let the scholar take heed!

Inwardly, one must cultivate oneself. Though near and easily sought, when the Elixir resides in the divine chamber, one must conceal its brilliance, turning the light inward to illuminate within. In non-action and silence, the root is deeply secured. Let the sun, moon, and all phenomena sink together, so that radiance remains hidden. The infant and the sacred pearl grow in warmth, nurtured without deficiency. Within the work of non-action, the divine work is revealed—not in form, but in the formless. Though near and easily sought, it is not obtained from without. It lies in the scholar’s hidden merit and virtuous conduct.

Peng Xiao said: Master Wei taught that those who wish to prolong life and avert death must understand the beginning and end of the body. The beginning and end are the primordial qi. The cultivation of the Returned Elixir relies entirely on primordial qi to achieve completion. It is by connecting the boundless primordial qi to the limited physical form that one does so. The boundless primordial qi is the long-lived true essence of heaven and earth, the yin and yang—the qi of the sagely father and numinous mother.
