The limited physical form is the short-lived, turbid, and chaotic qi of ordinary parents. Thus, by transforming the body of ordinary parents with the qi of the true parents, one becomes a being of pure yang true essence, attaining a lifespan equal to that of heaven and earth.

Taoist Master Tao said: Primordial qi is the root of humanity. An ancient song says: *"Refine it, nourish it for a thousand days—once the body is free of yin, how can death come?"* Thus, the pure yang essence and qi are indestructible. The measure of yin and yang: In the preparation of the divine elixir of golden liquid, one first establishes the measure of yin and yang, then sets up the Qian and Kun cauldrons and furnaces. The soul and spirit reside within, each serving as the other’s dwelling. This means the sun’s soul and moon’s spirit are bound within the golden chamber, forming the foundation of the Elixir.

The nature resides within, establishing the inner enclosure. Nature belongs to metal, which governs principle within and receives external signs to nurture the numinous mercury. The enclosure is the physical form. The emotions operate externally, constructing the firm city walls. Emotions are the fire of the talisman; fire moves through the six voids to become cinnabar mercury. The city walls and people are the cinnabar mercury. At this juncture, it is also due to the movement and stillness of Qian and Kun, the flowing of qi, and the distribution of essence. Earth resides in the central palace as the dwelling of the Dao. Yang subdues yin, and the cycle of peace and adversity alternates. The punishment and virtue of yin and yang are the fundamental paths of the medicine.

The nine returns, seven reversions, eight returns, and six residences refer to: metal’s generating number is four, its completing number nine; fire’s generating number is two, its completing number seven; wood’s generating number is three, its completing number eight; water’s generating number is one, its completing number six; earth’s generating number is five, its completing number ten.

The white male and red female: Metal initially generates water and belongs to yin, corresponding to the north and the Kan trigram, which symbolizes the male within Qian—hence the "Kan male." Metal is white, so it is called the "white male." Fire belongs to yang, corresponding to the south and the Li trigram, which symbolizes the female within Kun—hence the "Li female." Fire is red, so it is called the "red female." When true metal is within the crucible, it is the Kan male, the husband of the Li female. When outside the crucible, it reverses to become the Li female, the wife of the Kan male. Thus, the metal mother receives the qi of the great yang and produces the numinous mercury.

Water stabilizes fire: This means metal and fire restrain each other, preventing true water from flowing away. The beginning of the Five Phases: Water’s generating number is one. The highest good resembles water: Water is the mother of all things, clear and flawless, invisible in its form. The image of the Dao is subtle and operates within the crucible, transforming endlessly, beyond depiction. When it transforms and distributes: This means the Five Phases each guard their boundaries, aligning with fire and the four seasons without error.

In the preparation of the golden liquid Returned Elixir, there is an altar, upon which stands a furnace, upon which sits a stove, within which is a crucible. The crucible contains the divine chamber, and the divine chamber holds the metal and water. The divine chamber resembles a chicken’s egg, and the metal and water are the same—layered and enveloped. The black and white correspond: Metal and water envelop each other. Its length and width are one inch, to accommodate the numinous mercury.

If one uses the Qian and Kun pivot to control Kan and Li, the ten-month gestation forms a body no different from a human’s, with four limbs, five viscera, sinews, and flesh all complete. These several lines reveal Master Wei’s disclosure of the inner and outer symbolic forms and the appearance of cinnabar mercury.

The *Divine Chamber Song* of the Yin True Lord says:
*"The golden crucible of Earth’s womb gives birth to life and death,
Seven inches long, the divine chamber shines with threefold clarity.
Five inches in diameter, one inch across—primordial chaos wanders,
Heaven and earth, five stars, yin and yang unite in a form like a chicken’s egg.
Yellow and white, inside and out, thickness and thinness even,
Six and one firmly sealed—guard well the southern gate."*

The *Cantong Qi* also states: *"Use water to fly the earth mother and restrain it."*

Here the text notes: *"Smooth as maltose"*—Peng’s version reads *"smooth as lead,"* hence the annotation is not included.

The Kan male is the moon’s essence, seeking union through the symbol ㄈ; the moon’s spirit is golden cinnabar, the sun’s soul is fiery mercury. When the sun retreats, the moon seizes authority—gold and fire alternately apply their functions, mutually restraining and subduing without harm. Between the dark and new moons lies the moment of yin-yang succession, the time of lunar waxing and waning, when sun and moon eclipse, obscure, and overwhelm each other, dissolving their forms.

This illustrates how within the crucible, yin talismans and yang fire intermingle with each passing moment, as darkness departs and dawn arrives, hours and shadows shift and change. Since they obscure one another, it is said that calamity arises. Within this process, the Kan male and Li female—masculine essence and feminine nature—mutually require, inhale and exhale, gather and generate transformations, forming true essence into divine medicine.

Gold transforms into water—when both exhale in verification, fire reaches its peak at the second and eighth months, gold and water flow freely. Either water emerges from gold, or golden cinnabar transmutes into water, thus harmonizing and manifesting their function. When fire’s number is exhausted, its work returns to earth; earth guards the four seasons, preventing water from flying, thus halting its movement.

When the male moves and externally exerts, fire circulates outside; when the female remains still and internally conceals, gold abides in the central palace. Though fire encounters gold, it is constrained by water; once the yin spirit is bound, the yang soul also submits. Cold and warmth entwine, each finding its proper place, both exhaling in verification to nourish and generate the numinous mercury.

*"The ospreys cry on the islet..."*—the more one observes, the more discordant it appears. Those who refine golden liquid and return cinnabar must first clarify the root of lead and fire, then recognize the principle of yin and yang. Solitary yin cannot produce alone; solitary yang cannot complete alone. One must await the union of yin and yang, the mating of female and male, the dragon’s exhalation and tiger’s inhalation, the male’s completion and female’s production. Thus it is said: a hen cannot hatch its own chicks alone; two women living together will not harmonize in affection. Moreover, between heaven and earth, if yin and yang are separated, the myriad things cannot arise. Therefore, one gathers heaven and earth’s true essence, takes yin and yang’s fundamental root as the foundation of the medicine. If one uses metals, stones, herbs, ice, snow, frost, dew, salt, or brine—things not of the same kind, solitary yin or solitary yang—even if the Yellow Emperor tends the furnace, the Great One holds the fire, the Eight Lords forge, or Huainan harmonizes, though altars are grandly arrayed, offerings and libations abundantly prepared, and prayers reverently offered to all spirits, the attainment of returning cinnabar will be as futile as using glue to mend a cauldron or applying brine to heal a wound—flying toads and dancing snakes will never be achieved.

The *Highest Virtue* is non-action—water resides above; the *Lesser Virtue* is fire below. Water and fire achieve mutual completion, which is the meaning of Qian and Kun. Water above remains constantly still, non-active, dwelling in yin without seeking through observation. Fire below constantly moves, revolving through the twelve earthly branches, its function unceasing. The upper closure is called "having" (inner water); the lower closure is called "non-having" (outer fire). "Non-having" serves the above: fire circulates the qi of the four seasons and five phases to nourish the divine embryo, hence it is said that above resides the divine virtue—this is the divine embryo of golden mercury.

These two apertures, the method of gold and qi, also mutually require: this means the two qi of water and fire, yin and yang, interlock and mutually require to form the divine medicine; there is no other path. In this chapter, Master Wei profoundly elucidates the great outline of the method’s imagery and the direction of the divine medicine.

*Know the white, guard the black*—the white is gold, the black is water. Knowing the root of gold and water and using it as the foundation of the medicine, the numinous essence will naturally arise within the vessel, hence it is said the divine illumination comes of itself. White gold is born from water, thus becoming a divine vessel; the water’s body is unbroken, making gold and water’s dual nature the pivot of the Dao.

Water’s number is one, the beginning of heaven and earth, yin and yang, the five phases, and the myriad things. Water is one, fire two, wood three, gold four, earth five. *"The profound contains the yellow sprout"* refers to gold and water generating the yellow sprout. The master of the five metals is the northern river chariot; the river chariot is water and fire, meaning the two qi of water and fire circulate to generate the five phases.

Thus, lead’s exterior is black while its interior harbors golden radiance—before lead transforms into white gold, it is mixed within ore, its outer appearance black yet concealing inner golden splendor, like a madman in coarse cloth hiding jade. *Gold is the mother of water*—gold generates water; gold as the mother of water means gold and water are born yet reverse their forms in water, like a child within the mother’s womb. The child hides in the mother’s womb: this means black lead transforms and lodges in the west as the white tiger, the golden embryo. Water contains gold yet again conceals its essence within the golden womb—this is true mercury.

The true person’s utmost subtlety lies in each guarding its boundary: true mercury, compelled by external talismanic fire, drifts within the mother’s womb, now sinking, now rising, flying and hiding unpredictably, as if present, as if absent. Following the talismanic response, advancing and retreating, each guards its own domain and dares not transgress. In this chapter, Master Wei profoundly elucidates the inner imagery, the state of true essence beyond sight and hearing, the elusive and imperceptible appearance and disappearance. Profound and wondrous! Divine and sagely!

Gathering it, the white takes precedence—this means that at the initial movement, white metal leads first. Refining it, it becomes vermilion—this refers to the smelting process, where with blazing fire it is wrought into cinnabar. Forging it as an outer defense with white within as the true abode—this is to take white metal as the divine chamber.

Square and round, a *cun* in diameter, blending yet mutually restraining—this describes the golden embryo’s form, modeled on primordial chaos, crafted with upper and lower halves. Hollow within, square and round, a *cun* in diameter, it holds true mercury. Once the two halves are sealed together, tightly bound and impervious, yin and yang align without the slightest leak, nurturing dragon and tiger. As the ancient song says: *"Seal the embryo to prevent leakage, and transformation occurs in an instant."*

Before heaven and earth arose, lofty and exalted—this refers to true lead, which took shape before the existence of heaven and earth, yin and yang. Lead first attained the One, then gradually gave rise to heaven and earth, yin and yang, the Five Phases, and the myriad beings. Thus, lead is the father and mother of heaven and earth, the root and origin of yin and yang. The sage gathers the root of heaven and earth’s parents as the foundation of the great medicine, assembling the pure essence of yin and yang as the substance of the returning elixir. Such a creation is beyond the transformations of ordinary things.

At the outset of elixir refinement, one takes the root of heaven and earth as the root of the medicine, the mother of yin and yang as the mother of the elixir. If one cannot generate heaven and earth, yin and yang within this process, it is not the Way of the golden liquid returning elixir. If one uses substances produced after the arising of heaven and earth, yin and yang—such as the five metals, eight stones, herbs and trees, ashes and lye, morning frost, night dew, snow broth, ice water, green salt, or white brine—mixing them together, is this not difficult? Those of like mind should ponder this long and naturally awaken. Thus, a later passage says: *"After ten thousand repetitions, the divine may be glimpsed. If one reveals it to others, there are walls and gates beside."*

In refining the returning elixir, there are the altar, furnace, crucible, and hearth, upper and lower connected like Penglai’s isle, open on all four sides. The crucible has a cover and a divine chamber, the golden embryo intricately linked. Within the crucible, there are also pivots and bolts, tightly sealed to block evil and prevent the true qi from escaping, thus avoiding stray thoughts and needless worry.

One must firmly secure the defenses so that the divine qi fills the chamber. Then, one must regulate and harmonize yin and yang, alternating their functions to retain the true essence and bring about transformation. If the fire talismans are misapplied, even with true essence within, it will scatter and flee. All depends on harmonizing water and fire, guarding without loss—then success is assured. Thus, the true qi’s movement and stillness, activity and rest, all constantly accompany the practitioner.

Internally nurturing the self—when the divine embryo resides in the Central Palace, the qi of yin and yang and the Five Phases harmonize, allowing the divine embryo to nurture the self in tranquility, effortlessly abiding in emptiness and non-being, naturally transforming.

Originally, it illuminates within and reflects the body—this means that metal can illuminate and, by its own light, when joined with fire, further enhances its brilliance. Seal its *dui*—the *dui* mouth. Once the golden tiger’s numinous root is settled in the Central Palace, one must tightly seal the crucible’s opening, directing the workings of the Three Lights’ true essence to enter within, nourishing the pearl-child and numinous mercury. Thus, it is said: *"The Three Lights sink into the depths."*

The Three Lights are the yang fire, yin talisman, and golden embryo, symbolizing the sun, moon, and stars. Externally, there are also Three Lights, distributed within the moments of activity and stillness, the alternations of yin and yang’s talismanic fire, transforming and completing.

Since both inner and outer possess the bodies of yin and yang’s transformations, they cannot be exhaustively discussed. At this level of subtlety, nothing is not oral instruction, difficult to convey in writing.

When metal and mercury transform within the crucible, their changes are unfathomable, impossible to glimpse. Yet by employing the celestial mechanism to control the symbolic pivots, metal and mercury dare not flee, and dragon and tiger achieve union. Thus, it is said: *"Near and easy to seek."* The *Yin Martial Classic* states: *"The cosmos is in your hands, the ten thousand transformations arise from your body."*

Chen Zhixu said: This speaks of how one must first cultivate one’s nature before one may refine one’s destiny. What, then, is nature? It is the thing of Qian. If one can nurture it, the yang of Qian will not wane; essence will be guarded within, and qi will arise from without. With this, one may refine the elixir and enter sagehood. Yet people do not know that nature and destiny are two aspects of one thing. Some conjecture that nature is numinous clarity and awareness; others take it to be the stubborn heart of flesh; still others identify thought and consciousness as nature, or point to the intangible as nature, or liken it to empty nothingness. Some even take *ling* as destiny. How could they know that the subtle, elusive thing is nature, or recognize the gate of ceaseless generation and transformation as destiny? With only blind guesses and dreamlike fancies, how can they attain the Way of the sages?

The Yellow Emperor said: *"Heaven’s nature is human; the human heart is mechanism."* The noble one who attains it preserves his person; the petty one who attains it trifles with his life. Confucius said: *"Exhaust principle, fulfill nature, and so reach destiny."* Mencius said: *"Preserve the heart, nurture nature—this is how one serves Heaven."* *"Whether one dies young or lives long, one does not waver; cultivate the self and await it—this is how one establishes destiny."* The Way of the sages, as transmitted down to Mencius, is perfectly clear. Yi [Wing] said: *"In ancient times, the sages composed the *Yijing* to accord with the principles of nature and destiny."* Thus, they established the Way of Heaven as yin and yang, the Way of Earth as yielding and firm, and the Way of Humanity as benevolence and righteousness.

The immortals above say: *"If one cultivates nature but not destiny, how can one enter sagehood? If one cultivates destiny before nature, one enters the path of practice."* Some say Buddhists cultivate nature while Daoists cultivate destiny—how could there be two Ways in the world? This is because they have not studied Mencius’ Way of preserving the heart, nurturing nature, cultivating the self, and establishing destiny. To establish destiny, one must first nurture nature. Without awakening to nature, how can one know destiny? Thus, the *Doctrine of the Mean* says: *"What Heaven decrees is called nature; following nature is called the Way."* Therefore, the sage has no dual heart.

This is why, if one wishes to nurture nature and prolong destiny, one must first instruct people on what it means to nurture nature. Dongbin thus used *"refining the self"* to clarify it. But what is establishing destiny? Zhang and Xu used *"refining the elixir"* as a metaphor. *"Only essence, only unity—hold fast to the center"*—this is nurturing nature. *"Male and female unite in essence, and the ten thousand things transform and generate"*—this is establishing destiny. *"Accumulating essence, amassing qi"*—this is nurturing nature. *"Flowing wu to ji"*—this is establishing destiny.

Those who scrutinize the end but neglect the beginning are people who, though endowed with intelligence, cling to perversity and refuse to turn back. They say: *"Since there is birth, there must be death—how could there be longevity?"* Yet sages, immortals, and buddhas are all born of Heaven. How could a teacher grant this to others? How could they achieve it themselves without careful reflection? Those who resign themselves to death should first consider: *"Where does my body come from?"* If one says it arises from the qi of father and mother’s yin and yang, then the qi of yin and yang must be able to prolong destiny and enable one to become a sage, immortal, or buddha.

Thus, refining the Great Elixir is no different from the moment of receiving qi at birth—only the direction differs. Confucius said: *"If one does not know life, how can one know death?"* The sages clarified the causes of life and death in nature and destiny, and revealed the Way of yin and yang—how it proceeds in reverse or in sequence. That which follows the sequence to generate beings is the human; that which reverses the sequence to generate the elixir is the sage. This is called *"original essence spreading like clouds, relying on qi at the beginning."*

What is meant by *"yin and yang as measure, the abode of hun and po"*? Yin and yang take hun and po as their substance; hun and po take yin and yang as their dwelling. Li is the sun’s hun; Kan is the moon’s po. Po is the yang within yin, governed by wu earth; hun is the yin within yang, governed by ji earth. Hun and po mutually serve as chambers, and yin and yang interflow. Nature presides over treasuring essence within, establishing the inner barrier; emotion presides over subduing qi without, constructing the outer walls.

What is the *"city"*? It is the encasing of the radiant flower. What is the *"outer wall"*?

The chamber for refining the elixir—at this moment, Qian’s nature stirs and straightens, so essence and qi unite as one body; Kun’s emotion settles and converges, becoming the dwelling of the Dao. That which is firm and straight, once applied, retreats; that which is soft and transformative spreads its moisture to nourish the elixir, producing the child within the crucible and returning to complete the work.

What is called the *"Nine Returns"*? Earth generates metal in four phases, heaven completes silver in nine stages; dragon and tiger intertwine, the qi of metal and silver returns to the crucible—hence *"Nine Returns."* The *"Seven Reversions"*? Earth generates fire in two phases, heaven completes cinnabar in seven stages; hun and po yearn for each other, the essence of cinnabar and fire reflects back into the crucible—hence *"Seven Reversions."* The *"Eight Returns"*? Heaven generates water in three phases, earth completes mercury in eight stages; wu and ji unite as one, the true mercury returns to the refining crucible—hence *"Eight Returns."* The *"Six Residences"*? Heaven generates water in one phase, completing lead in six stages; emotion and nature resonate, the marvel of lead and mercury returns to reside in the crucible—hence *"Six Residences."*

The male is white, the female red; metal and fire constrain each other. The male belongs to the Azure Dragon’s wood, receiving the qi of dui metal, tempered to return as white. The female belongs to the White Tiger’s metal, refined by the essence of li fire, smelted to return as red.

Thus, the water must be stabilized, the fire regulated. The altar and its surroundings must be pure and solemn, the medicinal substances authentic and correct. One must adjust the quantity of water by drawing and adding, gauge the maturity of the fire by refining and tempering. At this time, it is the inception of the five phases’ transformation.

The highest good is like water: within water lies metal, capable of generating *li shui*. Seek its utmost clarity, utterly undisturbed, without substance or blemish—only then can it transform. This is called the image of the *"Supreme Good"* and the *"Dao."*

The male and female are the forms of the Dao; Qian and Kun are the images of the Dao. Within form and image, the true qi of the One is born—yet one cannot depict its form or map its image. Thus, this form and this image each follow their transformative mechanisms, dispersing and residing, wielding life and wielding death. Jia and yi roam from the east, geng and xin lodge in the west—hence it is said, *"each dwells alone."*

Like a hen’s egg: the returning elixir has form.

Where black and white align, yin and yang achieve union. A span of one *cun* marks the initial coagulation of the elixir—first as minute as a grain of millet, then gradually expanding to fill that breadth. Not only does the divine chamber swell with abundance, but the *qi* suffuses the four limbs, moistening the five viscera, sinews, and bones, all suffused with ease. After ten months of work, the elixir takes form, shedding its womb—this is called the *"Yang Spirit."* The image of the Yang Spirit is the *qi* of the primordial, congealing into bone; thus, it may be rolled supple as flesh, smooth as lead, unlike the *jing* and blood of the posterior heavens that form men and creatures, whose bones are heavy and flesh turbid, incapable of transformation.

The *Kan* male is the moon; both emit verifying signs. *Kan* is water, the moon, the middle son; *Li* is fire, the sun, the middle daughter. *Kan* is yin without and yang within, its center holding *wu* earth to store metal and water, nurturing its yin *po*—this is the inner essence, the gate of Dao and righteousness, the white within black. *Li* is yin within and yang without, its center holding *ji* earth to house cinnabar and mercury, governing its yang *hun*—this is the accomplished nature, the body of benevolence and virtue, the black within white. The moon’s body is inherently black; receiving the sun’s light, its radiance unfurls without diminishing its substance. When yang loses its counterpart, the union is broken. When yin encroaches upon yang’s brightness, eclipses occur: at the new moon, the moon obscures the sun’s light, directly opposing it—the sun above, the moon below, momentarily veiling the sun’s brilliance. This is called *"yang’s form wanes, yin’s calamity arises."* Thus, men of this world cannot preserve true yang, repeatedly scorched by yin’s encroachment.

When female and male attain union, inverted and mutually drawn, when man and woman seek each other in reversal, the transformative mechanism arises: *Dui* metal transmutes to generate *Kan* water. Without true earth, *Kan*’s water overflows and floods. *Li* contains fire, which generates *ji* earth to restrain *Kan*’s water—thus, the water does not flood. Therefore, the Dao of the male is *Qian*’s hardness, outwardly bestowed; the virtue of the female is *Kun*’s stillness, inwardly concealed. When *Qian*’s outward bestowal exceeds its measure, it is constrained by *Kun*’s female, the *po* restraining the *hun*. The *po* belongs to *Dui*, the *hun* to *Zhen*. When *Zhen* male and *Dui* female—yin and yang—unite, they must not indulge in excess. One must alternate cold and heat, advance and retreat in timely harmony, neither premature nor delayed. Only then will female and male each emit their verifying signs, and their efficacy be witnessed.

The crux of this chapter lies in this: when one exceeds bounds, indulges in excess, or transgresses measure, yin encroaches and calamity arises—like enacting summer’s heat in midwinter or winter’s cold in midsummer. When the male acts and the female does not follow, when yang sings and yin does not harmonize, yin and yang fall into discord, all due to exceeding bounds and indulging in excess. Refining the elixir is no easy task; one must not take it lightly. The Venerable Pure Yang, having received the transmission from the Elder Immortal Zhongli, entered seclusion and repeatedly applied effort, yet deemed it imperfect and did not immediately achieve the elixir. Later, Master Cui of the Primordial Yuan bestowed upon him the *Mirror of Ingredients*, through which he gained thorough understanding. He then composed a poem: *"Beholding Master Cui’s Mirror of Ingredients, / The heart’s ground grows ever clearer."* Thereafter, his work swiftly bore fruit.

Later fools, clinging to non-action and empty nothingness as the Dao, drift through days, resigned to life and death—these are the great sinners of the teaching, how dare they speak of cultivation? The *guan guan* cries of the osprey... the greater the discord. The immortal elder directly points out that the golden elixir must have paired ingredients: one yin, one yang, relying on mutual arousal; one female, one male, to achieve transformation. If one dwells alone in solitude, where lies the mechanism of ceaseless transformation? With many females and no male, where is the Way of ceaseless generation? To refine the returning elixir, one must seek the primordial *qi* to complete it.

This passage’s words are forthright and need not be reiterated. *"The highest virtue takes no action..."* This speaks of the highest and lesser virtues, as the *Dao De Jing* states: *"The highest virtue takes no action and has no purpose; the lesser virtue acts and has purpose."* The *Wenshi Jing* says: *"Forgetting essence and spirit transcends life; seeing essence and spirit prolongs life."* The highest virtue is emptiness extreme, stillness profound; essence naturally transforms into *qi*, *qi* naturally transforms into spirit, spirit naturally returns to emptiness—this is the learning of the Great Dao. Thus, it does not rely on discerning pursuit. The lesser virtue takes emptiness and stillness as its root, the fire tally as its function: refining essence to merge with *qi*, refining *qi* to merge with spirit, refining spirit to merge with emptiness—this is the method of using spirit to guide *qi*. Thus, its function does not cease.

The highest virtue is one who embodies complete power, a person of *"nothing not done."* When a man reaches sixteen, his true essence is whole and prone to leakage. One of complete power can preserve and cherish it without loss. Meeting a wise teacher who imparts the Dao of non-action and cultivation, he perpetuates his longevity—this is the person of *"nothing not done,"* the complete person of the highest virtue. This is the sage enacting the transformation of non-action, the great person accomplishing the work of non-action. The lesser virtue is one who steals from the transformations of creation, a person who plunders the myriad things. When a common man reaches sixteen, his true essence has not leaked—this is called *"pure Qian."* Once desire stirs, the middle line of *Qian* moves into the palace of *Kun*; *Qian* cannot remain pure, the heart emptied, becoming *Li*. Thereafter, day and night, leakage persists—how much remains stored? Only the perfected person, without waiting for depletion, practices the sage’s Way of returning to wholeness, immortalizing the body—this is the person of the lesser virtue, one who steals from creation. This is the sage’s Way of following nature, the divine person’s work of action.

The highest virtue takes no action yet leaves nothing undone, attaining the complete body of the Great Ultimate and accomplishing the work of the posterior heavens—this is called *"not relying on discerning pursuit."* The lesser virtue acts and has purpose, seizing the function of creation to accomplish the work of the primordial—this is called *"its function does not cease."* The immortal teacher said: *"Begin with action—no one sees; / Reach non-action—all know. / Though non-action seems essential, / Who knows action is the root?"* These words exhaust the matter of the golden elixir—it is not easy. The perfected person acts out of necessity. Laozi thus said: *"Fine weapons are inauspicious tools, / Used only when unavoidable."* This refers to the same principle.

*"Closing above"* is called *"having"*—closing above is *Kun*, and *Kun*’s virtue is its stillness, its gathering and possession. This is the *wu* earth within *Kan*, containing the primordial true *qi* of the One. *"Closing below"* is called *"lacking"*—closing below is *Qian*, and *Qian*’s virtue is its stillness.

Here the exclusive focus on *"lacking"* constitutes the *ji* earth within *Li*, which conceals the postnatal spontaneous mercury. At this point, the aperture of dual illumination and dual function is revealed. As the *Classic* states: *"Abide in constant non-desiring to observe its mystery; / Abide in constant desiring to observe its aperture."* These two emerge from the same source yet bear different names—together they are called *"the Profound, the ever more Profound."* The gateway of the myriad mysteries. The sage has no dual mind; his profound words and wondrous phrases are never lofty or remote, yet none in the world can fathom their beginnings or ends. Essential transmission from a teacher is required. Even the supremely sagely Yellow Emperor, had he not studied under Guangchengzi, could not have illuminated this Dao on his own.

Such is this aperture—even the sage can find no recourse but to describe it. When all is said and done, Fu Xi drew the trigrams, beginning with *Qian* and *Kun* to establish this aperture. King Wen clarified it without redundancy, and Confucius expounded the meaning of *Qian*’s straightness and *Kun*’s receptivity. Laozi spoke of *"the gate of the Profound Female, / Which is called the root of Heaven and Earth."* Śākyamuni pointed directly to the treasury of the true Dharma, the wondrous mind of nirvana. All these are direct indications, clearly visible.

The superior practitioner may illuminate this aperture on his own, yet its functions and applications remain obscure without a teacher. *"Lacking"* is the offering to what is above—it is the wordless self-nature. *"Having"* is the *wu* disposition; if the self’s nature can serve the *wu*’s disposition, then the meaning of disposition will not cling to the benevolence of nature.

Above, benevolence and virtue reside—this is the primordial *qi* of the One arriving from the midst of emptiness, the virtue of spirit. The immortal elder further emphasized: *"These two apertures, the method and the qi, mutually depend on one another."* These two lines are the key to the *Cantong Qi*, a passage that even millennia later, the wise and discerning Yan and Min could not penetrate on their own. Master Yunfang said:

*"The gate that gives me life, the door that brings my death—
How many wake? How many realize?
Last night, iron man, ponder deeply:
Long life and immortality are made by man alone.
Is not that gate of life, that door of death,
The very two apertures?"*

Having previously expounded the principles of *Qian* and *Kun*, the sun and moon, and the workings of creation, we now firmly establish the profound barrier and directly point to the two substances, metal and water, arranging the text in proper sequence without digression. The cultivator of the elixir, having grasped this aperture, must further investigate: What is the metal-water? What is their mutual dependence and function? Only with deep and thorough understanding can one proceed to refine the elixir.

To *"know the white"* is to guard the black—recognize the purity and untainted whiteness of the metal’s essence, which is *"knowing the white."* Guard the foundation of the black, awaiting the time when water is generated, which is *"guarding the black."* The initial arising of water is called *"prior Heaven,"* for its utmost truth is named *"spirit."* When white and black align, metal and water flourish abundantly. Upon encountering *ji* earth, water is restrained, gold is refined, and the crucible fills with metal and water. Thus it is said: *"Spirit and illumination come of themselves."*

What is called *"water"*? It is the pivot of the Dao, its number named *"one,"* for water arises from Heaven’s one, making it the origin of yin and yang. The *Wings* states: *"Though yin possesses beauty, it contains it to serve the king’s affairs."* Thus the Great Profound is water, and the yellow sprout is the elixir. Water contains the elixir, hence the metaphor *"the Profound contains the yellow sprout."* Within black lead lies hidden white metal; within the river chariot resides the yellow sprout. This is like a scholar concealing jade, or one clad in coarse cloth yet unharmed. Metal is the mother of water, for *Dui* metal generates *Kan* water, and the central line of *Kan* belongs to metal—hence it is called *"the mother concealing the child."* The water of *ren* and *gui* arises from the west, and the central line of *Dui* pertains to *Kan*—thus *"the child resides in the mother’s womb."*

The true person is most wondrous—the true person is the body of *Qian*. *Qian* as a thing is supremely spirit-like and wondrous; its form is sometimes present, sometimes absent. The great abyss is the image of *Kun*. *Kun* as a thing can sink or float; its form is neither visible nor audible. Thus *Qian* can transform, and *Kun*’s virtue is to nurture life. Once transformation and generation are complete, *Qian* and *Kun* distribute and each guards its domain.

To refine it to pure white is to extract silver from lead. When congealed into the inner elixir, cinnabar alone is refined into lead powder. If refining the outer elixir, the same principle applies: the outer elixir must be externally protected, appearing as red and lustrous as cinnabar, while internally preserving its true whiteness.

Round or square, a *cun* in diameter, mutually constrained—this describes the object within the obscure barrier, the state before the Supreme Ultimate divided, when the primordial true *qi* of prior Heaven was contained within. It resides before the Supreme Ultimate, which precedes all images—hence it is called *"lofty and exalted."* Surrounded by walls and gates, it resembles Penglai; *Qian*’s image also resembles walls and gates, or Penglai. These metaphors describe the vessel, the body, and the self. All above refer to the cauldron and its apparatus.

Encircling and sealing denote protection; the four directions are open yet guarded, with external defenses secure. Vigilant and steadfast defense cannot be relaxed for even a moment.

Should it be forsaken, it is not the Dao. To block and sever the pernicious and the deviant, one must regulate ingress and egress with measured discipline, keeping inner and outer distinct so that awe is instilled and the True Lord within remains immaculate in its whiteness.

Where winding corridors interconnect, altars and sacred precincts must be established, their solemnity a bulwark against unforeseen calamity. The Immortal Elder, in his compassion, from the encircling seals and closures downward, has meticulously pointed out the teachings—yet unforeseen harm may still arise. Thus, one must abide in thoughtlessness and fearlessness, free from anxiety and toil, for it is said: "One may be without thought, but not with sorrow or labor." Only thus can spirit and *qi* fill the chamber without depletion, for it is said: "He who guards it prospers; he who loses it perishes." Even the slightest lapse invites ruin. Therefore, in movement and stillness, rest and repose, not a moment may be spent in carelessness or neglect. Then the *qi* of the golden crucible remains firm between self and other, mutually harmonized and subdued. Hence it is said: "It ever abides with the person."

This passage directly points to the essence of the two apertures, elucidating the function of metal and water. For the practitioner of cultivation, upon reciting the *Cantong Qi* to this point, one realizes that the very first step is already a matter of great difficulty. Those of lesser virtue who practice the Dao of deliberate action rely entirely upon the refinement of the self. If the refinement of the self is even slightly deficient, the numinous will not come, and the inner self cannot be nourished. To nourish the self is to cultivate the body and refine the self.

Confucius said: "The noble person secures his person before acting, stabilizes his mind before speaking, and establishes his relationships before making requests. The noble person cultivates these three, and thus remains whole." The sage, in his profound foresight, prepares for adversity and thoroughly understands human nature. Every action, word, and request—these three are the ultimate principles for entering sagehood, the essential words for truly nourishing the self. To treasure essence and enrich *qi* is to nourish the self; to forget the mind amid circumstances is to refine the self; to remain quiescent yet responsive is to refine the self; to accumulate virtue and perfect merit is to refine the self. To endure hardship in practice is called "refinement through suffering"; to perfect one’s conduct is called "refinement through mastery."

The cultivator of the elixir must first refine the self, enduring hardship and forbearance, so that when the time comes to enter the chamber, the six roots are perfectly settled, and one attains such mastery that forgetting reaches its ultimate limit. Only then can affairs be accomplished. Thus, the *Great Learning* states: "The Way of the *Great Learning* lies in illuminating the luminous virtue, in renewing the people, and in resting in the highest good." Having illuminated virtue and renewed the people, to rest in the highest good is precisely the refinement of the self. Moreover, "Knowing where to rest, one may then be steadfast; being steadfast, one may then be tranquil; being tranquil, one may then be at ease; being at ease, one may then deliberate; deliberating, one may then attain." This aligns perfectly with the essentials of quiescence and emptiness.

"Emptiness" does not mean utter vacuity. The Immortal Master said: "The primordial unity of *qi* arises from the midst of quiescence and emptiness"—this is the ultimate of quiescence and emptiness. The root and origin must be sought where one’s own embodied life began, for only then can one know that true essence and true *qi* are the foundation of this body. The enlightened understand that most people dissipate their spirit and *qi* through the desires of their cleverness. The great cultivator dismisses cleverness, sets aside wisdom, inwardly illuminates the form, and outwardly forgets the self. Sealing the senses and fortifying the numinous root, withdrawing the gaze to warmly nurture the child-pearl—sealing the numinous root is the refinement of the self and the coagulation of spirit; warming and nurturing the child-pearl is the accumulation of essence and the amassing of *qi*. Only when the refinement of the self is perfected may one proceed to refine the returning elixir.

Yet most people, ignorant of the great matter of refining the self, rashly pursue the work of obtaining the medicine in half a day, vainly hoping for success—how foolish indeed! Thus, the *Qingyang Jing* states: "Inwardly observe the mind, outwardly observe the form, distantly observe things—only to perceive emptiness. Emptiness has no object; the object of emptiness being none, there is also no emptiness. With no emptiness, there is profound and constant stillness, stillness without any locus of stillness." At this point, to the ordinary person, it would seem the ultimate rest, the great liberation. Yet how can desire arise from this? If desire does not arise, it is true stillness. Only true stillness can respond to things. Upon careful reflection, one sees that arriving at the point of true stillness and responding to things is the beginning task for the novice. If one speaks of refining the elixir, it must be thus.

Yet today, ordinary people perform a single fast, erect a single pagoda, engage in the Three Peaks practice, sit in desiccated meditation, recite scriptures, practice "non-action," or offer prayers and sacrifices—all these may be considered virtuous, but if one claims to be cultivating the Dao, one has not yet entered the gate. Thus, if the work of nourishing the self is lacking even slightly, it will not suffice. As Lü Dongbin said: "The sevenfold returning elixir lies within the person; first refine the self and await the time." The Mud-Pill Palace states: "If words do not resonate, they are not kin; if the work is not perfected, it is not square or round." Master Zi Yang said: "If you wish to refine the ninefold cycle, first refine the self and hold fast to the mind." How can one not heed these words?

The Perfected of Great Emptiness, having received the teachings of Lord Huangfang, proceeded to the Wuyi Mountains to undertake the work. At the outset of refining the self, obstacles and demons arose in the hundreds. The Perfected, resolute, declared: "Bind and dispatch the demonic spirits!" He beheaded the six thieves, then sat in settled breath. This thought, once arisen, was severed at the root of love. Only then did he begin to learn contemplation. This matter of refining the self demands even greater reflection.

This passage speaks only of nourishing the self, without mention of the golden tiger, the crucible, or the chamber. To equate the Three Lights with the yang fire and yin tally, or the golden embryo, is mistaken.

Yu Yan said: "Whether foolish or wise, all share the same nature and life. The noble person knows that nature must be nourished and life extended; uniting nature and life in dual cultivation, how can one not attain longevity? Yet to seek longevity, one must seek that which preceded one’s own birth—how this body came to be—only then can one discuss the Way of nourishing nature and extending life." Thus it is said: "One must consider what came before."

When a person is born, it is through the union of the parents’ *qi* that form is constituted. Form is the root that receives *qi*; *qi* is the source from which form arises. If *qi* does not obtain form, there is no basis for its establishment; if form does not obtain *qi*, there is no means for its completion. The heavens have the ecliptic as their measure, and the sun and moon travel within it—this is their abode. In the human body, the ecliptic is the path where the yin tally and yang fire move, the direction where the sun’s hun-soul and moon’s po-soul reside. One who can observe the Way of Heaven and grasp its movements, who understands the progression of yin and yang and the abodes of hun and po, can circulate the three hundred and sixty-five degrees of the celestial revolution in the space of a single breath. The sun and moon emerge from the subtlety of exhalation and inhalation—exhalation is yang, inhalation is yin—sharing the same wondrous function as Heaven and Earth.

The sun, being great yang, unfolds its light by day, hence it is called the hun-soul. The moon, being great yin, gathers its radiance by night, hence it is called the po-soul. The sun’s hun-soul is rightly termed a yang spirit. The moon’s po-soul, though yin essence, is also called a spirit because it is ceaselessly active, circulating through the six directions, coming and going without fixed abode, neither constant in ascent nor descent. The hun-soul pertains to the east and wood; the po-soul pertains to the west and metal. Metal and wood are not two distinct bodies—the eastern neighbor is the western household. Coming and going, ascending and descending, they alternate in their ingress and egress.

Nature is quiescent, emotion is active; nature governs the interior and establishes boundaries. Sitting upright with an empty mind, turning inward to observe, one seizes the golden essence as the foundation of life.

Emotion governs the exterior and serves as the city walls—this is the pivot and mechanism that journeys through the eight directions day and night, like a dragon moving in fire. When the city walls are intact and all beings are at peace, qi naturally surges through the joints and penetrates them, essence fills to overflowing, and the Valley Spirit abides.

At this moment, when one unites Qian and Kun, the River Chariot of day and night does not pause for even an instant, silently aligning with the great creation and moving in unison. When Qian is active and Kun is quiescent, if one abides by Qian, qi flourishes and essence flows, spreading to fill the Three Lights without fail—just as heaven’s movement directly nourishes the myriad things and reaches throughout the cosmos. If one abides by Kun, qi gathers and essence congeals, returning to the Original Sea to become the dwelling of the Dao—just as earth’s stillness gathers the myriad things and stores them within the soil.

Now, when Qian’s yang descends to aid Kun, and Kun’s soft compliance receives it, the medicine is born. Once the fire of refinement is applied, the firm acts and the retiring yin ascends, transforming into sweet springs that moisten the Nine Realms. How is this different from the creative process where yang descends, yin ascends, and all things are nourished?

The numbers six, seven, eight, and nine correspond to wood, fire, water, and metal. In the hexagrams, they are Zhen, Li, Kan, and Dui; in the directions, east, south, west, and north; in the lunar lodges, Room, Void, Star, and Wall; in the images, the serpent, phoenix, dragon, and tiger; in the seasons, spring, summer, autumn, and winter; in the hours, zi, wu, mao, and you—all are the same things.

Now, nine is called *return*, seven is called *reversion*, eight is called *homecoming*, yet six alone is called *abiding*. This is because the north, the position of Kan, is the native place where True Lead resides. When True Lead abides here, the upright qi of the nine metals, eight woods, and seven fires—like spokes converging at the hub or rivers flowing to the sea—all gather here.

"Male and female, red and white" refer to the yin and yang within the body. When yin and yang inwardly stir and resonate, spirit and qi unite, just as metal and fire constrain each other. When metal and fire constrain each other and rest within the north, in the water of Kan, spirit congeals and qi gathers, and the water naturally settles. If one can, at this moment, turn the light inward and reflect, exhaling subtly and inhaling gently without interruption, then spirit and qi will return to the root. Gradually entering, gradually softening, gradually harmonizing, and gradually settling—after long settling, when both exhalation and inhalation cease, the medicine and ingredients will naturally coalesce.

Thus, water is the root-source of the Great Elixir, residing at the head of the Five Phases, the native place where the medicine and ingredients of our body are produced. The Great Dao of the Golden Elixir is pure and non-action, yet it acts without contrivance—what is called "all day like a fool" is the true and proper work. Water’s nature is still and settled; when anchored, it is clear; when disturbed, it is muddied. This is why it is used as a metaphor: when one can be still and settled, the Dao naturally comes to dwell. There is a thing, undifferentiated, born before heaven and earth—its name is the Dao. It has no form or image to seek, much less can it be captured in a diagram.

At the moment of profound stillness and silent darkness, when not a single thought arises and all myriad concerns vanish, it is vast and boundless like the Supreme Ultimate before its division, deep and obscure like the Two Modes before their manifestation. Only this one thing remains, clear and profound like the moon reflected in a limpid pool, utterly still like motionless water without a ripple. One does not know which is lead and which is mercury—this is called the True One. When the time arrives and qi transforms and distributes, the light and pure ascend while the heavy and turbid settle below. Thus, in the Kan palace there is lead, and in the Li palace there is mercury. What was once undifferentiated and obscure is now divided into two, each residing separately.

The Golden Elixir is conceived in the prior heaven and born in the posterior heaven; its marvel lies in the moment when the Supreme Ultimate is about to divide yet has not divided, when stillness has reached its extreme but has not yet moved, when yang is about to return but has not yet departed from yin. Here, the mechanisms of heaven and earth converge, and the place where the elixir coalesces is known. If one grasps the knack of where to begin, then within the indistinct and vague, one seeks the existent thing; within the profound and obscure, one draws in true essence. Only then does one realize that "from nothing, something is born," and the marvel of the True One truly cannot be captured in a diagram.

It is like a chicken’s egg—smooth as malt sugar, its black and white being the two qi of yin and yang. When a person first forms in the womb, it is from the father’s essence and the mother’s blood, the two qi of yin and yang temporarily uniting to form a chaotic whole, like a chicken’s egg. After a hundred days, the four limbs, five viscera, sinews, and bones take shape, and at the appointed time, the child is born, emerging from the womb with weak bones, soft sinews, tender flesh, and smooth skin—called an infant. Now, in the method of the elixir, spirit and qi unite and coalesce, "from nothing, something is born," following the same principle as the conception of a child in man and woman. The ten months of warming and nurturing, with true breathing gentle and unbroken day and night, are also no different from the time before the infant’s birth. From this, one knows that in refining the sacred embryo, there has never been a case where one could achieve success quickly without ten months of work.

Kan is the middle son, yet its body is Kun; its central line is Qian’s yang, hence "yin contains yang," and thus it has the image of the moon. Li is the middle daughter, yet its body is Qian; its central line is Kun’s yin, hence "yang contains yin," and thus it has the image of the sun. The sun bestows virtue, which is the husband’s Dao; the moon spreads its light, which is the wife’s Dao. Now, taking the moon as Kan’s male is making the wife into the husband; taking the sun as Li’s female is making the husband into the wife. This is the inversion of yin and yang within the body, which thus mutually support each other without harm.

The eclipses of the sun and moon are the conjunctions of their numbers. When the sun faces the moon, the moon is eclipsed; when the moon obscures the sun, the sun is eclipsed. Thus, the sun is eclipsed at the new moon, and the moon is eclipsed at the full moon. Yet there are times when there is an eclipse and times when there is not: when they conjoin, there is an eclipse; when they do not conjoin, there is no eclipse. Master Wei used the sun and moon to illustrate the mutual support of yin and yang, earnestly emphasizing the crescent and new moon, the partial and total eclipses—his intent surely lies herein. If scholars do not cling to the text or grasp at images but turn the light inward and reflect to seek the sun and moon within their own bodies, the crescent and new moon, they will begin to trust that his words do not deceive me.

"Male and female mutually assisting, both spitting forth verifying signs": male and female, yin and yang, are both the two things within our body. If one can turn the light inward and reflect, causing one’s own yin and yang to unite within, then firm and soft will pair, the Three and Five will return to the One—why seek elsewhere for male and female, yin and yang? When the two things’ qi mutually resonate, they naturally exhale and inhale, coalescing into an embryo. The Upper Clarity’s *Clouds* says, "Within one’s own body there is a husband and wife"—how people of the past must have laughed at this! Scholars should seek it through purity and non-action, not through deviant indulgence.

Gold is born in the Kan palace—it is merely qi.

Before it transforms into water, it is merely subjected to the true fire of the Great Yang, which lies concealed and steams from below, thus melting and transforming into water. The nature of water is to flow freely and abundantly—who could restrain it? Yet when fire reaches its extreme, it transforms into earth, and earth can restrain water; when water encounters earth, it halts, and thus cannot flow.

The *Mirror of Entering the Elixir* states: "Water can flow, fire can flame—within the body, this may be verified." Therefore, in harmonizing lead and mercury to form the elixir, one must not exceed the proper measure or overstep the nodal point. Now, the Kan male moves and emits outward, while the Li female remains still and conceals within. If the outward emission exceeds its measure or oversteps the node, the Li female will then restrain and control it, so that yin and yang may unite, hun and po may restrain one another, and excess does not lead to dissipation.

When gathering the medicine, the firing process must be neither too swift nor too slow, but rather harmonious, allowing for union. The *Fu Ming Pian* says: "Hard and soft mutually guard, qi evenly balanced—the subtlety lies in avoiding harm." The hexagrams Dui and Zhen emphasize that harmony is paramount.

The *Guan Guan* cries of the osprey—how much more do deviations appear perverse! The great Dao of the golden elixir is simply the interplay of one yin and one yang. This principle applies to human affairs and the patterns of all things. Now, Master Wei draws from the meaning of the *Guan Ju* ode, where the virtuous maiden matches the noble man, to illustrate the pairing of male and female within the body. He also takes the meaning of the intertwining of the dark and yellow dragons to illustrate the mutual dependence of the female and male within. This may be called a broad analogy, a detailed metaphor—nothing is left unaddressed.

Moreover, when the Dui woman and Gen man respond to one another above and below, it is called *Xian* (Union). When the two women of Li and Dui dwell together yet their aspirations differ, it is called *Kui* (Separation). This clarifies that yin and yang value mutual attainment and cannot be one-sided.

As for the matter of furnace and fire in refinement, yin and yang must be equally balanced. If the medicine and materials are not of the proper kind, or if their weights and measures lose their guiding principles, then even if the Yellow Emperor were to tend the furnace, the Great One to hold the fire, the Eight Lords of Huainan to refine and harmonize, using gold and jade for the altar steps, unicorns and phoenixes for the dried meats, fasting and bathing, holding the register and kneeling to present the blue memorial, bowing with the vermilion petition to pray to the gods above and below—I would still see it as a waste of time and effort, laboring in vain. It is like mixing glue with mud to patch a broken cauldron, or applying cinnabar to a festering sore, or trying to dispel cold with ice, or remove dampness with boiling water, or dancing with snakes—only to see the deviations grow more perverse.

Some may doubt the saying that "the male does not dwell alone, the female does not reside in solitude," arguing that if one refines the elixir without a woman, it becomes a case of solitary yang. They falsely cite this passage as proof and mock the orthodox Dao as impractical—all of which are perverse and fallacious arguments.

The *Dao De Jing* states: "Supreme virtue acts without contrivance; lesser virtue acts with intent." Here, "supreme virtue acts without contrivance" refers to the spirit guarding the mysterious palace in silent non-action. "Lesser virtue acts with intent, its function unceasing" refers to qi rising in the female palace in an unbroken stream.

This borrows from Laozi’s words to reveal the secret of the Kan and Li cavities. The upper closure is called "having"—the Li palace has form and conceals true water. The lower closure is called "non-being"—the Kan gate is formless, the crimson dragon. "Non-being" serves "having"; above, there is the spirit of virtue. When the original spirit dwells in its palace, qi follows and ascends—thus the subtlety lies in firmly sealing the eight gates.

These two apertures, "having" and "non-being," depend on one another: if the lower is not closed, fire will not gather; if the upper is not closed, the medicine will not ascend. Therefore, the Kan and Li cavities must be mutually paired and dependent.

Yet "non-being" does not oppose "having," nor does "above" oppose "below"; "spirit" does not oppose "qi," nor does "virtue" oppose "power"—this interchanging of terms is to prevent people from rigidly seeking "having" or "non-being," "above" or "below," "spirit" or "qi," "virtue" or "power."

White is metal; black is water.

The elixir method takes water as its foundation, and the essence of metal is born within water—thus one who knows the white must first guard the black. By guarding the black, the white naturally appears. How to guard it? Like a cat watching its burrow to await the mouse: the body remains unmoving, the eyes unblinking, the mind fixed solely on the mouse, with no other thoughts.

Spiritual clarity is the mechanism of heaven. If one wishes to steal this mechanism, one must first empty the heart. When the heart is empty, the spirit congeals, breath stabilizes, and both sides ascend together as one pound—then spiritual clarity naturally arrives.

Heavenly One generates water, standing at the head of the Five Phases, thus serving as the pivot of the Dao and the beginning of yin and yang. When water is augmented with earth (five), lead is produced within water. Nurtured by earth, it assumes the form of the Yellow Sprout. Lead, the master of the five metals, is born within water, residing in the northern direction within the Mysterious Barrier. When refined by true fire, it emerges, and the River Chariot dares not linger—its circulation ascends to the summit of Kunlun Peak.

Golden Flower is the Yellow Sprout, the essence of lead. Though the exterior of lead is black, the Golden Flower lies concealed within, like a supreme treasure hidden in the bosom of a coarse-clad man. Metal is born of water, thus metal is the mother of water, and water is the child of metal. Water contains metal, so the mother conceals the child’s embryo, and the child shelters the mother’s womb. If scholars understand the meaning of metal within water, they will know the source from which the medicine is produced.

The Perfected One’s utmost subtlety lies in its nearness and ease of pursuit—the Perfected One is the original spirit. It exists as if present, as if absent, continuously yet effortlessly maintained. Vaguely floating and sinking, the Perfected One moves to and fro within the great abyss, faintly drifting like a shadow, now rising, now descending.

The *Great Compendium of Golden Elixir* states: "On Kunlun’s peak lies the path of the Yellow Flower; at times it leads the original spirit away, then brings it back." For the great abyss is Kunlun itself. In the elixir method, one advances from *zi* and retreats from *wu*; advance and retreat are distributed, each guarding its own domain without encroachment.

White is metal; vermilion is mercury. To gather them, one takes the white—collecting the white metal from the northern *Kan* palace as the elixir mother. Yet white metal and vermilion mercury are of the same kind, naturally following one another. In cultivation, the vermilion (mercury) daily circulates fire to draw out the lead qi while adding mercury in its place—this is what is meant by "true lead does not use lead."

Scholars merely know to conjoin lead and mercury as the medicine, yet fail to recognize the fire-work of withdrawing lead and replenishing mercury. Without this, even if the elixir’s head is formed, it cannot avoid dissipation. Refining as external defense refers to the true fire operating in the outer furnace. The steadfast dwelling within the white signifies the Golden Elixir residing internally. The inner furnace holds the elixir, but it must be refined day and night by the fire of the outer furnace to reach maturity.

The *Tai Gu Collection* states: "At the crown, the Black Pearl circulates its light; within the abdomen, the secret treasure is stored, radiant and secure." Is this not the fire operating externally while the elixir is concealed within?

"Square and round, a span in diameter" refers to the Mud Pill Palace. Today, people merely call the heart "a square inch," unaware that the three fields of the human body are all hollow, each a span in size, with qi and vessels all interconnected. "Blended and mutually supporting" describes the nine palaces of the head, with the Mud Pill at their center. The *Yellow Court Classic* states: "The Mud Pill’s nine Perfected Ones each have their chambers; this is the square and round span of one inch."

"Born before heaven and earth" refers to the Mud Pill’s single aperture, the ancestral aperture of the body’s myriad orifices. When this aperture opens, all orifices open in unison. "Lofty and revered" describes the Mud Pill Palace atop Kunlun Peak, the dwelling of the original spirit, corresponding above to the Mysterious Capital, the gathering place of the ten thousand spirits.

"Flanked by ramparts and gates" refers to the seven orifices arrayed externally, with the Cavern Chamber and Yellow Gate situated before them. "Resembling Penglai" is like the three immortal mountains upon the sea, with Penglai at their summit. "Encircled and sealed" means withdrawing vision and returning hearing, carefully closing the gates. "Fourfold communication and lingering" describes the original harmony circulating internally, qi flowing unimpeded, as the Scarlet Phoenix soars around the Jade Terrace.

"Guard and defense, firm and secure" is like a dragon nurturing its pearl, the mind never forgetting, or a hen brooding its egg, the breath never ceasing. "Blocking and cutting off wickedness" means that no matter how demonic realms intrude, one simply wields the axe. "Interconnected winding pavilions to guard against the unforeseen" means that if the spirit departs, it must be recalled, and the Twin Lads will forever guard the cavern’s gate.

Yet if the heart is without action, qi harmonizes; when qi harmonizes, the supreme treasure coalesces. If the heart acts, qi scatters; when qi scatters, the essence disperses. Thus, one may be free from thought, but not from sorrow and toil.

Through long and sincere accumulation, supreme harmony overflows. In movement and stillness, rest and activity, one must always guard carefully. Guarding brings prosperity; loss brings ruin. One cannot depart from it for even a moment.

This is what is meant by "in walking, standing, sitting, and lying, never leave *this*—walking, place the feet on level ground; standing, concentrate the spirit in the great void; sitting, regulate the breath at the tip of the nose; lying, embrace the pearl below the navel." Through long and unbroken practice, this becomes the true work. Otherwise, the mind-monkey is restless, the intent-horse gallops, spirit and qi scatter externally—how can one hope to congeal the elixir?

Thus it is said: "Empty the heart, forget the form, guard the One, embrace the numinous, and you will be able to secure essence, preserve qi, and complete spirit." Wang Chongyang’s *Golden Perfected Collection* states: "The wondrous function of the Mysterious Pass is not difficult to fathom—it lies solely in wordless silence. Only in wordless silence, concealing emptiness and utmost stillness, does the original yang true qi naturally return."

The great principle of cultivation lies in returning to the root and reverting to the origin, turning the light inward to illuminate, like metal and water concealing their brilliance within the ear.

The *Dui* mouth is the numinous root of the *Kan* palace.

Closing the *Dui* mouth seals the upper from leakage; consolidating the numinous root of the *Kan* palace prevents the lower from seepage. When spirit does not stray outward, harmonious qi suffuses and fills, and beauty resides within.

The Three Lights and the Cavern Chamber are the numinous images. If one can gather the Three Lights and turn their illumination inward, the Central Upright Position will yield the Mysterious Pearl.

Though the great elixir of Golden Cinnabar possesses no visible form or substance, if one seeks it through proper method, it requires but a moment’s effort before golden blossoms fill the eyes—how distant could it be? As the *Wuzhen Pian* states: *"The effort is easy, the medicine not remote."* Those who break into laughter do so because the seeker overlooks what is near and readily attainable, instead pursuing it in the distant and abstruse.

---

**The *Ancient Text of the Kinship of the Three* with Collected Explanations, Volume I** *[Below: *Zibu, Daoist Works, Ancient Text of the Kinship of the Three with Collected Explanations*]*
**Imperially Commissioned *Siku Quanshu*: *Ancient Text of the Kinship of the Three* with Collected Explanations, Volume II** *[Above]*
**Ming Dynasty, Jiang Yibiao (editor)**
**The *Kinship of the Three*** *[Eastern Han, Wei Boyang (author)]*
**Lower Scroll** *[This speaks of the sagely and worthy’s path of consuming the elixir and ascending in flight through Golden Cinnabar.]*

Of old, the sages and worthies embraced the Mysterious and held fast to the True, ingesting and refining the Nine Cauldrons. Their traces dissolved into the dragon’s depths, where they nurtured essence, cultivated spirit, and communicated virtue with the Three Lights. Their fluids overflowed, sinews and bones strengthened, all perverse influences were dispelled, and upright qi abided. Through long accumulation, they transformed their forms and became immortals.

Pitying later generations who loved the Dao, they followed the winds and gathered the currents, inscribing ancient texts and crafting illustrated manuals to reveal the branches and leaves while concealing the root, assigning various names to obscure the truth. Scholars obtained these works, storing them in caskets, passing them from father to son and grandfather to grandson, yet generation after generation remained deluded, never glimpsing their meaning. Thus, officials abandoned their posts, farmers neglected their plows, merchants forsook their trades, and scholars impoverished their households. I grieve deeply for them.

This text is concise and easy to ponder, its matters simple and unencumbered. Its clauses are laid out for inspection, its measures and weights precise. Following their sequence, I have composed these disordered words. The gates and apertures lie within—those who understand should reflect carefully and observe *[or: contemplate]* them.

The River Worker and the Maiden are numinous and most spirit-like. When fire is applied, they take flight, vanishing without a trace of dust. Dragons and turtles hide, their whereabouts unknown. To restrain them, the Yellow Sprout serves as the root.

All things possess yin and yang; to defy Heaven and oppose the Origin is to leave the Mysterious Female unable to hatch her fledglings. Why is this? Because the union is incomplete, the Three and Five unjoined, hardness and softness sundered. The essence of transformation *[or: the Dao of transformation]* arises from Heaven and Earth’s natural movement: fire ascends, water flows downward—no teacher guides them to be so.

The Origin’s governance is upright and cannot be altered. Observe the mating of male and female: hardness and softness intertwine inseparably, attaining their proper rhythm without craft or control. The male is born prone, the female supine, receiving qi from the womb and placenta at the outset—not merely visible at birth. Even in death, they replicate this pattern. Neither parents nor instruction compel them; it is inherent in the union’s fixed arrangement from the beginning.

The Primordial Yang’s flowing pearl ever seeks to depart, but when it encounters the Golden Flower, it turns and follows, transforming into white liquid that congeals into hardness. The Golden Flower takes the lead, and between the nape and neck, it dissolves into water. The horse’s teeth gleam like *banggan* jade, and yang then proceeds to harmonize. Nature and disposition naturally compel restraint; the time of yin’s confinement approaches.

The compassionate mother nurtures, the filial son repays kindness; the stern father issues commands, teaching and instructing descendants. The Five Phases clash and flourish, each claiming dominance to generate fire. Fire’s nature melts metal, metal fells wood, and the Three and Five unite as One. Heaven and Earth’s purest essence can be transmitted orally but not inscribed.

You should turn right at noon, then rotate eastward between *mao* and *you*. The boundaries separate host and guest, the two names: the dragon calls to the tiger, the tiger inhales the dragon’s essence. Both drink and feed, each coveting the other, until they interlock, swallowing and chewing, devouring one another. Mars guards the west, Venus traverses the heavens—wherever their killing qi descends, what can stand unshaken? Foxes and dogs guard against them, birds and sparrows fear the goshawk, each fulfilling its nature—how dare they make a sound?

Without grasping the principle, one should not speak recklessly. Exhausting family wealth, leaving wives and children hungry and poor, from ancient times to now, countless seekers have failed to succeed. Rare are those who attain it. Seeking widely for famous medicines only leads one astray from the Dao.

Cinnabar and wood essence, when united with metal, form a pair. Where metal and water meet, water and fire become companions. These four merge in chaos, arrayed as dragon and tiger. The dragon is yang, its number odd; the tiger is yin, its number even. The liver, green, is the father; the lungs, white, the mother; the kidneys, black, the child; the heart *[or: *Li* trigram]*, red, the daughter; the spleen, yellow, the ancestor. The Five Phases begin with the child; the Three Things and One Family all return to *wu* and *ji*. Hardness and softness alternate, traversing and distributing.

The dragon resides in the west, the tiger in the east. Establishing the warp of *mao* and *you*, punishment and virtue converge in joyous union. Punishment governs suppression, virtue governs generation. In the second month, the elm’s fruits fall as the *Kui* star descends upon *mao*; in the eighth month, wheat sprouts as the *Tian Gang* star occupies *you*. North and south, child and noon, serve as the guiding principles. The number one-nine concludes and begins anew.

Embracing the Primordial in *Xu* and *Wei*, one disseminates essence at the *zi* hour. Diligently practicing day and night without rest, after three years of consuming the elixir, one soars lightly and travels afar. Unscathed by fire, unmoistened by water, one can preserve or abandon life, ever joyful and free from care. The Dao is accomplished, virtue perfected—one conceals oneself to await the time. The Great One summons, and one relocates to the Central Land. When merit is complete and work ascends, one receives the register and the chart.

If one carefully encounters and perceives its beginnings, using analogy to measure things from start to finish, the Five Phases mutually overcome each other, alternating as father and mother. The mother contains nourishing fluids, the father bestows and congeals essence, shaping form. Metal and stone do not decay. If one is meticulous and does not leak, one attains the Dao *[or: attains the upright Dao]*. Erect a pole, and the shadow appears; call into the valley, and the echo responds—how can this not be numinous?

Yet the supreme image of Heaven and Earth, if treated like a wild beast—a mere inch of croton seed, a mere ounce of *ba dou*—once swallowed, leaves one rigid, unable to bend or straighten. At such a moment, even if King Wen cast yarrow stalks, Confucius interpreted omens, Bian Que wielded needles, or Wu Xian beat drums, how could they revive the fallen or restore the dead?

---

Chen Xianfang said: Wei Jun, fearing that later generations would not grasp the underlying principle, pointed to the ancient sages and worthies who embraced the Mysterious and held fast to the True. Without exception, they consumed the Nine Cauldrons and communicated virtue with the Three Lights, dispelling perversity, preserving uprightness, transforming their forms, and becoming immortals. Pitying those of later times who loved the Dao, he lamented that they would recklessly annotate and practice immortal scriptures, not only failing to perceive the truth themselves but also leading their descendants into delusion. Thus, he grieved deeply and composed this scroll.

The River Maiden of the north, the upright qi transformed into a maiden, takes flight when fire is applied, emerging and vanishing, dragon-like and elusive. Only when the Yellow Sprout is refined can she be subdued—like a child and mother placed in a lapis lazuli bowl, where even divine powers cannot intervene.

Those who attain the heights of my Dao will naturally comprehend it—things possess neither yin nor yang [...]. As Master Zhang Ziyang’s poem states:
*"Do not mistake solitary yin for possessing yang;
Cultivating but one thing only weakens the frail."*

Master Zhongli’s poem declares:
*"Do not claim this body alone is the Dao—
Cultivating but one thing is solitary yin.
Know that the Dao is called ‘one yin, one yang.’
Only after the union of male and female, the essence of the myriad things transformed,
may one speak of the Returned Elixir.
If the two things do not merge, the Three and Five fail to interact,
water and fire remain uncrossed, hardness and softness stand apart—
then yin and yang are severed, Heaven and Earth sealed.
This is what is called ‘partial yin, partial yang’—a malady."*

The Radiant Pearl of Great Yang [...], difficult to describe in writing. Human life resides in the *mao* day: it emerges at *mao*, and the myriad things depend upon it to live. Thus, the myriad things all possess the essence of Great Yang to establish their lives.

The Radiant Pearl of Great Yang is the treasure of life. When this life-treasure lodges in spirit, it drifts restlessly in chaotic thoughts; chaotic thoughts lead it to follow things and transform. When it lodges in essence, it clings to fullness yet proves hard to preserve; hard to preserve, it stirs desires and risks leakage.

Thus it is said: *"Always seek to depart from others."*
Yet few among the world understand this principle.
Even among those who understand, fewer still can preserve it.

Yet the treasure of Great Yang is fundamentally the essence of the Wood-soul. If it meets the Flower of Metal and is subdued thereby, it transforms into a white liquid, congealing into the Yellow Sprout—resembling horse’s teeth or lustrous jade. Confined within the *you* gate, it undergoes transmutation. The method follows the principle of the Five Phases’ mutual consumption: like a father driving his son, like a child returning to its mother, like a mother nurturing her child, like a child repaying its mother’s kindness. Thus, one first employs fire to melt metal, then metal to fell wood—both acts akin to a stern father driving his son. Only then does it transform into water, which is the mother of wood.

Hence it is said: *"The mother nurtures."* Again, it congeals into metal and water, where water is the child of metal. As the child transforms into the mother, it is said: *"The child repays its debt."* The essentials do not exceed the Three, the Five, and the One—these three characters alone. From ancient times to the present, those who truly understand are rare indeed. Without encountering a perfected person to transmit the oral instructions, how could one lightly presume to fathom the celestial mechanism through written words?

O disciple, when you turn to the right—how dare there be sound? You come from the right, turning eastward from *mao*; *wu* comes from the east, revolving westward to *you*—each traversing nine revolutions. Thus, the Dragon emerges from within fire, the Tiger is born from within water. Mars guards the west to restrain metal, while Venus traverses the heavens and appears by day. Naturally, the Dragon calls to the Tiger, the Tiger inhales the Dragon’s essence. The fox borrows the Tiger’s might to restrain the leopard, while the sparrow fears the kestrel that gazes upon the sun. Each attains its function and dares not exhale—all following the principle of the Five Phases’ mutual restraint, which admits no alternative.

Without grasping this principle, one strays far from the Dao. Lacking this understanding, one seeks external medicines in vain, squandering resources. Cinnabar and Wood-essence—all return to *wu-si*.
