Metal and Wood, *jia* and *geng*, mutually assist in their function, for both hold true earth within. When four parts Metal unite with one part Water, they transform into five parts Earth. When three parts Wood unite with two parts Fire, they too transform into five parts Earth. Though the Dragon of eastern Water and the Tiger of western Metal—three and four, odd and even—are unequal, when divided into three families and united in one dwelling, all return to *wu-si*.

Hard and soft alternate in ascent. From *zi* to *si* is the Qian trigram, hard; from *wu* to *hai* is the Kun trigram, soft. Recognizing this principle of alternation, the Dragon naturally moves west, the Tiger east; *zi* lies south, *wu* north. *Mao* and *you* establish the warp, generating and killing, rising and subduing, each serving as the other’s guiding principle, each attaining its proper time. In the second month, life arises, and the west’s *you* governs it—thus the elm dies and returns to its root. In the eighth month, killing prevails, and the east’s *mao* governs it—thus shepherd’s purse and wheat sprout. Proceeding nine revolutions from west to *mao*, one beholds the south’s *zi*; reversing nine revolutions from east to *you*, one beholds the north’s *xu-wei*. This single ninefold number contains the primordial void of *xu-wei*. Disseminating essence at *zi*, diligently practicing—receiving the register, bearing the chart, persevering day and night without cease—within three years, the Elixir Path is accomplished. Then one may enter and exit fire and water, ascend lightly on distant journeys, transform with numinous efficacy, and roam freely at ease.

Yet once the Dao is achieved and virtue perfected, one must remain concealed among men, accumulating merit and amassing deeds, awaiting the summons of the Primordial Sovereign of Grand Unity. Only then may one ascend to the Central Continent, receive the chart and register, and when merit is complete, ascend bodily to the Purple Palace. This is what is meant by: *"When merit accumulates to three thousand, one becomes an Immortal of the Great Veil; when conduct fulfills eight hundred, one becomes a Guest of the Grand Veil."* The degree of one’s merit and conduct determines the outcome.

If one truly encounters this path and discerns its beginnings, one may seize Heaven and Earth’s creative power, measuring the end and beginning of all things. The Five Phases mutually overcome one another—how could one not grasp this? Once understood, one may hasten death or prolong life. Poison entering the mouth, even sages and worthies cannot revive; an elixir ingested, even ghosts and spirits cannot harm. The marvel of the numinous lies entirely in emptiness and clarity, rooted in the Five Phases’ transformative mechanism, the true body of Great Creation’s arising. Thus, a pole casts a shadow—all is the numinous clarity of spirit-fire; a valley echoes a voice—all is the responsive resonance of spirit-emptiness. Metal and stone endure because they arise from the formless, while lead and mercury are most numinous because they are born of creation. This may be called: *"Within the indistinct, there are things; within the dim and distant, there is essence."* Who but the most numinous under Heaven could comprehend this?

Peng Xiao says: The Wei Duke stated that the Three Sovereigns refined the Nine Cauldrons’ divine elixir, consuming it to nourish essence, cultivate qi, and penetrate the virtue of the Three Luminaries, dissolving form to become spirit-immortals and guests of the Supreme Emperor. Yet pitying later seekers of the Dao—some relying on ancient texts, others gathering fragmentary instructions, offering shallow guidance, revealing branches while concealing roots, falsely attributing their methods to minerals, and confusing the ancestral tradition—he did not reveal the truth, leaving posterity unable to trace its origins. Relying on texts and following whim, they take the false for the true, hoarding their secrets in caskets, leading one another astray. Thus, fortunes are ruined, careers destroyed, and the Dao-heart wasted. Therefore, he recorded this篇, leaving behind concise words easy to ponder, straightforward matters free of entanglement, exposing the core without obscuring it with disorderly phrases—hoping to awaken the uninitiated, directly discussing cinnabar and mercury. The wise, upon careful reflection, will surely grasp its true meaning.

The Maiden of the Yellow River is true mercury. When she sees fire, she soars and leaps like a dragon, vanishing without trace. To restrain her, one must obtain the Yellow Sprout as her mother to nurture and preserve her. The Yellow Sprout is true lead.

All things lack inherent yin and yang. Without their union, there is no fixed beginning or restraint. The *Yi* says: *"One yin, one yang—this is called the Dao."* Between Heaven and Earth, if yin and yang are separated, the myriad things cannot exist. Solitary yin or solitary yang cannot generate or complete anything. Thus, in the workings of the divine medicine, if the Three and Five remain unconnected, if Metal and Wood do not interact, how can the Elixir be formed?

Fire flames upward, water moistens downward—both are natural tendencies. When properly matched and cultivated, they naturally produce their essence and spirit, for each receives the primordial qi, and their natures are thus determined. For instance, a male is born facing downward and dies in the same posture; a female is born facing upward and dies likewise. Thus, from the union of male and female, true essence is inherited.

The Radiant Pearl of Great Yang is the coalescence of earthly and heavenly qi into a pearl-like dew—pure yang essence and qi, capable of generating the myriad things. Though it possesses qi, it lacks form, hence it is called the Red Dragon. Yang fire transforms qi into cinnabar; fire engenders earth, earth engenders metal. Metal is the mystery of Great Yin, capable of nurturing the myriad things. Though it possesses qi and substance, it is called the Flower of Metal.

The Red Dragon originates in the Wood phase of Jia and Yi, also called the Azure Dragon—yang, father, husband, and fire. The Flower of Metal resides in the Metal phase of Geng and Xin, also called the White Tiger—yin, mother, wife, and water. This is the meaning of the Azure Dragon and White Tiger. Alternatively, when yin and yang are inverted and the Five Phases mutually interact, male and female alternately become husband and wife, which is the same principle. As the *Song of the Supreme White Realized One* states:

*"The Five Phases inverted—art of reversal—
The dragon emerges from fire’s core.
The Five Phases unaligned—path askew—
The tiger arises from water’s store.
The root of Returned Cinnabar,
The ancestor of Dragon and Tiger:
The dragon from fire is born,
The tiger from water is bred—
These words are immutable."*

The Metal Wife first intones, the Water Husband sows his essence; the Divine Mother conceives and gives birth to the Maiden of Cinnabar. As the Maiden grows, her transformations are manifold: she may dissolve into water or take the form of horse-teeth jade. Though the Metal Mother arises from water, amid her transformations, the watery essence never ceases. When yang moves to harmonize, their natures naturally align—this is the mutual generation of Metal and Mercury.

When urgency presses, yin constrains and the Gate of Prohibition is sealed—this is the operation of yin qi, providing sustenance and defense within the Inner Palace. The Compassionate Mother nurtures, and the Filial Son repays her kindness: the Compassionate Mother is the inner embryo, the Filial Son the true Mercury. As the *Song of the True Yin Sovereign* states: *"Yang is true cinnabar, yin is true mercury."* Since Mercury is born of the Metal Mother, this is called repaying kindness.

Their mutual devouring is the exchange of qi between Dragon and Tiger. The Stern Father issues commands, instructing his descendants: the Qian trigram is the Father, ordering the hexagrams to assist in rotation—the Li Maiden, the Kan Male, and the Five Phases together generate the Mercury and Cinnabar offspring. Returned Cinnabar takes "Lead" and "Fire" as its ancestral principles, governing all operations from beginning to end. The remaining qi, temporal markers, and symbolic tokens serve as aids in establishing the divine foundation. Beyond this lies not the Way of Golden Liquor Returned Cinnabar.

Fire can melt Metal, yet Fire is born of Wood, and Metal in turn restrains Wood—thus Metal and Wood reverse their restraints to achieve harmony. An ancient song says:

*"Cinnabar and Water Essence unite with Metal,"*
and also:
*"When the Three Natures converge,
The Two Flavors naturally bind,
The embryo is secured, no leakage occurs,
Transformation transpires in an instant."*

The Three Natures are Fire, Metal, and Water; the Two Flavors are Lead and Mercury, Dragon and Tiger. Thus it is said: *"The Three, the Five, and the One—these are the supreme essences of Heaven and Earth."* Such wondrous principles are difficult to record—how could they be lightly discussed with the uninitiated?

The annotation "alternately becoming male and female" refers to the Divine Water restraining Fire, yet within the Divine Chamber, Fire in turn restrains Metal. Mercury is water within Metal, Metal is cinnabar within water—cinnabar generates Mercury, Metal generates water.

The Child should turn right—how dare it make a sound? The Child turns right to You; Wu rotates eastward to Mao. These twelve earthly branches follow the cyclic return of yin and yang, aligning with the fire phases. Mao and You mark the boundary where Metal and Water qi balance. The Host and Guest are designations for Metal and Wood.

When the Azure Dragon exhales qi, the White Tiger inhales essence; essence and qi intermingle, generating pure perfection. *Yinghuo* (Mars) represents the extreme yang fire number. Guarding the West means fire qi presses upon the Metal embryo. When Metal receives the fullness of fire, its radiance illuminates the vessel’s interior. Metal qi, borne by fire, traverses the heavens—thus it is said: *"When the killing qi descends, how could it not topple?"* Then the True Lead of the Maiden of Cinnabar dare not flee, like a cat seizing a mouse or a sparrow fearing the hawk.

*Taibai* (Venus) is the essence of Metal; "traversing the heavens" refers to Jia and Yi. Metal, Fire, and Wood alternately restrain one another, yet none are harmed—each achieves success, and no discordant sounds arise. Without grasping the principle, one strays far from the Dao. As Master Wei said: *"Without understanding the principle of Golden Liquor Returned Cinnabar, one must not act recklessly, wasting wealth and burdening wife and children."* From ancient times to the present, countless seekers of the Dao—numbering in the millions—have failed to meet a true teacher or encounter authentic instructions, ultimately achieving nothing.

*"Cinnabar and Wood Essence—
All return to Wu and Si."*

Cinnabar originally returns to the Metal embryo to coalesce, while Mercury is Metal refined by fire, producing cinnabar—thus cinnabar is able to merge with Metal. Metal and Wood transform themselves; Water and Fire mutually operate. The four intermingle within, as Dragon and Tiger traverse yin and yang, odd and even numbers, ascending and descending day and night.

The Liver, green, is the Father: Jia and Yi govern Fire and unite with the Metal Mother, hence the name "Father." The Lungs, white, are the Mother: the Metal Mother resides within the embryo, constantly using Fire as her tally, hence the name "Mother." The Kidneys, black, are the Child: the Metal Mother is born from Water, yet within the crucible, Water in turn generates Metal—thus it is the Water Child. The Water Child, number one, is the beginning of the Five Phases. Metal, Fire, and Wood share the same function, their transformations all converging in Wu and Si—thus the Spleen, yellow, is the ancestor of the medicine.

Hard and soft alternate in ascent; essence is sown into the Child Water. Fire and Water alternate in ascent, traversing and circulating through the twelve earthly branches. Dragon and Tiger divide at Mao and You; yin punishment and yang virtue rise and fall. When punishment and virtue meet, they mutually restrain and harmonize—hence it is said: *"Behold the joy!"*

In the second month, the elm scatters its pods: from Zi, fire advances to Mao. Though yang qi prevails, it has not yet reached pure yang, and residual yin remains—thus within yang lies yin, and it suffers punishment. Yin steals yang qi, causing the elm to shed its pods in the mid-spring month of growth.

In the eighth month, wheat sprouts: from Wu, yin tally retreats to You. Though yin qi prevails, it has not yet reached pure yin, and residual yang remains—thus within yin lies yang, and it receives virtue. Yang steals yin qi, causing wheat to sprout in the mid-autumn month of withering.

This analogy illustrates how, within the crucible, the Metal Mother, amid the waxing and waning of yin and yang, either contracts or expands her true essence, or causes the Mercury offspring to scatter. The South (Zi) contains yang within yin, symbolizing the Kan Male; the North (Wu) contains yin within yang, symbolizing the Li Maiden.

The number one is yin; the number nine is yang.

The beginning and end of yin and yang serve as the function of the elixir; primordial qi generates the void and peril, and yang qi once more sows essence into the Zi to give birth to true numen. Diligently practice this—when you have attained the register and received the chart, devote yourself day and night to unceasing cultivation. Once the elixir is completed, consume it for three years, and you will ascend to wander afar, untouched by water or fire, able to sit or stand at will, to live or die as you choose. Secretly perform hidden virtues, conceal yourself, and await the hour when the Great One summons you to dwell in the Central Land. Count the days until your ascension, when you receive the register and chart.

The Great One is the master overseer of the refined elixir; the Central Land is the Divine Land. When worldly people first attain the Dao, their names are engraved on golden tablets in this land, and upon receiving the chart and register, they ascend. If one truly encounters this and examines the beginning and end of things, and having received oral transmission, perceives its signs, then taking up the crucible to refine it will ensure success from beginning to end.

The five phases overcome one another, then rise again in sequence, driving and cycling. The five phases generate and overcome one another, alternately serving as father and mother, mutually acting as husband and wife. Father and mother bequeath to one another, giving birth to yin and yang, which depend on each other to establish the foundation and together form the immortal elixir. If one obtains this, one should guard it closely to perfect the Dao.

If even setting up a pole casts a shadow, and an echo answers in an empty valley, or if wild herbs entering the mouth can produce immediate effects, how much more so the Golden Liquor Returned Elixir? Chen Zhixu said: The ancient sages and great worthies necessarily understood the supreme Dao, which is why the books of Fuxi, Shennong, and the Yellow Emperor all speak of the great Dao. Thus, the Yellow Emperor refined the Nine Cauldrons’ great elixir at Mount Dinghu, rode a dragon to ascend, and this was not a matter of burning and refining metals, stones, herbs, or trees. Rather, it was about subduing the primordial qi to form the elixir. Thus, it is called "subduing and refining," not "consuming and refining." (The scripture speaks of consuming and refining.)

What is called "subduing and refining" involves various practices: embracing the mysterious and holding the true, transforming traces and concealing like a submerged dragon, containing essence and nurturing spirit, and connecting virtue with the Three Lights. Of these seven, the first matters must be practiced—this is called "furnace and fire," which refers to refining the self. If one can refine the self, true qi will steam and pervade the entire body, like fire in a furnace warming it—hence it is called "furnace and fire," and thus "refining the self."

When the fluids, interstices, sinews, and bones become firm, all evil is expelled, and upright qi abides long—these four are called "subduing qi," which is refining the elixir. "Embracing the mysterious" means internally holding the unified qi of the mysterious; "holding the true" means carrying the true essence of the Great One. "Transforming traces" means concealing one’s light and hiding one’s tracks so that others do not know me, and those who do are few, making me precious. "Concealing like a submerged dragon" means sinking and hiding so that others cannot recognize me. Thus, the ancients who were adept at being scholars guarded the mysterious and penetrated the profound, making themselves unrecognizable. "Containing essence" means encompassing the true essence of mercury to refine the self; "nurturing spirit" means externally nurturing the spirit of the entire body to harmonize with qi. Accumulating merit to this point is the great essential of connecting virtue with the Three Lights.

Virtue is the highest matter in cultivating the Dao. To cultivate the Dao without cultivating virtue is to have yin without yang; only when virtue and Dao are both cultivated do yin and yang naturally pair. If one cultivates virtue while claiming to have it but in truth does not, this is due to deluded thoughts, and virtue will not resonate. For those who cultivate the Dao, the deeper their virtue, the less they think of it. Heaven and earth, gods and spirits, sun, moon, and stars—all respond to virtue. Like Zhang Guo and Xu You, who accumulated and cultivated virtue from the heart without seeking it, this is what is meant by connecting virtue with the Three Lights.

Once refining the self is diligent and virtue resonates, one may subdue and refine the great elixir. The fluids and interstices: fluids are jade fluids, which are white snow; liquids are golden liquor, which is yellow sprouts. When jade fluids and golden liquor fill the chamber of spirit, the sinews and bones of the entire body become firm. All evil—all the yin of the hundred bones—is expelled, and upright qi, the primordial yang, abides long and unharmed. Accumulating this over time transforms the body into that of an immortal. This is what the Yellow Emperor’s refining of the Nine Cauldrons’ great elixir refers to.

The *Record of Auspicious Meetings* states: In his first life, the Xuanyuan clan served as commoners; in his second, as ministers; in his third, as sovereigns. Having accumulated merit to its fullest, they ascended to heaven. Pitying later students who loved the Dao, they followed their example, drawing and composing charts and texts to instruct later generations. Some revealed branches while concealing roots, or adopted various names to obscure the truth among the masses of texts. What was revealed was only a rough outline, such as "Qian and Kun are the gate of the *Changes*." As for the roots, they necessarily relied on oral transmission, hence they were concealed like submerged dragons.

What is called "adopting names to obscure" means that what could not be made explicit was metaphorically scattered among various books. Later, those with clear eyes, having obtained the texts but not met the master, kept them in a casket for their entire lives. Only those who are vessels of the Dao, like Gongsun and his successors, can succeed. Some students, though they record these texts, lack a teacher’s guidance and become confused, leading others astray into deviant paths. They do not understand yin and yang, confuse the five phases, and rush about aimlessly.

Later followers of the Dao who rely solely on these texts without oral instructions are like scholars, farmers, artisans, and merchants who have lost their livelihoods: officials seeking office have no path to the register, farmers wishing to till have no land to plow, artisans holding skills have no means to apply them, and merchants calculating profits have lost their capital. Just so, students who possess the texts but lack a teacher’s instructions have no starting point, and their wild guesses and reckless actions cannot lead to the Dao.

The immortal sages, pitying this, refined the furnace and opened the *Dinglu*, making this text a model for ten thousand generations. Its words are concise, its meaning easy to ponder; its matters are few, its principles not cumbersome. Its clauses are laid out, its essentials verified, making it easy to peruse. Within it, numbers and weights all have their methods. With a teacher’s single instruction, following and practicing this can lead to the completion of the elixir. What appears as disordered words is the meaning of "adopting names to obscure." What seems like holes and apertures encompasses the profound mystery, serving as the ladder to the great sea and the torch for the dimly lit path. Later sages and secondary worthies should carefully consider and use it with caution.

Only the sage can understand the great Dao; only the worthy can speak of it. Thus, a father cannot transmit it to his son, nor a minister present it to his lord. Is the Dao of the sages not to be transmitted? It is feared that without virtue, one cannot bear it, and if spoken lightly, one may later stumble—hence the wise carefully consider and observe.

The River Maid on the banks—here, the river is a metaphor for the river of love and sea of desire; the maiden is one’s own yin essence of mercury. Why is she called numinous and most divine? Because she can give life to people, but also kill them, and she can unite to form the elixir. When she is utterly still and unmoving, a single numen is nurtured within. Suddenly, she responds and connects, rushing like a spirit. When emotions move, fortune is born from form; when intent is corrupted, mercury chases fire and flies away like dust, never to be retrieved. The dragon hides its essence, the clouds scatter, and the sky is empty—emptiness is form. To control her, the yellow sprout serves as the root. The maiden, if followed, is easily lost; without the one yang of the yellow sprout, she cannot be subdued.

The yellow sprout is the primordial qi, called the true lead of the One. Burning this lead qi as the foundation, mercury will not flee. Why?

The pairing of yin and yang makes it so. If things lacked yin and yang, they would defy the heavens of creation and turn their backs on the origin of life. Those who refine the elixir do not depart from yin and yang in establishing their foundation. Once the true qi of the One has returned and the elixir is fully ripened, only then can one leap beyond yin and yang. The foolish people of the world, not having studied the elixir classics, claim that cultivators must dwell in deep mountains, must first live in solitude, must abandon wives and children, must practice grain avoidance, must embrace non-action, and must engage in seated meditation—these are their ways. How foolish! Without employing yin and yang, without investigating the five phases, without distinguishing metal and wood, without knowing dragon and tiger, without recognizing lead and mercury, without understanding kan and li, merely relying on silence—how can one attain the Dao? Thus, this book vigorously corrects such errors, repeatedly stating that only the matching of yin and yang can be called the Dao. Just as a hen, without a rooster, may brood over eggs, but no chicks will hatch—this is solitary yin without yang. If one wishes to hatch chicks, one must place water in a vessel at noon and expose it to the sun to warm it, borrowing yang qi so the chicks may fully form. Without this warmth, they will not come to life. Why is this so? It is like the workings of creation: without the union of the Three and Five, without the intercourse of hardness and softness, how can the generative Dao be fulfilled? Thus, in the Land of Women, where no men exist, if they wish to conceive, they must choose an auspicious day and, three times daily, gaze into the well’s depths to borrow the qi of true water. Observing the reflection in the well, they engage in sympathetic resonance to become pregnant. This is the essence of yin and yang’s transformative power, the natural mutual resonance of heaven and earth. Fire flames upward, water moistens downward—without a teacher’s guidance, how could this be so? The origin is governed by the unifying qi, already settled. The *Yijing* says: "Great indeed is the primal qi of Qian! All things owe their beginning to it; it governs heaven." The Dao of Qian transforms and changes, each fulfilling its nature and destiny. Once nature and destiny are settled, how can they be altered again? Thus, observe the moment when male and female mate: hardness and softness intertwine inseparably, attaining their proper rhythm. Without artificial control, the male naturally lies prone while the female arches her body—these are the utmost principles of creation, which not even the workings of nature can alter. How much more so in life? Even those who drown must die with the male prone and the female arched—this is not taught by parents but is determined at the moment of union. Common scholars may find such words trivial, but the intent of this book is to make people thoroughly understand the principles of yin and yang, hence the repetition is not tiresome.

The "flowing pearl of the Great Yang" [至] deviates from the Dao. This passage clearly designates the flowing pearl and golden radiance as the two entities of yin and yang, further revealing the secrets of refinement to prevent leakage—this is most detailed and precise. As for the oral instructions, they are difficult to record in writing.

Master Gongyang reiterated this meaning in a gatha:

The flowing pearl of the Great Yang is the sun within Li,
The maiden of Li Palace is neither form nor beauty.
The bright dragon of the Great Yang is truly called the dry father,
Within cinnabar lies mercury, mercury is the ancestor of cinnabar.
People, led astray by the six thieves,
Carve and chisel, causing the flowing pearl to drip [like a dragon’s saliva] or flee.
Metal originally dwells in Dui, lodging in Kan to generate radiance.
The true water of Kan can then restrain the cinnabar of Li.
Mercury, subdued by metal and water, transforms into a white liquid.
When refined in due time, metal returns to its former state, first spitting forth radiance.
The true earth of *jisi* must be spiritually potent;
The earth spirit surges, a jade platform ascends to the high seat.
Exposed to the sun, the soles of the feet are guarded.
Golden radiance transforms into water, its color like horse-teeth stalactites,
Resembling *banggan* jade.
The dry yang becomes the guest, seeking the host.
Yang’s nature, yin’s disposition—harmonious [熙] and intertwined.
Yin is pressed by yang, yang is urged by yin.
They urge and press each other, yin restrained and nurtured
Within the two kidneys, called the Gate of Prohibition.
One yin, one yang; one Qian, one Kun.
The compassionate mother says: "Metal."
Metal generates the water of Kan—water is the filial son of metal.
The stern father says: "Wood."
Wood generates cinnabar-mercury—the son begets a grandson.
The grandson’s descendants, if not single-minded and strict,
Will issue commands chaotically, the five phases misruling in inversion.
Lead and mercury, cinnabar and silver, rely on earth.
Fire flourishes, generating earth; earth is the mother of metal.
Fire reigns, melting metal; wood fears metal’s punishment.
Metal, felled by fire, allows wood to flourish.
In the southeast, five parts wood, three parts fire;
In the northwest, five parts water, one part metal, four parts earth.
At the center, *wu* and *si*—this is the number of the Three and Five.
From one to ten thousand, through countless eons, the numbers align with heaven and earth.
The utmost essence responds—this responds to that, uniting.
The oral instructions must clarify: the child should turn right.
Whether halting or hastening, yang metal is born at *zi* and *wu*, thus rotating eastward.
Yin mercury is born at *wu*, embracing and securing yang essence.
*Mao* and *you* govern east and west, host and guest.
The two names, metal and wood, though separated by ten thousand miles,
Cherish benevolence and virtue—metal follows wood’s joy.
Dragon calls, tiger inhales; metal yearns for wood.
Benevolence is the host, one guest, one host—eating and drinking in kinship.
One crow, one rabbit, both covetous,
Like a man and woman, mutually swallowing and gulping,
Like a *chī* and a snake, chewing and devouring each other.
The spirit of the south, the Vermilion Bird, guards the west with fiery brilliance,
Refining metal’s virtue, the killing power of Dui.
Be cautious, do not err.
Containing and storing the qi of life,
It is said: "The Great White traverses the Yellow Path,
Returning faithfully to Qian’s gate."
When the killing qi arrives, the qi of life naturally spreads,
Like a cat catching a mouse, like a sparrow fearing the *zhān*—
Each fulfills its function, how dare they make a sound?
Without the oral instructions, how can one guess?
Wasting family wealth, pursuing heterodox paths and crooked byways,
Engaging in sexual arts, misleading the true and deceiving the earnest—
The weight of sin is great, who would spare another’s poverty?
From ancient times to now, countless have sought this,
Yet few meet true teachers, and even fewer succeed.
Without understanding yin and yang, how can one be like the vast multitude
Seeking famous medicines, only to deplete the true?
True qi, metals, minerals, herbs, and trees—
These are not the multitude, they are without feeling, far from the Dao,
Separated by ten thousand miles.
The methods for later learners are outlined in general,
Expounding and clarifying the profound classics,
To become immortals and true elixirs.
Cinnabar and wood essence [至] all return to *wu-si*.

The most numinous and precious treasures of the human body are essence and qi.
The *Heart Seal Scripture* regards them as the work and medicine,
Zhang Ziyang regards them as the treasure of life.
The books composed by immortals share similarities yet differ.
What is the similarity? When metal returns to nature, only then is it called the returned elixir—this is the simplest and most shared point.
What are the differences? Qian and Kun, Kan and Li; the female and male, the bellows and key; opening and closing; non-being and being; yin and yang; sun and moon; the Mysterious Female and *wu-si*; hardness and softness; female and male; the pivot of the Dipper and the handle of the Northern Dipper; the crow and rabbit; hun and po; metal qi and spirit brightness; the yellow sprout and the river chariot; lead and silver; cinnabar and mercury; floating and sinking; white and black; the Primordial Chaos and the indistinct; the compass within and the pivot; emptiness and the obscure; the true person and the great abyss; the walls and gates; Penglai and the Vermilion Bird; the *chī* and snake; the White Tiger and the Green Dragon; the pivot of refinement and the flowing pearl; golden cinnabar and mercury; the eight minerals and yellow earth; the two orifices and the dwelling of spiritual virtue; the crescent moon furnace and the suspended embryo cauldron; the red-colored gate and the bright window’s dust; the upper and lower *nuan*; civil and martial fire; cinnabar and wood essence; the river work and the maiden; the walled city; horse-teeth stalactites and *banggan* jade; the Gate of Prohibition and the knife-point; golden radiance and autumn stone; temperament and nature; host and guest; white snow and yellow sprouts; the Mysterious Ditch and the River Drum; Mount Zeng and the faint shadow; stalactites and the blue-green liquid; the Three and Five and the two sevens; weights and measures; hexagrams and tally marks—
Such names and forms are all treasures within the body.
Some are metaphors for gates and doors, some refer to the chamber of the spirit,
Some denote the cauldron and vessel, some compare function and application,
Some mix shapes and images, some liken advance and retreat.
Thus, the *Yijing* uses Qian’s straightness and Kun’s breadth as the gates of life and death.
The elixir method uses the Magpie Bridge and the Yellow Path as the routes of coming and going—
Yet all do not depart from the body’s essence and qi.

These employ borrowed names and variant characters—thus, when it speaks of cinnabar and the essence of wood, it obtains metal and merges with the preceding "flowing pearl of Grand Yang." The meaning remains consistent: the flowing pearl, the maiden, and cinnabar all inherently possess yin yet lack yang, belonging to the posterior heaven and thus unable to form the elixir. Metal, the yellow sprout, and golden radiance are the lead of prior heaven, which can refine the returned elixir. Therefore, cinnabar and the essence of wood are the mercury within *li*, the father of fire, the wood of eastward *jia-yi* that engenders its essence—this is the central maiden. Thus, the wood of eastward *jia-yi* and the fire of southward *bing-ding* are one father and one daughter. The father and his daughter, being yang within yin, make *zhen*-wood and *li*-fire their companions. The yellow sprout and golden liquid are the lead within *kan*, the mother of water, the metal of westward *geng-xin* that conceives its liquid—this is the central son. Thus, the metal of westward *geng-xin* and the water of northward *ren-gui* are one mother and one son. The mother and her son, being yin within yang, unite *dui*-metal and *kan*-water in their dwelling. Wood, fire, metal, and water—these four, arrayed in primordial chaos, become dragon and tiger, one to the east and one to the west. The dragon dwells in the east, its number three, hence called the odd yang number of the dragon; the tiger belongs to the west, its number four, hence called the even yin number of the tiger. That wood and fire are companions means the dragon emerges from within fire; that metal and water unite means the tiger is born within water. The liver, green and belonging to wood, is the father of fire; the lungs, white and belonging to metal, are the mother of water; the kidneys, black and belonging to water, are the son of metal; *li*, red and belonging to fire, is the daughter of wood; the spleen, yellow and belonging to earth, is the ancestor of the four. The son resides at the inception of the five phases, thus is the chief of the single yang. Metal and water, water and fire, dragon and tiger—these are called the three substances. When these three substances converge and act as one household, they must rely on the power of the two earths, *wu* and *si*, to accomplish their function.

Hard and soft alternate in ascent—when essence is sown into the child, the green dragon belongs to the east and the white tiger to the west: this is their proper alignment. Yet when distributed further, the green dragon establishes its weft in the west, and the white tiger establishes its weft in the east. Here, punishment and virtue converge, and dragon and tiger rejoice in their inversion, meeting face-to-face. When dragon and tiger meet and unite in one place, the two substances rejoice, governing life as their virtue. If the dragon remains in the east and the tiger in the west, each fixed in its own position of generation and flourishing, the two substances contend and disturb, governing killing as their punishment. Punishment signifies the discord of yin and yang, the metaphor of male and female meeting; virtue signifies the inversion of the five phases, while punishment signifies their sequential progression. Punishment arises when yin diminishes yang; virtue arises when yang harmonizes with yin. Punishment occurs when yin is abundant and yang scarce; virtue occurs when yin is scarce and yang abundant. For instance, the hexagram of the second month has four yang and two yin: as yang grows and yin recedes, though yang is abundant, it remains in excess. Abundant yang constitutes virtue, while excess yin governs killing. Thus, in the three springs, the myriad things flourish, yet the elm’s pods fall—just as in humans. In the prime of life, when yang is abundant and yin scarce, one grows stronger day by day. Yet at this time, desire-fire blazes fiercely; though yang is abundant, it is consumed by yin. Even with excess yang, it cannot govern, and the hundred diseases assail. As yang nears depletion, one’s thoughts remain bound to the realm of desire, exhausting all effort to seek yin. Excess yang encounters yin and is entirely consumed, leading to sudden demise—this is virtue reversed into punishment.

Yet if one of superior wisdom seizes the remaining yang as a stepping-stone and swiftly practices the Way of the returned elixir, one may restore long life—this is the joint defense of punishment and virtue, the joy of mutual encounter. Conversely, the hexagram of the eighth month has four yin and two yang: as yang is consumed by yin, though yin is abundant, some yang remains. Abundant yin constitutes punishment, while residual yang governs life. Thus, in the three autumns, the myriad things wither, yet shepherd’s purse and wheat sprout—just as in humans. When one nears sixty-one, with yin abundant and yang scarce, one declines day by day. Yet if at this time one possesses residual yang and practices the golden elixir’s Way, one may restore yang—this is returning to youth and longevity, punishment reversed into virtue.

At the hour of *zi* in the second month, the Dipper’s *kui* star aligns with the *mao* position, while the *gang* star rests above *ji*—this orientation belongs to the southeast, governs life, and embodies virtue. At the hour of *xu* in the eighth month, the Dipper’s *gang* star occupies the *you* region, and the *kui* star aligns with *hai*—this orientation lies in the northwest, governs killing, and embodies punishment. The classics state: when the *gang* star points to *chou*, its body resides in *wei*; what it points to is auspicious, while its location is inauspicious—this principle applies to all positions. This analogy illustrates the function of elixir-refining.

*Zi* to the south and *wu* to the north invert the Five Phases. The immortals and sages declare: when the Five Phases flow in sequence, they become the fiery pit of the *dharmadhātu*; when inverted, they yield the seven treasures of the great earth. Thus, only when water and fire serve as mutual regulators can the *Completion* be achieved.

Yang arises from one and culminates at nine; when yang’s number reaches nine, it reaches its limit and returns to one—this is the cycle of one and nine, ending and beginning anew. The *Xu* and *Wei* lodges sow essence at *zi*—the divine work of the elixir lies in these two lines. For *Xu* and *Wei* are the day after the conjunction of sun and moon, the place where the first yang stirs, the coiled serpent’s knot—hence the *Taiyi* contains the primordial qi of precelestial origin. When true essence encounters *zi*, it is sown; this echoes the earlier principle of *zi-wu* circulation.

Ordinary people hear only of *mao* and *you* as the bathing phase, yet how can they grasp the analogy of punishment and virtue? Virtue and life parallel the moment of obtaining the medicine, while punishment and killing signify its instantaneous loss. Thus, when entering the chamber, one must guard against danger and contemplate peril—only then can the elixir be refined. The immortal elder likens this to the flourishing of spring, when all things grow, yet the elm’s pods wither: within virtue, one guards against punishment; within life, one guards against killing. In the solemnity of autumn, all things wither, yet shepherd’s purse and wheat sprout: within punishment lies virtue, and within killing lies life.

This text discusses these matters with utmost clarity, illuminating its central intent: to obtain the precelestial qi. If one carefully discerns and encounters the *return*, one may rise and drive it forth—this means that when meeting a teacher, one must first examine their origins: are they upright or perverse? Compare this with the *Cantong Qi*; if they are a true master, they will answer without ignorance, leaving no doubt. If they are a blind teacher, they will know nothing of ten things in nine, fabricating strange tales to lure people into the weeds—some claim silence is the Dao, others insist on sitting in meditation and observing emptiness. Ask them about cinnabar and mercury, dragon and tiger, metal and wood, the separation of the three days, or the inversion of the *Zhen* trigram’s precelestial use, and they will fall silent, unable to respond. Foolish worldly people hear only that someone has sat in meditation for years or entered seclusion in several places, and they clamor that such a person possesses the Dao—how would they know of Mazu’s rebuke of Nanyue for polishing a tile? How would they comprehend the principles of yin-yang’s mutual devouring and the dynamics of life and killing?

There are also vulgar sorts who parrot others’ words, wielding sharp Zen wit and debating endlessly, calling this the "Nature School" and claiming it as the Dao to deceive the world—how laughable! What do they know of Zen or nature?

What is nature? It is *qian* in its ninth line, dynamic and direct. If one fully realizes this, it is the *True Dharma Eye Treasury*. What is Zen? It is *kun* in its sixth line, dynamic and expansive. If one knows this, it is the *wondrous mind of Nirvana*. When Zen and nature unite, earth restrains lead; when metal and wood interact, lead subdues mercury. Benevolence and righteousness are applied to nurture directly—thus, the alternation of yin and yang is the Dao of the *Yi*. Cultivating concentration in the *Li* palace is the essence of Zen; seeking the mysterious elixir in the watery abode is the safeguard of the Dao. Though their names differ, the three teachings are ultimately one. Observe the methods of cultivation in the three teachings: all require the same foundation to succeed—this is what is meant by "comparing with the same standard."

What does it mean to measure the beginning and end of things? One must assess the causes of human and natural life, the waxing and waning of yin and yang. Thus, the Five Phases generate and overcome one another, flourishing and declining in endless alternation—this is the Dao practiced by parents and the highest sages, nothing more than yin and yang. That which governs the storage and nurturing of generative fluids is *kun* and *dui*, alternating to become the Holy Mother. That which governs the marvel of bestowing and completing life is *qian* and *zhen*, alternating to become the Numinous Father. Qi and fluids congeal into essence, flowing to form the elixir—firm as metal and stone, imperishable.

Careful examination and single-minded focus prevent leakage, enabling the attainment of the Dao. Like standing a hundred-foot pole to see its shadow, or calling into a thousand-peak valley to hear its echo, yin and yang naturally resonate and interact, most efficaciously aligning with the supreme image of heaven and earth’s creation. Even the wild *yedu* and croton—mindless plants—can kill; though King Wen, the Duke of Zhou, Confucius, the various sages, Bian Que the divine physician, Wu Xian the wise diviner, and the acupuncture and prayer masters were all great, they could not cure their poison. How much more, then, when the true yin and true yang—sentient beings of the same standard—unite, can they fail to congeal the numinous elixir?

Yu Yan said: Laozi said, "Concentrate your qi to attain suppleness, able to be like an infant"—this is the meaning of embracing the mysterious and holding the true. The nine cycles of the nine tripods’ firing times are the nine transformations. The sages of old, without seeing or hearing, embraced the spirit in stillness; when the nine cycles of firing times were complete, they transformed and roamed freely, riding the void and soaring through the space between heaven and earth.

Those who contain essence do not externally exhaust their bodies; those who nurture spirit do not internally strain their minds and wills. With prolonged effort and deep practice, spirit is stored within, harmonious qi fills and overflows, and the hundred bones and myriad orifices are all unobstructed—naturally flowing like the Milky Way. The three *yuan*—upper, middle, and lower *dantian*—when primordial qi is preserved, primordial essence is produced, blood transforms into marrow, and fills the space between the skin.

Refining essence is most critical for filling the brain, for the brain is the sea of marrow. When the brain’s marrow is full, pure yang flows and fills all the marrow; then the bones are solid, the sinews strong, and one is forever free from the worries of cold and heat. Those who cultivate and attain long life do so by stealing the upright qi of heaven and earth. The ability to steal this upright qi arises from their breathing: exhalation reaches the root, inhalation reaches the stem, returning to the *dantian* and residing there continuously.

Yet the Dao cannot be attained in a single step—it must be accumulated through the refinement of essence and the accumulation of qi. Thus, after the initial moment of embryo formation, a hundred days bring spiritual efficacy, ten months complete the gestation, one year achieves minor success, three years major success, and after nine years of full accomplishment, all worldly affairs are exhausted—then one can transcend time, stand alone, grow feathers, and ascend as an immortal.

It is also said: one year in heaven is like one breath in humans. After the winter solstice comes exhalation; after the summer solstice comes inhalation—this is the yearly breath of heaven and earth.

After midnight comes exhalation; after noon comes inhalation—this is the daily breath of the human body. In one day, the human body has thirteen thousand five hundred exhalations and thirteen thousand five hundred inhalations. Each exhalation and inhalation constitutes one breath, and within each breath, one subtly seizes the movement of heaven, which corresponds to thirteen thousand five hundred years. In one year, there are three hundred sixty days and four million eighty-six thousand breaths, subtly seizing the movement of heaven over four million eighty-six thousand years. Thus, the turbid body is entirely transformed into a body of pure yang. First, the qi is altered; next, the blood; then the vessels, flesh, marrow, sinews, bones, hair, and finally the form. After nine years, nine transformations are completed. Only then is all yin qi refined away, transforming into pure yang, and one’s years become equal to those of heaven and earth—this is the Dao of long life and transcendence.

The *Yi Jing* states: "The Dao of heaven moves with vigor; the noble person ceaselessly strengthens the self." The *Yin Fu Jing* says: "Observe the Dao of heaven and grasp the movement of heaven." Alas, it is all here!

The *Zhi Xuan Pian* says: "Those who seek immortality but do not recognize true lead and mercury idly read thousands of volumes of alchemical texts." For the so-called lead and mercury in alchemical texts are all metaphors. It is for the learner to expand upon them through personal realization—one should not abandon the body to seek externally.

Only the ancient sages and worthies, pitying later generations of Dao-seeking scholars who could not grasp the teachings, thus gathered peripheral insights, illustrated ancient texts, and composed diagrams and books to guide them. Their true intention was to provide seekers with a path to trace back to the source. Yet in their writings, they often concealed the root, refusing to speak plainly, instead using the names of the five metals and eight minerals as codes to reveal only the branches. Later generations, failing to investigate deeply, merely stored these texts away, remaining confused for life, even passing this bewilderment to their descendants. They exhausted cinnabar and mercury in vain, achieving nothing. Thus, officials abandoned their posts, farmers neglected their fields, merchants lost their livelihoods, and scholars had no means to sustain themselves—how lamentable!

Master Wei thus composed the *Cantong Qi*, a book in which every measure and number is precise, not a single word carelessly uttered. Following its sequence without deviation, not a single character is superfluous. When its branches and leaves are unfolded, the core is immediately revealed—its words are concise, its meaning easy to ponder, and its affairs unburdened by complexity.

Some may see its three chapters as excessive, suspecting redundancy. Yet the three chapters are but one phrase. A single oral formula is dispersed throughout the three chapters, its words deliberately disordered and its gates concealed, lest the secret be fully disclosed. Those of wisdom, if they carefully ponder and contemplate its meaning, will surely awaken to the truth.

The true mercury arises from *li* (☲, the south), which is feminine and resides in *wu* (the seventh earthly branch, noon). By regional division, *wu* corresponds to the Three Rivers, hence it is called the "maiden of the river." Investigating its origin, it is obtained through emptying the heart and concentrating the spirit. Since it emerges from one’s own heart, it is called "spirit-mercury" or "divine mercury." Its nature is fierce and violent—when exposed to fire, it flees without a trace, like a dragon or serpent vanishing into hiding, its whereabouts unknown. Without the yellow sprout as its root, how could it be restrained? The yellow sprout is true lead.

Only when mercury obtains true lead can it be captured, restrained, and bound, thereafter unable to flee. This is why it is used as the root of the golden elixir. The Dao is spoken of as the union of one yin and one yang; an excess of either yin or yang is called illness. For example, if a hen lays eggs without a rooster, there will surely be no chicks. Why? Because solitary yang does not generate, and solitary yin does not complete.

Heaven and Earth enact transformation like water and fire—burning and moistening—yet this is not by human effort but occurs naturally. The human head models Heaven, and the abdomen mirrors Earth. Simply conceal the spirit within, guarding it without interference or assistance, and regulate the breath through the nose without indulgence or restraint. Naturally, there will be opening and closing, receiving and bestowing, no different from the Dao of Heaven and Earth’s transformative work. When the time arrives and the qi stirs, the mechanism moves: fire arises from below the navel, and water is born within the crown. Its wonder lies in its spontaneity, not in striving through effort or force.

Thus, this Dao is a creation of Heaven and Earth, fixed and unchanging. The *Cuixu Pian* states: "It is like a husband and wife in conjugal union—when they guard the essence and blood, the infant of the golden elixir is formed." This union refers to the internal resonance of yin and yang, the intermingling of spirit and qi. When spirit enters qi, and qi merges with spirit, they meet at the right moment, forming an inseparable bond of hardness and softness. There is no craft or control to be applied; one simply observes its occurrence without knowing how it comes to be.

The *Xinyin Jing Zhu* says: "Men and women receive the same endowment; it is only the sequence of resonance that distinguishes yin and yang." The *Qu Yi Shuo* states: "Opening qi is male; closing qi is female." Yang qi gathers in the front, so men are front-heavy at birth and must lie prone. Yin qi gathers in the back, so women are back-heavy at birth and must lie supine. This remains true even at death, as can be observed in those who drown. Was this taught by parents? No—it was determined at the moment of receiving qi, when the sequence of opening and closing was already fixed.

Master Wei’s intent is for students to thoroughly understand the yin and yang within their own bodies, to know the natural principles of creation and transformation. The "flowing pearl of great yang" is spirit-mercury. The *Lingyuan Dadao Ge* says: "This substance has no fixed position; it changes with time and shifts with the heart’s intent. In the body, when stimulated by heat, it becomes sweat; when the nose encounters wind, it becomes mucus; when the kidneys unite, it becomes essence; when the eyes grieve, it becomes tears." Thus, it constantly seeks to leave the body.

Golden radiance is true lead. When mercury obtains true lead, it turns and clings to it, naturally ceasing to flee or wander, lingering within and transforming into golden liquid, congealing into utmost hardness. True lead arises from the *kan* (☵, the north) position, first as liquid, then congealing into the yellow sprout, hence the metaphors of "horse’s teeth" and "red jade." The *Cuixu Pian* says: "The cinnabar head is nothing but primordial qi; when refined, the yellow sprout emits jade blossoms."

Lead is the ruler; mercury is the minister. Lead moves first, and mercury responds—like a ruler initiating and a minister harmonizing. Lead belongs to metal; mercury belongs to wood. Wood’s nature loves metal, and metal’s essence yearns for wood. When yin and yang meet, they naturally resonate and unite. In elixir-making, one should closely observe the moment when yin reaches its extreme and welcomes the first stirring of yang, seizing the mechanism to advance the fire. It must not be excessive nor insufficient—only then can the hundred vessels be urged to return to the source, the gates of exhaustion be penetrated to the core, and the sovereign fire, ministerial fire, and common fire all be gathered in the yellow palace to forge the supreme treasure.

True lead is conceived in the womb of the *kun* mother, nurtured like a loving mother’s care. When the flying work reaches the *gan* palace, it captures and restrains true mercury, uniting with it to form the elixir, which then returns to the *kun* mother’s abode—this is the filial son repaying kindness, the kind crow feeding its parent in return. Yet without the fierce fire of scorching and extreme refinement, true lead cannot take flight—this is the strict father’s instruction and command.

Metal generates water, and wood generates fire—this is the normal sequence of the Five Phases. But in elixir-making, wood and fire become companions, and fire reverses to generate wood; metal and water unite, and water reverses to generate metal. Thus, it is said: "The Five Phases are arranged in mutual dominance to generate." Fire’s nature melts metal—this is moving the fire of the southern *li* palace to refine the metal within northern water. Metal cuts down wood’s flourishing—this is moving the metal within northern water to restrain the wood within southern fire.

One (water) and two (fire) combine to form three; five is earth. Three and five become one—water, fire, and earth intermingle and fuse into a single qi. At this time, the mysterious yellow intermingles, and the clear and turbid are not yet separated, like the primordial chaos of Heaven and Earth. After a short while, when the time arrives and qi transforms, something arises from nothing. In the profound and indistinct, a mass coalesces, and the supreme essence of Heaven and Earth is conceived within.

Qutu Shenhe said: "If one understands the unity of three and five, one may become an earth-walking immortal. Its wonder must be received by the heart and transmitted orally—it cannot be fully expressed in writing." Without encountering a wise teacher, students can only speculate on their own. You should turn right from the west, rotating to the *zi* position, where the tiger is born from water. *Wu* is the east rotating to the south, where the dragon emerges from fire. *Zi* and *wu* are north and south, water and fire; *mao* and *you* are east and west, metal and wood.

Right rotation and left rotation, one concealing and one arising—then water and fire unite, and metal and wood naturally do not separate. However, *mao* and *you* as east and west are alternate names for metal and wood, not the directions of Heaven and Earth or the left and right of the human body. Yet one must not abandon the body to seek elsewhere. When making the elixir, the southern *li* dragon is driven downward to call to the northern *kan* tiger, which then inhales and absorbs the dragon’s essence. With each exhalation and inhalation, the two consume and merge into one.

When Mars guards the west, fire enters the metal region. When Venus traverses the heavens, metal passes through the southern *wu* position and appears. When making the elixir, the spirit-fire is directed to illuminate the metal region. Pressured by fire, the metal takes flight and ascends, appearing in the south. This metal is true lead.

Wherever the killing *qi* descends, what can remain unshaken? True lead ascends to capture true mercury, and true mercury naturally emerges to surrender to true lead. At this point, spirit enters *qi* to form the embryo—like a wildcat or dog lying in wait for a rat. The medicine, refined by fire, becomes the elixir—like birds and sparrows fearing the hawk. This arises from the mutual restraint of their natures: at first sight, they submit of their own accord, and thus dare not make a sound.

The method is supremely divine and exceedingly simple, yet one may fail only by not knowing what the dragon and tiger truly are. The *Returning to Metal Chapter* states: "Without grasping the origin of yin and yang, one labors in vain to discuss the mysterious workings—how much less can one succeed?" Without understanding the principle, how can one dare to speak recklessly from mere conjecture?

From ancient times to the present, countless seekers have pursued famous medicines, consorted with wandering adepts, and squandered their family wealth, causing brothers, wives, and children to scatter—yet in the end, none achieved harmony. How rare are those who succeed in the world! Why? Because they entered the gate in error, straying far from the Dao, and never encountered a luminous teacher to illuminate the principle.

Now, mercury is originally the essence of wood, and its nature is to flee and wander, making it difficult to subdue. Only when cast into the lead crucible does it merge with lead—this is what is meant by "moving to the western position to stand alongside metal." The *Celestial One* generates water, dwelling in the north, its symbol the Dark Warrior. The *Earthly Two* generates fire, dwelling in the south, its symbol the Vermilion Bird. The *Celestial Three* generates wood, dwelling in the east, its symbol the Azure Dragon. The *Earthly Four* generates metal, dwelling in the west, its symbol the White Tiger. These four each reside in their own quadrant, fundamentally unrelated. Yet when we speak of "metal and water uniting," it refers to the metal of the western *White Tiger* descending into wood. When we speak of "water and fire as companions," it refers to the wood of the eastern *Azure Dragon* ascending into fire. Thus, metal is no longer in the west but unites with water in the north; water is no longer in the east but becomes fire’s companion in the south. The White Tiger transforms into the Black Tiger, and the Azure Dragon becomes the Red Dragon—this is what is meant by "the yang dragon originally emerges from the *Li* palace, and the yin tiger returns from the *Kan* position." When metal, water, wood, and fire gather as one, they merge into a primal chaos like the undivided *Taiji*. When they separate into two, the *Zhen* dragon-mercury emerges from the *Li* region, and the *Dui* tiger-lead is born in the *Kan* palace.

Though the four each have their own symbol, we speak only of dragon and tiger because north and south form the warp, while east and west form the weft. As Zhang Ziyang said: "The Four Symbols do not depart from the Two Bodies"—this truly captures the essence of their mutual restraint.

The liver is green and the lungs are white, representing metal and wood. The kidneys are black and the heart is red, representing water and fire. Metal generates water, and wood generates fire, so the liver and lungs are likened to parents, while the heart and kidneys are likened to children. Yet metal and wood both arise from earth, so the spleen is called the ancestor.

Why, though the kidneys are considered the child, are they assigned the first of the five? It is because the kidneys belong to water, and the number of water is one. The *qi* of the day begins at *zi*, born in the kidneys, then cycles through the liver, heart, spleen, and lungs. Yet why are the five not called the Five Viscera but instead the Three Substances? It is because metal and water unite, water and fire become companions, and together with *wu-ji* earth, they form the Three.

The Four Symbols and Five Phases all rely on earth; without *wu* earth, the elixir cannot form. This is why the Three merge into the One Earth. The Golden Elixir is originally formless, born from the center—it is not a tangible substance but merely uses the Five Phases as a metaphor. Within the human body, the two *qi* nourish each other, circulating and flowing through the celestial stems and branches. Like the movement of the celestial net, when *mao* and *you* align, metal and water naturally remain inseparable.

The subduing of the killing *qi* is like its return to the ocean depths—akin to autumn’s solemnity, gathering all things inward. The arising of the generating *qi* is like its ascent to the southern sea—akin to spring’s vitality, flourishing outward. In the second month, *mao* is established, and the lunar general is *He Kui*, which belongs to *xu*. Within *xu* lies *xin*, the withered elm—this month, the killing *qi* of *xin* metal descends upon *mao*. In the eighth month, *you* is established, and the lunar general is *Tian Gang*, which belongs to *chen*. Within *chen* lies *yi*, the sprouting wheat—this month, the generating *qi* of *yi* wood occupies *you*.

When the sun travels the western path, it is spring, and in the second month at *mao*, the western path lies in the northeast, while the eastern path is in the south. When the sun travels the eastern path, it is autumn, and in the eighth month at *you*, the eastern path lies in the southwest, while the western path is in the north. North and south are the warp of Heaven and Earth; east and west are the weft. Within the crucible, when *Kui* and *Gang* revolve, the dragon flees west, the tiger flees east, *zi* is in the south, and *wu* is in the north—just like the creative transformations of the second and eighth months at *mao* and *you*. This is a metaphor for the Heaven and Earth within the body: ascent contains descent, descent contains ascent, and fire holds peril, warning one to guard the mind like a city, lest it scatter.

Yang is born from one and completed at nine. When the yang number reaches nine, it reaches its limit and returns to one. The *Xuan Wei* is the place where the sun and moon unite, between *hai* and *zi*—the moment when yin reaches its extreme and yang is born. *Zi* is where the first yang stirs. The elixir method lies in the *prior-heaven*, containing only the *qi* of the *Taiyi*. In the *post-heaven*, it transforms into true essence and is disseminated.

The Great Dao of the Golden Elixir differs from the minor, fleeting methods of side paths. One must sever worldly affairs, focus the mind, and diligently practice day and night without the slightest lapse. After entering seclusion for a year, one must continue to nurture the elixir and not hastily venture far. Only after three years will the body become like mist and clouds, walking as if flying—able to lightly ascend and travel afar. Though great floods rise to the heavens, they cannot drown me; though severe drought melts metal and stone, scorching mountains and earth, I remain unscathed. Life and death lie within my control, and I dwell in eternal joy without care. Yet once the nurturing is complete, one must still conceal oneself among people, accumulating merit and virtue, guiding and enlightening the lost, before one can ascend as an immortal. Thus, Zhu Linggen admonished Huangfu Lüdao to guide later students, warning that one cannot seclude oneself in obscurity—one must first benefit all beings before ascending as an immortal. How can one merely cultivate oneself in solitude?

Generally, the minor arts of side paths are complex and difficult, easy to encounter but hard to accomplish. The Great Dao of the Golden Elixir is simple and easy, hard to encounter but easy to accomplish. Once one discerns the signs of its encounter and compares them to the mutual resonances of things, measuring the beginning and end of their principles, then within Heaven and Earth—whether vast or minute, high or low, sentient or insentient—everywhere is the Dao, and all things touch and nurture it. Like the crane’s midnight cry or the rooster’s crow at dawn—why is this?

When one aligns with the qi of Heaven and Earth, all resonates in accord. Consider the cold cicada drinking the wind, the rhinoceros gazing at the moon, the jade-green pool beneath the night moon, or the evening clouds veiling the blue mountains—all are manifestations of the Golden Elixir’s imagery. As Zhang Ziyang said: *"Those who cultivate and refine must thoroughly comprehend yin and yang, deeply penetrate the workings of creation."* Is this not true?

The essence of the Elixir’s method lies in nothing greater than the Five Phases. The marvel of the Five Phases lies in refining metal with fire, subduing wood with metal. When fire flourishes, water tempers it; when water flourishes, earth restrains it—this is the mutual conquest of the Five Phases. Metal generates water, for water is the child of metal, yet within water, metal is born. Wood generates fire, for fire is the child of wood, yet within fire, wood is born—thus they alternate as father and mother. The father is like Heaven, the mother like Earth: Heaven’s qi descends to Earth, and Earth receives it to fulfill the work of generation and nurturance. Hence it is said: *"The mother contains the nourishing fluids; the father bestows the endowment."* When the essence of the Five Phases coalesces into metal and stone, they endure for a thousand or ten thousand years without decay. How much more so for a person who congeals the essence of their own Five Phases into a treasure—shall they not endure eternally with Heaven and Earth? How can mere metal and stone compare?

At this critical juncture, one must fix the mind unwaveringly upon this single point, maintaining focus without distraction, silently sustaining it day and night, like a hen brooding over her eggs. Only then will the work remain pure, the medicinal materials avoid dissipation, the fire’s power remain unbroken, and the Dao be accomplished.

When one abides in stillness, the Dao naturally arrives. It is like standing a pole and seeing its shadow, or calling toward a valley and hearing the echo—there has never been a resonance that did not elicit a response. Just as the sun and moon hang in the sky: when one seeks fire, fire arises; when one seeks water, water comes. The response is so swift—how can it not be supremely efficacious?

Yet in the world, all see it, yet none truly know.

The wild *yegu* and croton are the most virulent poisons among herbs—ingesting even a *cun* of *yegu* or a *liang* of croton will cause instant collapse, and even if the sages Zhou or Kong were to divine with milfoil stalks or the physicians Bian Que or shamans were to ply their needles and drums, none could revive the stricken. Yet people know only that poison taken by mouth can swiftly kill, while refusing to believe that an elixir ingested can grant longevity. Why do they not reflect more deeply on this?

Later in this text, the rustic scholar of Kuai, dwelling in a secluded valley, embraces simplicity and shuns worldly glory. Content in obscurity, he disregards fame and profit, holding fast to tranquility, seeking peace in quietude. Thus, he composed this work, singing of the *Great Change* and the legacy of the Three Sages, examining their intent to unify all under heaven in shared principles, aiming to align with the Dao, radiate vitality, and let the spirit’s transformation flow freely, bringing harmony to the four seas. He set forth this model as a sequence to be followed for ten thousand generations, a guide for governance without excess, drawing inward to nurture nature, embracing the natural Way of the Yellow Emperor and Laozi, wherein profound virtue lies in returning to the root and reverting to the origin—close to the heart, never leaving the self. Embracing the One without abandonment ensures enduring existence. Paired with ingestion of the dual essences, he arranged the four substances (the following eight characters absent in modern editions), guarding the five phases in cyclical rotation, discarding the eight minerals of Wudu, and carefully employing the refined elements prized by the world. Three stems, their branches intertwined, emerge from one source under different names—this is no mere ornate verse but a text of true substance, worthy of contemplation. Had I feigned falsehoods, I would have incurred blame; thus, I composed the *Cantong qi*, guarding its essence. Its words are few, yet its Dao vast, and later ages should heed it, casting off harm and entrusting themselves to the mountains, wandering the vast expanse, befriending immortals, transforming their forms to transcendence, and vanishing without a trace. For a hundred generations hence, they may roam the mortal realm, spreading their wings to the four directions. Yet in times of calamity—whether scorching drought or flooding rains, when wells are cut off and leaves wither, losing their splendor—each bears their own burdens. Only the steadfast path of longevity remains secure.

*Collected Explications of the Ancient Text Cantong qi*, Middle Scroll [Part One]
*Imperially Commissioned Complete Library of the Four Treasuries*

*The Collected Explications of the Ancient Text Cantong qi*, Middle Scroll [Part Two]
*Compiled by Jiang Yibiao of the Ming Dynasty*
*Annotations on the Cantong qi by Xu Jingxiu of the Eastern Han*

**First Section**

Qian and Kun are the gates and doors of the *Yi*, the father and mother of all hexagrams. Kan and Li frame the void, turning the hub and axle. The four trigrams Qian, Kun, Kan, and Li serve as bellows and keys, covering and enveloping the Dao of yin and yang. Like a charioteer holding the reins and bit, wielding the plumb line and compass, they follow the wheel tracks, abiding in the center to govern the exterior. Their calculations are recorded in the pitch pipes and calendars, with months and nodes divided into five and six. The warp and weft serve the sun, combining to form sixty. Hard and soft have their outer and inner aspects. At dawn, *Zhun* is established; by dusk, *Meng* is received. Day and night each govern one hexagram, applied in sequence. Before *Ji Ji* arrives and as twilight ends, the cycle begins anew. The sun and stars set the measure; movement and stillness have their early and late phases. Spring and summer rely on the inner body, from *Zi* to *Chen* and *Si*. Autumn and winter engage the outer function, from *Wu* to *Xu* and *Hai*. Rewards and punishments correspond to spring and autumn; darkness and light follow cold and heat. The words of the lines embody benevolence and righteousness, manifesting joy and anger in due time. Thus, they align with the four seasons and five phases, attaining their proper order.

**Master Zhenyi (Peng Xiao) says:**
Before *Taiyi*, *Taixu*, and *Taichu*, though the wondrous was contained in emptiness, no signs had yet appeared. At *Taishi* and *Taisu*, during the *Taiji*, there was primordial chaos—hun-tun. Within it lay the essence of *Zhenyi*, the beginning of heaven and earth, the mother of the myriad things. Once the single *qi* took form, the Two Modes divided, and then there were Qian and Kun. There were yin and yang, the Three Powers and Five Phases, and the myriad things with their countless names. Thus, Qian and Kun are the guiding principles of heaven and earth, the bellows and keys of creation. With Qian and Kun established, yin and yang move within them.

**Master Xu** (originally written as *Wei*, hereafter the same) states: The refinement of golden liquid and returning elixir shares the same path as creation. Borrowing the images of the *Yi* to discuss it, all begin by gathering the primordial *qi* of heaven and earth’s chaos and *Zhenyi* as the root foundation. Then, they take the essence of Qian and Kun, the subtle movements of their operation, as the model. Following the numbers of Kan, Li, Pi, and Tai, they establish the mechanisms of punishment and virtue, stealing the transformations of yin and yang to complete winter and summer. Yang arises after *Zi*, and movement begins at the start of yang’s nine; stillness follows yin’s six—this is the great principle of elixir refinement. Thus, Qian and Kun serve as the crucible and vessel; Kan and Li frame the void. Water and fire become husband and wife; yin and yang, dragon and tiger. The Five Phases weave to contain true essence; the Three Powers warp to gather purity. Cold comes and heat goes, circulating through the 384 lines. The hare rises and the crow sinks, ascending and descending over 384 days—all beginning with the substance of Qian and Kun’s two hexagrams and culminating in transformation. Hence, it is said: Qian and Kun are the gates and doors of the *Yi*, the father and mother of all hexagrams.

In refining golden liquid and returning elixir, the crucible contains the Golden Mother and the Flowery Pond, also called the Golden Womb and Spirit Chamber. Qian, Kun, Kan, and Li’s four hexagrams serve as the crucible, medicine, bellows, and keys—their pivots and bolts. Covering and enveloping refer to wrapping and encasing. Thus, there are the yin crucible and yang furnace, hard fire and soft tally, all following the sixty-four hexagrams in endless cycles, mutually employed. Within this, they operate the twenty-four solar terms of spring, summer, autumn, and winter, dividing the seventy-two pentads. A year’s twelve months of *qi* and climate are compressed into one month; a month’s *qi* and climate are gathered into a day and night’s twelve hours. The clepsydra’s marks are set, dividing the two watches, separating *Zi* and *Wu*. Yin and yang communicate through darkness and new moon, uniting dragon and tiger. Aligning with heaven and earth’s great numbers, they harmonize with yin and yang’s transformative mechanisms. If control is not errant and movement not lost, then externally, yin and yang’s tally will match; internally, the dragon and tiger’s bodies will form. Thus, it is said: The skilled artisan relies on the plumb line to avoid error; the capable charioteer holds the reins and bit without wavering, conforming to the compass and wheel tracks.

This metaphorically describes the alchemist’s regulation of fire times. The "five and six" of the monthly nodes refer to thirty days; each day and night governs one hexagram, totaling sixty hexagrams. Qian, Kun, Kan, and Li are the father and mother of the medicine, the pivots and bolts of the crucible. Beyond the day and night’s numbers, the *Cantong qi* speaks comprehensively, combining them into sixty-four hexagrams. The warp serves the sun, and the weft refers to the pitch pipes and calendrical numbers. Hard and soft have their outer and inner aspects: yang hard, yin soft; water and fire, metal and wood mutually serve as outer and inner. In all encounters of day and night, yin and yang’s ascent and descent, fire numbers follow the hexagrams’ lines. Day and night each govern one hexagram, beginning with *Zhun* and *Meng* as the first two. From dawn’s *Zhun* to dusk’s *Meng*, this is the sequence. *Ji Ji* and *Wei Ji* refer to the hexagrams *Ji Ji* and *Wei Ji*. *Hui Shuang* refers to the alternation of darkness and new moon, yin and yang’s brightness and obscurity. "The sun and stars set the measure; movement and stillness have their early and late phases" means yang pertains to movement, yin to stillness. Within the twelve hours, early and late divide yin and yang’s ascent and descent, fire numbers circulating endlessly, mutually employed.

Yang fire advances from *Zi* to *Si*, pure yang in operation—inner yin seeking outer yang. Yin tally retreats from *Wu* to *Hai*, pure yin in operation—outer yang attaching to inner yin. This is the principle of inner and outer bodies, of flourishing and decline, beginning with *Fu* and ending with *Kun*, all measured by the lines’ images. Spring’s *qi* generates, called reward; autumn’s *qi* withers, called punishment. From *Zi*, *Chou*, and *Yin* is spring; *Mao*, *Chen*, and *Si* are summer—yang fire times. *Wu*, *Wei*, and *Shen* are autumn; *You*, *Xu*, and *Hai* are winter—yin tally times. Within the twelve hours, fire tally operates, corresponding to these four seasons and five phases, darkness and light, cold and heat, benevolence and righteousness, joy and anger. The lines’ images must not err by even a hair’s breadth. Thus, it is said: "Not losing" means the crucible’s four seasons are unharmed; the images’ cold and heat are unified—then the elixir will surely form.

An ancient song says:
*The sage seizes creation’s intent,*
*Hand-rolling sun and moon, setting crucible and furnace.*
*Subtly ascending, inverting heaven and earth’s essence,*
*Gathering yin and yang, the spirit immortal’s sun soul and moon spirit.*
*Whoever recognizes this is a true immortal child.*
*Chain it, feed it—after a thousand days,*
*The body, free of yin, cannot die.*

Thus, refining golden liquid and returning elixir, if not modeled on heaven and earth’s creation and natural principles, has no other path.

**Master Baoyi (Chen Xianwei) says:**
The *Jinbi Jing* states: The Spirit Chamber is the elixir’s pivot, the father and mother of all minerals. Master Xu’s saying—"Qian and Kun are the gates and doors of the *Yi*, the father and mother of all hexagrams"—shares the same meaning.

Without the Spirit Chamber, there is no means to refine the elixir; without Qian and Kun, there is no way to perceive the *Yi*. Qian and Kun are the hexagrams of pure essence. The Six Children break this pure essence to form hexagrams. As Master Mayi says: "Qian and Kun intermingle to give birth to the Six Children." The Six Children are the broken essence of Qian and Kun. Thus, the Six Children arise from Qian and Kun, and the sixty-four hexagrams likewise emerge from Qian and Kun. Hence, they are called the father and mother of all hexagrams. The essence of father and mother is originally pure yang and pure yin. With the birth of the Six Children, the pure yang and pure yin are broken—how can they remain pure? Chen Xiyi says: "Refine the broken essence to restore the pure essence." From this, we know that refining the broken essence can return it to pure essence and enter the Dao, just as refining the myriad minerals can return them to the Spirit Chamber to form the elixir. Yet the myriad minerals are no other than the hexagrams within our body, and the Spirit Chamber is no external thing—it is the Qian and Kun within our body.
