To refine the great elixir, first establish Qian and Kun as the Spirit Chamber. Once the Spirit Chamber is set, transformation resides within it.

When Qian and Kun are established, yin and yang naturally unite. Qian descends to unite with Kun, forming Kan; Kun ascends to unite with Qian, forming Li. With Kan and Li complete, transformation again resides within them. It is also said: The sun is heaven, yet it does not descend from the west to unite with earth; the moon is earth, yet it does not ascend from the east to unite with heaven—this is the image of male and female uniting in essence. The sun and moon’s movements give rise to cold and heat, the four seasons, the division of day and night, and the settling of yin and yang. Heaven and earth do not create cold and heat; cold and heat arise from the sun and moon’s distance. Heaven and earth do not create the four seasons; the four seasons arise from the sun and moon’s north-south movement. Heaven and earth do not create day and night; day and night arise from the sun and moon’s rising and setting. Heaven and earth do not create darkness and light; darkness and light arise from the sun and moon’s conjunction and separation. Though yin and yang are subtle, they do not transcend the sun and moon; though creation is vast, it does not transcend Kan and Li.

Thus, though the changes of the hexagrams are not uniform, they do not depart from the central lines of Kan and Li, which circulate through the six positions—like the spokes of a wheel, though varied, all revolve around a single hub. The central lines of Kan and Li refer to the one yang within Kan and the one yin within Li, ascending and descending within the six lines. Laozi says: "Thirty spokes share one hub; it is the emptiness within that gives the wheel its use." Those who attain the Dao move the sun and moon within their bodies, while ordinary people, ignorant of this principle, violate the yin and yang of creation and thus cannot avoid the peril of collapsing moats or the fault of broken spokes—what then remains of the wheel’s frame and true axle?

Qian is pure yang, the male hexagram; Kun is pure yin, the female hexagram. Kan contains yang within yin, and Li contains yin within yang—these are the hexagrams of male and female union. Hence, they are called the four hexagrams of male and female. The remaining sixty hexagrams—some with partial yin above, partial yang below; some with partial yang above, partial yin below—lack pure yin and yang and do not unite male and female, so they cannot be called male and female. Only these four hexagrams encompass the Dao of yin and yang, serving as the bellows and piston. Qian and Kun are the body of the bellows and piston; Kan and Li are their function. Knowing the body and function of these four hexagrams is like a craftsman following the inked cord and adhering to the compass and square, or a charioteer holding the reins and bit to follow the wheel tracks—abiding by principle within and mastering subtlety without, so that all actions are without error and every movement accords with nature.

Within the calendar’s lunar month of thirty days, the remaining sixty hexagrams beyond the four male and female hexagrams are assigned to the days, with two hexagrams per day—one as the warp, one as the weft. From morning *Zhun* and evening *Meng*, morning *Xu* and evening *Song*, to *Jiji* and *Weiji*, the hexagrams’ inner and outer, hard and soft essences shift: in the morning, the upper position; in the evening, the lower; hardness on the outside, softness within. Together with the four preceding hexagrams, the sixty-four hexagrams of the *Zhouyi* are all employed for our use. Thus, the Dao of the great elixir can be examined through the *Yi*, and this is why the *Cantong Qi* was composed.

Master Mayi says: "The waxing and waning of day and night do not cease in the words; beyond the words, the intent becomes clear." He also says: "Hexagrams have reversal and correspondence, which are most crucial. When the reversed form is profound, the corresponding form is especially subtle." *Zhun* is a hexagram; its reversal is *Meng*. From *Xu* and *Song* onward, all are arranged in reversed order. For hexagrams like *Yi* and *Xiaoguo*, which cannot be reversed, their corresponding forms are used instead. Thus, *Yi* is followed by *Daguo*, which is its corresponding form. From the new moon’s morning use of *Zhun* and *Meng* to the last day’s morning use of *Jiji* and *Weiji*, the cycle ends and begins anew. From this, we see that morning is yang and evening is yin; day is movement and night is stillness. Intimacy and distance alternate, host and guest shift, waxing and waning succeed one another, avoidance and approach, life and death, advancing fire and retreating tally—beware of the slightest error in a moment, the tiniest deviation in a hair’s breadth. Extraction and addition must distinguish floating and sinking; operation must discern dawn and dusk. Scholars should seek intent through images and, having grasped the intent, forget the images. As the *Wuzhen Pian* poem says: "Within this, grasp the intent and do not seek the images; if you exhaust the hexagrams, you will only weary the mind." This is the truth.

Hexagrams have inner and outer bodies: the inner trigram of three lines models the spring and summer of a year and the period after midnight to noon of a day; the outer trigram of three lines models the autumn and winter of a year and the period from noon to before midnight of a day. The inner trigram models yang, the outer trigram models yin—this is the image of Qian and Kun in harmonious union. Spring and summer nurture yang; autumn and winter nurture yin. After midnight, advance the fire; after noon, retreat the tally—this principle is consistent. Reward is yang, punishment is yin; benevolence is yang, righteousness is yin; joy is yang, anger is yin. In the morning, act with yang to correspond to spring and summer; in the evening, act with yin to correspond to autumn and winter. If one can respond appropriately to the four seasons, one naturally attains the principle of the five phases—not through the trivial acts of bestowing rewards or punishments, hastily practicing benevolence or righteousness, or feigning joy or anger. Master Xu merely used this to illustrate the life and death of yin and yang.

Moreover, the elixir is the essence of utmost yang. If even a trace of yin remains, and refinement is incomplete, it will never be accomplished. Practitioners of the true Dao—can they not be cautious in movement and stillness, speech and silence? Furthermore, acts such as cherishing life and benefiting others, kindness and forgiveness, compassion and love, loyalty and trust, harmony and joy, purity and tranquility, truthfulness and sincerity—these are all yang. Acts such as harming life, cruelty and jealousy, greed and stinginess, oppression and arrogance, harshness and obstinacy, lust and desire, deceit and falsity—these are all yin. By abstaining from yin and cultivating yang, one will naturally eliminate yin and perfect yang, thereby attaining the true Dao.

Master Shangyang Chen Zhixu says: Fu Xi observed the River Chart and first drew the eight trigrams; the Yellow Emperor, Yao, and Shun wore robes and ruled the world, taking Qian and Kun as their model. The *Guicang* of the Xia dynasty took Kun as its head; the *Lianshan* of the Shang dynasty took Gen as its head. The *Zhouyi* of King Wen took Qian and Kun as its head. Confucius’ commentary says: "Are not Qian and Kun the gates and doors of the *Yi*? Qian is the yang thing."

Kun is the yin thing. It is further said: When Qian is still, it is concentrated; when it moves, it is straight—thus it greatly engenders. When Kun is still, it is contracted; when it moves, it opens—thus it broadly engenders. Master Wei deeply grasped the precautions of the Three Sages and composed the *Cantong Qi* with annotations, the *Upper Wings* of which states: "The Way of the Three Sages ferries all people of the world." Its opening line directly points out: "Qian and Kun are the gates and doors of the *Yi*, the father and mother of all hexagrams." Qian, as a thing, is yang—hence it is the door of the *Yi*. Kun, as a thing, is yin—hence it is the gate of the *Yi*. Without the gates and doors of Qian and Kun, how could the Supreme Polarity take form? How could Heaven and Earth establish their positions? How could the sun and moon shine? How could the myriad beings transform and generate? How could the sage practice the Dao?

Thus, when Qian moves and straightens, yang reaches the Supreme Polarity and generates yin. When Kun moves and opens, yin reaches the Supreme Polarity and generates yang. Yin and yang intermingle to form Li and Kan. Confucius said: "The *Yi* has the Supreme Polarity, which generates the Two Modes. The Two Modes generate the Four Images, and the Four Images generate the eight trigrams." The function of Qian is nine: first riding Kun to form *Fu* (Return); within *Fu*, *Zhen* (Thunder) is conceived; from *Fu* comes *Shi* (Army); within *Shi*, *Kan* (Water) is conceived; from *Shi* comes *Qian* (Modesty); within *Qian*, *Gen* (Mountain) is conceived; from *Qian* comes *Yu* (Enthusiasm); *Zhen* is born from *Yu*; from *Yu* comes *Bi* (Union); *Kan* is born from *Bi*; from *Bi* comes *Bo* (Splitting); *Gen* is born from *Bo*. Thus, the three sons—*Zhen*, *Kan*, and *Gen*—are all conceived and born from Kun.

The function of Kun is six: first riding Qian to form *Gou* (Encounter); within *Gou*, *Xun* (Wind) is conceived; from *Gou* comes *Tongren* (Fellowship); within *Tongren*, *Li* (Fire) is conceived; from *Tongren* comes *Lü* (Treading); within *Lü*, *Dui* (Lake) is conceived; from *Lü* comes *Xiaoxu* (Small Accumulation); *Xun* is born from *Xiaoxu*; from *Xiaoxu* comes *Dayou* (Great Possession); *Li* is born from *Dayou*; from *Dayou* comes *Guai* (Parting); *Dui* is born from *Guai*. Thus, the three daughters—*Xun*, *Li*, and *Dui*—are all conceived and born from Qian.

When male and female intermingle and mutually assist one another, Qian twice joins Kun to form *Lin* (Approach); from *Lin* comes *Tai* (Peace); from *Tai* comes *Dazhuang* (Great Strength); from *Dazhuang* comes *Guai*—this is yang seeking yin. Kun twice responds to Qian to form *Dun* (Retreat); from *Dun* comes *Pi* (Stagnation); from *Pi* comes *Guan* (Contemplation); from *Guan* comes *Bo*—this is yin responding to yang. This is how Qian generates the three daughters and Kun generates the three sons. Proceeding from this, the three sons and three daughters alternately become husband and wife, and the sixty-four hexagrams emerge in sequence. This is what is meant by Qian and Kun being the father and mother of all hexagrams.

What is meant by Kan and Li as the framework? When yang rides yin, Qian’s center becomes empty and forms Li. When yin rides yang, Kun’s belly becomes full and forms Kan. Thus, Kan and Li succeed the body of Qian and Kun as the framework of yin and yang. Compared to Qian and Kun, Kan and Li are like the spokes to the hub and axle. Qian and Kun are the true spokes of Kan and Li; Kan and Li converge at the hub of Qian and Kun. Laozi said: "Thirty spokes share one hub"—this is the same principle as the great and small *Xi Jun*.

The terms "female" and "male": the female is the mother of livestock, the male the father of livestock. Thus, the female is the general term for yin things, the male the general name for yang things. Together, Qian, Kun, Kan, and Li are called the four hexagrams of male and female. They serve as the bellows and key, just as yin and yang serve as the ebb and flow. The bellows symbolizes the gate of yin; the key, the door of yang. Qian, Kun, Kan, and Li are like a bellows and key within Heaven and Earth.

To employ the Way of the bellows and key to generate beings is called "beings." To employ the Way of the bellows and key to generate humans is called "humans." To employ the Way of the bellows and key to transcend the ordinary and enter sagehood is called "sage." The sage is one who skillfully seizes the transformations of creation, who skillfully employs Kan and Li. One who skillfully seizes the Way of creation is like a master craftsman who uses the plumb line and inked cord to correct the compass and square—what task cannot be accomplished? One who skillfully employs the Way of Kan and Li is like one who holds the reins and bit to follow the tracks—where can one not succeed? The sage is one for whom the cosmos is in hand and the myriad transformations are born from the body.

The sage is one who skillfully abides in the center to govern the external, who illuminates the pitch-pipes and calendrical reckoning to comprehend the numerical patterns. The pitch-pipes correspond to the seasonal indications of creation, while the calendar records the revolutions of the celestial sphere. When the first yang arises, the pitch-pipes respond to the Yellow Bell; from *zi* to *hai*, the celestial degrees complete their circuit, and thus when a year has fully passed, yang is reborn at *zi*. The lunar nodes consist of five or six: the lunar nodes divide two nodes into one month; five or six means five days form one *hou* (seasonal marker), and six *hou* make one month—thus one month has thirty days. One day has twelve hours, and two hexagrams with twelve lines correspond to one day, forming its warp and weft; one month has sixty hexagrams, serving as its guiding framework.

At the new moon’s dawn, *Zhun* governs affairs; by dusk, *Meng* is to be received—what does this mean? The hexagram *Zhun* (䷂) consists of *Zhen* below and *Kan* above: *Zhen* is the eldest son, capable of restoring the yang within *Kan* to bestow the power of generation and nurture—hence it is said that *Zhun* governs affairs. The hexagram *Meng* (䷃) consists of *Gen* above and *Kan* below: *Gen* is the youngest son, capable of gathering the yang within *Kan* to perform the function of warming and nourishing—hence it is said that *Meng* is to be received. Each day and night is governed by one hexagram, and within the sixty hexagrams, yin and yang mutually bestow generation and nourishment.

The dark and new moons mark the beginning and end of a month; morning and evening regulate a day’s activity and stillness. The four seasons determine a year’s rewards and punishments: those who achieve excellence are rewarded, while those who fail in their tasks are punished—without losing the proper balance of benevolence, righteousness, joy, and anger. The essential principle is to grasp the patterns of the four seasons and five phases. The overarching meaning of this chapter summarizes the unifying thread of the *Cantong Qi*: it designates Qian and Kun as the gate and door of the *Yi*, not merely referring to the elixir, medicinal substances, or firing times.

Master Quanzhenzi, Yu Yan, said: "Closing the door is Kun; opening the door is Qian. The ceaseless alternation of closing and opening constitutes transformation and penetration—this is why Qian and Kun are the gate and door of the *Yi*." Qian gives birth to the three sons; Kun gives birth to the three daughters. The union of male and female generates the myriad beings—this is why they are the parents of all hexagrams. *Kan* is the moon; *Li* is the sun. The sun and moon travel along the ecliptic, circulating endlessly day and night like the rim of a wheel. The hub is like the body; the axle is like the heart. To set the hub in motion, one must first align the axle—this metaphor means that to circulate the sun and moon within the body, one must first rectify the heart. Qian is the pure yang, the male hexagram; Kun is the pure yin, the female hexagram.

*Kan* contains yang within yin; *Li* contains yin within yang—these are the hexagrams of intermingling female and male. In the elixir method, Qian and Kun are positioned above and below, while Kan and Li are arrayed east and west. The closing and opening of Qian and Kun, the coming and going of Kan and Li, resemble the bellows and its pipe—both hollow vessels. Such is the Dao of the elixir. If one grasps it, it endures; if one neglects it, it perishes. Thus, one must guard the inner gate and be vigilant without, just as the *Liezi: Chongxu Zhide Jing* describes the driving of Tai Dou: "The reins are held with harmony, their tension and ease regulated by the lips; the chest and diaphragm maintain steadiness, the grip and grasp align inwardly with the heart and outwardly with the horse’s intent. Thus, advance and retreat follow the plumb line, and turns conform to the compass."

The numbers in the *lü* and calendar records correspond to the thirteen positions traversed, matching the twelve pitches of the *lü*, the twelve months of the year, and the twelve-year cycle. The five or six divisions of the lunar nodes arise from dividing a thirty-day month into six segments. The warp establishes Qian and Kun’s fixed positions north and south, while the weft reveals Kan and Li’s subtle functions east and west. Those who serve the sun’s commands move in accord with heaven’s revolutions, rising and setting with the sun without the slightest deviation.

Combining these yields sixty: excluding Qian, Kun, Kan, and Li, precisely sixty hexagrams remain. Each day comprises twelve hours, with two hexagrams governing affairs—each line corresponding to one hour. Two hexagrams total twelve lines, matching the numbers of the *lü*, calendar, and cycle. A thirty-day month thus yields sixty hexagrams. Arranged across thirty days, the first day at dawn is *Zhun*, at dusk *Meng*; from there, dawn is *Xu*, dusk *Song*, dawn *Shi*, dusk *Bi*, progressing in sequence until the month’s end, when *Jiji* and *Weiji* are used. From the last day to the first, the cycle repeats endlessly. Within this sequence, the hexagrams’ inner and outer, hard and soft structures may oppose or align, illustrating the greater phases’ rise and fall—yet one need not rigidly adhere to this.

Chen Ni’s *Ninefold Verdant Void* states: "Heaven’s twelve forms are clearly divided; all people divide them into stages of elixir refinement." For the sun is fire, and the celestial stems are markers. If one can reverse the celestial stems and seek them within the body, then the sun’s position becomes the hour. The active rise with the sun’s emergence, the quiescent rest with its setting. Within the body, the sun’s rising and setting naturally align with the fire phases’ movement and stillness, all subtly harmonizing with heaven’s measures.

The hexagrams comprise six lines: the lower three form the inner, the upper three the outer. Observe the hexagrams *Zhun* and *Meng*: at dawn, *Zhun* is employed, its yang fire ascending. *Zhun*’s initial nine corresponds to the *zi* hour within the body, moving from inner to outer—thus it is said that spring and summer occupy the inner, proceeding from *zi* to *chen* and *si*. At dusk, *Meng* is employed, its yin tally descending. *Meng*’s top nine corresponds to the *wu* hour within the body, moving from outer to inner—thus it is said that autumn and winter occupy the outer, proceeding from *wu* to *xu* and *hai*. Yang qi arises in spring, yin qi withers in autumn, illustrating the reward and punishment of elixir refinement. The long day emerges from the east at *yin*, the short day enters the west at *shen*, illustrating the dawn and dusk of elixir refinement. The line texts speak of benevolence and righteousness, joy and anger—these lie hidden or revealed, advance or retreat, wax or wane, each according to its time. Is there truly such a thing as the four seasons, the celestial stems and branches, dawn and dusk, brightness and dimness, or benevolence and righteousness, joy and anger, reward and punishment? All are metaphors for the waxing and waning of yin and yang within the body, the measures of excess and deficiency. To truly rely on tidal phases and seasonal markers in practice would be mistaken.

Heaven and earth establish their positions, and the *Yi* moves within them. Heaven and earth are the images of *Qian* and *Kun*. "Establishing positions" refers to arranging the positions of yin and yang in mutual pairing. The *Yi* refers to *Kan* and *Li*. *Kan* and *Li* are the two functions of *Qian* and *Kun*. These two functions, without fixed line positions, circulate through the six voids, coming and going without fixed abode, neither above nor below, nor any constant reality.

Peng Xiao’s *One Child* states: "Heaven and earth establish their positions" refers to the *jiji* cauldron as a vessel modeled on *Qian* and *Kun*. "The *Yi* moves within them" refers to the yin and yang of *Kan* and *Li*, the tally-fire, circulating within it. The cauldron, modeled on *Qian* and *Kun*, is then filled with the Golden Mother to complete the three powers of heaven, earth, and humanity. "*Kan* and *Li*’s two functions have no fixed line positions" means that externally applied water and fire, in their ceaseless movement and stillness, circulate through the six voids, coming and going, above and below, without fixed position.

Bao Yi Zi, Chen Xianwei, says: This is Xu Jun’s self-explanation, taking *Qian* and *Kun* as the divine chamber, arranging the positions of yin and yang in mutual pairing, causing *Kan* and *Li* to interact within them to achieve the power of transformation. The *Yi* refers to *Kan* and *Li*—the sun and moon as the *Yi*. The sun and moon are the *Yi* of heaven and earth; *Kan* and *Li* are the *Yi* within the human body. *Qian* and *Kun* are its substance; *Kan* and *Li* are its function. These two functions, without fixed line positions, circulate through the six voids, coming and going without fixed abode, neither above nor below, nor any constant reality.

Shangyang Zi, Chen Zhixu, says: The *Ten Wings*’ *Xici* states, "Heaven is noble, earth is base—*Qian* and *Kun* are thus determined." Xu Jun’s commentary says: "Heaven and earth establish their positions, and the *Yi* moves within them." His further annotation states: "Heaven and earth are the images of *Qian* and *Kun*. 'Establishing positions' refers to arranging the positions of yin and yang in mutual pairing. The *Yi* refers to *Kan* and *Li*—*Kan* and *Li* are the two functions of *Qian* and *Kun*." His words are already detailed, his intent too earnest—how could he mean that the world’s people, lacking faith and slow to awaken, would fail to grasp it? The *Wings* say: "Dividing yin and dividing yang, alternating soft and hard, thus the *Yi*’s six positions form a pattern." The two functions: *Qian* employs nine, *Kun* employs six. There is function but no fixed position. "Employing nine: seeing a flock of dragons without a leader is auspicious" speaks of the noble one practicing the Way until virtue reaches its utmost. "Employing six: profit lies in eternal steadfastness" speaks of *Kun*’s virtue—the Way of earth, the Way of the wife, the Way of the minister.

The Way of earth achieves no completion yet succeeds in bringing things to their end. Thus, *Kun*’s function, being solitary yin, accomplishes nothing—this is why "employing six" follows *Qian* and proceeds in tandem with it. Yet *Qian* "employing nine" pairs with *Kun* in the southwest to gain companions. *Qian* and *Kun* transform and each rectifies its nature and destiny: *Qian*’s Great Beginning, "employing nine," rides the yang of *Kun*, containing its yin in emptiness to form *Li*; *Kun*’s Great Unity, "employing six," bears *Qian*’s yin, containing its yang in fullness to form *Kan*. Thus *Kan* and *Li* attain the exclusive embodiment of yin and yang, their transformations and functions enveloping and nurturing without cease. Like the sun and moon in the heavens—now waxing, now waning; now darkening, now renewing—so too in the human body: yin and yang now at the Supreme Ultimate, now in constant flux. The Way of the *Yi* repeatedly shifts, its movements never abiding. If *Qian* and *Kun* were destroyed, the *Yi* would have no means to manifest. The sage therefore purifies the heart, retreats to concealment, and reverses the course of the *Yi*’s Way.

Master Quanyang, Yu Yan, said: "Heaven’s position is above, earth’s below, and the two qi circulate between them, ascending and descending without end—like a bellows. Humans receive the harmonious qi at birth and are no different in substance from heaven and earth. If one can embody emptiness and stillness, qi will naturally circulate throughout the body: when it issues forth, it resembles earth qi rising; when it withdraws, it resembles heaven qi descending. Thus one may attain the same longevity as heaven and earth—this is the *Yi*. How could it belong to heaven and earth alone? *Qian* is heaven, *Kun* is earth—these are the cauldron and vessel of our body. *Li* is the sun, *Kan* is the moon—these are the medicinal substances of our body. The precelestial eight trigrams place *Qian* in the south and *Kun* in the north, arranging the positions of heaven and earth in pairing; *Li* in the east and *Kan* in the west divide the gates of the sun and moon’s ingress and egress. Seeking within our body, the principle is the same. *Qian* and *Kun* are the body, *Kan* and *Li* are the function. These two circulate, ascending and descending through the six voids, coming and going above and below—originally without fixed positions. When *Kan* and *Li* operate within the cauldron of our body, heaven and earth lie concealed within—how could there be fixed positions? The *Yi* is imagery, and no imagery is more luminous than the sun and moon. The sun combines the essences of the five phases; the moon receives the measures of the six pitch-pipes. Five and six make thirty degrees, and when the degrees are complete, the cycle renews. Exhausting the numinous to know transformation: when yang departs, yin arrives, converging like spokes in a wheel, revolving in alternation, issuing and withdrawing, coiling and uncoiling. The *Yi* has three hundred eighty-four lines; the elixir has three hundred eighty-four measures. Twenty-four *zhu* make one *liang*; three hundred eighty-four *zhu* make sixteen *liang*—this is the tally of two eights. The lines embody the principle of yin and yang’s ascent and descent. Extracting the trigrams to form tally-signs and observing the signs to regulate the fire—these tally-signs are the lines of the day, not separate from them. In the language of the *Yi*, they are called trigrams; in the language of the elixir, they are called tally-signs. Thus it is said: the tally-signs refer to the sixty-four hexagrams.

Master Zhenyi, Peng Xiao, said: "The *Yi* is imagery, for it is formed by the union of the sun and moon. The golden liquid returned elixir is likewise formed by the union of the sun and moon’s yin and yang essences. 'The sun combines the essences of the five phases' means that each month it once conjoins with the moon. 'The moon receives the measures of the six pitch-pipes' refers to the metal and water within the cauldron, which each month receive half a month’s pitch-pipe qi. [The twelve pitch-pipes are divided between the sun and moon.] The sun, moon, and five stars weave together to generate the myriad things—this is likened to the cauldron receiving the external yin and yang qi, ascending and descending at the midnight and noon tally-signs, arranging the essences of the five stars, and conjoining the purest essences of the sun and moon. Negation and affirmation follow one another, cycling without end—beginning anew after completion, this is *Kun*. Thus the yin and yang essences issue and withdraw, coil and uncoil, circulating day and night in an endless cycle. Following the sixty-four hexagrams and their three hundred eighty-four lines, extracting the tally-signs according to the lines, the fire advances and retreats as yin arrives and yang departs, yang conceals and yin extends. The eastern and western qi intermingle, the sentiments of husband and wife unite. At this moment, 'the *Zhen* hexagram arrives to receive the tally-sign': heaven and earth commingle their essences, the sun and moon join their souls and spirits. The *hun-dun* is the divine chamber, resembling an egg with its two *hun* conjoined—like the *hun-dun*. The yang dragon and yin tiger within the *hun-dun* bear and respond to each other’s qi, establishing the root and foundation, nurturing the budding sprout to ultimately congeal the spirit and form the body, becoming a refined entity. Thus within the cauldron-chamber is another heaven and earth. All crawling and wriggling creatures arise from this. The male yang belongs to heaven and is *xuan*; the female yin belongs to earth and is *huang*. These are the old yang and old yin—*Qian* the father and *Kun* the mother—mutually employing the fire tally to its utmost measure. Thus we know that within a single cauldron, the workings of creation are each a clear image of heaven and earth’s movements, giving rise to the myriad things. If the firing times are missed, the additions and withdrawals are excessive, the cold and heat do not correspond, or the advances and retreats are disordered, then by what seasonal nodes would heaven and earth generate things? By what yin and yang would they produce dragons and tigers?

Master Baoyi, Chen Xianwei, said: "The *Jinbi Jing* states: 'No alchemical technique is more luminous than the gold and fire.' The subtleties of gold and fire exhaust transformation: when yang moves, yin dissolves. Master Xu used the *Xici* of the *Yi* to verify this. The great Way of the *Yi* hangs its imagery in the sun and moon; the great Way of the elixir manifests its luminosity in gold and fire. Gold and fire are *Kan* and *Li*. Thus when the essence of metal flourishes, the Jade Hare’s radiance increases; when the power of fire prevails, the Golden Crow’s brilliance doubles. This passage uses five and six to clarify the functions of water and earth. The number of earth is five; the number of the sun is five, and the number of the five phases is also five. The number of water is six; the number of the moon is six, and the number of the six pitch-pipes is also six. From *jia* to *gui* are the ten celestial stems, called the ten days—five stems are firm, five are yielding—this is the number five of the days. The twelve pitch-pipes consist of six *lü* and six *lü*—this is the number six of the moon. Five times six makes thirty degrees, and when the degrees are complete, the cycle renews—this is the ceaseless alternation of dark and new moons. The two substances, gold and fire, mutually arise and vanish between the dark and new moons. Once the scholar has exhausted the numinous and known the transformations, causing yang to depart and yin to arrive, converging like spokes in a wheel, revolving in alternation, mutually issuing and withdrawing, coiling and uncoiling—taking the lines of the *Yi* as tally-signs and observing the sun and moon’s dawn and dusk to regulate the fire—naturally one seizes the mechanism of heaven and earth and steals the marvel of creation. The *Yi* has three hundred eighty-four lines; the medicine has three hundred eighty-four measures. Twenty-four *zhu* make one *liang*; three hundred eighty-four *zhu* make sixteen *liang*—this is the tally of two eights. The lines embody the principle of yin and yang’s ascent and descent. Extracting the trigrams to form tally-signs and observing the signs to regulate the fire—the tally-signs are the lines of the day, not separate from them. In the language of the *Yi*, they are called trigrams; in the language of the elixir, they are called tally-signs. Thus it is said: the tally-signs refer to the sixty-four hexagrams."

When the hexagram aligns with *Zhen* (Thunder), where yang is born, the fire advances with the tally-sign of one yang. At this moment, the divine chamber refines its essence-metal, and fire and qi move in concert—yang the dragon is propelled, yin the tiger follows. The one who scatters the mystery is the dragon soaring into the celestial void to descend as rain; the one who transforms the yellow wrap is the tiger entering the earth to produce metal. This is the image of heaven above and earth below in primordial union. Here, the foundation is laid, the root established; through nurturing cultivation, the boundary is fostered. Once its spirit congeals, its form naturally takes shape—from the vastness of heaven and earth to the tiniest creeping things, all that possess form and qi arise from this. If this single barrier is produced externally, it descends from root to branch, becoming the Dao of ceaseless generation; if produced internally, it returns to the root and restores the source, becoming the Dao of long life and transcendence.

Master Shangyang Zi, Chen Zhixu, said: Confucius said, "Nothing is more vast in adaptability than the four seasons; nothing is more luminous in manifesting clarity than the sun and moon." Master Xu Jun further elucidated this, drawing it out to affirm its truth. This book models its patterns on the sun and moon to illustrate yin and yang. The sun and moon adhere to heaven, exhibiting waxing and waning, conjunction and opposition; yin and yang exist in the world, displaying sequence and reversal, generation and completion. The sun is pure yang’s essence, called the Great Yang; the moon is pure yin’s essence, called the Great Yin. The celestial sphere measures three hundred sixty-five degrees and a quarter. Each day and night, heaven completes one revolution, constituting a single day. The Great Sun travels one degree per day; after thirty degrees, a month is formed. The Great Moon travels thirteen degrees and a fraction per day; completing one revolution around heaven constitutes a month. The sun’s movement of one degree is called a day. The sun combines with the five phases’ essence in: *zi*, *chou*, and *yin* (the months); the sun aligns with the five planets in the north at *mao*, *chen*, and *si*; in the west at *wu*, *wei*, and *shen*; in the south at *you*, *xu*, and *hai*. In the time of Yao, the celestial heart was established at *zi* and *jiachen*, with the winter solstice at the position of *Xu* (Void) and the Rat. In the Han *Taichu* era, the winter solstice shifted to *Qianniu* (Oxherd); in the Tang *Taiyan* era, to the eastern *Dou* (Dipper); from the Song to the present, to the southern *Ji* (Winnowing Basket). This is called the precession of the equinoxes. Thus, the Great Sun gains the essence of fire and earth, its radiance enhanced, and the brilliance of metal and water further intensified. The moon is governed by the six pitch-pipes: the *lü* (yang) and *lü* (yin) harmonize in a cycle of one, five, one, six, uniting at thirty-three and thirty degrees, completing two revolutions of the sun and moon. Hence it is said: when the degrees are exhausted, renewal begins. The sun and moon circulate endlessly, giving rise to fullness, waning, darkness, and conjunction. The world follows these phases, experiencing the alternation of cold and heat; people follow this alternation, undergoing birth, aging, illness, and death.

What is meant by "exhausting the spirit to know transformation"? Nothing is more spirit-like than heaven and earth; nothing is more wondrous in transformation than yin and yang. The Dao of heaven revolves leftward, completing one revolution each day, traversing over five hundred fifty thousand *li*. Earth rests within its qi, unmoving like a floating plank upon water. The spirit of the Great Sun is the primordial qi of heaven and earth. Its body is as resplendent as metal; all things rely on its yang fire, the blazing red qi, for growth, maturation, and fruition. Its diameter spans eight hundred forty-five *li* and a fraction. Its movement does not follow the ecliptic or celestial equator but shifts within the yellow path, inside and outside. By day, it resides north of the celestial equator, shortening the day; by night, south of the yellow path. Why speak of north and south, inside and outside? Because the north contains the Purple Tenuity Enclosure, where the Emperor’s seat resides—hence the north is called "inside," and the south "outside." Its spirit is unfathomable: this is the spirit of the Great Sun.

The spirit of the Great Moon is the supreme essence of heaven and earth. Its body is entirely black; all things rely on its yin water’s transformative power for gestation, birth, and nourishment. Its diameter spans six hundred seventy *li* and a fraction. Its movement does not follow the ecliptic or celestial equator. The yellow and celestial equators, like two intersecting rings, are separated by twenty-four degrees. The moon travels through their midst, deviating from the yellow path by approximately six degrees. Though its body is black, it shines when illuminated by the sun. The *Dushu* uses the image of a black lacquer ball hung beneath the eaves to instruct: when sunlight strikes the ball, its light projects onto a dark wall. The moon’s round body, like the black lacquer ball, shows light where the sun strikes and darkness where it does not. Thus, it is always half-illuminated and half-dark. At full moon, the moon opposes the sun: at night, the moon is above, the sun below, and the illuminated side fully faces the world, while the dark side faces heaven, unseen by the world. At conjunction, the moon shares the sun’s path, lying beneath it; the illuminated side faces heaven, the dark side earth. When the moon separates from the sun by twenty-five degrees, people begin to see its faint glow; as the distance increases, the light grows. When the moon is ninety degrees from the sun, half its light is visible—this is called the first quarter. After full moon, the light gradually diminishes.

Thus, the moon’s body is not inherently round or crescent; it depends on the amount of sunlight it receives. Foolish people may claim that the earth obstructs the sun and moon at opposition, unaware of heaven’s vastness and the principle of yin and yang’s qi, which, though seemingly obstructed, secretly communicate. The apparent blemishes in the moon are the shadows of the great earth. The sun’s body is large, the moon’s small; the sun is distant from heaven, the moon even more so. Yet the moon’s transformations cannot be fully revealed—this is the spirit of the Great Yin.

This is what is meant by "exhausting the spirit to know transformation." *Zi* is the beginning of one yang; by *si*, yang reaches its peak, and yin is born. *Wu* is the beginning of one yin; by *hai*, yin reaches its peak, and yang is born. Cold and heat alternate, warmth and coolness balance; yin reaches its extreme, and yang is born; yang departs, and yin arrives. Yin attaches to yang like spokes to a wheel—spokes converge, and the wheel turns. Yin coils, and yang unfolds. The *Yi* has three hundred eighty-four lines; extracting tally-signs from the lines means that one hexagram has six lines, and one line has three tally-signs. Each day has two hexagrams, totaling thirty-six tally-signs. Yin and yang interact, yet this does not last a single hour, nor exhaust the function of a single line. Within an hour, there are three tally-signs, but only one is used. The activation of one tally-sign occurs when one yang is born beneath *Kun* (Earth), forming *Zhen* (Thunder). *Zhen* is one yang that subdues two yins. Thus it is said: when *Zhen* arrives, the tally-sign is received.

At this juncture, heaven and earth unite their essences, and the myriad things, suspended in emptiness, receive life. The sun and moon hold fast, the crow and hare intertwine without separation. Yang, male and firm, stands erect, scattering the mystery; yin, female and receptive, transforms the yellow wrap to contain and nourish. In the profound darkness of primordial chaos, the two unite in congress. *Quan yu* (the beginning) refers to the initial union, the planting of the root. *Quan yu* means "beginning"; the ancients created the steelyard starting with the *quan* (weight), and the cart beginning with the *yu* (chassis). This analogy speaks of the inception of creation.

Moreover, *quan* signifies "temporary," while *yu* signifies "stable"—this speaks of the work at hand being both provisional in its operation and most steadfast in its execution, never faltering or collapsing. Nurturing *qi* with meticulous care to foster the *yinqie* (the protective enclosure), congealing and spreading the essence of yang to form the bodily frame—this passage merely describes the initial division of the Great Ultimate, the beginning of yin and yang’s harmonious flow, and the birth of humanity and all things. Thus it is said: "The multitude tread forth, and all that writhes and stirs arises therefrom." Yet people remain unaware that the Dao of post-celestial sequential flow also draws upon the metaphor of "morning *tun* and evening *meng*," and that the root and foundation of *yinqie* are likened to the alchemical crucible of returning elixir—this is not so. Quanyangzi Yu Yan states:

The *Yi* (Book of Changes) embodies the union of sun and moon, for among all things between heaven and earth, none are as manifest and luminous as these two luminaries. Thus they are pointed to as exemplars, enabling one to perceive clearly the subtle workings of heaven and earth’s yin and yang, and to silently grasp the marvels of divine transformation. The sun possesses five *gan* (celestial stems): five *gan* of yang, five *gan* of yin. The moon possesses twelve *guan* (lunar phases): six *guan* of yin, six *guan* of yang. In the Dao of the Elixir, the sun is fire, the moon is the medicine. The sun contains the essence of the Five Agents—one entity embracing five hues. The moon receives the governance of the six pitch-pipes, as white metal is refined through the six trigrams. The Great Yang traverses one degree each day, reaching thirty degrees before uniting with the Great Yin, cycling endlessly. Sun and moon traverse the ecliptic, emerging and entering in alternation, ascending and descending, coiling and uncoiling, their diurnal revolution like a turning wheel. If one can reflect inwardly upon the sun and moon within the body, they are no different from those in heaven and earth.

The entire *Yi* comprises three hundred eighty-four lines; a single piece of the Great Medicine weighs three hundred eighty-four *zhu*. Herein lies the correspondence between the Dao of the Elixir and the Dao of the *Yi*. The *yao* (lines) are the strokes of the trigrams; the *fu* (tally-signs) are the integrated bodies of the trigrams. In elixir methods, *Qian* and *Kun* serve as the crucible and vessel, *Kan* and *Li* as the medicinal substances, while the remaining sixty trigrams govern the fire phases. Now, observing *Qian* and *Kun*: from *Tun* and *Meng* to *Ji Ji*, every instance of reversed and corresponding bodies aligns perfectly. Thus it is evident that "one yin and one yang are called the Dao."

Between the dark of the moon and the new moon, *Qian* intermingles with *Kun* to form *Zhen*—within the body, the solitary yang arises. At this moment, spirit and *qi* unite, *qi* and spirit merge, akin to heaven and earth commingling their essences, sun and moon forming a single disk. Heaven and earth, sun and moon, unite in timely congress to mold the myriad things and accomplish the year’s work. *Qian* and *Kun*, *Kan* and *Li*, unite in timely congress to seize the workings of creation and form the sacred embryo. This borrows the time of sun and moon’s union as a metaphor—it is not to say that only the dark and new moon are suitable for practice.

The male yang scatters the mystery, as heavenly *qi* descends to earth. The female yin transforms the yellow wrap, as earth receives heavenly *qi* to generate beings. The *Wuzhen Pian* states: "When sweet dew descends, heaven and earth unite; when the yellow sprout emerges, *Kan* and *Li* conjoin, augmenting the elixir method." The birth of medicine and the generation of beings by heaven and earth are no different—they are but the interplay of yin and yang, the congress of mystery and yellow. Yin and yang, a single *qi*, ascend and descend in mutual embrace, merging into oneness. Thus it is called "primordial chaos," which is the encompassing boundary of heaven and earth, the womb of the myriad things. In elixir methods, this serves as the *quanyu* (beginning) to establish the root and foundation. Then heaven and earth, the myriad things, are all within one’s own body, and there is no need to seek them externally.

The crux lies in the *jingying yinqie*—*yinqie* being the root and stem. In elixir practice, when one engages in *jingying* (meticulous nurturing) and turns the light inward, spirit yearns for *qi* and congeals, *qi* yearns for spirit and abides. Naturally, they intertwine and form the embryo. This Dao is the source of generation and transformation. Not only do humans and beasts emerge therefrom, but all that flies, writhes, walks, or breathes does so likewise. Yet when applied to humans, it generates humanity; when preserved within oneself, it generates the medicine. The distinction between sage and commoner, reversal and sequence, lies herein—yet this is truly the origin from which the human Dao arises.

Thus Confucius praised the *Hongmeng* (Vast Obscurity), declaring the virtue of *Qian* and *Kun* to be profoundly empty. He examined antiquity, tracing back to the primordial sovereign, where the *Guanju* established the beginning: the intertwining of *qi* in the first year, the sprouting of the bud. Zhenyizi Peng Xiao states:

Confucius praised the *Yi*, dividing *Qian* and *Kun* as the foremost of the myriad things, establishing *Xian* and *Heng* as the foundation of husband and wife. He cleaved the *Hongmeng* and chiseled the primordial chaos, revealing the forms of gods and spirits, and communicating the sentiments of heaven and earth. Thus the principles of ruler and minister, father and son, husband and wife, male and female, the mutual generation and restriction of the Five Agents, and the mechanisms of the myriad things’ transformations are all encompassed. *Qian* and *Kun* illuminate their profound emptiness. Hence Master Xu used the *Yi* as an analogy, creating the crucible and vessel, moving the celestial mechanism, uniting the forms of dragon and tiger, and harmonizing the bodies of husband and wife. Initially, there was no form or substance, as in the midst of *Hongmeng* and primordial chaos. Once the fire was kindled and the tally-signs moved, the *qi* of male and female intertwined. Thus, from the moment the fire is first applied, it corresponds to the first year’s sprouting. Day by day, month by month, the bodies of dragon and tiger take shape.

Baoyizi Chen Xianwei states:

Before form and *qi* are constituted, it is called *Hongmeng*; once constituted but not yet separated, it is called *hunlun*. The *Yi* states: "The *Yi* possesses the Great Ultimate, which generates the Two Modes." The *Yi* is like *Hongmeng*; the Great Ultimate is like *hunlun*. *Qian* and *Kun* are the transformations of the Great Ultimate. United, they are the Great Ultimate; divided, they are *Qian* and *Kun*. Thus, when *Qian* and *Kun* are spoken of together, it is called *hunlun*; when spoken of separately, it is called heaven and earth. Confucius praised the *Yi*, first presenting *Qian* and *Kun* as the gateway of the *Yi*, for the virtue of *Qian* and *Kun*’s profound emptiness contains the *Yi* of *Hongmeng*. The *Guanju* poem and the rites of capping and marriage take the intertwining of the two *qi* as their meaning. Before *Qian* and *Kun* divide, it is called *hunlun*; when yin and yang intertwine, it is called the beginning. The *Liezi* states: "The Great Beginning is the inception of *qi*; the Great Commencement is the inception of form." Thus it is said: "The *Guanju* establishes the beginning."

The Golden Elixir is the Supreme Unity, the Primordial Sovereign, who takes the *qi* of *Qian* and *Kun* before their division, the *qi* of yin and yang before their separation, and transforms it into the true and eternal, refining it into the true elixir. Hence it is said: "There is a thing formed in chaos, born before heaven and earth." The *Jinbi Jing* states: "The Primordial Sovereign first refined the mercury in the divine chamber, containing the profound emptiness and the mystery of white, giving birth to the Golden Duke. Majestically, he established the beginning." This "beginning" refers to the first year. At the outset of cultivation, in the first year, the yellow sprout grows, and the Golden Elixir is accomplished.

Scholars must observe the seasons and conditions, commence practice, gather the supreme treasure, and conjoin the elixir’s head. Once the elixir’s head is obtained, one naturally guards the celestial mechanism in silence and ascends to the sacred realm.

Shangyangzi Chen Zhixu states:

The *Wings* state: "The *Yi* possesses the Great Ultimate, which generates the Two Modes"—this is Confucius’ praise of *Hongmeng*. The virtue of *Qian* and *Kun* is the profound and wondrous emptiness of primordial chaos; the Primordial Sovereign is the initial splitting of Pan Gu. This is Confucius’ examination of antiquity. The *Guanju* concerns the proper human relations of men and women, the foremost of husband and wife, and the rites of capping and marriage. Confucius ordered the *Book of Songs*, placing husband and wife first to rectify the Dao of yin and yang without deviation. Confucius appended the *Wings* to the *Yi*, placing *Qian* and *Kun* first to clarify the principle that hardness and softness must be paired. The *Appended Judgments* state: "The *Yi* accords with heaven and earth, thus it can encompass the Dao of heaven and earth. Looking up, one observes the patterns of heaven; looking down, one examines the principles of earth. Thus one knows the causes of the obscure and the manifest, tracing back to the beginning and returning to the end, thereby knowing the portents of life and death."

What is meant by "tracing back to the beginning"? It is the Dao of yin and yang’s sequential flow, which generates beings. Hence it is said: "The rites of capping and marriage intertwine *qi*." This is called "knowing life." What is meant by "returning to the end"? It is the ability to reverse the flow of the pre-celestial Dao, transcending the common and entering the sacred. Hence it is said: "The first year sprouts the bud." This is called "knowing death."

Quán Yángzǐ Yú Yǎn states: Thus, the *Yijing* begins with *Qian* and *Kun*, the *Book of Documents* esteems "investigating antiquity," the *Book of Songs* places *Guān Jū* first, the *Book of Rites* emphasizes the capping ceremony and marriage, and the *Spring and Autumn Annals* takes the first year as its primary principle. The sages composed the classics, all with a foundational premise. How could the alchemical method disregard its beginning?

The sages did not arise in vain. Looking up, they observed the manifest celestial signs. These signs advance and retreat, contract and extend, responding to the seasons. Thus the *Yijing* unifies the heart of heaven. The *Fù* hexagram establishes the beginning, where the sprouting Yang inherits the father’s body and founds its omen upon the mother. The waxing and waning correspond to the pitch pipes, the rising and falling follow the pivot of the Dipper. On the third day, brightness emerges; *Zhèn* receives *Gēng* from the west. On the eighth day, *Duì* receives *Dīng*, and the upper crescent levels like a taut cord. By the fifteenth day, the celestial stem’s body reaches fullness; *Jiǎ* in the east, the toad and hare’s essence, the dual brightness of sun and moon’s *qi*. The toad gazes at the hexagram’s nodes, while the hare spits forth radiance. The seven-and-eight Dao is complete, then bends, lowers, and descends. On the sixteenth day, it turns to receive governance; *Xùn* and *Xīn* appear in balanced brightness. Descending to the twenty-third day, it mirrors the upper crescent, with metal and water each at half. On the thirtieth day, *Kūn* and *Yǐ* in the northeast lose their companion; the nodes are exhausted, and the bodies alternate in succession. The dragon of *Rén* and *Guǐ* pairs with *Jiǎ* and *Yǐ*; *Qián* and *Kūn* encompass the beginning and end. The numbers seven and eight, fifteen and nine, six and six likewise correspond. The four combine into thirty, and the Yang *qi* withdraws, vanishing into concealment.

Zhēn Yīzǐ Péng Xiào states: Fú Xī, the sage, looked up and observed, looked down and examined, establishing the numerical images of the *Yijing* and knowing the conditions of the ten thousand things. He bequeathed this to later generations, enabling those yet unenlightened to grasp the apertures of heaven and earth, steal the essence of yin and yang, recognize the root of creation, and distinguish the forms of correspondence. Generation and conquest, advance and retreat, contraction and extension—all lie within one’s grasp. Thus it is said: the *Yijing* unifies the heart of heaven.

Therefore, the method is devised with symbolic images, gathering the utmost essence. The crucible and furnace are prepared, the talismanic fire is moved, the clepsydra is followed, the hexagrams and lines are employed, and the hours and seasonal nodes are determined to exhaust the great numbers of heaven and earth. The *Fù* hexagram establishes the beginning, where the sprouting Yang inherits the father’s body and founds its omen upon the mother. The *jī* refers to the six yin lines below, where the first transforms into a single yang line, forming the *Fù* hexagram. Thus it is said: "establishing the beginning, sprouting." It is called "founding its omen upon the mother" because the lower line of the *Kūn* hexagram transforms into a *Qián* line, and the inner body becomes *Zhèn*, as *Zhèn* is conceived within *Kūn*. Thus *Zhèn* is *Qián*’s eldest son, and from here, it gradually transforms with the seasons until the fifteenth day, when it becomes pure *Qián*, the father. Thus it is said: "inheriting the father’s body."

This is also like the moon, which appears on the third day and by the eighth day forms the upper crescent, with the yang number reaching half—analogous to the crucible’s metal and water each being half. By the fifteenth day, it is full and round, emerging in the east. The toad and hare’s essence shine doubly, analogous to the crucible’s metal and water being full and attaining the fire’s timing. Master Xú used this hexagram to illustrate the moon’s waxing, condensing the three half-month phases into six hours of a single day, advancing the Yang fire and withdrawing the *chou* within the crucible, where it receives the fire’s talisman and undergoes these transformations and sprouting omens.

The "seven-and-eight Dao" refers to the fifteenth day, when the *Qián* body is complete. "Bending, lowering, and descending" refers to the text below, where after the sixteenth day, the Yang fire retreats and the yin talisman is employed. "Turning to receive governance" refers to the sixteenth day onward, when the Yang fire first retreats and the yin talisman begins to arise. "*Xùn* and *Xīn* appear in balanced brightness" is like the initial advance of the Yang fire, akin to the moon’s third day. "Descending to the twenty-third day" again mirrors the upper crescent, with metal and water each at half. "*Kūn* and *Yǐ* on the thirtieth day in the northeast lose their companion" means the yin talisman is exhausted by this point, and the Yang fire vanishes. Since within a month yin and yang are each half, alternating in succession, and water and fire mutually require one another, once the month ends, it returns to the beginning, again employing the *Fù* hexagram to commence. Thus it is said: "the body succeeds, and the dragon is reborn." *Rén* and *Guǐ* are yin; *Jiǎ* and *Yǐ* are yang.

The pairing of yin and yang refers to the Elixir Mother within the Qian-Kun cauldron receiving the qi of the yang dragon and yin tiger in union—thus it is said that Qian and Kun encompass the beginning and end. The numbers seven, eight, nine, and six together make thirty days. Within thirty days, seven and nine correspond to yang numbers, six and eight to yin numbers; Qian and Kun each take half, and by the thirtieth day, both yin talisman and yang fire are complete.

Bao Yizi Chen Xianwei said: "What manifests the celestial talisman are the sun and moon. The sun and moon have their advances and retreats, their extensions and contractions, which correspond to the principles of the *Yi* hexagrams’ yin-yang ascent and descent, their comings and goings, their transformations. Thus we know the *Yi* unifies the celestial mind, and the sages who composed the *Yi* rendered great merit and virtue to the world—how could this be empty? The Great Dao takes form in creation, yet creation is most difficult to fathom. If one can fathom creation and discern its mechanism, then one can probe the wonders of the Dao and steal its use—who but true immortals and supreme sages could achieve this?

The ancient perfected sages, benevolent toward the world’s heart, were beyond comprehension. Having discerned the mechanism of creation to benefit themselves, they further illuminated the wonders of creation to benefit others. What lies within oneself can indeed be known by the heart and guarded by intention, but to reveal it to others, how could one do so without borrowing images and entrusting them to writing to make it clear, enabling others to silently comprehend? Thus, looking up and down between heaven and earth, nothing reveals the wondrous workings of creation more than the sun and moon. Searching through the classics, nothing records the wondrous principles of creation more than the *Yi* hexagrams. Moreover, the sun and moon’s waxing and waning, their traces of fullness and decline, when aligned with the principles of the *Yi* hexagrams’ line movements and transformations, are all mutually corroborating and unified. This is why the *Cantong Qi* was composed—to "triangulate the principles of the Great Simplicity, unify the wonders of creation, and seal the Dao of the Great Elixir."

Now observe: when the first yang arises, its hexagram is *Fu*. *Zhen* is below, *Kun* above; *Zhen* is the eldest son, *Kun* the mother, *Qian* the father. The *Fu* hexagram is originally pure *Kun*, with one yang coming from *Qian* to transform the lower into *Zhen*—thus it is said, "by the mother the foundation is established." *Zhen* possesses the body of *Qian* in a subtle way; gradually accumulating, two yangs become three yangs, and the body of *Qian* is complete. Thus it is said, "the eldest son succeeds the father’s body." The waxing and waning correspond to the twelve pitch-pipes: each month, one line is added. According to the *Doushu*, each hour, one line advances.

On the third day of each month, the moon appears faintly bright in the west at the *geng* position, corresponding to *Zhen*’s first yang arising. In the *Zhouyi*’s "Receiving the Stem" method, the *Zhen* hexagram receives six *geng*s—thus the principles of creation and the *Yi* are so interwoven. On the eighth day, the moon appears in its first quarter in the south at the *ding* position, corresponding to the *Dui* hexagram’s two yangs arising; in the "Receiving the Stem" method, *Dui* receives six *ding*s. By the fifteenth day, the moon is full in the east at the *jia* position, and the *Qian* hexagram receives six *jia*s. At this time, the hexagram is complete with three yangs, the hare and toad both flourishing. The toad is the essence of metal qi, thus it waxes with the hexagram’s progression; the jade hare is the spirit of *mao* wood, thus it emits light when facing the great yang.

Seven and eight make fifteen. When the Way of three and five is complete, what was full recedes, what was extended contracts, what was high descends, and what was ascending lowers. By the sixteenth day, one yin arises, and yin begins to take charge. The moon appears at dawn in the west at the *xin* position, corresponding to the *Xun* hexagram receiving *xin*. By the twenty-third day, the moon appears at dawn in the south at the *bing* position, corresponding to the *Gen* hexagram receiving *bing*. By the thirtieth day, the moon sets in the east at the *yi* position, corresponding to the *Kun* hexagram receiving *yi*. When the cycle ends, it alternates again, and yang resumes its charge, the body succeeding and the dragon reborn—the dragon is *Zhen*.

Among the eight hexagrams, only *Kan* and *Li* do not participate, for their two functions ascend and descend within the six hexagrams. The functions of *Kan* and *Li* are great indeed! *Qian* receives the six *jia*s and six *ren*s; *Kun* receives the six *yi*s and six *gui*s. Among the eight hexagrams, only *Qian* and *Kun* receive two stems; the rest receive only one. Thus it is said, "*Ren* and *gui* pair with *jia* and *yi*; *Qian* and *Kun* encompass the beginning and end," revealing that within *Qian* and *Kun* lies true water.

The young yang number is seven, the young yin number eight; together they make fifteen. The old yang number is nine, the old yin number six; together they make fifteen. The four numbers combined make thirty, corresponding to a month’s days. Seven, eight, nine, and six are the four images. The principles of the *Great Yi* and the principles of creation are entirely unified.

By the thirtieth day, at the moon’s setting, the yang qi is utterly extinguished and concealed. Beyond this, one yang arises again. Revolving in cycles, it ends and begins anew.

Master Shangyang, Chen Zhixu, said: "Thus, when the sages descended into the world, they looked up and down, carefully examining yin and yang, taking yin as the talisman and yang as the mandate. What is meant by 'looking up to reveal the celestial talisman'? The yin qi in heaven and earth is called the celestial talisman; the yang qi in heaven and earth is called the celestial mandate. The yin qi within the human body is called the fire talisman; the yang qi within the human body is called the nature and life. When yin and yang extend and contract, the talisman becomes the indicator of advance and retreat. When the talisman’s indicator is precise, timing is not lost.

Thus, the Dao of the *Yi* unifies the celestial mind. The celestial mind is the correct position of *Qian*’s *zi*, the gate of yang’s arising. *Qian* is the yang thing; the celestial talisman is pure yang. *Qian*’s yang first arises beneath two yins, becoming *Zhen*. *Zhen* is the eldest son; *Fu* arises from *Kun*. *Fu* is one yang hidden beneath five yins; *Kun* is the mother—thus it is said, 'by the mother the foundation is established.' The correspondence of the pitch-pipes and the *Doushu* refers to the Yellow Bell’s pitch at *zi* and the *Doushu*’s movement establishing *zi*, both corresponding to the time when the first yang begins to sprout. On the third day’s dusk, the moon’s faint yang arises in the southwest; yang arises beneath the moon, resembling *Zhen*—thus it is said, '*Zhen* receives *geng* in the west.' On the eighth day, the first quarter appears; *Dui* receives *ding*. The yang fire ascends to half, level as a string. On the fifteenth day, at opposition, the west has the moon, the east the sun; the hare fully emits its light, the yang fills the hexagram’s body, and *Qian* is complete.

On the sixteenth day, at dawn, *Xun* appears at *xin*; the yin talisman envelops the yang, preventing its escape. By the twenty-third day, the yin talisman half-conceals the light, halting the lower crescent. On the thirtieth day, the moon’s body is entirely dark, white exhausted by black, its pure light facing heaven. In the northeast, it loses its companion—the northeast is *Gen*, the region of *jishui*; *Gen* is the ghostly path. In the human body, when *gui* is full, the meridians move, and its qi is diminished. When the cycle ends and *gui* is exhausted, one yang arises again—thus *ren* and *gui* pair with *jia* and *yi*. *Qian* begins with *Fu*; the numbers seven, eight, nine, and six end at thirty. When thirty ends, *Kun* is complete, and true qi returns to concealment."

Quán-Yángzǐ Yú Yǎn states: As for the subtleties of the *huohou* (fire-timing), there are aspects not easily expressed in words. Thus, the perfected ancients observed the Dao of Heaven and devised symbolic methods to instruct humanity. They likened Heaven and Earth to the crucible, the sun and moon to the medicinal substances, and the waxing and waning of the sun and moon to the *huohou*. The moon traverses the heavens once each month, conjoining with the sun—hence it is called the "celestial tally," responding to the seasons. There are twelve hours in a day.

From the first day of the month onward, the moon’s light gradually increases: the *hun* grows while the *po* wanes, yin recedes and yang extends—this mirrors the period from *zi* to *si* within a single day. From the sixteenth day onward, the light gradually diminishes: the *po* grows while the *hun* wanes, yin advances and yang recedes—this mirrors the period from *wu* to *hai* within a single day. The progression and retreat, the extension and contraction of the *huohou*, follow the same pattern.

The *Yi* speaks of yin transforming into yang. After the tenth month, the first yang returns in revival—this is the heart of Heaven, manifest in the center of the *zi* position in the north. The revival corresponds to the eleventh month’s hexagram, precisely aligned with the *zi* position in the north. Its qi responds to the winter solstice, accumulating to form a single yang line concealed beneath the hexagram—hence it is called *Fu* (Return).

This mechanism does not lie solely in the initial moment of the winter solstice at *zi*; it resides within our own bodies. Without profound comprehension of the workings of creation, its secrets remain unknown. The *qian* line descends to the initial line of the *kun* mother, forming *zhen* (Thunder). *Zhen* is the eldest son, thus it is said, "The eldest son inherits the father’s body." *Zhen* arises from the body of *kun*, just as an infant is born from its mother’s womb—hence it is said, "It establishes its foundation through the mother." Scholars who grasp this will understand that in refining the elixir, one need not seek the winter solstice; within the body, a single yang naturally arises, unbound by rigid adherence to the calendar.

There are years, months, days, and hours. From *zi* to *si* is *xi* (growth); from *wu* to *hai* is *xiao* (decline). The waxing and waning correspond to the twelve pitch-pipes: each month, one pipe is changed, and over a year, all twelve are cycled through—just as the *huohou* waxes and wanes. From below to above is *sheng* (ascent); from above to below is *jiang* (descent). The ascent and descent follow the pivot of the Dipper: each hour, the Dipper shifts one position, traversing all twelve celestial markers in a day—just as the *huohou* ascends and descends.

If one reverses this and seeks it within the body, the subtleties may be silently guarded. On the third day of the month, the moon begins to wax...

At the birth of light, dusk resides in the west at the *geng* position, its image like *Zhen* (☳), corresponding to the *Zhen* hexagram’s reception of *geng*. In terms of the body’s *huohou*, this is the moment when the "River Chariot" dare not pause even briefly.

By the eighth day of the month, the moon reaches *shangxian*; dusk is in the south at the *ding* position, its image like *Dui* (☱), corresponding to the *Dui* hexagram’s reception of *ding*. This illustrates the halfway ascent of the body’s yang fire.

By the fifteenth day, the moon faces the sun; dusk is in the east at the *jia* position, its image like *Qian* (☰), corresponding to the *Qian* hexagram’s reception of *jia*. This signifies the body’s moment of full yang abundance.

The toad is the essence of the moon; the rabbit’s soul is the light of the sun. Sun and moon are not doubly bright: the sun shines by day, the moon by night. When the sun rises, the moon sets; when the moon rises, the sun sets. Day and night alternate in illumination, their emergence and retreat like the rolling and unfurling of a scroll.

Man’s breath is none other than the sun and moon—how is this different?

In the alchemical method, the first half of the month is yang, belonging to *Zhen*, *Dui*, and *Qian*; the latter half is yin, belonging to *Xun*, *Gen*, and *Kun*. Thus it is said: the toad gazes at the hexagrams’ nodes. The moon is *Taiyin*, governing reception; the sun is *Taiyang*, governing emission. The moon has no light of its own but receives the sun’s light to become white—hence it is said to "emit born light." Seven and eight make fifteen: the yang fire ascends from *Zhen* until, by the fifteenth day, it is pure *Qian*, having reached fullness. The momentum of the first half of the month is at its peak, its Dao perilous—it can no longer avoid bending, turning, and descending.

By the sixteenth day of the month, the "living soul" is born; dawn is in the west at the *xin* position, its light a single crescent. Its image is like *Xun* (☴), corresponding to the *Xun* hexagram’s reception of *xin*. In terms of the body’s *huohou*, this is the moment when yin receives yang’s succession, the path winding and turning.

By the twenty-third day, the moon reaches *xiaxian*; dawn is in the south at the *bing* position, its light half a crescent. Its image is like *Gen* (☶), corresponding to the *Gen* hexagram’s reception of *bing*. This illustrates the halfway descent of the body’s yin tally.

By the thirtieth day, the moon is dark; dawn is in the east at the *yi* position, its light entirely lost. Its image is like *Kun* (☷), corresponding to the *Kun* hexagram’s reception of *yi*. This signifies the body’s moment of yin tally’s exhaustion.

A month is divided into six nodes, each node comprising five days. From the new moon’s dawn, five days belong to *Zhen*, then sequentially to *Dui*, *Qian*, *Xun*, *Gen*, and *Kun*—thus the six nodes are complete.

Yet after the conjunction of sun and moon, yang again receives yin’s succession, transforming back into *Zhen*. *Zhen* is the dragon, with one yang stirring beneath two yin.

*Qian* receives *jia* and *ren*; *Kun* receives *yi* and *gui*. Thus it is said they pair: the ten Celestial Stems begin with *jia* and *yi*, end with *ren* and *gui*—hence they encompass beginning and end. The body’s *huohou* in its drawing and adding is no different from the moon’s waxing and waning.

Seven is the number of fire; eight is the number of wood.

When combined, they yield fifteen—the nine being the number of metal. Six is the number of water; combined, they also yield fifteen. The four together total thirty, corresponding to the thirty days of a month. When the count of thirty is complete, sun and moon conjoin as jade disks, and the *Yi*’s images vanish into concealment.

This resembles the mid-winter node, when grasses and trees all wither and assist yang in its seclusion. Merchants and travelers halt their journeys; the lord deeply conceals himself. The image accords with the season’s decree: mouths close, and none speak of the Dao of Heaven—so vast and boundless, so profoundly formless, so empty and still that it cannot be perceived. Only by dissolving the outer frame can delusion and error be dispelled, lest one’s undertakings end in ruin. Thus, I set forth these Four Images to enlighten those blind to the truth.

**Zhenyi Zi (Peng Xiao) says:**
In cultivating the elixir, the fire-timing may branch and flourish like stems, or bear fruit in ripened abundance, or wither like grasses and trees—responding to mid-winter’s opening and closing, all in accord with the fire’s movement. The merchants and travelers who assist yang in its seclusion are like sealing the passes and prohibiting travel, symbolizing the firm closure of the crucible to prevent leakage of true essence and true qi. The lord who deeply conceals himself is the Golden Mother of the Central Palace. The closed mouth that does not speak is the chamber of *Dui* metal. The Dao of Heaven is vast and boundless, so the transformations within the crucible are limitless; the Great Profundity is formless, so the fluids of metal and water are unfathomable, their subtle workings infinite, their divine transformations without direction. As the four seasons shift, the outer frame dissolves, and the forms of metal and water transmute.

**Xu Jun says:**
The Great Dao nurtures the true master without form. If one does not regulate and harmonize yin and yang with method and imagery, restraining and binding them to subtle transformations, how can one contend with the celestial mechanism to draw in the essences of sun and moon, retaining them within the crucible’s narrow chamber to achieve wondrous transformation? If one errs in the thread of affairs, ruin is certain. Thus, I compose this text to awaken those yet unenlightened.

**Baoyi Zi (Chen Xianwei) says:**
At the mid-winter node, the myriad things return to their roots. At this time, the workings of creation are unfathomable. Observing the Dao of Heaven, one finds it sealed and impassable, vast and unknowable. Examining the Great Profundity, one sees only emptiness and stillness, hidden and formless—what basis is there to fathom the mechanism of creation? Xu Jun distinguishes the Four Images to instruct later generations, as detailed in the following chapter.

**Shangyang Zi (Chen Zhixu) says:**
From *"This resembles the mid-winter node..."* to *"to enlighten those blind to the truth"*—fourteen sentences remain unclear.

**Quanyang Zi (Yu Yan) says:**
In the alchemical method, the winter solstice, the last day of the lunar month, and the new moon all serve as metaphors for the moment when yin reaches its extreme and yang begins to stir. When mid-winter arrives, grasses and trees wither completely. The *Yi* says: *"Thunder lies within the earth—*Fu*. The former kings, on reaching the solstice, closed the passes; merchants and travelers did not journey; rulers did not inspect the regions."* This is to cultivate stillness to nurture movement and to accord with the Dao of Heaven.

Yet the Dao of Heaven is vast, and the true mechanism lies within a single instant; the Great Profundity is formless, and the wondrous resides within the subtle and obscure. Thus, at the moment of the winter solstice, one must first seal the *Dui* trigram, calm the heart, and guard silence, causing metal and mercury to return together to the crucible. Like sun and moon conjoining as jade disks, their outer frames hidden, they sink into the profound emptiness. Then spirit congeals, qi gathers, and the golden liquid coagulates. This is the oral instruction for elixir cultivation: gathering qi and congealing spirit are the foremost principles.

If scholars do not know to gather qi and congeal spirit, forgetting restraint and silence, they will recklessly ascend the towering edifice, dissipating into the vast emptiness, becoming like inert wood or dead ashes—thus inviting their own ruin. Later scholars, failing to grasp the essentials, blindly lead the blind, mistakenly taking the waxing and waning of the three-day moon as the fire-timing for a full month. Therefore, I distinguish these Four Images of seven, eight, nine, and six to enlighten the unenlightened, that they may understand the thirty days of a month as metaphorical, not literal—referring to the third day’s fire advancement and the sixth day’s receipt of the tally.

If scholars comprehend that seven, eight, nine, and six correspond to the thirty days of a month, then all other indirect metaphors can be silently recognized. Why cling to the text or adhere rigidly to images?

The Eight Trigrams are arrayed; the luminaries shift without losing the central origin. The subtle essence is difficult to perceive, so one infers, verifies, and establishes standards. By observing images, one models their forms; by setting markers, one defines the scope. Through divination, one determines fortune or misfortune, issues commands in accord with the seasons, and never misses the moment of a moving line. Above, one observes the patterns of the Milky Way; below, one sequences the flows of earth’s terrain; in the center, one examines the human heart. By integrating and investigating the Three Powers, when active, one observes the trigram’s nodes; when still, one follows the *Tuan*’s words. *Qian* and *Kun* are employed to act as Heaven and Earth—only then is there order.

**Zhenyi Zi (Peng Xiao) says:**
When circulating the fire for a month, one follows the Eight Trigrams’ array and the luminaries’ movement—north and south, east and west—drawing and adding, ascending and descending. Thus, yin and yang unfold and contract, harmonizing metal and water. If the fire’s movement is mistimed, leading to unseasonable rains or droughts, the principles of growth and completion are disrupted, and the forms of dragon and tiger cannot be retained. Those who circulate the fire must carefully consider this.

*Yuanjing* is the divine spirit within the crucible, the true essence, the primordial qi of Heaven and Earth. It cannot be grasped or seen, yet it subtly follows the mechanism of transformation, giving birth to the myriad things. Though profoundly subtle and difficult to perceive, one must infer, verify, and establish standards. By issuing commands and enacting decrees in accord with celestial signs, one observes the celestial patterns above and follows the terrestrial principles below, thereby uniting the souls of Heaven and Earth, harmonizing the severity and gentleness of yin and yang, and establishing the Three Powers. By aligning with the trigrams and following the line texts, arranging morning and evening, sequencing ascent and descent, *Qian* and *Kun* achieve peace, husband and wife harmonize, dragon and tiger unite, and Heaven and Earth are ordered.

Above, all is discussed in terms of following the clepsydra’s timing, circulating the tally-fire, clarifying the drawing and adding, distinguishing advance and retreat—none deviating from the movements of sun, moon, and stars. Thus, within the crucible, the myriad things are born in accord with the four seasons, and the divine essence is perfected.

**Baoyi Zi (Chen Xianwei) says:**
If the movement does not lose its center, it accords with creation without error and aligns with the trigrams and lines without deviation.

*Yuanjing* is the most numinous and divine treasure, born from emptiness and nothingness, without form or image to perceive, dwelling in the subtle and indistinct, without trace or track to pursue. To gather it, one must thoroughly understand yin and yang, deeply penetrate creation, infer its signs, verify its methods, and establish standards. When still, observe images to model their forms; when active, set markers to divine fortune or misfortune. Above, examine the celestial patterns; below, observe the tides; in the center, examine the human heart. One must follow the trigram’s nodes: when yang is active, do not miss the moment of a moving line; when yin is still, do not lose the principle of utmost softness and contained radiance. In this way, the powers of *Qian* and *Kun* are at one’s disposal, and the divine essence of spirit can be gathered.

Moreover, when the eight winds harmonize, sweet dew descends; when yin and yang are balanced, sweet springs arise—these are the signs of Heaven and Earth in order.
