Master Shangyang, Chen Zhixu, states: Thus we know the eight trigrams—*Qian* and *Kan* reside in the north, *Gen* and *Zhen* return to the east, *Xun* and *Li* face the south, *Kun* and *Dui* revert to the west—interweaving and arraying their radiance, shifting and revolving without losing the center. This center is the celestial heart, the Great Central Ultimate, the primordial essence of the original *qi*, subtle and beyond perception. Heaven generates the sage, who infers, measures, and verifies through signs and proofs. Observing the images of the sun and moon, one models their forms; like the celestial observer who erects a gnomon to measure shadows and set standards, one divines the seasons and determines fortune or misfortune. At the first command, one must align with the seasons, emulate the movement of lines, and know when yang arises. Above, examine the *Hetu* to clarify the images of *Qian* and the interplay of yin and yang; below, sequence the earth’s forms to detail the body of *Kun* and the wondrous transformations of metal and water. Within, examine the human heart, issuing commands in accord with the seasons, moving in step with the trigram’s nodes. When *Zhen* stirs first, stillness follows the *Tuan* commentary. How great is *Qian*’s primordial power! Clouds drift, rain falls, the myriad beings flow and flourish, embracing vastness and brilliance. Gentle and yielding, it moves with steadfast integrity. When the celestial patterns are examined, the earth’s forms sequenced, and the human heart rectified, the gates of *Qian* and *Kun* open, and the Dao of yin and yang is enacted, allowing Heaven, Earth, and all beings to attain the governance of spontaneity.

This passage cites the sage’s investigation of antiquity and observation of Heaven as a metaphor: the union of sun and moon, the functions of *Qian* and *Kun*, enabling people to distinguish yin and yang, recognize advance and retreat, comprehend creation, model forms, respond to signs, and regulate movement and stillness. In this way, the Heaven and Earth within the body are brought to order.

It is not a matter of extracting or adding within the cauldron of dragon and tiger. Master Quanyang, Yu Yan, says: In the method of elixir refinement, the eight directions are arrayed with the eight trigrams, and the twenty-eight constellations are arranged in a circuit. Yet the sun and moon travel along the ecliptic, never deviating from the center. Thus the monkey of the mind does not bolt outward, and the elixir naturally coalesces.

The *Daodejing* states: “Abstruse, oh! Obscure, oh! Within it is essence. Its essence is most real; within it is sincerity.” The primordial essence resides in the body, subtle and unobservable. Yet the elixir method aligns with creation: when Heaven’s sincerity arrives, the jade bell tolls once, and the twin caves of the Jade Grotto open. When the *Kan* and *Li* of the body intermingle, the two banks ascend together and merge as one—this is the sign of the primordial essence’s generation.

Moreover, in stillness and profound silence, observe the image of *Qian*: “The movement of Heaven is strong; the noble person ceaselessly strengthens the self.” This is the movement of Heaven and the modeling of its form. Though the elixir method does not rely on fixed hours, when spirit and *qi* harmonize, inner and outer align; when spirit is dull and *qi* restless, the shadow and time diverge, and one cannot but stabilize them. Thus, like the celestial observer who erects a gnomon to measure shadows and set standards, one may divine whether the firing times are balanced and determine fortune or misfortune.

When the thunder of Earth shakes and the gate of *Xun* opens, the dragon faces east, and the deep pool leaps—this is the moment when a single yang line stirs within the body. At this time, warm *qi* surges and merges, the heart feels a fleeting trance, and one must advance the fire without delay, lest the moment be lost. The *Huanyuan* chapter states: “When the myriad winds first rise and the thousand mountains’ moon is newly full, hasten to issue the command and circulate the *zhou tian*—this is the meaning.”

Thus we see that Master Xu’s words also follow the *Yi*. The *Shangqing* Collection states: “By observing the Dipper’s handle circulate the *zhou tian*, one suddenly awakens to the immortal’s wondrous secret. When true yang arises in the *Kan* position, it repairs the deficiency of the *Li* palace. Since ancient times, *Qian* and *Kun*, *Kan* and *Li*, the sun and moon have never ceased. This year, at the winter solstice, the plum blossoms still congeal in the snow. The ancient sages closed their gates on this day, cutting off all comings and goings—all for the sake of the multitude of beings. Every being contains the intent of generation; it lies in the first moment of *zi* and the last of *hai*. Creation is soundless, yet fire arises in water—its wonder lies in the *xuwei* cavity. Now that the golden crow has been seen flying into the toad’s cave, this too is the Dao of Heaven.”

The ancients who refined the elixir looked up to observe the celestial patterns, looked down to examine the earth’s principles, and examined the human heart within. Thus they emptied their minds, moved their spirits, turned the celestial gate, and revolved the earth’s axis. Above, they aligned with the luminous revolutions of the Milky Way; below, they corresponded to the ebb and flow of the sea tides. Though Heaven and Earth are vast, their sun, moon, stars, five phases, and eight trigrams are all gathered within the body—some as the crucible, some as the medicinal substances, some as the firing times. In a single inward gaze, the Three Powers are complete within the self, never seeking outside the body.

When the dragon appears and thunder sounds, a single yang stirs beneath six yin—one change yields *Fu*, two changes yield *Lin*, three changes yield *Tai*, four changes yield *Dazhuang*, five changes yield *Guai*, and six changes yield *Qian*. Thus yang *qi* pervades the six voids. The great medicine of the golden elixir must arise within utmost stillness. The *Tuan* commentary on *Kun* states: “Utmost, oh! *Kun*’s primordial power. The myriad beings rely on it for birth; it compliantly receives Heaven.” *Kun* is Earth, capable of embracing and receiving Heaven’s *qi*, thus all flourishing beings arise upon it. In the elixir method, yang *qi* gradually advances, just as the trigrams transform; the spirit returns to illumination, just as *Kun*’s virtue. This is why one must follow and emulate it.

Yet the intermingling of *Kan* and *Li* is called the lesser *zhou tian*. Only when *Qian* and *Kun* unite in *Gou* does the inner and outer both intermingle, initiating the operation of the greater *zhou tian*. Thus yin and yang intermingle, harmonious *qi* brings auspiciousness, and Heaven and Earth are ordered.

As for the utmost sages: Fu Xi first drew the eight trigrams, modeling Heaven and Earth; King Wen, the ancestor of emperors, structured the body and composed the line statements; Confucius, the most eminent of sages, supplemented them with the Ten Wings. These three lords, Heaven’s chosen, arose in succession, each governing their time. Though their steps varied in excellence and their merits differed, their achievements did not diverge. Their creations followed precedent, their measurements were precise to the fraction, their forms were easy to contemplate, and their portents were difficult to foresee. Their undertakings became models for the world, and they established these books as standards. Though lacking prior knowledge, Master Xu (referring to himself) awakened through his teacher (referring to Wei Boyang), as if lifting the curtain and gazing from a high terrace. The *Huoji*’s six hundred chapters point the way without confusion. Yet the text is solemn, and people do not ponder deeply to trace its source. The obscure and the manifest originally dwell together. I humbly speak for the worthy—how dare I treat the book lightly? Yet if I were to seal my lips and silence the Dao, I would incur the crime of punishment. To express my thoughts on bamboo and silk might risk leaking Heaven’s tally. Alone, I sigh in anticipation, looking up and down, and compile these foolish words. The methods of refinement have their standards, which cannot be fully expounded. I briefly outline their essentials; the branches and leaves will be seen in their luxuriance.

Master Zhenyi, Peng Xiao, states: Master Xu praised the three sages—Fu Xi, King Wen, and Confucius—Heaven-endowed with heroic spirit and mutually illuminating virtue. In expounding the *Yi*, they penetrated the sentiments of Heaven, Earth, and the myriad beings; in editing the *Book of Songs* and *Book of Documents*, they arranged the teachings of rulers, ministers, and the multitude. Moreover, they determined the line images, analyzed the fractions, and controlled the calculations of the celestial cycles, exhausting the great Dao. Though Heaven and Earth are vast, they cannot conceal the mechanisms of closure and opening; though yin and yang are utterly empty, they cannot hide the numbers of movement and stillness. Thus the sages followed them, taking counsel from the great Dao. They used *Qian* and *Kun* to image the crucible and chamber, causing the embrace of the One *qi* to govern the myriad spirits, and the passage of yin and yang through the time markers to traverse the six voids and generate the multitude of beings. Yet the forms that are easy to contemplate belong to Heaven and Earth.

The signs without omen are difficult to strategize—these are yin and yang. If not for the Three Sages’ unfolding of the *Yi*, by what path would water and fire be deployed? If not for the teachings bequeathed through ten thousand generations, by what gate would the dragon and tiger be harnessed? Thus it is said: "Without prior knowledge, one awakens through this." As if lifting the curtain, clarity reveals what is seen; as if ascending a high tower, one still cannot fathom its limit—this metaphor illustrates the Dao of the sages: the higher one gazes, the loftier it becomes; the deeper one delves, the more impenetrable it grows.

Following the Dao of the *Yi*, the *Fire Record* was further elucidated. The *Fire Record* of six hundred chapters embodies the great reckoning of the cyclical stars. "Morning *tun*, evening *feng*"—each corresponds to a hexagram, directly aligning with affairs. Thus, the six hundred chapters, in their sequential order, are all identical. Therefore, the year aligns with the month, the month with the day, the day with the hour.

Master Xu, though unwilling to seal his tongue and obscure the great Dao, also dared not openly inscribe bamboo and silk to leak the celestial mechanism. Hesitant and sighing, he deeply pondered the inadequacy for posterity. Thus, he briefly outlined the essential principles, slightly revealing the branches, while the remaining subtleties were preserved in oral instructions.

Master Chen Xianwei of Baoyi said: Master Xu’s innate endowment was exceptional, yet even he relied on his teacher for awakening. Thus, he sighed that the wondrous principle of the Golden Elixir, being without form or omen, is truly difficult to fathom. Though the Three Sages embedded this principle within the *Yijing*, and the eight trigrams interweave the mysterious workings of creation, the images point to the marvels of formed things, and words manifest the achievements of foreseeable signs. Comparing this with the *Cantong Qi*, it still adheres to worldly methods. Though the sages meticulously divided and crafted, enabling learners to explore and seek, how could they guard the secret within the text, transcending images? If the *Cantong Qi* were not composed, how could this be clarified? This is why Master Xu, in accordance with books that exhaust principle and fulfill nature, illuminated the path of transcending the ordinary and entering sageliness—like holding the left tally, like lifting the front curtain. It fundamentally arises from the teacher-disciple transmission, not from prior knowledge. Thus, we know that though the celestial mechanism is distant, every person has a share; though the great Dao is not remote, each generation finds its inheritor. Do not say that only those with innate immortal bones may aspire—know only that with a willing heart, nothing is impossible.

Reviewing Master Xu’s humble words before and after, one realizes the compassion of past perfected beings. Scholars of later ages, encountering alchemical classics and purple texts, descriptions of fire phases, the functions of advancing and retreating, civil and martial arts, and the distinctions of beginning and end, start and stop—few are without doubt. Thus, many scriptures state there are six hundred chapters to convey the functions of fire phases. Yet they do not know that though the words differ, the principle is one; though the applications vary, the use is the same. Once one recognizes the reversal of "morning *tun*, evening *feng*," one understands the cyclical return of "day *gou*, night *fu*"—like the turning of the celestial sphere, like following wheel tracks. Though the wording is solemn, the transmission is earnest. Thus, the six hundred chapters complete the three hundred days, never exceeding the two hexagrams of advance and retreat. It depends solely on the sincerity of beginning and end, the precision of intent, and unwavering focus.

Alas! The sagely words are not concealed, yet without deep reflection—Master Xu further expounded three chapters, all embodying the same principle. His aim was to systematically analyze the essentials and expound the methods, making it easier for later learners to comprehend. How vast!

Master Chen Zhixu of Shangyang said: These words on the Three Sages’ establishment of teachings and transmission of instructions follow the intent to honor past and future sages. Confucius, in his *Wings*, said: "In ancient times, when Fuxi ruled the world, he looked up to observe the images in heaven, looked down to observe the patterns on earth, and examined the markings of birds and beasts and their suitability to the land. He drew near and took from his own body, drew far and took from other things. Thereupon, he first drew the eight trigrams to communicate with the virtue of the numinous and to categorize the conditions of the myriad things." King Wen structured the body and unfolded the line statements, revealing the wonder of "gaining friends in the southwest." What are the Ten Wings? They assist the *Yi*.

The *Yi* as a book: Fuxi drew the trigrams, King Wen appended the line statements, and the Duke of Zhou wrote the line texts—these form the two chapters called the *Main Classic*. Confucius, after the *Main Classic*, added ten chapters as wings: *Upper Commentary on the Decision*, *Lower Commentary on the Decision*, *Great Images*, *Small Images*, *Appended Statements (Part One)*, *Appended Statements (Part Two)*, *Words of the Text*, *Explaining the Trigrams (Part One)*, *Explaining the Trigrams (Part Two)*, and *Explaining the Trigrams (Part Three)*—these ten chapters are the Ten Wings. Originally, they explained the *Yi*, yet they are not called "explanations" but "wings," for they assist the Dao of the *Yi*.

Without the Ten Wings, how would the Dao of the *Yi* be illuminated? How would words communicate change? How would movement be recognized? How would vessels be fashioned? If the Dao is not communicated through words, the resonance of feelings and nature cannot be attained. If the Dao does not know change and movement, the transformation of metal and water cannot be achieved. If the Dao is not skilled in fashioning vessels, the use of the crucible and furnace cannot be obtained. If the Dao does not comprehend fortune and misfortune, the principles of reversal and compliance cannot be grasped. The *Wings* say: "The *Yi* contains the four Dao of the sage"—this is it. Thus, when the noble person is about to act...

When about to take action, one inquires and, through words, receives the mandate—as an echo responds without regard to distance, remoteness, obscurity, or depth, thus knowing what is to come. For obscurity and depth are what we call "dim and indistinct, faint and elusive." When the noble person acts or moves, it must be within this dim and indistinct, faint and elusive realm that they know what is to come. If not for the utmost refinement in all under heaven, who could partake in this?

Master Xu said: "The Ten Wings assist it"—these words exhaust the matter. The sage’s merit and function equally benefit later generations, yet scholars may lack the great good fortune and virtue, the great wisdom, to fully inherit it. A thousand kinds of obstructions prevent them from seeing or hearing. The Three Sages arose in succession, creating and measuring, establishing a model for ten thousand generations. We must revere the supreme Dao: heaven produces sages and wise ones, yet how could there be self-realization without a teacher’s transmission? Those endowed with intelligence, planning, and deliberation may still encounter obstacles. But if a benevolent and worthy person diligently investigates to the utmost, suddenly gaining the teacher’s guidance, their mind and chest become open and clear—just as if, in a room, the curtains were drawn back, revealing everything distinctly and penetratingly, as if one had opened their eyes and ascended a high terrace: what would not be seen?

If one does not seek a teacher, how could they awaken? Should they find a teacher, they must first examine the *Book of the Kinship of the Three*. If they can clarify every sentence and thoroughly comprehend every chapter, this is true attainment. But if even one sentence remains vague or ambiguous, it cannot be trusted. To claim there is another path to accomplish the Dao apart from this book is a great deception of others. Why? Because this book is what King Wen, the Duke of Zhou, and Confucius inherited from Fuxi.

Thus Master Xu said: "Among the many sages and heroes of later ages, who could surpass Confucius?"

Alas! Moreover, the *Yellow Emperor’s Three Hundred Characters of the Yin Talisman* and Laozi’s *Five Thousand Characters of the Dao and Virtue* align without discrepancy—only by illuminating this Way. Thus, the Celestial Master of the Ancestral Lineage, Xu, and the immortals Fuqiu and others all entered the Dao through this. Likewise, works such as *Ziyang’s Awakening to Reality*, *The Supervisor of the Back Channel*, and *Questions on the Golden Elixir* all trace their origins to the *Book of the Kinship of the Three*. When compared with Buddhist sutras and Daoist scriptures, all harmonize with this text. Yet if someone claims, "My teacher is so-and-so, who in turn studied under so-and-so," and their teachings flow like a river, yet when asked about the *Book of the Kinship of the Three*, they remain largely ignorant—these are seeds of hell. They falsely assert that the supreme Way does not reside in written texts, deceiving and blindly leading others. Who would know that only by verifying this book’s authenticity can one truly begin?

Moreover, the *Six Hundred Fire Records*—with their timings, hexagram symbols, esoteric words, and wondrous phrases—from beginning to end are solemn and weighty. Yet if one does not deeply ponder and trace their source, how can they grasp the shared mystery of the hidden and the manifest? This book is meant for the wise to discuss; how dare one speak of it lightly? If one were to remain silent, where would the Way be expounded? Yet to fully reveal it in writing would leak the celestial tally. Those who hesitate and sigh in regret are like the creature called *youyu*—a beast of many doubts. Dwelling in the mountains, upon hearing a sound, it climbs up and down trees repeatedly, unable to decide. Thus, it is named *youyu* ("hesitation"). Master Xu himself said: "Hesitating at the riverbank, fearing the peril, one only increases their sighs, looking up and down again and again, delaying this text’s transmission."

Yet in refining later generations, there are methods of concealment and revelation, though their oral instructions cannot be fully disclosed. Here, only the main principles are outlined, with branches briefly glimpsed. Quanziyang Yu Yan said: "Fuxi modeled heaven and earth to create the *Changes*, first drawing the eight trigrams. In the *Primordial Heaven* diagram, *Qian* is above, *Kun* below, *Li* to the east, *Kan* to the west, with *Zhen*, *Xun*, *Gen*, and *Dui* arrayed at the four corners. King Wen followed the trigrams’ structure to compose the *Changes*’ line statements. In the *Later Heaven* diagram, the two woods *Zhen* and *Xun* generate *Li* fire, followed by *Kun* earth to store it; the two metals *Dui* and *Qian* generate *Kan* water, followed by *Gen* earth to restrain it. Though Confucius held no official position, he became the teacher of emperors for ten thousand generations—truly the foremost among the many sages. He composed the *Ten Wings* to elucidate the *Changes*. These three noble men, all ancient great sages, arose in succession to govern their times. Though their steps and strides differed in excellence, when measured by their merit and virtue, they were of one standard, without distinction.

Moreover, in the eight trigrams’ line statements and the *Ten Wings*, there are no superfluous words, no extraneous characters. Their deliberations on weight and light are exceedingly detailed and precise, and their compositions all follow a precedent. Now, the *Book of the Kinship of the Three* by Wei Boyang borrows the *Changes*’ trigram imagery to expound the Dao—truly, it follows a precedent. The elixir method has no form, no sign; it cannot be measured by calculation or deliberation. If not demonstrated in words, how could later generations take it as a model? This is why the *Book of the Kinship of the Three* was written.

Yet how could Master Xu have expressed his own views? He merely recorded the teachings passed down by his teacher. If one today can seek a teacher and visit friends with a sincere and empty heart, true immortals will follow at their doorstep, imparting oral instructions. In an instant, all doubts vanish, as if the curtains were drawn back and all obstructions removed—bright as ascending a high terrace to gaze upon the vast wilderness. How joyous!

Later learners, failing to grasp the true trap, see the *Six Hundred Fire Records* and are invariably dismayed, thinking the fire timings so intricate and difficult. They do not realize that the *Six Hundred Records* are like the sixty hexagrams representing a month’s timing, while the *Six Hundred Records* span ten months’ timing. For the sixty hexagrams are uniform, and the six hundred records are likewise similar. Thus, it is said: "Their aims are not different." From ancient times to today, elixir texts number in the thousands, with countless words—could this not be called solemn and weighty? Yet when tracing their source, it is but one yin and one yang. These two, though named differently, share the same origin. The ancient immortals poured out their hearts to inform others, yet the people of the world, their blessings and karmic conditions shallow, had no leisure to ponder deeply.

Now, the *Book of the Kinship of the Three* elucidates the origins of the cauldron and vessel, the sources of the medicinal substances, the methods of fire timing, and the esoteric words on existence and nonexistence, host and guest, priority and sequence. Every word is thorough and substantial, composed for the world’s worthy ones. How could it be lightly written to fish for empty fame? The *Supreme Mysterious Regulations* states: "To meet someone and not transmit is to lose the celestial Way; to transmit to the unworthy is to leak the celestial treasure. If one obtains the right person, their body gains merit; if one recklessly transmits, for seven generations they will suffer its consequences." Thus, Master Xu wished to remain silent, fearing that uttering a single word might sever the Dao’s lineage and incur guilt. Yet to express his feelings in writing would risk leaking the celestial tally and inviting punishment. Hence, he hesitated, sighed, looked up and down, and pondered deeply. Though he wished to speak plainly, he dared not lightly reveal the true mechanism. Instead, he described the cauldron, medicinal substances, and fire timings in broad strokes, allowing later generations to infer the meaning from the words. Perhaps this Way would not perish in transmission.

Yet unable to bear either speaking clearly or remaining silent, he could only present its grand principles and extract its essential mechanisms, refining and shaping the methods scattered across the three chapters—like branches spreading out in luxuriance.

*Collected Explications of the Ancient Text of the Kinship of the Three*, Middle and Lower Scrolls
*Section: Masters, Daoist Category*
*Collected Explications of the Ancient Text of the Kinship of the Three*, Lower Scroll [Part One]
*From the Siku Quanshu*
*Middle Scroll*
*Written by Ming Dynasty’s Zhu Yibiao*
*Annotations on the Kinship of the Three*
*Composed by Xu Jingxiu of the Eastern Han*

The burning-mirror draws fire only when facing the sun; the moon-pearl yields no water unless turned toward the moon. These two qi, though distant and subtle, still respond and transform—how much more so the intimate qi residing within the body, close at heart and breast, where yin and yang pair as sun and moon, water and fire, manifesting their effects as clear signs.

Master True Unity Peng Xiao says: The burning-mirror and moon-pearl respond to qi and still produce water and fire. Heaven and earth, sun and moon, set forth their images—how could they not generate and complete? Taking the near from the body and the far from things, one first gathers the Mother of Heaven and Earth to lay the foundation of the elixir, then unites the essence of sun and moon to serve as the ancestor of the medicine. When lead and mercury give rise to one another, water and fire restrain each other—how could there be no proof of efficacy?

Master Embracing Unity Chen Xianwei says: The burning-mirror is made by refining five-colored stone into a mirror, which, when turned toward the sun with mugwort, draws fire. The *Huainanzi* calls it the "fire-pearl." There is also the "water-pearl," made of crystal, which, when turned toward the moon, yields water—this is called the "yin-mirror." Without the sun and moon, neither the burning-mirror nor the moon-pearl can produce water or fire. Within the human body, the ascent and descent of yin and yang move in harmony with the transformations of heaven and earth. How could the principle of water and fire interflowing be external to the mechanism of the sun and moon’s mutual approach and exchange? This is sought as proof of their effects.

Master Upper Yang Chen Zhixu says: The burning-mirror is a yang object, containing qi within, so when it responds to the sun, it can generate fire. The moon-pearl is a yin object, containing essence within, so when it responds to the moon, it can generate water. The sun and moon hang high and distant in the sky, yet the burning-mirror and moon-pearl, though far apart, still allow yin and yang qi to respond and transform. How much more so the true yin and true yang within the human body, close at heart and breast, intimate and easily sought—how could they not respond and transform? The obstacle lies only in later generations failing to grasp the Way.

The yin and yang of the human body correspond to the sun and moon in the sky, while the water and fire of the various mirrors serve as metaphors for the essence and qi within the body. Even inanimate objects still respond thus—how much more so beings with consciousness and spirit, which naturally interact! Among all things between heaven and earth, humans are the most spiritually endowed. Even the basest and most foolish know the principle of yin and yang’s transformative nurturing without needing instruction to act accordingly. Yet all the deluded and confused know only to follow the natural course to generate beings. As for reversing its use, without a teacher, none can fathom it. Reversing the use of yin and yang is the Way of refining essence and subduing qi to form the elixir.

This passage simply clarifies how the two qi of yin and yang respond and transform to accomplish creation.

Master Complete Yang Yu Yan says: The *Rites of Zhou*, in the "Autumn Office," states that the Officer of Fire is in charge of using the wooden burning-mirror to draw bright fire from the sun and the moon-pearl to draw bright water from the moon. The moon-pearl, or yin-mirror, is a large clam, polished smooth and lustrous. When turned toward the moon, water is produced. The sun and moon hang in the vastness of space, countless thousands of miles from earth, yet the burning-mirror obtains fire from the sun, and the moon-pearl obtains water from the moon—how swiftly they respond and transform! How much more so the water and fire that naturally reside within the body, close at heart and breast, where the medicinal materials already exist. If one can empty the mind, concentrate the spirit, and inwardly settle without a single stray thought, gathering the light of one’s own sun and moon within the body, then over time, when the qi stabilizes and stillness arrives, extraordinary effects will naturally arise. The supreme Way is not distant—it is always before our eyes. When yin and yang attain harmony, they naturally respond and transform. Hares conceive, clams form pearls—they still steal from heaven and earth, seizing the work of creation. Yet people remain deluded and forget to return, unable to seek within.

The three treasures—ears, eyes, and mouth—must be closed and sealed, not allowed to issue forth. The Perfected One dives into the deep abyss, floating and roaming, guarding the center of the compass. Rotating and curving, sight and hearing open and close, all converging to serve as the body’s pivot. In movement and stillness, never exhausted, the qi of *li* and *kan* inwardly flourish, while the defense of *kan* does not employ hearing, and the closure of *dui* does not engage speech. Following the primordial chaos, these three are locked and sealed. The body rests in an empty chamber, the will entrusted to emptiness, with no-thought as the constant. The proof is difficult to shift: the mind is focused, never straying. In sleep, the spirit is embraced; in waking, its presence or absence is awaited. The complexion gradually moistens, the bones and joints grow stronger. Expelling the host of yin demons, one then establishes the true yang and cultivates it without ceasing. May the qi of clouds and rain flow abundantly, like spring rains, trickling like melting ice. From the head, it flows down to the feet, then ascends again, circulating endlessly, penetrating the infinite. The inner countenance is suffused with warmth, returning to the center—this is the verification of the Way. The weak is the handle of virtue. Plowing and weeding the long-accumulated filth, the fine qi becomes harmonious and smooth. The turbid is the path to clarity; prolonged darkness will give way to brightness.

Master True Unity Peng Xiao says: The *Book of Changes* states: *Kan* is the ear, *li* is the eye, *dui* is the mouth. *Kan*, *li*, and *dui* are water, fire, and metal. The key is to seal water and fire, enclosing the Golden Mother. The infant and the maiden are the Perfected Ones, floating and roaming within the womb, causing the mother’s body to relax and rest in the empty vessel, with no movement of thought, to verify its naturalness—the mutual generation of male and female.

Once the cauldron’s yin and yang ascend and descend in harmony, the dragon and tiger within the womb rise and fall, embracing and securing the true essence. The complexion gradually moistens, the bones and joints grow stronger. Cultivating without cease, over time, proof will appear. Drawing out the yin fire means expelling yin demons; inserting the yang tally means properly arousing the yang. Circulating endlessly, the spirit chamber becomes peaceful. Clouds move, rain falls, the golden liquid flows perpetually, like ice melting, suffusing from the feet to the head. The Perfected One’s body comes and goes, the Golden Mother’s form is restored. The fire qi is pent up within the cauldron, the golden essence guards the weak within the compass. Prolonged darkness gives way to brightness—this is the moment when yin reaches its extreme and yang arises, when clarity emerges from turbidity, when fire returns and water resumes. The four seasons alternate, the five phases interact. The reverse is the verification of the Way; the weak is the foundation of virtue.

Yang extends, yin withdraws: *kan* is the ear. To make it unhearing is to harmonize the yin spirit, causing the true water not to flow away. *Li* is the eye. To make it unseeing is to warmly nurture the yang soul, causing the true mercury not to escape. *Dui* is the mouth. To make it unspeaking is to seal the golden womb, preventing the opening and closing that would leak the red dragon’s essence.

Master Xu’s *True Kinship* in three chapters uses metaphors and allusions, drawing from the far and applying to the near. It speaks plainly yet conceals its meaning, neither wishing to openly reveal the celestial mechanism nor to obscure the supreme Way. Thus, it fully clarifies that the inner image and outer use are one. Master Embracing Unity Chen Xianwei says: The ear not hearing causes the water of *kan* to settle inwardly; the eye not seeing causes the fire of *li* to nurture inwardly; the mouth not speaking causes the metal of *dui* to remain silent. Once these three are closed, the Perfected One roams freely within. One must use no-thought, no-anxiety, never fretting or laboring, entrusting the will to emptiness, the mind focused and not straying. Resting the body in an empty chamber has no other meaning.

Thus, one must embrace the feminine, hold to unity, and relax the body, dwelling alone in an empty chamber. In sleep, the spirit embraces itself; in wakefulness, it secretly observes presence and absence. Only then can yin evil be expelled, pure yang accumulate within the body, and supreme harmony overflow—like ice melting into fluid, like spring’s warmth dissolving all, as qi moves like clouds and rain, like mist ascending and descending, flowing from head to foot. With unceasing practice, filth is entirely purged, sinews and bones harmonized, the complexion radiant, the skeletal frame sturdy, and blood transformed into white marrow. Both spirit and form attain perfection.

Yet once the seven gates return, one nearly forgets life; all meridians settle, form and qi dissipate, strength wanes, and one cannot endure—dazed and turbid as if drunk. This is the verification of the Dao, the handle of virtue.

Obscurity is the foundation of clarity; turbidity is the source of purity. From this point onward, perfect illumination, penetrating vision, emptiness, and spiritual communion all begin with obscurity and turbidity.

Master Upper Yang Chen Zhixu says: This passage meticulously reveals the secrets of refining the elixir in the chamber. Once a disciple receives the master’s oral instructions, they must recite this passage ten thousand times, pondering each sentence and scrutinizing every character. Do not read carelessly, for even a single word missed will fail to complete the elixir. This passage is the sharpest point of convergence in the *Cantong Qi*, where the unspeakable is treasured by heaven.

Moreover, though human nature at birth is without ungoodness, as one grows older—unless endowed with the capacity of a sage and resolutely guarding integrity—few remain untainted. External enticements by wanton friends and internal delusions by sensual pleasures and desires ensnare the six sense gates, where every sight, sound, and taste becomes an object of attachment. Day and night, each act erodes and poisons—most grievously, the ears, eyes, and mouth.

Hear with the ears, see with the eyes, and the mouth craves flavors—from this cause, fortune follows form to ruin, harm follows sound to arrive, illness enters through the mouth, and drunkenness and dreamlike death follow one another endlessly. How can there be an end? Most scholars fail to achieve the Dao because they are led astray by the three neighbors of the ears, eyes, and mouth, which mutually entice and drown their true nature. The immortal elders regard the ears, eyes, and mouth as the "Three Treasures," holding them in reverence and never daring to let them loose. Thus, they close and seal them, allowing no leakage. Only when entering the chamber does their great function manifest, and the six roots attain perfect stability—then one may gather and refine the true person, who is none other than one’s own earth.

Those who dwell in the deep abyss use their own earth to overcome water in seeking the elixir. Those who float and guard the compass’s center—this compass center is called the "cavern of creation." When refining the great elixir, in the instant of this single talisman, one must be most cautious and secretive. The floater must remain ever still yet ever responsive, never departing from this function for even a moment. The guardian must diligently turn inward, illuminating with sincerity, for there is indeed something to await. These two lines are again the sharpest point of convergence in the *Cantong Qi* annotations, akin to the work of wielding a great axe. Thus, the true immortals and sagely masters have uttered these profound words and dharma teachings, which no one in ten thousand generations can fathom. Master Shangyang, having fully revealed them, merely wishes that all may understand this Dao and practice it.

To "turn and curve in seeing and hearing" is not a dull, closed-off blockage. Within, one must turn and curve, yielding and cautious, to guard and regulate seeing and hearing, so that the opening and closing of earth (wu) are neither hidden nor exposed, merging with one’s own earth in harmony. If one swallows or guards, one’s nature may become reckless and wild—only by making earth (wu) its pivot and control can this be remedied. This is called "making it the pivot and control of oneself." What does it mean to be inexhaustible in movement and stillness? The Dao of oneself: in movement, it is direct; in stillness, it is focused. If skillfully employed, it will not lead to exhaustion.

To "detach qi and internally nourish and guard" is to withdraw the eyes’ gaze and inwardly illuminate. For "kan, do not use hearing" is to turn the ears inward and listen, so that hearing does not leak. For "dui, harmonize without speech," one refines words and regulates breath to comply with the transformations of primordial chaos. Only when these three are skillfully managed at the gate can the body relax in the empty chamber. To "relax the body" is to be at ease without strain; the "empty chamber" is stern and pure without杂. One entrusts the will to emptiness and nothingness, for nothingness is the place where qi is born. This is called the "primordial qi that comes from nothingness." To obtain this qi, one must bend and curve the will and thoughts to seek it.

"Without thought" is most useful as a constant. The true person dwelling in the deep abyss responds with "without thought." The floater guarding the compass’s center awaits with "without thought." Inhalation and exhalation nurture with "without thought." When the three natures unite, one enters with "without thought." To be able to respond, await, nurture, and enter—this work is only mastered through abundance, thus making it a constant.

That which is difficult to shift is called earth (wu), known as the White Tiger. The tiger’s nature is changeable, restless, and hard to stabilize. If one uses one’s own earth to guard it, one must never allow it to run rampant or shift. Should the heart lack focus, disasters and anomalies may arise. Earlier, it was said "without thought," and now it is said "the heart must be focused and not run rampant." Can one not reflect deeply on this?

In sleep, when spirit and qi embrace, the heart neither runs rampant nor shifts. At this moment of sleep, spirit and qi naturally unite as one. Yet one must remain ever awake and alert, awaiting the presence or absence of this single qi. Only at this stage of refining the elixir does one begin to personally know and verify the work. In the past, when I received my master’s instructions on this, I found it exceedingly difficult. Recently, practicing this great work on the northern slope of Mount Daluo, I have come to realize its ease. The great essential is to cultivate it without ceasing or resting, and only then can the full achievement be completed.

Thus, the immortal elders repeatedly clarify that during the gathering, firing, and refining of the elixir, not even a hair’s breadth of error is permissible. From the gradual moistening of one’s complexion downward, every line is sharply applied, with not a single line left idle. Only then, after obtaining the elixir, will the natural effects appear, and various signs of verification manifest. Those who cultivate this Dao dwell in the turbid, evil world of the five defilements, practicing the Dharma beyond the world. If one practices without interruption for a long time, success is certain. Do not, on account of minor demonic obstacles, abandon the path midway. This is what is meant by "the path of turbidity clears, and after long darkness, brightness returns."

Master Quanyang, Yu Yan, said: To regard the ears, eyes, and mouth as the "Three Treasures" and say "close and seal, do not leak" is to wish that one restrains the ears’ and eyes’ clarity and seals the tongue’s true qi, gathering it all within and not letting it escape outward, like treasuring and hiding a precious jewel. To regard the original spirit as the "true person" and say "dwell in the deep abyss, guard the compass’s center" is to wish that it follows the true breath’s coming and going, allowing the true qi’s ascent and descent, so that from dawn to dusk, the original spirit always abides in the muddy pellet.

This is the simple and easy Dao. Gently stirring the gate at the crown of the head, evenly and silently lifting, the three palaces naturally ascend and descend, and the hundred bones and ten thousand apertures naturally flow and circulate. The *Huangji Jingshi* says: The heavens’ spirits dwell in the sun; humans’ spirits emerge from the eyes. At birth, one dwells in the realm before heaven and earth; at death, one returns to the realm before heaven and earth. The five phases gather here; the five viscera store the numinous here. Saliva, nasal mucus, essence, fluids, qi, blood, and liquid—these seven form the essence here. The great heaven and earth can contain it, yet the smallest speck of dust cannot enter—this is the great treasure within our bodies.

The elixir method of turning the light inward to illuminate the cinnabar field causes breath to unite with it, and the three palaces naturally ascend and descend, circulating endlessly without cease, like a wheel with its movement governed by the heart. The wheel’s pivot lies in the eyes, and its control lies in the linchpin. This is what is meant by "containing the light is the medicine of long life." "Detaching qi and internally nourishing and guarding" is to withdraw the eyes’ gaze so that the light does not leak. "For kan, do not use hearing" is to turn the ears inward to listen so that hearing does not leak.

For *dui* harmony, do not rely on speech; rare words follow the vast primordial chaos. Let the depths of the heart grow cold as ashes, silent and wordless—this guards the sacred embryo. With these three principles thus established, the next step is to relax the body in an empty chamber, moving slowly and at ease without straining the form. Enter a quiet room, free from worldly entanglements, and commit the will to guarding emptiness. Let the heart be free of stray thoughts, the mind not wander outward, and thus dwell all day in the play of true emptiness.

To make this a constant practice is to ensure thoughts follow one another without interruption, ever maintaining the qi’s bearing in accord with the Dao of heaven. Generally, in cultivation, each measure of effort yields a corresponding sign of verification. If one practices diligently, day and night without rest, after a hundred days of efficacious work, the two kidneys will simmer like boiling water, the bladder’s fire will blaze, the eyes will gleam with spirit-light, the ears will hear subtle resonances, the nose will detect rare fragrances, the mouth will fill with sweet fluids, and the entire body will melt into a flowing essence. These verifications advance day by day. What is most precious is a concentrated heart, never scattered or wayward—lest the principles remain obscure and, within the stillness of samādhi, one falls into a state between dream and waking, or is seized by demonic illusions.

For when spirit and qi first congeal, they are easily lost. During sleep, one must hold fast to the spirit, never sinking into confusion or drowning in dreams. Upon awakening, one should be like a hen brooding over her eggs, maintaining a continuous warmth lest the fire grow cold and the elixir’s formation be delayed. Thus, one must attend to its presence or absence throughout the twelve hours, whether moving, staying, sitting, or lying down, ever holding this in mind. Only then will the effort become pure, the medicinal ingredients not dissipate, and the firing times not falter—this alone may be called true cultivation. As Xia Yunfeng said: *"When the natural timing arrives, the dream elixir manifests."*

The Confucian method of "ever-wakeful awareness" applies here as well. As the complexion grows lustrous and the bones and joints grow strong, the deeper the treasure’s accumulation, the more flourishing the essence’s radiance—like jade concealed in a mountain, its brilliance shines forth, or a pearl hidden in a marsh, its beauty enchants. In the human body, all that has form is nothing but the turbid dregs of yin and impurity. The immortals’ cultivation consists in generating being from non-being, seizing the one true yang of heaven and earth to form the elixir’s beginning. Then, day and night, they circulate the fire to refine away the yin qi. After ten months, the embryo is complete; after three years, the work is fulfilled. Only then does the body transform into pure yang, the form transmuted into that of an immortal.

The *Tongxuan Secret Formula* states: *"Day and night, without rest, work the great elixir. When the essence pierces the crown, the hundred spirits submit in reverence. Able to dwell alone in a quiet chamber, continuing the work night after day without cease, one day it will suddenly penetrate through. Naturally, within the cinnabar field and sea of qi, the essence of great yin crosses the *weilü* cavity, guards the *jiaji* gate, blocks the *fengfu* wind-house, ascends to the *niwan* mud-pill, then descends through the *mingtang* bright hall, enters the nose’s bridge, and flows into the *huachi* flower pond, where it transforms into sweet fluids. Swallowed, it irrigates the five viscera and six bowels, reaching the cinnabar field. Above and below, it circulates in endless cycles, filling the four great limbs and returning to the beginning. The body is pervaded without exception, melting like mountain clouds rising into the great void, misting like fine rain moistening the wilds, abundant as spring waters filling the four marshes, fluid as river ice about to melt. It flows up and down, the hundred vessels surging and harmonizing, filling the valleys within and coursing through the four limbs. One feels the heart brimming with spring, the state resembling that of slight intoxication."*

The *Mirror of Entering the Medicine* says: *"The primordial qi and the postnatal qi—those who obtain them are ever as if drunk."* If one practices only in the morning and abandons it by evening, how can one ever attain this with a scattered will? *"Reversion"* means turning back. The *Daodejing* says: *"Attain utmost emptiness, guard deep stillness. The ten thousand things arise together; I thereby observe their return."* For the effects of elixir refinement emerge from utmost emptiness and deep stillness, uniting with heaven and earth. Only then does the primordial qi return with the one yang. If emptiness is not utmost and stillness not deep, it is like building a mountain nine *ren* high but losing the final basket—how can one ever see verification?

*"Weakness"* means softness and yielding. The *Daodejing* says: *"Concentrate the qi to attain softness, able to be like an infant."* For the root of elixir cultivation lies in holding fast to the will without violence to the qi, like an infant’s softness and weakness. If the will is not guarded and the qi not nurtured, what is gained will be but a fraction, and what has been accumulated will crumble like a mountain cliff—what then can serve as the root and handle?

The elixir method of *"weeding and hoeing the old impurities"* means to quiet all concerns, dispel inauspicious qi, expel the ghostly corpse, and pacify the six senses. Within the five aggregates, in the midst of right mindfulness, one attains clarity, brightness, emptiness, and radiance. Then the five viscera grow cool, the six bowels harmonize, the three hundred and sixty joints and eighty-four thousand pores are free of obstruction, all flowing smoothly, and the Dao-qi abides constantly.

It is like cutting away the roots of noxious weeds to prevent their regrowth, allowing the good to flourish. The *Cui Xu Pian* says: *"When spirit and qi unite at the proper time, bones and flesh melt and harmonize—one does not even know it."* At this moment, the three fields are full of qi, and one feels as if in a dream. Could this be called turbid confusion? The student who reaches this state must not slacken, for turbidity will eventually clear and obscurity will eventually brighten. When the time arrives and qi transforms, the spirit-light will naturally appear, each part growing bright and clear, all manifesting within the realm of the indistinct. How could this end in turbidity?

Gradually, the *"yellow center"* opens, the principle permeates, and moisture reaches the skin. The beginning is upright, so the end is cultivated; the trunk stands firm, and the branches may be grasped. One who conceals this to deceive the world remains unknown. Peng Xiao of *Zhenyi Zi* said: The *Book of Changes* states: *"The noble one is centered in the yellow, the principle permeates, the position is correct, the body is upright, and beauty resides within, flowing to the four limbs and manifesting in affairs—this is the height of beauty."* It speaks of the golden tiger in the crucible, initially receiving the external yin and yang essences, gradually moistening the skin. Once it receives the first qi, the sprout begins to grow. The trunk is made upright, and in the end, the fruit is formed. This is a metaphor for the true gold-sand’s first sprouting shoots. *"One"* is water, for the water’s root is true gold, securely sealed within the vessel—ordinary people cannot know it.

Chen Xianfeng of *Baoyi Zi* said: *"Yellow is the color of the central palace; cinnabar is the treasure of the central palace."* The *Book of Changes*, in the *Wenyan* commentary on the sixth line of the *Kun* hexagram, says: *"The noble one is centered in the yellow, the principle permeates, the position is correct, the body is upright, and beauty resides within, flowing to the four limbs and manifesting in affairs—this is the height of beauty."* How much more so when the body contains the great elixir and harbors the supreme treasure—then the spirit and energy are extraordinary, and the skin’s moisture is self-evident.

Generally, to become an immortal, one must first become a noble person. If one’s virtue and conduct fall short of the noble person’s standard, the human Dao is still incomplete—how much more so the Dao of immortality? Thus, Master Xu drew upon the *Book of Changes*’ words on *"dwelling in the center and treading the upright"* to elucidate the principle of *"having the center and manifesting it externally."* Those of superior capacity who hear this will accumulate deeds and amass merit to ascend as immortals; those of middling capacity who hear it will at least not fail to become good persons and noble ones.

The sutra states: "When the One is realized, the myriad affairs are complete." Yet how easily can this wondrous principle of the One be known? If one truly knows this One, then one may attain this One—such is the true One, the One of oneness, not the numerical One. The true One of oneness is born of the Dao, whereas the numerical One is the "Heavenly One generates Water." Knowing that the One arises from the Dao, the Way of the celestial elixir leaves no residue unencompassed. Thus it is said: "The One serves to conceal." Yet the world’s people cannot comprehend it.

Master Shangyang, Chen Zhixu, said: "Only thus can one attain the *yellow center penetrating principle* and the moist, lustrous skin." At the outset, this is the work of refining the self; in the end, it is the matter of approaching the furnace. The *qian* hexagram stands complete in the path of refining the self, while the later stages hold the merit of entering the chamber. The One is the self, the *wu* (戊). Guard this single word—it is the point where one penetrates to the heart-ground of Boyang.

Confucius said: "My Way is threaded through with a single principle." Laozi spoke of "attaining the One, and the myriad affairs are complete." The Buddha declared: "The myriad dharmas return to the One." Thus, "Heavenly One generates Water"—one must know that this Water is born from within the One. The One within is the central line of the *kan* trigram. When the One is concealed, essence gathers and spirit coalesces; when the One is obscured, numinous power disperses and qi scatters.

If one can know the One, the cosmos lies within one’s grasp. If one attains the One, the myriad transformations arise within the body.

The marvel of the One cannot be illuminated without a teacher—thus it is said that the One is concealed and hidden, unknown to the world. If one truly encounters the transmission of an authentic teacher and can practice this Way, attaining sagehood and realizing the truth may be hoped for in days. Its efficacy is most wondrous and swift, hence it is called the Divine Elixir.

Quanyangzi Yu Yan said: When the Elixir Field contains substance and the one qi flows unimpeded, harmony accumulates within and radiance naturally manifests without. This is what is meant by "when the exterior is upright, the shadow is straight; when the source is deep, the flow is long." Attain the One, and the myriad affairs are complete—this is the teaching of the beginning, middle, and end, the trunk and branches.

The *Zhixuan Pian* states: If one can ensure that with every breath all is mutually attended to, transforming the entire bodily frame, the Jade Fluid flows. If exhalation lacks the governance of the spirit-master, even a single breath is incomplete; if inhalation lacks the governance of the spirit-master, each breath remains interdependent. When spirit and qi guard one another, thought after thought continues unbroken, forging them into a single whole—only then are form and spirit both wondrous, merging with the Dao in truth.

The *Lingyuan Dadao Ge* says: Spirit is nature, qi is life. When spirit does not stray outward, qi naturally settles. Ordinary people exhale qi within, yet their spirit is constantly entangled and enslaved without. From youth to old age, not a single breath rests within the body, causing spirit and qi to move separately without mutual guardianship. Ultimately, the dwelling becomes empty, the walls crumble, and the master cannot sustain himself—how could this be the doing of heaven and earth or the harm of ghosts and spirits? It is the loss of the Way that leads to self-departure.

Those with aspiration for the Elixir Way must not fail to establish the trunk from the outset. In sum, the ancestor of the Great Dao does not transcend the one qi to accomplish transformation. When divided, it becomes black and white; when separated, it becomes blue and yellow. Metaphorically, it is called the sun and moon, dragon and tiger—all these manifold distinctions are merely the two of yin and yang, lead and mercury, which in truth are one thing. Likewise, spirit water, Flowery Pond, lead furnace, and earthen cauldron are all one place. The two characters, when split, become zi and wu, mao and you, new moon and full moon, the twenty-four qi, the seventy-two seasonal nodes—one year’s conjunctions, one month’s revolutions—are all one time. Yet this one time is one place, and one place is one thing, all issuing forth from within this One—what is called the True One. How many in the world truly know this One?

Those who do not know it resort to methods such as internal visualization of the viscera, contemplation of the five organs’ radiance, pacing the Dipper and walking the celestial stems, aligning with days and hours to perform the Ninefold Obscuration, exhaling the upright and inhaling the turbid, consuming primordial qi through the primordial womb, fasting from sleep day and night, ceaselessly laboring through new and full moons. Their bodies grow daily weary, their spirits confused and disoriented, appearing as if deranged. Their hundred vessels seethe like a boiling cauldron, never attaining clarity. They dwell in earthen huts, erect altars, and morning and evening pay homage to ghosts and spirits, seeking apparitions in dreams. With hearts moved and minds elated, they believe they will surely prolong life—only to die prematurely, their corpses exposed, their forms decayed. With every gesture, they violate and rebel, losing the pivotal mechanism. Such arts are myriad, numbering in the thousands, yet all deviate from the Yellow Emperor and Laozi, twisting and contorting to the Nine Capitals. The wise examine these methods and suddenly understand their origins.

True One Master Peng Xiao said: The methods of internal visualization of the viscera and the contortions to the Nine Capitals—Master Xu referred to them as the crooked paths of secondary gates, pursued by those who do not grasp the fundamental origin of the Great Dao. There are those who practice minor arts of inward contemplation, pacing the Dipper and stepping the celestial stems, aligning with the six jia and days and hours—these are but the first steps for the uninitiated. Others engage in bedroom arts of aligning with scenes and joining qi, bending and stretching, grasping and sealing, holding the breath and swallowing saliva. Through these, qi becomes disordered, spirit exhausted, hun damaged, po depleted, leading to the yang spirit fleeing the palace, yin thieves plundering the lungs and liver. Truly, this is due to internal and external mutual encroachment, the clash of yin and yang at the hours of yin and shen. The seven po wander among the dead, the three corpses wage war in the eyes, the hundred vessels boil, the three fields collapse. What was hoped to be longevity instead harms life—how can one attain clarity?

Others externally erect altars and shrines, sacrificing to licentious ghosts, hoping to encounter the Dao and beg for extended years. Instead, ghostly qi transmits to their essence and po, and perverse winds arise in their heart-chambers. Some commune in dreams, others see forms and hear voices, believing longevity is within reach—unaware that their life is in their own hands, they hasten their demise, discarding their decaying forms. These many kinds all belong to heterodox paths: the more one seeks to advance, the more one stagnates; the more one seeks speed, the slower one becomes. They rebel against the utmost truth, deviate from principle, violate heavenly reason, and falsely claim to be high Daoists. They obscure and conceal the true teachings, entirely losing the true transmission of the Yellow Emperor and Laozi. Thus, Master Xu did not wish for people to practice secondary gates, but to directly enter the correct path. Even if the methods of internal visualization yield minor accomplishments, they ultimately cannot escape death and decay. Only the Golden Fluid Returning Elixir, after ingestion, reverses aging and restores youth, allowing one to attain the rank of True Person and share the longevity of heaven and earth.

Hence, the following text says: When golden sand enters the five viscera, mist and fog arise like wind and rain, steaming and reaching the four limbs. The complexion becomes radiant, the hair turns from white to black, new teeth grow, the old man regains youth, the aged woman becomes a maiden. The form is transformed, worldly calamities are avoided—this is called the True Person. The *Huangting Jing* also states: Even at one hundred and twenty years, one may still return. Beyond this, cultivating the Dao becomes exceedingly difficult. Though one may await the nine transformations and the eight jade elixirs, the essence of the sun and moon, to rescue the aged and infirm, it is known that this method is the supreme path of ascending to heaven in broad daylight and attaining longevity. This is now clear.

Master Xu admonishes those who delight in the Dao to reflect deeply on these words, and they will naturally become enlightened.

Baoyizi Chen Xianfang said: The arts and techniques in the world are innumerable. Even the methods of internal visualization of the viscera require the learner to discern the false from the true, to examine right from wrong. Practices such as closing the eyes to inwardly visualize and contemplate the radiance of the five viscera, pacing the Dipper to align with the upright qi of the celestial stems, aligning with days and hours to perform the Ninefold Obscuration and practice yin arts, exhaling the upright qi and inhaling the turbid, consuming primordial qi through the primordial womb, fasting from sleep day and night without rest—these cause the body to grow weary, the spirit confused and disoriented. Some erect altars to sacrifice to ghosts, others seek spirits in dreams. Though such arts are many, none are the correct method. What was hoped to prolong life instead hastens death, causing the form to decay and stink, vainly harming the mandate of heaven—truly lamentable. How could they know that the Golden Fluid Returning Elixir transforms the fleshly body of one’s parents, allowing one to ascend and soar?

The aforementioned methods all violate the Way of the Yellow Emperor and Laozi. Observing their conduct, they instead incur calamity in the Nine Capitals’ precincts. Those who understand this Dao reflect on Master Xu’s words and know the proper path of cultivation.

Shangyangzi Chen Zhixu said: As a cultivator, one must understand the correctness of the Great Dao. Without the union of yin and yang, the transformations of kan and li, all else are secondary gates and heterodox paths. Practices such as inwardly visualizing the five viscera, imagining and exhaling, outwardly pacing the Dipper and stepping the celestial stems, grasping mudras, or engaging in bedroom arts to control the Three Peaks with women, or practicing the Ninefold Obscuration, sword methods, the Five Affairs, aligning with scenes to join qi, and disturbing the primordial womb—these are all defiled practices, the lowest of secondary gates. Others exhale the upright and inhale the perverse, endure hunger and consume qi, or discuss years of sitting in meditation without sleep day or night, or erect altars to sacrifice to ghosts and spirits, seeking apparitions. These are the chaotic paths of secondary gates, causing the spirit to become disoriented, the hundred vessels to seethe, the heart to rejoice daily, and dreams to manifest nightly. Though there are a thousand paths and a hundred byways, all are without merit. They violate the teachings of the Yellow Emperor and Laozi, do not study with true teachers, and fail to grasp the harmony of yin and yang. Their twists and turns invite calamity from the Nine Capitals—how can they congeal the pearl of unity? What was hoped to extend life instead shortens it. If there are those of discernment who encounter the words of a true teacher and reflect on the *Cantong Qi*, they will suddenly know the correct path and not be deluded by secondary gates and heterodox paths.

Master Quanyang Yu Yan said: The three thousand six hundred methods of life-nurturing and the dozens of schools swarm in confusion, too numerous to count. Some, like those who shut their eyes to visualize inwardly through the five viscera or tread the Dipper’s steps in empty space to invoke the celestial stems in sacrifice—how can these be the Dao of the Elixir? As for those who practice the Yin arts, taking "nine shallow, one deep" as their fire-timing, they only stir chaos in the primordial womb. Those who ingest qi, using "exhaling the old and inhaling the new" as their medicinal substance, merely cause the bowels and stomach to growl with emptiness. Those who sit in stubborn vacuity endure sleepless days and nights, while those who exert themselves in labor disregard bodily fatigue. Some shake their heads and sway their brains, clench their fists and strain their strength, until the hundred vessels seethe and erupt into abscesses and ulcers. Others pile earth to erect altars, worshipping at dawn and sacrificing at dusk, only to dream of ghostly couplings at night and fall prey to demonic seductions. At first, their hearts rejoice, believing they can surely prolong life, yet suddenly, midway, they meet an early death, their forms decaying all the same. All these practices are perverse in their grasp, flagrantly violating the writings of the Yellow Emperor, their every action errant and wholly losing the safeguards of Lord Lao. Clinging to delusion, they reach this end—how they incur calamity! And who is to blame?

Thus, Master Wei composed this *Cantong Qi*, resplendent as a torch on a darkened thoroughfare, luminous as a needle in a bewildering sea. He desired that later men of discernment, through its words and safeguards, might suddenly achieve penetrating clarity, knowing the path by which to refine and cultivate.

*Ancient Text of the Cantong Qi: Collected Explications, Volume the Lower*
*Imperially Commissioned Complete Library of the Four Treasuries*
*Ancient Text of the Cantong Qi: Collected Explications, Volume the Lower [Lower]*
*By Jiang Yibiao of the Ming Dynasty*
*Annotations on the Cantong Qi [Composed by Xu Jingxiu of the Eastern Han]* *Lower Chapter*

Amidst fire, lead white turns corrupt and reverts to lead; ice and snow, when warmed by hot water, dissolve into the Great Mystery’s gold. With cinnabar as the essence, it harmonizes with mercury, transforming through its authenticity. From beginning to end, all follows in sequence. Those who seek to refine and consume the elixir of immortality must employ like-kind elements: just as planting grain requires grain, and hatching chicks demands eggs, so too must one rely on the natural order to complete the work. Smelting fish eyes cannot yield pearls; thistles cannot become sandalwood. Only like-kind elements coalesce, while disparate things fail to form treasures. Swallows do not hatch phoenixes; foxes and hares do not nurse colts. Water does not flow upward in flames; fire does not moisten downward.

In this world, many scholars—profound and gifted—chance upon no true encounter. They exhaust their resources, clinging to texts and fabricating interpretations without foundation. Their measurements falter, their methods stray. They grind and refine chalcanthite, mica, alum, magnetite, sulfur, and mercury, burning cedar wood to concoct the Five Minerals, using copper as a catalyst. Yet their natures clash, refusing to unite. A thousand attempts yield ten thousand failures. Hoping for reversal, they achieve only folly; fortune eludes them to the end.

The Perfected alone comprehend this. From youth to white-haired age, they waver midway, doubting the Way, clinging to delusion. They stray from the correct path, entering crooked byways, peering through a tube to glimpse the sky—how can such narrow vision fathom what lies ahead?

**True Unity Master Peng Xiao says:**
Lead white is refined from black lead; cast into fire, it reverts to lead. Ice and snow, formed from water’s qi, dissolve into water when warmed. Gold, cinnabar, and mercury are of one body: gold as the mother gives birth to cinnabar and mercury. Thus, planting grain requires grain, and hatching chicks demands eggs—all things of like-kind generate one another, beginning and ending in mutual dependence, following the Dao of nature. To use metals, minerals, herbs, frost, dew, ice, snow, salt, or brine is misguided; later passages clarify this.

The root of Heaven and Earth becomes the root of the medicine; true gold mother becomes the mother of the medicine, producing yin and yang to form the essence—gold cinnabar and numinous mercury—into the elixir of longevity. Is this not so? Hence, swallows do not hatch phoenixes; foxes and hares do not nurse colts. Fire’s nature is to flame upward; it cannot moisten downward. Water’s nature is to moisten downward; it cannot flame upward. Extending this principle of natural root-kind, it becomes clear that the gold mother produces gold cinnabar.

Master Wei observed that many learned scholars, versed in the Dao and fond of alchemical pursuit, fail to meet enlightened teachers. They rely solely on texts and formulas, fabricating interpretations, squandering wealth, and grinding miscellaneous medicines in vain hope of longevity and transcendence. Years pass; their hair turns white, yet they persist in error, clinging to narrow views, straying into crooked paths. A thousand attempts yield ten thousand failures—how can they gauge what lies ahead? The incompatibility of disparate natures has already been explained in prior annotations.

**Embracing Unity Master Chen Xianwei says:**
Things of like-kind resonate without premeditation, inevitably so. Lead white, refined from lead, reverts to lead when cast into fire. Ice and snow, formed from water, return to water when warmed. The birth of supreme treasures arises from the true essence of the Great Yang, coalescing into numinous radiance. When cinnabar first forms, it already contains true mercury. Mercury separated from its mother is called "quicksilver." Quicksilver, refined in the great crucible by the Great Yang over years, congeals into silver; over time, silver transforms into gold. Thus, gold takes cinnabar as its essence—cinnabar is the spirit, mercury the essence.

In the initial stages of elixir refinement, one first gathers yang essence to transform it into cinnabar, then takes mercury to blend with the cinnabar, grinding and harmonizing the two. Through smelting, they become gold. Only after gold is formed does one use lead to nurture it. The true essence arises from the Great Yang, born through its union with the Great Yin. Only true lead is fit for consumption; true lead is born of the Great Yin. Thus, like-kind elements must be used: gold finds true lead as a child finds its mother. With this mutual assistance, smelting is easily accomplished—like planting grain with grain or a hen hatching eggs, all resonate with like-kind, mutually dependent qi.

Without this knowledge, seeking disparate elements is futile, wasting effort and resources—like swallows failing to hatch phoenixes or foxes and hares failing to nurse colts. Water cannot steam things; fire cannot moisten them. Yet when cinnabar and mercury unite, spirit and essence are born; when water and fire merge, steam and moisture arise. Fire beneath water can fulfill its upward-flaming nature; water above fire can achieve its downward-moistening virtue. Who but the most refined in the world can attain this? The principle of the Golden Elixir surpasses creation, transcending the bounds of thought. Without encountering a Perfected One, all efforts are in vain. Using external substances is even more delusional. Clinging to error and forsaking the correct path is no virtue of the worthy.

Many who study the Dao refuse to steadfastly seek teachers or friends, to endure hardship and diligently inquire. Some begin but do not persist; others waver midway, wasting years until their hair turns white and old age arrives without achievement. All this stems from peering through a tube at the sky—self-satisfied, self-conceited.

Yet the sun and moon shine brightly; Heaven and Earth endure. Cold and heat alternate; day and night succeed one another—all embody the principle of the Great Dao’s unfolding. Without encountering a Perfected One, it is difficult to fathom. Scholars should first accumulate merit and virtue to invoke resonance. Let none deceive themselves, reaching this treasure mountain only to return empty-handed.

**Upper Yang Master Chen Zhixu says:**
Lead white is refined black lead; when exposed to fire, it reverts to its original nature. Ice and snow are congealed cold water; when warmed, they return to their source. Gold enters mercury and turns white; with fire, it regains its red hue. People, led astray by desires, disrupt their nature. Preserving one’s nature enables longevity. What does it mean for gold to take cinnabar as its essence? What does it mean to harmonize with mercury?

Those who cultivate the Dao must understand creation, penetrate yin and yang. To refine the Golden Elixir, first accumulate mercury from the inner fire and blend it with the water mercury of the Jade Pool. Then use the water of Kan to aid the fire of Li. When water and fire are balanced, gold and cinnabar unite in form, transforming through the authenticity of like-kind. From beginning to end, yin and yang serve as the foundation. Thus, those who seek to refine and consume the elixir of immortality must employ like-kind elements—this is certain. Just as planting grain requires grain and hatching chicks demands eggs, so too must one proceed to become an immortal or Buddha. Without like-kind elements, even if one enters the marketplace a hundred times or sits in meditation for a thousand years, it will all end in emptiness.

Those who do not follow the Way of Like-Kind but pursue heterodox paths—whether through bedchamber arts, controlling women, or the "Three Peaks" battle of essence—all tread deviant paths, mistaking fish eyes for pearls and mugwort for sandalwood. How could they know that transformation arises from authenticity?

Swallows and sparrows are flying birds, yet cannot give birth to phoenixes; foxes and hares are running beasts, yet how could they nurse horses? These are not of the same kind. Water’s nature is to moisten downward, yet cannot blaze upward; fire’s function is to flame upward, yet cannot moisten downward. For yin and yang to interact, they must accord with the natural Dao.

Master Xu taught that to seek an enlightened teacher, one must first grasp the *Cantong Qi* and harmonize yin and yang before speaking of the Elixir. When the Purple Jade Elder first received instruction from the True Man of Great Void, the first question upon entering the chamber was: *"Is the* Cantong Qi *for understanding the* Changes *or for practicing it?"* The Great Void replied: *"The* Changes *concerns only the two matters of yin and yang. One who can understand and practice them is a sage."* Thus, the *Changes* states: *"In the southwest, one gains companions and proceeds with like-kind."* This is what "like-kind" means—following the same path. If matters diverge, no treasure is formed. First understanding, then practicing—this is the sage’s way.

Yet some later practitioners, though endowed with fine talents, do not seek true teachers. Instead, they rely on ancient texts, arbitrarily measuring and speculating: some use Shaoyang’s chalcanthite to refine gold, others employ Chensha’s cinnabar to burn silver; they pound and refine the Five Metals, smelt the Eight Stones, take the Three Yellows as like-kind, and use purified red copper as an auxiliary pivot. But these stones and metals are not of our like-kind—how could they willingly merge with our qi and dwell within our bodies?

Long ago, Zhang Xiang of Jiujiang refined and consumed elixirs. Lü Dongbin, pitying his earnest devotion to the Dao, transformed into a master to visit and save him. Zhang, arrogant from long elixir consumption, believed himself certain of ascension and treated the master with disdain. Dongbin abruptly departed, leaving only a poem on his seat:

*"Alas for Zhang, Minister of Jiujiang,
Who lost his sight from elixirs, his spirit withered.
He did not ponder the formless Returning Elixir,
But foolishly consumed metals and stones—how blind!"*

Later, Zhang indeed went blind and died. Such people, though gifted, cling stubbornly to error, willingly misled by blind teachers who point to metals and stones as like-kind. They waste their wealth on refining and consumption, yet to their deaths remain deluded. Like those who peer at the sky through a tube, how could they know that true immortals and sagely teachers exist in the world?

People are habituated to the commonplace and unwilling to transcend it, to broadly seek and learn. With narrow vision and low aims, how could they hear or see lofty and distant matters? Yet they strut about, calling themselves "great men"—all while squandering their fine talents, lost in self-reliance and complacency.

Master Quanyang, Yu Yan, said: *"Hu powder is made from black lead; if cast into fire, it reverts to lead. Ice and snow are formed from yin qi; if drenched with warm water, they melt into water. Why? Because returning to the source is a principle that cannot be otherwise."*

The method of refining gold takes cinnabar as its foundation and blends it with mercury, for mercury is born from cinnabar and shares its like-kind nature. Thus, from beginning to end, they interact to produce transformation through authenticity.

The *Wuzhen Pian* states:
*"To mend broken bamboo, use bamboo;
To hatch a chick, use an egg.
All else is not of like-kind—vain labor,
Like true lead meeting the sage’s mechanism."*

For true mercury to meet true lead is for one yin and one yang to resonate in qi—this is like-kind. It is like planting grain requiring millet, or hatching a chick requiring an egg. Following like-kind, the Way unfolds naturally, effortlessly achieving transformation. But if one abandons like-kind to seek other things, it is like mistaking fish eyes for pearls or mugwort for sandalwood—no transformation can occur. Just as swallows and sparrows can only produce swallows and sparrows, not phoenixes; foxes and hares can only nurse foxes and hares, not horses.

Fire’s nature is to blaze upward—can it be made to moisten downward? Water’s nature is to moisten downward—can it be made to blaze upward? Thus, to refine and consume the elixir of immortality, one must employ like-kind. Like-kind follows the same path; divergence fails to form treasure. This is certain.

Few among the world’s gifted scholars and earnest learners fail to seek, yet rare are those who encounter a true master and receive the correct transmission. Some possess the medicines of the Three Yellows and Four Spirits, mistakenly believing the Dao lies therein.
