The *Fuming Pian* says: 'This Dao is most sacred; do not lightly disclose it. It depends entirely on virtue and conduct; frivolity and shallowness must be strictly avoided.' This shows that the Dao inherently detests both indiscriminate disclosure and failure to disclose to the right recipient. Grasp this one point, and all matters are complete—how true! There is no second Dao; the sages have no second mind. The myriad volumes of immortal scriptures all converge on the Golden Elixir as their root and origin. If scholars repeatedly ponder and savor this book, one day their minds will comprehend and their spirits will guard it—then they will know that the essential words are indeed fully revealed, bright and clear, without the slightest obscurity."

**Postscript:**
The *Cantong Qi* employs few words yet conveys great meaning; its language is subtle yet its principles profound. It arrays the Five Emperors to establish governance and matches the Three Sovereigns to institute order. Though rulers and ministers differ in rank, with no fixed standard for superiors and inferiors, if governance is thus ordered, great peace will not be achieved. The method of "subduing and consuming" cannot confer longevity; learning to nurture nature does not extend lifespan. Yet when it dissects Yin and Yang, harmonizes their weights, aligns the waxing and waning of the sun and moon, and forms the images of the Eight Trigrams—when male and female enact transformation, hardness and softness move and rest, and the grains of rice are distinguished—it takes the *Yijing* as its proof, its intent robust indeed. Thus, I have written this commentary to transmit it to later worthies. Only those who understand the Great Image will attain longevity, strengthen themselves, and benefit their bodies. Those who pursue this Dao must pay it utmost heed.

**Collected Explications of the *Ancient Text of the Three in One* (Volume III) [From the *Zibu* (Masters Section), Daoist Category, *Baopuzi Nei/Wai Pian* (Inner and Outer Chapters of the Master Who Embraces Simplicity)]**
*Imperially Commissioned Siku Quanshu*
*Section Fourteen of the Masters Section: *Baopuzi Nei/Wai Pian* (Inner and Outer Chapters of the Master Who Embraces Simplicity)*
*Daoist Category – Synopsis*

[Your Servants] respectfully note: The *Baopuzi Nei/Wai Pian* in eight *juan* was composed by Ge Hong of the Jin dynasty. Hong, styled Zhichuan, was a native of Jurong in Danyang. He was deeply devoted to esoteric arts and once heard that consuming cinnabar could prolong life. He petitioned to serve as Magistrate of Julou, later retiring to Mount Luofu to refine elixirs and compose texts, expounding on the arts of nourishing life, *huangbai* (aurifaction), and other techniques. He styled himself *Baopuzi* ("Master Who Embraces Simplicity"), hence the title of the work. In his preface, he states that the *Neipian* (Inner Chapters) comprise twenty *juan* and the *Waipian* (Outer Chapters) fifty *juan*. However, the *Sui Shu*, *Tang Shu*, *Tongzhi*, and *Tongkao* record varying numbers of *juan*, suggesting discrepancies due to differing divisions in transmission. Chao Gongwu noted that the *Jin Shu* records 116 *pian* (chapters) in total for the Inner and Outer Chapters, while the version transmitted today contains only forty. Chen Zhensun further observed that the *Guange Shumu* (Catalogue of the Imperial Library) lists fifty *juan* for the Outer Chapters, though these have not been seen. Additionally, the *Yongle Dadian* includes a table of contents that, when compared to the present edition, reveals the loss of eight *pian*, including the *Dansha Fa* (Method of Cinnabar). This indicates that complete versions were already rare by the Song and Yuan dynasties. The present edition was collated and printed by Lu Shunzhi of Wucheng during the Ming dynasty, based on an acquired Song edition and two versions from the imperial library. Compared to other editions, it alone preserves the fewest omissions, and its chapter count aligns with Ge Hong’s original preface, confirming that Hong likely treated each *pian* as a single *juan*. As for Chao’s reference to 116 *pian*, its source remains unknown.

The *Waipian* (Outer Chapters) discuss the successes and failures of contemporary governance, the merits and demerits of human affairs, and abound in allusive analogies, displaying a wealth of reasoned argument. The *Neipian* (Inner Chapters) expound on matters of immortals, breath cultivation, and talismanic writs. Some Confucian scholars dismiss these as heterodox, yet the work’s diction is incisive, its erudition vast, and its literary elegance resplendent—qualities unmatched by later Six Dynasties compositions. It should not be summarily dismissed merely because it originates from the Daoist tradition.

*Respectfully submitted in the eleventh month of the forty-fifth year of the Qianlong reign.*
*Chief Compilers: [Your Servants] Ji Yun, Lu Xixiong, Sun Shiyi*
*Chief Collators: [Your Servants] Lu Feichi*

---

**Preface to the *Baopuzi Neipian***

My nature lacks transcendent talent, yet I happen to favor the practice of non-action. Were I to unfurl my wings, I could soar through the empyrean; were I to spur my hooves, I could chase the wind and tread upon shadows. Yet I choose instead to restrain my mighty pinions among the flock of wrens and conceal my soaring traces within the herd of lame donkeys. How much more so when Nature endows me with wings of but ordinary span and Creation lends me hooves of the most halting gait? Having carefully assessed my abilities, I know my limits and dare not proceed. How could I, like a fly, aspire to the heavens’ vaulting flight, or like a lame turtle, pursue the hare’s swift course? How could I adorn the ugliness of Mo Mu to seek the beauty of the matchmaker, or demand a thousand gold from a goldsmith’s forge for a heap of sand and pebbles?

To stretch the strides of a *jiaoyao* dwarf to match the tracks of Kuafu is why the man of Yue stumbles and fails. To strain the frailty of Yao Li to shoulder the task of lifting a tripod is why the man of Qin breaks his back. Thus, I renounce the path of glory and honor, contenting my aspirations in the realm of poverty and adversity. To me, pulse and greens are as sweet as the eight delicacies, and a thatched hut brings the joy of carved rafters. Therefore, though the gates of the powerful and noble lie but a footstep away, I do not follow. Yet for those who know the Dao, though the way be arduous and distant, I must journey there.

Having perused no small number of rare and wondrous texts, I find that most abound in cryptic language, scarcely to be readily understood. Unless one’s spirit is supremely refined, one cannot plumb their depths; unless one’s practice is steadfast and sincere, one cannot fully perceive their truths. Among Daoist scholars, those of profound learning and wide discernment are few, while those who indulge in reckless conjecture and groundless assertions are many. From time to time, there arise men of earnest intent who wish to cultivate and refine themselves, yet in their haste they know not where to begin. When doubts arise in their minds, there is none to whom they may turn for counsel.

Thus, I have composed this work, roughly outlining the principles of long life. Its most sublime truths cannot be fully conveyed in written words; what is set down here is but a coarse and sketchy account, offered as a corner of the whole, in the hope that those who are stirred to earnest inquiry may reflect upon it and thereby grasp more than half the matter. How could this suffice for those of dim and obstructed vision to fathom its subtleties or traverse its vast expanse? It is but a preliminary discussion of what must come first.

The scholars of this world, shackled by their unquestioning devotion to the teachings of Zhou and Kong, cling to death and refuse to believe in the affairs of immortals, dismissing them as heretical delusions. Should they chance upon this work of mine, they will not only laugh it to scorn but also slander and revile what is true and authentic. Therefore, I have not sought to conform to the world’s expectations. Among the many works I have authored, I have set this one apart, titling it *Inner Chapters*, comprising twenty scrolls, each arranged in its proper sequence alongside the *Outer Chapters*.

Though it may not be worthy of concealment in a famous mountain’s stone chamber, I intend to seal it within a golden casket, to be shown only to those who recognize its value. Let it not be seen by those with whom such matters cannot be discussed. My aim is that future generations of seekers of long life may have their confusions dispelled—how could I seek validation from those who refuse to believe?

Respectfully inscribed by Ge Hong, Zichuan. *From the Imperial Collection of the Four Treasuries.*

**The Inner Chapters of the Master Who Embraces Simplicity, Scroll One**
*Composed by Ge Hong of the Jin Dynasty*

**Chapter One: Elucidating the Dao**

The Master Who Embraces Simplicity states: The Dao is the primordial ancestor of all spontaneity and the great origin of the ten thousand differences. How profound its depths—thus it is called "mysterious." How boundless its expanse—thus it is called "wondrous." Lofty, it crowns the nine heavens; vast, it envelops the eight directions. Brighter than the sun and moon, swifter than lightning, it now flickers and vanishes like a shadow, now scatters like drifting stars, now shimmers like a limpid abyss, now billows like floating clouds. From the faintest stirrings, it gives rise to being; in utter stillness, it becomes nonbeing. It sinks into the deepest obscurity, soars beyond the farthest reaches. No metal or stone can match its hardness; no dew or mist can equal its softness. Square yet unbound by the carpenter’s square, round yet unconfined by the compass, it comes unseen and departs untraced.

Heaven is exalted by it; Earth is humbled by it. Clouds drift by its motion; rain descends by its bestowal. It shapes the primordial unity within the womb, forges the Two Modes from its mold. It exhales and inhales the Great Beginning, smelts and tempers the myriad things. In ceaseless revolution, it completes the twenty-eight constellations, steers the numinous mechanism of chaos, breathes forth and draws in, encompasses the obscure and the silent. It expands and contracts, sinks the turbid and raises the pure, measures the Yellow River and the Wei like a wine steward—adding without overflow, ladling without depletion. Bestow, and it does not flourish; seize, and it does not wither.

Where the Dao abides, joy knows no end; where the Dao departs, vessels decay and spirits flee. The five tones and eight timbres, the flowing strains of *qingshang*—they deafen the sharp-eared. Rare blossoms and dazzling hues—bright as flame, resplendent—they blind the clear-eyed. Feasting in ease, fine wine and fragrant spirits—they disorder the nature. Alluring beauty, painted faces, leaden powder and white silk—they sap the life. Those who know the Dao may attain enduring existence; those who know it not can scarcely preserve their lives.

A glance may become the instrument of death; lips and tongue, the pivot of rise and fall. Ornate pavilions overlook the Milky Way; gilded chambers, carved rafters, and embroidered curtains interlace like clouds. Silken canopies drift like mist, kingfisher plumes adorn the inner chambers. Golden goblets flash in the revelry, clear melodies and clamorous pipes blend in unison. The dances of Zheng swirl in profusion, mournful flutes soar to the clouds, feathered canopies float upon rippling waters. Plucking fragrant blossoms in orchid groves, toying with crimson petals in pools of pearls, ascending heights to gaze afar and forget a hundred sorrows, peering into depths to dispel morning hunger—entering banquets aglow with a thousand gates, departing in chariots of vermilion splendor.

Yet when joy reaches its peak, sorrow follows; when fullness is attained, loss is near. Thus, when the music ends, sighs arise; when the feast concludes, the heart grieves. This is the inevitable summons of principle and circumstance, like echo answering sound, shadow following form. No borrowed illusion, but truth itself—things pass as if forgotten.

The Dao of the Profound—those who attain it turn inward, those who lose it turn outward; those who employ it are spirit, those who forget it are mere tools. This is the essential teaching of the Profound Dao.

Those who attain it hold honor without need of golden axes’ might; they embody wealth without reliance on rare treasures. Lofty beyond ascent, deep beyond fathoming, they ride the streaming light to guard against time’s flight, soaring through the Six Voids, pervading and dissolving—emerging from the realm above all, entering the realm below none, traversing the boundless gate, roaming the distant wilds. In carefree wandering through the indistinct and vague, in lingering amid the faint and fleeting, they swallow ninefold blossoms amid cloud peaks, savor the six qi upon cinnabar clouds, meander through the obscure, soar through the subtle, tread the coiled rainbow, stride the revolving sphere. Such are those who attain it.

Next are those of true contentment. Knowing sufficiency, they can withdraw in reclusion, nurturing their radiance in mountain forests, unfurling phoenix and dragon wings among the tiniest creatures, cultivating vast qi within humble thatched huts. They wear hempen robes and woven belts, yet do not envy the splendor of embroidered silks; they walk with staff and sandals, yet do not exchange them for the trappings of four-horse carriages. They hide luminous pearls in lofty peaks, untouched by other mountains’ assaults; they sink scales and armor into dark abysses, avoiding the calamity of drilling and scorching. In movement and stillness, they know when to stop—nothing is lacking. They cast aside the dazzling pomp of court, shun the perilous paths of overturned chariots, chanting and whistling among green cliffs where all things become mere dust, delighting beneath lush boughs where vermilion gates are but hempen cords. Holding the plow in open fields, they wave their insignia as if merely wielding a whip; sipping tea and rinsing with spring water, they find the grandest feast no better than wild greens. Thus, they abound in joy within the realm of non-action, rejoicing equally in high and low where no strife exists. Embracing purity and guarding simplicity, free from desire and care, they preserve their true nature within the empty vessel, dwelling in equanimity, vast and unbounded—equal to the undifferentiated, in harmony with creation’s accord. Like darkness turning to light, like turbidity clarifying, seemingly slow yet swift, seemingly waning yet full, how could they deign to cast aside the dust of sacrificial priests, abandon the master craftsman’s role, or usurp the butcher’s place with ignorant hands? They do not wield the carpenter’s line to aid the hand-wounding laborer, nor fret over trifles like the stench of rats, troubling the common man’s joys and sorrows. Detached, they do not delight in the praise of the vulgar; unshaken, they do not fear the slander of the herd. They do not let external things disturb their utmost essence, nor allow profit and harm to stain their purity. Thus, neither extreme wealth nor supreme rank can tempt them, nor can praise bring them pleasure. Even the threat of naked blades or boiling cauldrons cannot coerce them.

How could slander and defamation ever distress them? Ever without intention amid the myriad vexations, they have never mingled with things.

Consider those who wield a Sui pearl to shoot a sparrow, guard against Qin’s hemorrhoids to secure a chariot post, climb a rotten rope to raid a nest, or swim the Lüliang rapids to catch fish—who by day strike the pose of a lone lord and by night scavenge like a fox or crow. Their beams sag, their tripods overturn, they founder and cannot recover. Such are the deeds that make the world race and strain to keep pace, yet chill the heart and freeze the marrow of those who have attained the Way.

Thus the perfected person silently embodies the music of Shao and Xia, veils their radiant hues, and unfurls their six pinions in the wasteland of the five domains without need of reed-stalk defenses. They conceal their scales and horns in places of no use, without relying on crooked or superfluous preparations. Below, they are free from the haughty screech of the owl; above, they know no regret for overreaching. No one recognizes them—they are distant indeed.

**Discourse on Immortals, II**

Someone asked: “Immortals do not die—can this truly be attained?”
The Master Who Embraces Simplicity replied: “Even with the keenest sight, not all forms can be seen; even with the sharpest hearing, not all sounds can be heard. Though one possess the wisdom of Yu, Yi, Qi, and Xie, what one has recognized is far less than what one has not. The ten thousand things teem—how could they not include immortals? The bamboo and silk records are filled with them. Why would the Way of undying be absent?”

The questioner laughed loudly: “Whatever has a beginning must have an end; whatever exists must perish. Thus the sages of the Three Dynasties and the Five Emperors, the wisdom of Jie and Ping, the eloquence of Duan and Ying, the valor of Ben and Yu, and the strength of the Five Ding—all died. This is the constant principle of human existence, the great inevitability that must come to pass. We have heard of plants withering before the first frost, wilting in midsummer, bearing ears that never bloom, or withering before they fruit—but never of one who enjoys ten thousand years of life, an age of enduring vision. Therefore, the ancients did not seek immortality in their studies, nor speak of marvels in their words. They rejected heterodox paths and upheld this natural order, relegating the crane and the tortoise to another realm, equating life and death with morning and evening.

“To torment the heart and constrain the self in pursuit of futile endeavors is like carving ice or chiseling rot—ultimately, no certain success can be achieved. Would it not be better to unfurl lofty defenses for the age, summon the blessings of the present, causing purple and azure to intertwine in layered splendor, black tripods to stand in place of plows, and swift chariots to replace weary steps? Is this not far more splendid?

“Often I ponder the *Poet’s* rebuke in ‘Fufu,’ and deeply reflect on Confucius—both are proof of mortality. Why grasp at the wind’s formless breath, chase shadows impossible to seize, seek what cannot be obtained, or tread paths that cannot be traversed? To abandon glory and endure hardship, forsake the easy to pursue the most difficult, is like a mourner chasing a wandering woman—inevitably leading to double loss. The blind faith of a single-minded zealot invites swift calamity, both internal and external.

“The striped autumn cicada cannot shave tile or stone into a needle’s point; Ou Ye cannot forge lead or tin into a Gan Jiang blade. What cannot be done, even spirits cannot accomplish; what cannot be achieved, even heaven and earth cannot bring to fruition. How could the world possess a miraculous formula to restore the old to youth or revive the dead? Yet you seek to prolong the mayfly’s life to span epochs, to nurture the morning mushroom’s brief bloom into the accumulation of months and years. Are you not mistaken?”

“I urge you to reflect further,” said the Master Who Embraces Simplicity. “Many things share a common root yet diverge in their ends—they cannot be lumped together. To say that whatever has a beginning must have an end is often true, but to conflate them all is not sound reasoning. One may say summer must be long, yet shepherd’s purse and wheat wither; one may say winter must be barren, yet bamboo and cypress flourish. One may say all that begins must end, yet heaven and earth are endless; one may say all that lives must die, yet cranes and tortoises endure.

“Scorching summer should be hot, yet there are cool days even in midsummer; deep winter should be cold, yet there are moments of warmth even in the harshest season. The hundred rivers flow east, yet some surge northward; the earth’s nature is utterly still, yet it may quake and crumble. Water is inherently cold, yet there are boiling springs in warm valleys; fire is by nature scorching, yet there are icy flames on Xiaoqiu. Heavy things should sink, yet the Southern Sea has mountains of floating stone; light things should float, yet the Fang branch has streams where feathers sink. The myriad differences cannot be judged by a single standard—so it has long been.

“Of all living things, none are more spiritually endowed than...”

“Of all living things, none are more spiritually endowed than humans, yet their natures should not be assumed identical. The wise and the foolish, the upright and the perverse, the fair and the foul, the long-lived and the short-lived, the pure and the turbid, the chaste and the wanton, the swift and the slow, the hasty and the patient—what they pursue and what they desire through ear and eye differ so utterly that between them lies a gulf as vast as that between heaven and earth, as irreconcilable as ice and burning coals. Why then should it seem strange that immortals differ from ordinary mortals in not sharing their inevitable death?

“If one claims that all beings receive a fixed measure of qi, then how is it that the pheasant becomes a clam, the sparrow a mussel, the loess gains wings, the river frog takes flight, the water-rat turns into a clam, the water-shield plant into maggots, the field mouse into a rail, the rotting grass into fireflies, the alligator into a tiger, and the snake into a dragon? If one claims that humans are endowed with a singularly upright nature while all other creatures receive their mandate from Heaven without distinction, then how is it that Niu Ai turned into a tiger, the old woman of Chu into a soft-shelled turtle, Zhi Li into a willow, and the maiden of Qin into stone—only to revive, exchanging male for female, transforming their forms? Why is it that Lao Peng lived long while Shangzi died young?

“If such differences exist, then what limit can there be to their strangeness? As for the immortals, they nurture the body with medicinal substances and prolong life through arts of calculation, preventing internal illness and warding off external harm. Though they may live long without dying, their original form remains unchanged. If one possesses the Dao, this presents no difficulty.

“Yet those of shallow understanding, bound by convention and clinging to the commonplace, all say, ‘We have never seen an immortal, so such beings must not exist in the world.’ But what can truly be known by what the eye has once seen? Within the vastness of Heaven and Earth, boundless and without limit, how can its myriad wonders be so quickly exhausted? A man may wear the sky as his canopy yet never know what lies above it; he may tread the earth all his life yet never fathom what lies beneath it. Even his own body, which he possesses, remains a mystery to him—he does not understand how his heart and lungs function. Lifespan lies within his own power, yet he does not know how to extend or shorten it. How much less, then, can those who rely on their shallow senses to judge the existence or nonexistence of the profound mysteries of immortality and the abstruse principles of the Dao be anything but pitiful?

“Suppose there were a sage of great talent who, shunning worldly employ, concealed his brilliance and veiled his splendor, casting off falsehood and abandoning desire, holding fast to the uncarved block in the midst of perfect purity, and leaving trivial affairs to the vulgar world. Even such a man would seldom be recognized by his contemporaries, for few can discern the true nature of one whose aspirations soar beyond the nameless, whose spirit dwells within a humble form. How much less, then, could the immortal be perceived? His pursuits are singular, his path extraordinary. He regards wealth and nobility as misfortune, glory and splendor as defilement, luxury as dust and dirt, and fame as morning dew. He treads through scorching winds yet remains unburned, steps lightly upon dark waves, stirs the dust with beating wings, and rides the wind with a chariot drawn by clouds. He soars upward to the Purple Pole, rests below on Kunlun. How could such a being be seen by those who are but walking corpses?

“Even if he were to wander playfully through the human realm, concealing his true form and disguising his marvels, appearing no different from the common crowd, shoulder to shoulder and step for step—who would be able to recognize him? If all immortals were like the man from the suburbs with his square pupils or Qiong Shu with his double ears, then indeed they might stand out.”

Those like the Yellow Thearch riding dragons or Prince Jin personally driving white cranes—some with scaled bodies and serpent heads, others in golden chariots and feathered robes—might yet be known. But if they are not thus, how could one not of penetrating vision ever glimpse their forms, or one not of acute hearing ever catch their voices?

Ordinary people, already lacking belief, recklessly heap slander and reproach, which the perfected ones find loathsome, driving them ever deeper into concealment. What the common crowd cherishes, the lofty scholar detests; what the vulgar hold dear, the realized one scorns. Even the noble scholar of vast capacity, who nurtures his boundless *qi*, still finds no joy in the company of the shallow and worldly—how much less would the divine immortals trouble themselves to make known their existence to those no better than straw dogs? What could they possibly seek from such as these? Yet people marvel at what they have never truly known.

Eyes that can barely discern objects a hundred paces away presume that what they see exists and what they do not see does not—yet how much must remain unseen in the world! This is like measuring the sea with a finger and claiming the water ends where the fingertip reaches. How could the mayfly compare itself to the giant turtle, or the white orchid fathom the great *chun* tree?

Emperor Wen of Wei, who had read widely and heard extensively, claimed to have experienced all things under heaven, declaring that no blade could cut jade nor fire-washed cloth exist. Yet when he wrote his *Dian Lun*, scarcely had he spoken these words when both objects appeared before him. The emperor sighed and swiftly destroyed his treatise, for nothing is certain, and all things may yet prove otherwise.

Prince Si of Chen wrote his *Shiyi Lun*, saying at first that the arts of the Dao were mere tricks to deceive the foolish, empty words without substance. But after witnessing Emperor Wu test Zuo Ci and others, who went without grain for nearly a month yet showed no pallor and retained their strength, claiming they could go fifty years without food—what doubt remained then? He also saw Gan Shi feed fish with an elixir before boiling them in seething oil: those without the drug cooked and became edible, while those that had swallowed it swam playfully all day as if still in water. Gan Shi also fed silkworms with a powdered mulberry drug, and they lived until the tenth month without aging. He fed chicks and newborn puppies with an "eternal youth" elixir, and they ceased growing. He fed white dogs with a "returning black" drug, and within a hundred days, their fur turned entirely black. Thus, he realized that the affairs of the world cannot be fully known, nor can one rely on conjecture alone.

Yet he lamented that he could not renounce sensual pleasures and devote himself wholeheartedly to the study of the Dao of long life. Both Cao Cao and Cao Pi were men of learning, with no book left unread, and talents that marked them as paragons of their age. Yet at first, they dismissed such things as nonexistent, only to later acknowledge their reality. Their sighs of regret at not matching such beings were profound—how could those who do not believe in immortals be blamed?

Liu Xiang, a scholar of vast learning, delved into the subtlest mysteries, his thoughts far-reaching and his reasoning clear, discerning truth from falsehood and verifying existence from nonexistence. In his *Liexian Zhuan*, he recorded over seventy immortals—if these accounts were fabrications, why would he have composed them? The events of remote antiquity cannot be witnessed firsthand; we rely on records and transmitted accounts. The *Liexian Zhuan* shines with undeniable truth—yet because its words did not emerge from the gates of the Duke of Zhou nor pass through the hands of Confucius, the world ultimately refuses to believe. If this were so, then all records in ancient histories would be false—how could only this one matter be an exception?

Ordinary people, greedy for glory and eager for advancement, chase after fame and profit, projecting their own hearts onto the ancients. Thus, they disbelieve that there were those who fled imperial abdications and scorned the honors of ministerial rank—men like Chao Fu and Xu You, Lao Lai and Zhuang Zhou. How much more difficult, then, to comprehend the divine immortals? How could one seek to make them known to such people? Yet in this age, many still believe in Liu Xiang, though he was no sage, and dismiss his records as unverifiable—this is truly cause for sighing.

The *Spring and Autumn Annals* could not match the virtue of heaven and earth, yet Confucius relied on them to compose his classic. The *Records of the Grand Historian* could not rival the brightness of the sun and moon, yet Yang Xiong praised them as true accounts. Liu Xiang was a renowned Confucian of the Han dynasty—how could his records be discarded?

The reason people do not believe that immortality can be learned or that life can be prolonged is precisely because the First Emperor of Qin and Emperor Wu of Han sought it in vain, and because men like Luan Da and Shao Jun failed to produce results. Yet one cannot deny the wealth of Tao Zhu and Yi Dun simply because of the poverty of Qian Lou and Yuan Xian, nor deny the beauty of Xi Shi and Nan Wei because of the ugliness of the woman from Wuyan or the woman with the goiter.

Even in worldly pursuits, there are those who do not succeed: farmers may fail to reap a harvest, merchants may suffer losses. How much more so for the Way of immortality? Why should the failures of a few determine the impossibility of success? Those who dismiss what they have never known are like the man who measures the sea with his finger and declares the water ends where his fingertip reaches. How could the ephemeral mayfly compare to the giant turtle, or the fleeting white orchid fathom the great *chun* tree?

Even in warfare, there are those who achieve no merit—how much more so for the pursuit of immortality, a far more difficult endeavor? Why must every seeker succeed? Those two sovereigns and their two ministers may have sought in vain, or perhaps they began with diligence but grew lax, or never encountered a true master. How could their failure prove the nonexistence of immortals?

The quest for longevity and the cultivation of the supreme Way depend upon resolve, not upon wealth or rank. For those unsuited to the path, high position and vast riches become nothing but heavy burdens. The methods of immortal learning require serenity, detachment, and the cleansing of desires—turning inward, dwelling in stillness, and abiding without thought. Yet emperors bear the weight of all under heaven, managing the affairs of state, their minds strained by ten thousand concerns, their spirits scattered across the cosmos. A single misstep disrupts the royal Way, and the people’s suffering disturbs the harmony of qi. *[Text missing]* Allurements sever the roots of life, dissipating essence and wearing down the mind, eroding the purity of the spirit. *[Text missing]* The matter cannot be fully expounded here.

When mosquitoes bite the skin, one cannot sit in peace; when lice swarm, one cannot sleep at ease. How much greater are the affairs of the four seas—how could one possibly shield one’s senses, still one’s breath, maintain long fasts, and purify oneself, rising early and retiring late to tend the furnace and refine the eight minerals?

Emperor Wu of Han reigned longer than any other, having already gained some small benefit in nurturing his nature. Yet the aid he received was but a drop in the bucket compared to the vast expenditures he made. *[Text missing]* His lavish patronage of fangshi led them to dare such wild fabrications. Had Luan Da truly possessed the Way, how could he have been executed? Those who possess the Way regard rank as a cauldron of boiling water, seals of office as mourning garments, gold and jade as dirt and dung, and grand halls as prisons. How could they clench their fists in empty boasts, hoping to chance upon glory and splendor? Dwelling in vermilion chambers, receiving boundless gifts, bearing the seal of the Five Powers, marrying imperial princesses, and wallowing in power and profit without restraint—such men clearly never attained the Way.

According to the *Household Records of Li Shaojun* compiled by Dong Zhongshu, Shaojun possessed the formula for immortality but was too poor to purchase the necessary ingredients. Thus, he entered Han service as a pretext to seek funds, and once his Way was complete, he departed. Furthermore, the *Daily Records of the Han Palace* state that before Shaojun’s departure, Emperor Wu dreamed of ascending Mount Song with him. Halfway up, a messenger riding a dragon descended from the clouds, bearing a tally, and said, “The Supreme One summons Shaojun.” Upon waking, the emperor told his attendants, “As in my dream, Shaojun will soon leave me.” Days later, Shaojun claimed illness and died. After some time, the emperor had his coffin opened, and only his clothes and cap remained.

The *Scriptures of the Immortals* state: the highest adepts ascend bodily into the void, becoming celestial immortals; the middling roam the famous mountains, becoming terrestrial immortals; the lowest die first and then shed their forms, becoming corpse-liberated immortals. Shaojun must have been one of the latter. In recent times, Master Hu took Fei Zhangfang away, and the Daoist Li Yiqj departed with two disciples. Later, they were seen in Pixian County. Their families each opened their coffins and found only a bamboo staff inscribed with cinnabar script—all were corpse-liberated immortals.

In the past, Wang Mang cited the classics to adorn his treachery, yet one cannot say all Confucians were usurpers and thieves. Sima Xiangru eloped with Zhuo Wenjun through the pretext of playing the zither, yet one cannot say all refined music promotes licentiousness.

Those who choke to death cannot blame Shennong for sowing grain; those burned to death cannot resent Suiren for drilling fire; those drowned cannot resent the Yellow Emperor for inventing boats; those who perish from alcohol cannot condemn Du Kang for brewing wine. Seeing Zhao Gao and Dong Zhuo, one cannot claim there were no Yi or Zhou in antiquity; seeing Shangchen and Modu, one cannot claim there were no Boqi or Xiaoji in ancient times.

Moreover, the *Collected Writings on Divine Immortals* contains methods for summoning spirits and exorcising demons, as well as techniques to make people see demons. Common folk, upon hearing this, dismiss it as empty rhetoric. Some say there are no demons in the world; others claim they exist but cannot be summoned or exorcised. Still others say that those who see demons—men called *xi*, women called *wu*—must possess the ability naturally and cannot acquire it through learning. Yet the *Book of Han* and the records of the Grand Historian both state that the Qi man Shaoweng was appointed by Emperor Wu as General Wencheng. When the emperor’s beloved Lady Li died, Shaoweng enabled him to see her as if she were alive, and even allowed him to behold the kitchen god. These are clear accounts in the historical annals.

If techniques can make demons appear in visible form and even allow those who have never seen them to perceive them, then what else might not be possible? Demons and spirits often manifest strange and wondrous transformations in the world, and many such accounts are recorded in the classics. Yet even these, common folk refuse to believe—how much less, then, would they accept the existence of immortals? Immortals dwell in lofty, distant realms, their pure currents flowing apart from the turbid. Ascending beyond, they depart and never return to the world. Without attaining the Dao, how could one witness or hear of them? The Confucians and Mohists, knowing such matters cannot serve as instruction, thus never speak of their existence. Is it any wonder that common folk do not believe?

Only those with true insight, who examine and test myriad methods, can verify their signs and confirm their existence—this is knowledge attainable only by the discerning. It cannot be forced. Thus, not seeing demons or immortals does not mean they do not exist in the world.

All people, wise or foolish, know their bodies possess *hun* and *po* souls. When these souls depart partially, illness follows; when they depart entirely, death ensues. If they depart partially, practitioners of the arts have methods to restrain and recall them; if they depart completely, the rites prescribe means to summon them back. These are the closest of all phenomena to us. Yet from birth to death, none have ever perceived them directly. Can one then deny their existence simply because they have not been seen?

Consider the demon of Lord Fu, who repaid kindness; the spirit of King Tang, who raged against Qi; the ghost of Shensheng, who spoke through a fox; the specter of Du Bo, who exacted vengeance on King Xuan of Zhou; Pengsheng, who took the form of a black boar; Ruyi, who borrowed the body of a gray dog; Guan Fu, who guarded the field of Tian Ben; the descent of Rushou at the mourning of Duke Jian of Yan; the sojourn of the Marquis of Luan in a commoner’s home; the discourse of Su Jiang on weft prophecies; the writings of filial sons; the words of the Divine Lord to Emperor Wu; and the service of Yang Shi in the court of Wu. Accounts of demons and spirits fill the bamboo and silk records, so numerous they cannot all be recounted. Yet the blind still deny their existence—how much more so the rare and seldom-heard-of matter of immortality? To demand absolute belief in such things is like expecting a mosquito to carry a mountain or a frog in a well to discourse on the sea.

Common folk, having never seen dragons, unicorns, *luan* birds, or phoenixes, claim such creatures do not exist, dismissing them as empty auspicious omens invented by the ancients to encourage rulers to unceasing self-improvement in hopes of attracting such treasures. How, then, can they be made to believe in immortals?

When Liu Xiang failed to transmute gold, people concluded that Su Qin’s strange conduct and fondness for propagating emptiness meant his *Arrayed Traditions of Immortals* was also a fabrication. Alas! This is like discarding a foot-long luminous pearl because of a tiny flaw, or casting aside a priceless pure hook because of an ant-nose sword. It is not the far-reaching insight of Jing He or the true discernment of Feng Hu. This is why Zhu Gong was filled with sorrow and Xue Zhu sighed endlessly.

The methods for transmuting gold are all found in the *Collected Writings on Divine Immortals*. The Prince of Huainan copied them out to create the *Hongbao* and *Pillow Book*. Though the texts exist, their essentials are kept secret, requiring oral instructions and on-the-spot explanations before they can be practiced. Moreover, the ingredients used often have altered names, making them impossible to identify and use directly. Liu Xiang’s father, Liu De, obtained these books while overseeing the Prince of Huainan’s imprisonment. Liu Xiang himself had no teacher in the Daoist arts; he merely chanced upon these texts and assumed their meaning was fully contained on the page. Thus, his failure to transmute gold was inevitable.

As for his *Arrayed Traditions of Immortals*, he compiled it from the Qin Grand Historian’s records and the *Book of Han*, or from what he had personally witnessed—it was no fabrication. Even madmen’s ramblings and children’s rhymes are sometimes heeded by sages, and the words of woodcutters may not be discarded. One gathers *feng* and *fei* without rejecting their roots—how can a single error in a hundred considerations lead one to dismiss the classics entirely? Just as one does not deny the great brilliance of the celestial bodies because the sun and moon sometimes eclipse, so too should one not reject the possibility of immortality.

Foreigners make crystal bowls by combining five kinds of ash, and today in Jiaozhi and Guang many have mastered this method and cast them. Yet when told this, common folk refuse to believe it, insisting that crystal is a natural substance, a kind of jade or stone. How, then, could they accept that naturally occurring gold might be artificially produced? Fools do not believe that red lead and ceruse are made from transformed lead, nor that mules and *ju* are born of donkeys and horses. They claim each thing has its own kind—how much more so for matters difficult to comprehend!

The less one sees, the more one finds strange—this is the way of the world.

Indeed, how true are these words! Though the matter concerns the brilliance of heaven, people dwell beneath an upturned steamer—how could they recognize the profound truth?

*Responding to the Common, Part Three*

Someone objects: "That Lao Peng exists among men is like the pine and cypress among trees—both are endowed by nature. How can this be learned?"

The *Baopuzi* replies: "Of all things shaped by the potter’s art and the transformations of creation, none is more numinous than man. Those who grasp its surface can command the myriad things; those who penetrate its depths can attain long life and enduring vision. Knowing that the supreme medicines extend years, they ingest those medicines to seek immortality. Knowing that cranes and tortoises enjoy remote longevity, they emulate their guiding and pulling to increase their years. Now, the pine and cypress differ from other trees in their branches and leaves, and the crane and tortoise differ from other creatures in their form and appearance. Yet Lao Peng remains but a man—no different in kind—yet his life alone is long. This is due to attaining the Dao, not to nature. Other trees cannot imitate the pine and cypress, and other creatures cannot learn from the crane and tortoise; thus, they are short-lived and perish. If a man possesses wisdom and can cultivate the Dao of Lao Peng, he may achieve the same merit.

If one claims there are no immortals in the world, then consider this: the records of former sages list nearly a thousand, each with surname and given name, each with deeds and origins—these are no empty words. If one claims they were all uniquely endowed with exceptional qi, then know that their transmission all involved teachers and the ingestion of medicines; it was not inborn knowledge. If the arts of the Dao cannot be learned, then how does one account for the transformations—swallowing swords, spitting fire, sitting yet vanishing, standing yet disappearing, summoning clouds, raising mists, calling forth insects and snakes, assembling fish and turtles, instantly turning thirty-six stones into water, dissolving jade into paste, liquefying gold into broth, entering the depths without drowning, approaching blades without injury? The arts of illusion number over nine hundred, and when tested and practiced, none fail to take effect. Why, then, refuse to believe that immortality can be attained?

The Dao of immortality is slow to mature and entails many prohibitions. Without transcendent aspirations and steadfast resolve, one cannot uphold it. Some may harbor slight interest but waver in doubt, abandoning the path midway, and thus conclude that the Dao of immortality and long life are truly unattainable. The *Scriptures of the Immortals* say: 'Ingest the elixir, guard the One, and you shall unite with heaven. Return the essence, embrace the embryo, and your life shall be without limit.' These are the essential words of the supreme Dao.

Even among the gentlemen of the common folk, there are those who inwardly do not betray their hearts, outwardly do not shame their shadows, above do not deceive heaven, and below do not break their word. How much more so for the true persons of antiquity? Would they fabricate empty texts to deceive future generations with matters that are certainly unattainable? If one lacks the destiny for it, they will never believe—how can they be forced to do so?

Someone objects: "The long life of cranes and tortoises is but an empty saying of the world. Who has accompanied these creatures from beginning to end to know this?"

The *Baopuzi* replies: "If one grasps the essentials, then what lies beyond the eight directions is as clear as the palm of one’s hand, and the events of a hundred generations past are as if occurring in the same moment. One need not wait for them to be in the courtyard or within sight to know them."

The *Yufang Ji* states: "The thousand-year-old tortoise displays all five colors, and the two bones atop its head rise like horns, floating above lotus leaves or beneath clusters of milfoil. At times, white clouds coil and swirl above it. The thousand-year-old crane cries in accordance with the seasons and can perch upon trees—those not yet a thousand years old will never alight upon branches. Their plumage is pure white, and their brains have turned entirely vermilion; thus, upon seeing them, one may know their age at once. Yet creatures that have grown old are often wise, and most conceal themselves in distant, secluded places, so few people ever encounter them.

According to the *Yufang Ji* and the *Changyu Jing*, not only these two creatures possess such longevity. It is said that the thousand-year-old pine tree sprouts branches on all four sides, while its crown ceases to grow, appearing from a distance like an inverted canopy. Within it, one may find beings resembling green oxen, green sheep, green dogs, or green humans—all of which live for a thousand years. It is further said that serpents possess boundless longevity; macaques live eight hundred years before transforming into apes, and apes live five hundred years before becoming *jue*. The toad lives three thousand years, the qilin two thousand, and the *tenghuang* horse and *jiguang* beast each live three thousand years. The thousand-year bird and ten-thousand-year fowl both have human faces and avian bodies, their lifespans matching their names. Tigers, deer, and hares all live a thousand years; when they reach five hundred, their fur turns white, and those that live five hundred years gain the power of transformation. Foxes, jackals, and wolves live eight hundred years; at five hundred, they skillfully assume human form. Mice live three hundred years; at a hundred, their fur turns white, and they gain the ability to lean upon humans and divine, known as *Zhong*—able to foretell auspicious and inauspicious events for the year and matters a thousand *li* away. Such examples cannot all be recorded here, but those of broad learning can name things upon encountering them, and those of extensive knowledge suffer no confusion in principle. Why must one constantly keep company with tortoises and cranes to know these things? If one fails to recognize creatures, even the plants, trees, birds, and beasts in one’s own garden or fields may remain unknown—how much more so for those beyond?"

How extraordinary indeed! The *Records of the Grand Historian*’s "Biography of *Xuan* and *Fang*" states that in the region between the Yangtze and Huai Rivers, when villagers were children, they would prop up their beds with *xuan*—and even after death, their families would move the bed while the *xuan* remained alive, living thus for no fewer than fifty or sixty years. To go so long without eating or drinking and yet not die is a difference far beyond that of ordinary creatures—why then doubt those said to live a thousand years? Do not the immortals’ methods of emulating the *xuan*’s breath have their basis?

Thus, Chen Zhonggong of Yingchuan, the venerable scholar and Grand Administrator of Taiqiu, was a man of rigorous discourse. In his *Record of Strange Tales*, he wrote of a man from his commandery, Zhang Guangding, who during a time of chaos often took refuge in the wilderness. He had a daughter of four who could not walk long distances and could not be carried—calculating that abandoning her would surely lead to starvation, he did not wish her bones to be left exposed. At the edge of the village stood an ancient, massive tomb whose summit already bore a hole. He placed her in a vessel and lowered her into the tomb, leaving her with several months’ worth of dried rice and water before departing. When the world at last grew peaceful, three years later, Guangding returned to his hometown and wished to retrieve the bones of the daughter he had abandoned in the tomb to rebury them properly. When he went to look, the girl was still sitting in the tomb, and upon seeing her parents, she recognized them and rejoiced. Her parents, at first, feared she was a ghost. Drawing near, they realized she was not dead. When asked how she had obtained food, the girl said that when her provisions had first run out, she grew very hungry. Then she saw a creature at the corner of the tomb stretching its neck and swallowing *qi*. She tried imitating it and found she was no longer hungry. Days and months passed in this way until the present. The clothes and bedding her parents had left remained in the tomb, untouched and unspoiled, so she suffered no cold. Guangding then searched for the creature the girl had described and found it was merely a large *xuan*. When the girl first ate grain again, her stomach ached and she retched, but after some time, she grew accustomed to it. This further confirms that the *xuan* possesses a method of immortality, and those who practice the Dao may emulate it to attain the same longevity as the *xuan*.

Sima Qian and Zhonggong were not men of reckless speech. The creatures of the world—birds and beasts—are many indeed.

The ancients singled out these two creatures alone to illustrate that they possess qualities distinct from all others. Observing even one is enough to awaken understanding.

Someone may object: "The *xuan* can hibernate underground, and the crane can soar in the heavens. If one were to hibernate for but a moment or fly for an instant, one could not even match their longevity—how then could one learn from them?"

The *Baopuzi* replies: Many creatures can hibernate, and countless birds can fly. Yet only the *xuan* and the crane are cited for their longevity, for their immortality does not arise from hibernation or flight. Thus, the true adept need only emulate their *daoyin* to prolong life and follow their method of consuming *qi* to abstain from grain—without learning their underground hibernation or celestial flight. Those who attain the Dao can ascend to the clouds above or conceal themselves in the rivers and seas below. Thus, Xiao Shi soared with the phoenix through the void, and Qin Gao rode the crimson carp into the deep abyss—such are the proofs. How could this be mere momentary hibernation or fleeting flight?

Dragons, serpents, jiao, chi, monkeys, *ju*, *tuo*, and *li* can all fast through the winter yet grow plumper than when they feed. None have grasped their method. Moreover, many creatures surpass humans in singular virtues—not just the *xuan* and crane. Thus, Taihao learned to weave nets from the spider, Jintian observed the wild goose to regulate the seasons, Emperor Xuan awaited the phoenix’s song to tune the pitch pipes, Tang Yao studied the *mingjia* to mark the months’ end, Gui knew the past by watching the magpie, the Fish Earl discerned floods and droughts by *qi*, the mayfly recognized hidden springs, the white wolf foretold the rise of the Yin, and the *yuzhuo* heralded the Zhou’s prosperity. That the *xuan* and crane alone understand nurturing life is hardly strange.

Furthermore, the immortal scriptures teach hundreds of methods for abiding long in the world—only the pace and complexity vary. Not all need imitate the *xuan* and crane.

Someone may ask: "If one could truly attain immortality through learning, rising above the clouds and forsaking the world, abandoning ancestral rites—would the ancestors not know they go unfed?"

The *Baopuzi* replies: To preserve the body unharmed is called perfect filial piety—how much greater, then, to attain the Dao, achieve long life, and endure beyond heaven and earth, surpassing mere preservation of the body? If one could ascend the void, tread the radiant clouds, ride the rainbow canopy, feast on morning mist, drink the pure essence of *xuanhuang*, sip jade nectar and golden broth, eat crimson mushrooms and jade herbs, dwell in palaces of jasper and chambers of jade, and wander freely through the Great Purity—would the ancestors not share in this glory? One might then assist the Five Emperors, oversee the hundred spirits, attain rank without seeking it, savor celestial delicacies, command the forces of Fengdu, wield awe over the pillars of the earth. If one truly grasps the Dao and comprehends its wonders, who would go hungry?

The highest attainment in the Dao was none other than Boyang. Boyang had a son named Zong, who served as a general in Wei, rendered meritorious service, and was enfeoffed at Duangan. Thus, those who study immortality today all have descendants to continue ancestral sacrifices—why should they sever the line?

Someone may ask: "Those who attain the Dao master breath control and dietary arts, hear across a thousand *li* with covered ears, and see the future with closed eyes. Some ride dragons instead of fine steeds, abandon the Middle Kingdom for Penglai and Yingzhou, linger among the common folk, or wander freely among people—why do they not simply vanish into the void? Their aspirations are the same, yet their departures differ. Why?"

The *Baopuzi* replies: I have heard from my teachers that immortals may ascend to heaven or remain on earth—both paths lead to long life, and their choice of abode depends on personal preference. Moreover, the methods of consuming the elixir of returned cinnabar and golden liquor vary: if one wishes to remain in the world, one takes half the dose and sets the rest aside; if one later seeks to ascend to heaven, one consumes the remainder. Once the matter of immortality is settled, there is no fear of sudden demise. If one merely wishes to roam the earth or enter famous mountains, what cause for concern remains?

Pengzu said that heaven is filled with exalted officials and great spirits, while new immortals hold lowly positions with many duties—hence the toil is not worth serving in heaven, and they remain among people for over eight hundred years. He also said that some ancient immortals grew feathers and wings, transformed, and flew away, losing their human form to assume other shapes—like the sparrow becoming a clam or the pheasant turning into a *shen*—which is not the human way.

The human way is to eat rich foods, wear fine clothes, engage in yin and yang, hold office, and enjoy keen senses, strong bones, radiant complexion, and ageless vigor. To live long, sit where one pleases, and remain untouched by cold, heat, wind, or damp, unharmed by malevolent spirits or baleful *qi*, unscathed by weapons or poisons, and unaffected by sorrow, joy, slander, or praise—this is true nobility. To abandon wife and children, dwell alone in the mountains and marshes, cut off from human affairs, and live like a stone among trees is not worthy of praise.

In the past, Master Anqi, Lord Longmei, Ning, and Yang Yin all achieved long life by consuming half a dose of golden liquor. They remained in the world for nearly a thousand years before departing. To speak earnestly, those who seek long life simply cherish what they desire today.

Those who seek the Dao are not eager to ascend to emptiness or take flight as if it were superior to remaining on earth. If one is fortunate enough to stay at home and not die, why must one hasten to ascend to heaven? If attaining immortality means no longer attending to worldly affairs, it is but a single matter. The words of Peng Zu were merely accommodating to human sentiment.

Some say: "We people of today do not understand the principles of long life—how did the ancients alone come to know them?" The *Baopuzi* replies: This is the narrow talk of the ignorant and benighted, not the concern of those of broad understanding. The lofty scholar’s thoughts reach far and wide, naturally profound and unobstructed, yet it is difficult to infer the distant concerns of divine beings from the shallow sentiments of the vulgar. Now, as with the methods of astrologers—[text missing]—they pace the waxing and waning of the Seven Governors, discuss past transgressions and encroachments, examine rise and decline in what is to come, gaze upward to discern the omens of clouds and celestial phenomena, and look downward to determine the auspiciousness or inauspiciousness of terrestrial signs. They manipulate the Three Divination Sticks to determine the success or failure of military campaigns, apply the Nine Talismans to foretell the fortunes and misfortunes of regions, and through a single calculation of addition and subtraction, fathom the conditions of spirits and gods. They interweave the Six Emotions to assess the boundless good and ill—such principles have roots that can be investigated, and their patterns can be sought. Yet even those of mediocre talent and limited capacity cannot master them, instead exhausting their sharp minds on dregs, unable to plumb their profound subtleties.

Consider the crude craft of carving tenons and mortises—even Wheelwright Bian possessed an untransmittable marvel. Or the humble skill of catching cicadas—yet the hunchbacked old man achieved a mastery bordering on the divine. Such excellence lies in the person’s utmost refinement. How much more so with the Way of immortality, whose subtleties run deep and whose roots are not easily grasped? Even the likes of Master Red Pine and Wang Qiao, though they attained its effects, may not have fully understood its underlying principles—how much less so ordinary people? Yet its methods can be learned, and so the ancients recorded and transmitted them for those of discernment.

If one’s mind comprehends and intention aligns, one may trust and cultivate it. But if doubt lingers in the heart, it is merely because one’s fate is not meant to be. Why question why the ancients alone understood this while you alone do not? Indeed, I now know that immortality can be attained. I can cease taking grain and abstain from food. I can preserve the Flowing Pearl and take flight.

The pursuit of alchemical gold and silver can be achieved, yet if you demand of me their fundamental principles, I must confess I truly do not know them. If the world insists that whatever cannot be grasped by thought must not exist, then few things in this world would remain. Thus, Laozi spoke of using a cat’s head to cure rat bites and a woodpecker’s beak to prevent tooth decay—these too are methods one may seek. Yet the crab’s transformation into lacquer or hemp’s decay into wine cannot be deduced by reason. With myriad phenomena so diverse, how can the mind fathom them all?

Suppose one suffers from a grave illness and requires potent medicine but refuses to take it until understanding why Shennong and Qibo chose this herb for this ailment—would they not perish? Some say life and death are fated, longevity predetermined, and no medicine can alter them. Once a finger is severed, reattaching it is impossible; once blood is spilled, swallowing it brings no benefit. How much less could consuming the strange elixirs of pine and cypress extend one’s fleeting years? This is far from the truth.

The *Baopuzi* states: If such arguments held, then only identical substances could bring benefit. Yet a severed finger and spilled blood were once part of the same body—why, then, can the one not be reattached and the other not be ingested? I have witnessed people use snake-oil salve to reconnect severed fingers and swap a chicken’s foot for a duck’s with beans—such benefits from strange substances cannot be denied. If you claim no reliance on external things, then you should pound flesh to mend bones for sword wounds or boil skin and hair to cure baldness. Yet water and soil, though not of the same substance as plants, sustain their growth; the five grains, though not born of humans, are essential to life. Fat is not fire’s kind, nor is water the fish’s kin, yet when fat is exhausted, fire dies, and when water dries, fish perish. Cut down a tree, and the mistletoe withers; mow the grass, and dodder wilts. When river crabs do not return, oysters decay; when mulberry trees are felled, borers perish. From these, one may grasp the truth.

Gold and jade placed in the nine orifices preserve the dead from decay; salt brine applied to flesh and marrow keeps dried meats from rotting. How much more so when substances that nourish the body and extend life are taken into oneself—why marvel that they grant longevity?

Some object: The formulas in books on immortality seem plausible but are likely fabrications by meddlesome enthusiasts, not teachings from the hands of the Yellow Emperor or Laozi, nor witnessed by Master Red Pine or Wang Qiao. The *Baopuzi* replies: If such were the case, they would not prove effective. Yet even minor techniques never fail. I have seen people use a *fangzhu* vessel to draw water from the evening moon, a burning mirror to kindle fire from the morning sun, dissolve forms into nothingness, alter appearances into strange beings, toss a headscarf to the ground and make it run like a hare, or stitch a red sash and make it slither like a snake. Fruits ripen in an instant, fish and dragons leap in basins—all as described. If such arts exist, how can the Way of long life be false?

The *Records of the Grand Historian* and *Book of Han* recount how Luan Da first met Emperor Wu by making chess pieces move and collide on their own, while the *Book of Later Han* records Wei Shang’s ability to vanish while sitting and reappear while standing, and Zhang Kai’s power to summon clouds and raise mists—all attested by reliable historians. These arts belong to the realm of immortals; their authenticity cannot be denied. If the *Records* are trustworthy, why should the Way of long life be any different?

Someone asks: Should those who seek the Dao first establish merit and virtue? Is this correct? The *Baopuzi* answers: Indeed. According to the middle chapter of the *Jade Bell Classic*, the highest merit lies in saving others from peril and sparing them from disaster, followed by curing illness to prevent unjust death. Those who seek immortality must take loyalty, filial piety, harmony, benevolence, and trust as their foundation. Without cultivating virtue but merely pursuing the mysterious Dao, it is of no benefit.

The gods who oversee destiny observe human transgressions. For major wrongdoings, the Director of Destiny seizes a *ji* (twelve-year cycle); for minor faults, it seizes a *suan* (one-year cycle), the severity determining the amount taken. A person’s lifespan is predetermined: those endowed with abundant destiny find their cycles difficult to exhaust and die late, while those with meager endowment who commit many offenses see their cycles swiftly depleted and die early.

It is also said: To become an earthly immortal, one must perform three hundred good deeds; to become a heavenly immortal, one thousand two hundred. If one has performed one thousand one hundred and ninety-nine good deeds but suddenly commits a single evil act, all previous merits are lost, and the count must begin anew. Thus, goodness need not be great, nor evil slight. Even if one commits no evil, merely speaking of past deeds or demanding repayment for charity nullifies that single good act—though not all merits are lost.

Moreover, if one’s accumulated good deeds are incomplete, even taking immortality elixirs is of no use. If one neither takes elixirs nor performs good deeds, though one may not attain immortality, one can at least avoid sudden death. I suspect that even Peng Zu and his kind lacked sufficient merit, which is why they could not ascend to heaven.

**On the Golden Elixir**
The *Baopuzi* states: I have examined books on nurturing life and collected methods for prolonging existence, having perused thousands of scrolls. None fail to regard the Reverted Elixir and Golden Liquid as the ultimate essentials. Indeed, these two are the pinnacle of the immortal’s path. If consuming them does not lead to immortality, then no immortals have ever existed.

In former times, when the central states fell into chaos, none did not scatter and flee in all directions. I wandered through the regions of Xu, Yu, Jing, Xiang, Jiang, and Guang, traversing several provinces, and encountered hundreds of wandering Daoist practitioners. Some I had long heard of, their names as distant as clouds and sun, yet they were all alike in their resemblance. Their knowledge and insights, whether profound or shallow, were insufficient to surpass one another. Though each possessed dozens of scrolls, they could not fully comprehend them—merely copying errors from one another. At times, some knew methods for circulating *qi* or abstaining from grains, or techniques for consuming various herbs and woods, and their manuals were roughly uniform in content. Yet none lacked the *Daoji Jing*, which they regarded as the utmost secret, claiming it was composed by Yin Xi. I told them: "This was written by Wang Tu, a military supervisor of the Wei dynasty—it is not the work of an ancient." Wang Tu, ignorant of the great elixir, sought immortality through *qi*-circulation and chamber practices, composing the *Daoji Jing* under the delusion that the Dao was fully encompassed therein. This is a grave misguidance of others.

I occasionally questioned these Daoists about matters of divine elixirs, golden liquids, the *Sanhuang Wen* for summoning celestial and terrestrial spirits, yet not one among them knew of them. Most boasted and praised themselves, deceiving others by claiming long lifespans or past travels with immortals. Those whose claims could be fully verified were exceedingly few. Some had vaguely heard of the golden elixir but insisted it could no longer be obtained, asserting that only immortals of the distant past had mastered it. Others possessed fragments of esoteric teachings but lacked the true scriptures, or held miscellaneous elixir formulas, believing these exhausted the methods of alchemy.

In the past, Zuo Yuanfang of Tianzhushan concentrated his thoughts, and a divine being bestowed upon him the *Jindan Xianjing*. Fleeing the chaos of the Han’s end, he had no time to compound the elixir and instead crossed the river to Jiangdong, intending to seek a renowned mountain to cultivate this Way. My great-uncle, the Immortal Master, received the teachings from Yuanfang, including the *Taiqing Danjing* in three scrolls, the *Jiuding Danjing* in one scroll, and the *Jinye Danjing* in one scroll. My teacher, Master Zheng, was a disciple of my great-uncle, the Immortal Master, and from him I also received these texts. As my family was poor and unable to purchase the ingredients, I personally served him, sweeping and attending for many years, until at last I established an altar on Maji Mountain, took vows, and received the transmission, including all the oral instructions not recorded in writing. These texts were previously unknown in Jiangdong; they originated with Zuo Yuanfang, who transmitted them to my great-uncle, who passed them to Master Zheng, who in turn bestowed them upon me. Thus, no other Daoists knew of them.

Yet I have received these teachings for over twenty years now, possessing no wealth—no grain, no coin—to pursue them. I can only sigh in vain. Those who hoard gold filling chests or amass fortunes like mountains remain ignorant of this deathless method. Even if they were to hear of it, not one in ten thousand would believe. Just as one who drinks jade nectar finds common broth insipid, or one who gazes upon Kunlun finds hills and mounds base, so too does one who beholds the Way of the golden elixir lose all desire to regard lesser manuals. Yet the great elixir is ultimately difficult to obtain, and one must for now rely on minor medicines to sustain oneself. Though consuming other medicines by the bushel may yield slight benefits, they can never grant true longevity.

Thus, the oral instructions of Laozi say: "Without obtaining the Reverted Elixir or Golden Liquid, you labor in vain." Even the five grains sustain life—one who obtains them lives, while one deprived of them dies. How much more so the divine medicines of the highest grade? Their benefits surpass the five grains ten thousandfold. The golden elixir, when refined, grows ever more wondrous with time. Gold, cast into fire a hundred times, does not melt; buried, it does not decay through the ages. Consuming these two medicines refines the body, enabling one to avoid aging and death. This is akin to borrowing external substances to fortify oneself—like fat nourishing a flame to keep it from extinguishing, or verdigris coating wood to prevent rot when submerged. Yet the golden elixir, entering the body and permeating the meridians and vessels, far surpasses such external applications.

Most in the world do not believe in the supreme Way, and such skeptics are everywhere. Yet if by chance there arises one who is earnestly devoted but never encounters this method or meets a renowned teacher, they will never learn of this wondrous matter in the world. I now briefly outline the essentials of the golden elixir to share with those of like aspiration. They must diligently seek it and not cling to shallow, common formulas, believing these sufficient to transcend the world. Those who fail to encounter it should instead devote themselves wholeheartedly to the boundless marvels. Upon hearing its teachings, they will surely recognize how one emerges from stagnant puddles to float upon the vast sea, turns from fireflies to face the sun and moon, perceives the crudeness of cloth drums beside thunder, or grasps the insignificance of minnows beside the great whale. Once they understand the futility of their former pursuits, how can they persist in seeking ascent through inferior medicines? It would be no different from chasing the swift wind on a lame donkey or crossing a great river in a wicker boat.

There are many minor elixir formulas, but their effects vary in depth and potency, with no comparison between the superior and inferior—just as a single brew of wine cannot rival the richness of nine fermentations. Yet even the lesser elixirs far surpass the finest herbs and woods. All common herbs and woods turn to ash when burned, but cinnabar, when refined, becomes mercury, which upon further transformation reverts to cinnabar—its distance from ordinary plants and trees is vast. Only the divine immortals perceive this principle. How immeasurably distant are they from the common folk?

People of the world, knowing little, find much strange. Some, unaware that mercury derives from cinnabar, refuse to believe it, asking: "Cinnabar is red—how can it become this white substance?" Others say: "Cinnabar is a stone. When other stones are burned, they turn to ash—why does cinnabar alone not burn to cinders?" If such simple matters remain incomprehensible, is it any wonder they laugh at the Way of immortality? The true persons of antiquity, pitying those of later ages who might be taught, devised methods in intricate detail, wishing to deliver them from the calamity of death. Their words were most profound, yet the common people persist in disbelief, dismissing them as empty rhetoric. If these were empty words, how could the nine transformations and nine changes, completed in precise days as described, come to pass? The true persons’ understanding of this cannot be sought through ordinary, shallow thinking.

From my youth, I loved the arts of the Dao, traveling on foot to seek instruction, unafraid of peril or distance. Every unusual teaching I heard brought me joy, and though I faced ridicule, I did not grieve.

Those who know the future will not surpass the present—this is why I have written this to show the discerning. How could I merely esteem the strange and embellish empty words, wishing only to circulate books in the world and win the trust of the vulgar? When flourishing yang cannot revive withered wood, and the highest wisdom cannot transform the lowest ignorance, books are transmitted for the enlightened to read, and affairs are valued by the wise to know. If a farmer were given a vermilion bow to scare birds, or a southern barbarian a court robe to gather firewood, how could those who do not understand be forced to comprehend?

The people of this world, sated with food all day, may not even apply themselves to the studies of the Confucians and Mohists or cultivate the work of advancing virtue. Instead, they idle away their years in carefree wandering, pursuing only what brings honor or profit. Some chase birds and hunt beasts across the plains, some linger over cups in bustling markets, some drown in music and silk, some exhaust their strength in riding and archery, some squander their efforts in gambling. When they hear the words of the supreme Way, they act as if drunk; when they see the discourse on the great Dao, they yawn and doze. They neglect the cultivation of their bodies, hastening toward death, yet refuse to seek the methods of nurturing life. They willingly subject themselves to the torments of cutting, boiling, emaciation, and exhaustion. But those who possess the Way treasure and guard their knowledge, asking nothing of others—how could they be willing to force their teachings upon them?

The common saying goes that if long life were attainable, the wealthy and noble of antiquity would have already obtained it, and since none did, such a Way does not exist. Yet they do not realize that the wealthy and noble of old were no different from those of today—all disbelieved and did not seek it. If there truly were an immortal Way in the world, would it not again be mocked by those who attain it? The sun and moon cannot illuminate everything; how can the human heart alone be trusted?

The *Baopuzi* states: According to the *Huangdi jiuding shendan jing* (Scripture of the Nine Cauldrons’ Divine Elixirs of the Yellow Emperor), the Yellow Emperor consumed them and thus ascended as an immortal. It also says that while breathing exercises, guiding and pulling, and consuming herbal medicines may extend life, they cannot prevent death. Ingesting divine elixirs grants boundless longevity, allowing one to endure with heaven and earth, ride clouds, and mount dragons, ascending and descending the Great Clarity. The Yellow Emperor transmitted this to Master Xuanzhi, warning him: "This Way is most weighty; it must be entrusted only to the worthy. If one is unfit, even if their jade were piled as high as a mountain, do not reveal this Way to them." The recipient must cast a golden figurine and golden fish into the eastern flowing waters as a pact, swearing a blood oath. Without the bone of immortality, one cannot behold this Way.

To compound the elixir, one must go to a famous mountain, a place without people, with no more than three companions. First, fast for a hundred days, bathe in the five fragrant waters, and maintain utmost purity. Avoid defilement and association with the vulgar, and do not let those who disbelieve in the Way know of it. If they slander the divine medicine, the elixir will not form. Once completed, it can make an entire household immortal, not just oneself.

The people of this world do not compound divine elixirs but instead trust in herbal medicines. Yet herbs and plants rot when buried and burn to ash when burned—they cannot preserve themselves, so how can they preserve humans? The nine elixirs are the essence of long life, not something ordinary people should hear or see. The teeming masses, foolish and ignorant, know only to covet wealth and honor—are they not merely walking corpses?

When compounding the elixirs, one must perform sacrifices according to the illustrated methods in a separate scroll. The first elixir is called *Danhua* (Cinnabar Splendor). First, refine *xuanhuang* (mysterious yellow), using realgar water, *bin* stone water, a bowl of mercury, halite, brine, *bin* stone, oyster shell, red clay, talc, and lead powder, each in quantities of several dozen catties. Seal with *liuyi* mud and fire for thirty-six days. Consuming it for seven days grants immortality. It can also be formed into *xuangao* (mysterious paste) pills. Placing this elixir over a fierce fire briefly turns it into gold. Mixing 240 *zhu* of it with a hundred catties of mercury and firing it also produces gold. If the gold forms, the elixir is complete; if not, reseal the medicine and fire it again for the same number of days—it will invariably succeed.

The second elixir is called *Shendan* (Divine Elixir), also known as *Shenfu* (Divine Talisman). Consuming it for a hundred days grants immortality. One can walk on water by applying this elixir to the soles of the feet. Ingesting three *daogui* of it eliminates the Three Corpses, Nine Worms, and all illnesses.

The third elixir is called *Shendan* (Divine Elixir). Consuming one *daogui* for a hundred days grants immortality. Feeding it to livestock also renders them immortal. It repels weapons, and after a hundred days of consumption, immortals, jade maidens, and mountain and river spirits will attend you, appearing in human form.

The fourth elixir is called *Huandan* (Returning Elixir). Consuming one *daogui* for a hundred days grants immortality. Vermilion birds and phoenixes will hover above you, and jade maidens will approach. Mixing one *daogui* with a catty of mercury and firing it instantly produces gold. Applying this elixir to money or objects causes them to return the same day. Writing with this elixir on a mortal’s eyes causes all spirits to flee.

The fifth elixir is called *Erdan* (Nourishing Elixir). Consuming it for thirty days grants immortality. Spirits will attend you, and jade maidens will appear before you.

The sixth elixir is called *Liandan* (Refining Elixir). Consuming it for ten days grants immortality. Mixing it with mercury and firing it also produces gold.

The seventh elixir is called *Roudan* (Softening Elixir). Consuming one *daogui* for a hundred days grants immortality. Mixing it with the juice of *quepen* and consuming it allows even a ninety-year-old man to father children. Firing it with *jingong* (lead) instantly produces gold.

The eighth elixir is called *Fudan* (Subduing Elixir). Consuming it grants immortality the same day. Shaped like a jujube pit [text missing], it deters bandits, tigers, and wolves, who dare not approach.
