The ninth elixir is called *Handan* (Cold Elixir). Consuming one *daogui* for a hundred days grants immortality. Immortal boys and girls will attend you, and you will be able to fly lightly without wings.

Of these nine elixirs, obtaining even one grants immortality; it is not necessary to compound all of them—it depends on one’s preference. Those who consume the nine elixirs may ascend to heaven if they wish, or remain among humans as they please. They can come and go freely, and none can harm them.

The *Baopuzi* states: There is also the *Taiqing shendan* (Great Clarity Divine Elixir), whose method originates from the Primordial Lord, the teacher of Laozi. The *Taiging guantian jing* (Great Clarity Scripture of Observing Heaven) has nine scrolls. The upper three cannot be taught; the middle three are unworthy of transmission in this world and should be sunk beneath the three springs. The lower three scrolls are precisely the elixir scriptures, comprising three volumes: upper, middle, and lower.

The Primordial Lord was a great immortal who could harmonize yin and yang, command spirits, summon wind and rain, and ride nine dragons and twelve white tigers. All the immortals under heaven practiced under him.

Those who have attained it still claim their achievement comes from studying the Dao and ingesting elixirs—not from natural endowment. How much less can ordinary people expect otherwise? The scriptures state: the superior practitioner who obtains the Dao ascends to become a celestial official; the middling practitioner who obtains the Dao dwells upon Kunlun; the inferior practitioner who obtains the Dao enjoys longevity in the world. Yet the ignorant masses do not believe, dismissing such teachings as empty words. From dawn to dusk, they engage only in actions that hasten death, never seeking life—how could Heaven force life upon them? Ordinary people know only fine food, fine clothing, sensual pleasures, and wealth, indulging their desires without restraint until they suddenly perish. Take care never to speak of divine elixirs to such people, lest they mock the Dao and slander the true transmission. If the elixir scriptures do not find their proper recipient, misfortune will surely befall the bearer.

If one encounters a person of sincere faith, one may share a portion of the completed elixir, but never lightly transmit the formula. What need has one who understands this Dao for kings and marquises? Once the divine elixir is complete, it not only grants longevity but can also be used to create yellow gold. When the gold is made, take one hundred *jin* and first perform a grand offering—this ritual has its own separate method in one scroll, distinct from the Nine Cauldrons offering.

The offering should be apportioned as follows, with each portion labeled and recorded:
- Heaven: twenty *jin*
- Sun and Moon: five *jin* each
- Northern Dipper: eight *jin*
- Great Unity: eight *jin*
- Well: five *jin*
- Stove: five *jin*
- River Earl: twelve *jin*
- Earth Altar: five *jin*

To the Gate Spirits, Neighborhood Spirits, and Pure Lords, five *jin* each—altogether eighty-eight *jin*. The remaining twelve *jin* should be placed in a fine leather pouch. On an auspicious day, go to a bustling market, and at the height of its activity, silently cast it among the crowd before departing without looking back. Only when one has used more than one hundred *jin* may one freely employ the gold. If one does not first offer gold to the spirits, one will surely incur misfortune.

It is also said: The Dao of longevity does not lie in sacrifices to spirits, nor in guiding and pulling or bending and stretching. The essence of ascending as an immortal lies in the divine elixir. To know this is not easy; to practice it is truly difficult. If you can refine it, you may attain enduring existence.

In recent times, during the late Han, Lord Yin of Xinye refined this Supreme Clarity Elixir and achieved immortality. He was originally a scholar, gifted in thought and skilled in composing poetry, as well as in writing eulogies and prefaces for elixir scriptures. He recorded the beginnings of his Daoist studies, his reception of his teacher’s teachings, and all he knew of those who had attained immortality—over forty individuals—with great clarity.

Refining this Supreme Clarity Elixir is somewhat more difficult than the Nine Cauldrons, yet it is the method for ascending to heaven in broad daylight. When compounding it, one must first prepare the Flower Pool, Red Salt, Gen Snow, Mysterious White, and Flying Talisman, as well as the Three-and-Five Spirit Water, before kindling the fire.

The divine elixir, once transmuted, may be ingested; after three years, one will attain immortality.

The two-transmutation elixir, when ingested, grants immortality in two years. The three-transmutation elixir, in one year. The four-transmutation elixir, in half a year. The five-transmutation elixir, in a hundred days. The six-transmutation elixir, in forty days. The seven-transmutation elixir, in twenty days. The eight-transmutation elixir, in ten days. The nine-transmutation elixir, in three days.

If one takes the nine-transmutation elixir and places it within the divine crucible after the Summer Solstice, firing it, the crucible’s lid will suddenly blaze with radiant, five-colored divine light—this is the transmutation into the Return Elixir. Ingesting even a knife-tip’s measure will cause one to ascend to heaven in broad daylight.

As for the nine-transmutation elixir, when sealed and coated within an earthen cauldron, fired first with gentle heat and then with strong, each transmutation—from the first to the ninth—varies in duration and quantity. From this, one may discern: the fewer the transmutations, the longer the time required, for the elixir’s power is insufficient, thus necessitating more time for ingestion and delaying immortality. The greater the transmutations, the more potent the elixir’s power, thus requiring less time for ingestion and hastening immortality.

There is also the Nine Radiance Elixir, differing in method from the nine-transmutation elixir yet largely similar. Its preparation involves combining various ingredients and firing them to transmute the Five Stones—the Five Stones being cinnabar, realgar, orpiment, malachite, and magnetite. Each stone, when transmuted, yields five colors, resulting in twenty-five hues from the Five Stones. Each is weighed in taels and stored in separate vessels.

To revive one who has died within three days, take a knife-tip’s measure of the green elixir, mix it with water, and bathe the deceased. Then place another knife-tip’s measure into their mouth, and they will immediately revive.

To summon provisions, take the black elixir, mix it with water, and apply it to your left hand. Whatever you desire or speak of will manifest, allowing you to summon all things under heaven.

To conceal your form, foreknow future events, or attain ageless longevity, ingest a knife-tip’s measure of the yellow elixir, and you will immediately achieve immortality and eternal youth. You will also be able to perceive events thousands of miles away, knowing fortune and misfortune as if they were before your eyes. You will comprehend the destined prosperity, decline, lifespan, and fate—wealth or poverty—of all people. These methods are all detailed in the middle volume of the *Supreme Clarity Scripture*.

The *Master Embracing Simplicity* states: There is also the *Scripture of the Divine Elixir* in one scroll, containing five methods. It employs cinnabar, realgar, orpiment, sulfur, malachite, orpiment, magnetite, halite, *taiyi* (Grand Unity) *yuyuliang* (limonite), as well as Six-One Mud and the divine chamber. After performing rituals and offerings, the elixir is compounded in thirty-six days. It also uses the Five Emperors’ Talismans, inscribed in five colors, which grant immortality, though they are not as potent as the Supreme Clarity or Nine Cauldrons elixirs.

There is also the Min Mountains Elixir method, obtained by the Daoist Zhang Gai through deep meditation in a stone chamber on Mount Min. Its method involves smelting yellow copper to create a *fangzhu* (square vessel) to collect moonlight, covering it with mercury to capture the essence of the sun’s fire. Long-term ingestion grants immortality. If this elixir is placed in a bronze vessel with realgar, covered, and exposed to the sun for twenty days, then refined and ingested with well-flower water in the size of a small bean, the blind will regain sight, the ill will recover, white hair will turn black, and fallen teeth will regrow.

The Wuchengzi Elixir method uses *basha* (mercury ore) and mercury placed in an eight-inch copper plate, set over a charcoal-filled earthen furnace with three sides dug out. The plate is covered, and sulfur water is poured over it to maintain a mud-like consistency. After a hundred days of ingestion, one attains immortality.

The Xianmenzi Elixir method mixes three *sheng* of wine with one *jin* of elixir, exposing it to the sun for forty days. Ingesting it for a day dispels the Three Corpses and all illnesses; after three years, the Dao of immortality is achieved, and two jade maidens will attend you, ready to be commanded and to summon provisions. This elixir can also suppress malevolent spirits, ward off the baleful influences of the four directions, and protect against the misfortunes of the dead or construction disturbances. Hang it facing the afflicted area to avert harm.

There is also the Instant Elixir, which has nine variations resembling the Nine Cauldrons but is less potent. Its essential method, according to another source, involves taking orpiment and realgar, burning them, and casting the residue into a copper vessel. This vessel is then inverted over three-year-old pure bitter wine for a hundred days, during which red stalactites, several *fen* long and sometimes five-colored like *langgan* jade, form. These are buried and ingested, granting longevity. They can also be mixed with *tusi* (dodder), the root of its initial growth, which resembles *cong* (scallion). Extract its sap and blend it with this elixir; ingestion allows instantaneous transformations at will.

Alternatively, mix it with *zhucao* (vermilion grass), which resembles a small date tree, growing three to four *chi* tall with red branches and leaves. Its stem is like coral, thriving in famous mountains beneath cliffs. When cut, its sap flows like blood. Jade, the Eight Stones, gold, or silver placed within it can be shaped like clay; over time, it becomes liquid. Gold added to it is called *yuli* (jade nectar), and ingestion grants longevity.

There is also the Subduing Elixir method, which states that among the world’s waters, those named after elixirs—such as the Dan River in Nanyang—contain elixir fish. Ten days before the Summer Solstice, observe them at night: the elixir fish will float near the water’s edge, their red glow illuminating the surface like fire. Capture them, but do not take all, even if many are caught. Smear their blood on the soles of your feet to walk upon water and dwell indefinitely in the depths.

The Red Pine Master’s Elixir method involves taking the sap of thousand-year-old *lü* (creeping fig) and *lin* peach, soaking the elixir in a non-leaking vessel, sealing it, and burying it three *chi* underground for a hundred days. Then, squeeze the juice from the red fruit of the *ning* tree, mix it with the elixir, and ingest it to turn one’s face and eyes red, granting longevity. Was not the Red Child of the Central Yellow Immortal one who ingested this?

The Stone Master’s Elixir method takes fledgling birds before their feathers grow, feeds them cinnabar mixed with beef, and when their feathers turn red, slaughters them. After drying them in the shade along with their feathers, grind them into powder. Ingesting a knife-tip’s measure grants a lifespan of five hundred years.

The Kang Fengzi Elixir method uses sheep, crow, crane eggs, and sparrow blood, mixed with the juice of *shaoshi tianxiong* (aconite from Shaoshi Mountain), formed into pills, and placed inside a pigeon egg. This is then sealed in mica water for a hundred days, transforming into a red liquid. Ingesting one *ge* extends life by a hundred years; one *sheng* grants a thousand years.

The Cui Wenzi Elixir method involves placing elixir inside a duck’s belly and steaming it. Ingestion extends lifespan, and long-term use grants immortality.

The Liu Yuan Elixir method involves placing cinnabar in *xuanshui* (mysterious water) for a hundred days until it turns purple and can be held without staining the hands. Mixed with mica water, sealed in a tube, and submerged in a well for a hundred days, it transforms into a red liquid. Ingesting one *ge* grants a hundred years of life; long-term use grants immortality.

The Le Zichang Elixir method involves steaming *zengqing* (azurite) with lead in cinnabar for eighty days. Ingesting a dose the size of a small bean for three years grants immortality.

The Li Wen Elixir method involves wrapping cinnabar in white silk and boiling it in bamboo sap, naming it the "Crimson Spring Elixir." Steamed over floating water and mixed with *xuanshui* (mysterious water), ingesting one *ge* daily for a year grants immortality.

The Yin Zi Elixir method involves mixing cinnabar with mica water, sealing it tightly, and placing it in the Golden Flower Pool for a year. Upon retrieval, ingesting one *daokui* (scant measure) until a full *jin* is consumed grants five hundred years of life.

The Taiyi Zhao Fangbi Elixir method employs the Five Minerals and seals them with Six-One Mud, resembling the Nine Elixirs. It excels at reviving those who have died suddenly within three days: place one pill in the mouth of the deceased, and another mixed with sulfur, both washed down with water to reach the throat, and they will revive immediately. It is said that messengers bearing tallies will come to summon them.

The Cai Nü Elixir method involves mixing cinnabar with rabbit blood and *qi* (malt), steaming it for a hundred days. Ingesting a pill the size of a *wutong* seed, one large dose thrice daily, will summon two divine maidens to attend and serve after a hundred days.

The Ji Qiu Zi Elixir method involves mixing pure sesame oil, Hundred-Flower Wine, Dragon Ointment, and sealing it with Six-One Mud, then simmering it with chaff fire for ten days. Ingesting a pill the size of a small bean until the entire dose is consumed grants five hundred years of life.

The Mo Zi Elixir method involves heating mercury and the Five Minerals’ liquid in a copper vessel, stirring with an arsenic spatula for ten days until it reverts to cinnabar. Ingesting one *daokui* cures all illnesses; long-term use grants immortality.

The Zhang Zihe Elixir method involves sealing *qi* (malt), mercury, *zengqing* (azurite), and water together, steaming them in red millet for eighty days. Mixed with jujube paste into pills and ingested in doses the size of a large bean for a hundred days, it grants five hundred years of life.

The Qi Li Elixir method first sublimes the Five Minerals to obtain "jade dust," then combines it with cinnabar and mercury in a large copper vessel, boiling it for a hundred days. Ingesting it grants immortality. Using a hundred *jin* of lead and a hundred *daokui* of the elixir, refining them with fire produces silver. Mixing with realgar water and firing for a hundred days yields gold. If the gold is too hard, boil it in lard; if too soft, boil it in white plum.

The Yu Gui Elixir method involves mixing cinnabar with Hua Chi (Flower Pool) liquid, covering it with powdered *zengqing* (azurite) and sulfur, layering it in a bamboo tube, and steaming it in sand for fifty days. Ingesting it for a hundred days summons jade maidens, the Six Jia and Six Ding spirits, to attend and serve, granting knowledge of all affairs under heaven.

The Zhouhou Elixir method involves mixing cinnabar with Golden Flower, sealing it in a dry tile, and steaming it for eighty days. Taking a dose the size of a small bean and placing it on a plate to face the sun, its light connects with the sun. Ingesting it in such doses grants longevity. When the elixir is cast into molten realgar copper, it transforms into gold.

The Li Gong Elixir method uses true cinnabar and the liquids of the Five Minerals, each one *sheng*, mixed to a mud-like consistency and fired in a cauldron for thirty-six days. Upon retrieval, it is mixed with liquid stone sulfur. Ingesting it for ten years harmonizes one’s lifespan with heaven and earth.

The Liu Sheng Elixir method involves mixing chrysanthemum sap, *dichu* (earth paper mulberry) sap, and *chu* (ailanthus) sap with cinnabar, steaming it for thirty days. After grinding and combining, ingesting it for a year grants five hundred years of life. Old men who ingest it become youthful and unrecognizable; youths remain ageless.

The Wang Jun Elixir method involves placing *basha* (realgar) and mercury inside a chicken egg, sealing it, and having a hen incubate three eggs on a *wangxiang* (auspicious) day. Ingesting it halts aging; children who ingest it cease growing. When fed to newborn chicks and puppies, they no longer grow; the same applies to birds and beasts—this has been verified.

The Chen Sheng Elixir method involves mixing white *qi* (malt) with cinnabar, sealing it in a copper vessel, and submerging it in a well for a full year. Ingesting it eliminates hunger for years; consuming a full *jin* grants a hundred years of life.

The Han Zhong Elixir method involves simmering cinnabar with lacquered *qi* (malt) and ingesting it to extend lifespan and preserve vision. Standing in the sun, one casts no shadow. Beyond this, there are dozens more methods that cannot all be enumerated.

The *Baopuzi* states: The Golden Liquid is what the Great One ingested to attain immortality, second only to the Nine Elixirs. Its preparation requires an ancient scale, one *jin* of gold, along with *xuanming* (mysterious brightness), Dragon Ointment, Taiyi Xunshou Stone, Ice Stone, Purple Wandering Maiden, *xuanshui* (mysterious water), liquid gold-transforming stone, and cinnabar. Sealed for a hundred days, it transforms into a liquid.

The *True Scripture* states: When the Golden Liquid enters the mouth, the body turns golden. Laozi transmitted it to the Primordial Lord, who said, "This Dao is most profound, emerging but once in a hundred generations. It must be concealed in a stone chamber. Its preparation requires a hundred days of purification, during which one must not associate with the vulgar. It should be refined beside a famous mountain by an eastward-flowing stream in a separate purified chamber. After a hundred days, ingesting one *liang* grants immortality. If one does not yet wish to depart the world but desires to become an Earth-Water Immortal, purify for a hundred days and ingest half a *liang* to attain agelessness, impervious to all harm and poisons. One may then keep a wife and children, hold office, and pursue desires without restriction. To ascend to heaven, purify and abstain from desires for a year, then ingest another *liang* to achieve flight."

The method of the Golden Liquid as the mighty and victorious elixir involves taking the Golden Liquid and mercury, combining and boiling them for thirty days. Upon retrieval, store it in a yellow clay jar, seal it with Six-One Mud, and fire it intensely for sixty hours until it transforms into an elixir. Ingesting a dose the size of a small bean grants immortality. One *daokui* of this elixir can transmute a *jin* of mercury into silver. Taking a *jin* of this elixir, placing it over fire, and fanning it melts it into flowing red gold, called "Elixir Gold." Coating swords and blades with it repels weapons for ten thousand *li*. Using this Elixir Gold to make plates and bowls, and eating from them, grants longevity. Catching the liquid from the sun and moon with it is like obtaining water with a *fangzhu* (a legendary mirror). Drinking it grants immortality.

Refining the Golden Liquid with yellow earth in a Six-One Mud vessel over intense fire transmutes it entirely into gold for internal use. Further firing transforms it back into an elixir. Ingesting a dose the size of a bean allows one to enter famous mountains and great rivers as an Earth Immortal. One *daokui* of this elixir can instantly transmute mercury into silver; one *liang* of silver mixed with a *jin* of lead also becomes silver.

Following the *Golden Liquid Scripture*, cast eight *liang* of gold and silver into an eastward-flowing stream, swear an oath by drinking blood, and then receive the oral instructions. Those who steal the method and attempt to refine it without adhering to the original method will never succeed.

Only those of utmost sincerity may be given the elixir; its scripture must not be lightly transmitted, for both giver and receiver will suffer calamity. The celestial spirits observe mortals closely—this is unknown to most.

The *Baopuzi* states: The Nine Elixirs are indeed the supreme method of immortal medicines, yet their preparation requires many rare ingredients. If the four directions are unobstructed, these may be obtained in markets; if the nine regions are divided, the materials become unattainable.

Moreover, one must kindle the fire and tend it day and night for several dozen days, vigilantly attending to its intensity lest it deviate from the proper measure. The labor is arduous and exacting—far more so than the preparation of *Golden Liquid*, which is comparatively simple. The sole difficulty in compounding *Golden Liquid* lies in obtaining gold. By the ancient scale, one *jin* of gold now weighs two *jin*, and its cost seldom exceeds thirty thousand cash. The other ingredients required are somewhat easier to procure. Furthermore, no fire-tending is needed; one merely places the materials in the *Flower Pool* for a set number of days, and the elixir forms of itself. The total expense may reach forty thousand cash, yielding one dose sufficient for eight immortals. Yet if the ingredients are even slightly deficient, their *qi* will lack the potency to transform and coalesce—just as brewing wine from a few *sheng* of rice will never succeed.

The *Baopuzi* states: Next in efficacy is the method of ingesting refined gold. Though it does not equal *Golden Liquid*, it far surpasses all other medicines. Some methods involve refining gold with pig’s back fat and wine; others treat it with the bark of the *ailanthus* tree; still others dissolve it in *vitex* wine with magnetite. Some allow the gold to be drawn into threads like silk, while others instantly liquefy it for consumption. Each method has its prohibitions, though none are as stringent as those for *Golden Liquid*. Some combine realgar and orpiment with the gold, stretching and weaving it like leather—all methods for attaining earthly immortality. Silver and large pearls from mussels may also be transmuted into liquid and ingested, but these require long-term consumption without interruption, and thus still fall short of *Golden Liquid*.

The *Baopuzi* states: To compound *Golden Liquid* and the Nine Elixirs, one must expend wealth and withdraw into renowned mountains, far from human habitation. Thus, few can undertake it, and among thousands or tens of thousands, only one may chance upon the scripture. This is why most Daoist texts scarcely mention the *Golden Elixir*. The foremost prohibition is this: never allow skeptics who disbelieve in the Dao to slander or deride the process, for this ensures failure. Master Zheng explained that the reason lies in the necessity of offering sacrifices when compounding such great medicines. Upon sacrifice, the Supreme Unity Primordial Sovereign, Lord Lao, and the Mysterious Maiden all descend to oversee the alchemist. If one does not seclude oneself in a remote, hidden place, allowing vulgar and foolish people to witness the process, the celestial spirits will reproach the alchemist for failing to uphold the scriptural precepts. Once such people utter slander, the spirits withdraw their protection, allowing malevolent *qi* to intrude and the medicine to fail. One must enter a renowned mountain, observe a hundred days of purification, abstain from the five pungent vegetables and raw fish, and avoid contact with ordinary people—only then may one prepare the great medicine. Even after its completion, one must continue the purification, not merely at the outset.

Master Zheng said: Lord Lao warned that no minor mountain is suitable for compounding *Golden Liquid* or the divine elixirs. Lesser mountains lack true spirits to govern them and are instead inhabited by the essences of trees and stones, thousand-year-old creatures, and blood-consuming demons. These beings are all of perverse *qi*; they do not seek to benefit humanity but only to cause harm. A Daoist must employ arts to shield himself and his disciples, yet these entities may still spoil the medicine. Even today, physicians compounding fine medicines or salves avoid exposure to chickens, dogs, children, and women, for if such beings violate the process, the medicine loses its efficacy. If a dyer’s work is marred by an ill-omened gaze, its beauty is lost—how much more so for the great medicines of immortality? Thus, ancient Daoists prepared divine medicines only in renowned mountains, never in ordinary ones, precisely for this reason.

Furthermore, the immortal scriptures state that for those who wish to focus their minds and compound medicines, the following mountains are suitable: Mount Hua, Mount Tai, Mount Huo, Mount Heng, Mount Song, Mount Shaoshi, Mount Chang, Mount Taibai, Mount Zhongnan, Mount Nüji, Mount Difei, Mount Wangwu, Mount Baodu, Mount An

Knead it between the fingers until it becomes like mud. If it does not boil or fails to emerge between the fingers when kneaded, melt it again and immerse it in clear wine—this may be repeated countless times. Once prepared, take it in the size of a pellet, either one or two, or divide into smaller pills. After thirty days, one will be free from cold and heat, and divine beings and jade maidens will attend to you. Silver may also be ingested in the same manner as gold. Consuming these two substances enables one to dwell in renowned mountains or stone chambers; after a year, one will become light and ascend. Even if taken only among mortals, one will become an earthbound immortal. Do not transmit this recklessly.

*Two Modes Elixir: Method for Ingesting Gold*
Take three *jin* of pig’s back fat and one *sheng* of pure bitter wine. Place five *liang* of gold in a vessel and heat it over an earthen stove. Dip the gold into the fat a hundred times, then into the bitter wine a hundred times. Consuming one *jin* grants longevity spanning heaven and earth; half a *jin* grants two thousand years of life; five *liang* grants twelve hundred years. Regardless of quantity, it may be ingested. Prepare and take it on a day of royal vigor. The effects are wondrous—do not transmit or show it to others, lest the medicine fail to form or lose its potency. When ready to depart, one should ingest cinnabar.

*Principles Profound, Fifth*
The Master Who Embraces Simplicity said: "The subtleties of defense are difficult to discern, and those who doubt are many. How could my wisdom surpass others?"

Those with partial understanding may happen upon insights—cranes know midnight, swallows know the hours of Wu and Ji, yet they may not grasp other matters. Through comparison and verification, one knows that longevity is attainable and that immortals are not born of lineage. The subtleties of the Dao cannot be fully recorded in writing, and its more accessible aspects are not worth expounding. In ancient times, Gengsang Chu and Laozi toiled diligently in their studies for many years before receiving the great transmission—there was good reason for this.

Of those with rounded heads and endowed with qi, who does not delight in life and fear death? Yet glory and power seduce the mind, unadorned beauty and jade-like skin dazzle the eyes, and the melodies of clear *shang* and flowing *zheng* confuse the ears. Love and aversion, benefit and harm agitate the spirit; fame and reputation bind the body. These all arrive unbidden and form without instruction. Unless one is destined to transcend, comprehends the Dao in solitude, perceives transformations beyond the ordinary, and wields discernment in the realm of the profound and boundless—awakening to the intimacy or estrangement of body and name, lamenting the fleeting swiftness of time like lightning—how could one forsake fleeting social ties, suppress desires, relinquish immediate cravings, and pursue the distant achievement of the Way?

For being arises from non-being, and form is established through spirit.

The existent is the palace of the non-existent; form is the dwelling of spirit. Thus, it may be likened to a dike—when the dike collapses, water does not remain. It may be compared to a candle—when the candle melts, the flame does not abide. When form is wearied, spirit scatters; when qi is exhausted, life ends. When roots wither and branches flourish, the verdant leaves depart the tree. When qi is fatigued and desires prevail, the essence-spirit departs the body. That which passes has no return; once decayed, there is no principle of rebirth. How lamentable for those who comprehend the Dao!

How could one not have reason to value seclusion over worldly splendor? Thus, those who nurture their nature in mountain forests and transcend convention to attain their intent regard lofty honors as mere excrescences, comparing all things to the wings of a cicada. How could they idly boast or feign disdain for worldly affairs? Truly, their vision is clear, and they abandon such things as if forgetting them.

Therefore, they dwell in remote seclusion, concealing their brilliance and veiling their splendor. They restrain the eyes that crave sights, dismiss colors that dazzle the vision, and block the ears that seek sounds. They distance themselves from clamorous voices, purify their profound contemplation, embrace the feminine and hold to the One, refine their qi to achieve suppleness, and stabilize themselves with tranquility. They dispel the perverse emotions of joy and sorrow, disregard the external gains and losses of honor and disgrace, sever the poisonous attachments of indulgent living, and silence the mechanisms of excessive speech. Only after turning inward do they hear with clarity; only after inner vision do they perceive the formless. They cultivate the spiritual root in the profound crucible, eliminate enticing desires in their interactions with the world, and cut away trivial concerns to govern themselves with ease and contentment. Through non-action, they preserve the principles of Heaven.

Thus, they chew and absorb the precious blossoms, bathe their spirits in the Great Clarity. Externally, they expel the five luminaries; internally, they guard the nine essences. They fortify the jade lock at the Gate of Life, bind the Northern Culmen to the Yellow Court, and draw the Three Radiant Ones into the Hall of Light. They soar with the Primordial Beginning to refine their form, savor the divine liquid upon the golden beam, drive the white away and retain the green, congeal the limpid spring within the cinnabar field, and draw the sunken pearl from the five cities and jade tripod. Below, they stoke the cauldron with the aquatic fowl; above, they make the splendid blossoms sing. The celestial deer sprouts its jade antlers, and the heavy jade ring is suspended in the Crimson Palace. The nine lights are concealed within the cavernous abyss, clouds darken and stretch to the heavens, and the long valleys run deep and intertwine. They tread upon the Qian and Dui trigrams, summon the Six Ding spirits, and recline in the purple chamber. They chew the golden petals, their countenances radiant as autumn chrysanthemums, crimson blossoms, jade-green stems, and crystal nectar overflowing like dew. They cure hunger and quench thirst, their bodies lightened, enabling them to ride the winds and clouds, soar through the void, merge with the primordial chaos, and attain eternal life.

Yet, dust accumulating to the depth of a foot cannot be sought in a fleeting moment, nor can the resonant flow of a mountain stream be attained in an instant. Those who achieve this are exceedingly rare, while those who fail are numerous and conspicuous. The world cannot comprehend those who succeed but only sees those who fail, and thus declares that immortality is but an empty path.

The *Baopuzi* states: When a dam is firm, water does not leak and waste; when tallow is abundant, the flame does not flicker and fade. The Dragon Spring sword, though unused, remains ever-sharp; axes and hatchets, though used daily, quickly wear out. Snow, though shunned in warmth, is stored through summer; ice, though buried deep, endures the heat. A single cloth may shield a mirror from scorching, and common plants may cover the ground through winter. Mud and earth are easily dissolved, yet when fired into pottery, they endure as long as Heaven and Earth. Oak and willow swiftly decay, yet when burned into charcoal, they may last for eons. Swine raised in comfort die young, while fine horses, though climbing steep paths, perish early. Cold-loving insects live twice as long, and southern trees thrive in warmth. Yet when exposed to killing frost, they wither, and when touched by spring’s harmony, they flourish. All things share the same nature, yet their flourishing and decay differ in function. How can there be fixed limits to autumn’s harvest or immutable laws for winter’s storage?

Human life and the span of death are not as predictable as plants in the cold of winter. Yet the principles of nurturing life and the methods of remedying harm offer benefits far beyond mere warmth. Why, then, do the effects of prolonged observation not convince the world? People cling to the familiar and the narrow, dismissing the Dao of immortality as fantastical and the teachings of the Yellow Emperor and Laozi as mere delusion. How lamentable!

The foolish refuse to trust even in medicine and acupuncture—how much less in matters deeper than these? They say: "If physicians like Yu Fu, Bian Que, Qin Yueren, and Cang Gong could cure illness, why did they not avoid death?" Or: "The wealthy and noble have no lack of medical skill, yet they do not live longer—is this not simply the natural course of fate?" To demand that such people believe in immortals is like making an ox climb a tree or a horse chase a bird.

The *Baopuzi* states: Summoning the lesser elixirs—three agents in pills or the five flowers and eight minerals—small medicines may instantly dissolve solid ice or float upon water. They can sever demonic influences, repel tigers and leopards, break up accumulations in the viscera, annihilate illness in the depths of the body, revive the suddenly dead from their abandoned corpses, and restore departed souls. These are but pills, yet they can bring the dead back to life. How, then, could the supreme medicines fail to grant immortality to the living?

The physician from Yue revived the prince after his death; the Hu shamans revived Su Wu after his breath had ceased. Chunyu Yi could open the skull to treat the brain; Hua Tuo could cut open the abdomen to cleanse the intestines. These are but minor skills of physicians, yet they achieved such feats. How much more can the Dao of immortality accomplish?

The reason people die is the harm wrought by desires, the afflictions of the hundred diseases, and the poisoning of noxious influences.

The harm wrought by perverse qi, the assault of wind and cold—these too are causes. Now, through guiding and pulling to circulate qi, returning essence to replenish the brain, regulating food and drink, moderating activity and rest, ingesting medicinal substances, contemplating the spirit, and guarding unity; by upholding celestial prohibitions, observing precepts, wearing talismans and seals, and utterly shunning all that harms life—thus one may avoid these six afflictions.

Today, physicians prescribe the "Pill for Illuminating Kidney Qi," the "Powder for Internal Supplementation of the Five Networks," the "Decoction for Filling the Bones with Wolfberry," and the "Decoction for Fortifying the Center with Astragalus." Those who take these all attain robust health. Lacquer leaves and *qingzhen* herbs—mere withered plants—were consumed by Fan A, who lived two hundred years, his ears and eyes still keen, and he could yet wield the needle to treat illness. These are recent factual accounts recorded by faithful historians.

It is further said that Wu Pu, having learned the "Five Animals' Play" from Hua Tuo to replace guiding and pulling, lived over a hundred years. These are but the most rudimentary of medicinal arts, and yet they achieve such results. How much more can be accomplished by those who employ their subtleties?

Now, when one tells the common folk that the "Regulating the Center" and "Four Harmonies" formulas can save those with cholera, that *kuandong* and *ziwan* can cure cough and counterflow, that *lilu* and *guanzhong* can slay the nine worms, that *danggui* and *shaoyao* can halt colicky pain, that *zhenjiao* and *duhuo* can dispel the eight winds, that *changpu* and *ganjiang* can arrest numbness and dampness, that *tusi* and *congrong* can supplement depletion and fatigue, that *gansui* and *tingli* can expel phlegm and accumulations, that *gualuo* and *huanglian* can heal wasting-thirst, that *jini* and *gancao* can resolve a hundred poisons, that *luru* and *yiyu* can prevent all wounds, and that *mahuang* and *daqing* can master cold damage—they still refuse to believe. They would rather slaughter living beings to entreat blessings, divide milfoil to divine misfortune, and refuse to trust the skilled physician’s attack on illness, instead relying on the chaos of shamans and scribes. How much less, then, would they heed the teaching that golden elixirs can transcend the world or that magic mushrooms can prolong life?

In ancient times, Zhang Liang, the Marquis of Liu, devised extraordinary strategies—none of his contemporaries could match his foresight and planning—yet even he declared that immortality could be attained. Though his wisdom and intelligence surpassed those of ordinary men, he said, "I shall abandon worldly affairs to follow Master Red Pine," and so he practiced *daoyin* and grain avoidance for a year, seeking the Way of lightness and ascension. When Empress Lü pressed him to devise a plan to secure the position of the Crown Prince, Liang had no choice but to write to summon the Four Whiteheads. As he had foreseen, Empress Lü, grateful for his counsel, compelled him to eat heartily, thus preventing the completion of his Way.

According to the *Secret Records of Kong Anguo*, Liang had received from the Yellow Stone Master not only the art of warfare but also the method of immortality. It further states that Liang’s true teachers were the Four Whiteheads—Master Jiaoli, Master Qili, and their companions—all of whom were immortals. Liang had fully received their divine formulas. Though Empress Lü forced him to eat and drink, he soon resumed his practice of the immortal arts, secretly transcending the world. Yet the common people, unaware of this, claimed he had died. If Kong Anguo’s words are true, then Liang indeed attained immortality.

Moreover, Zhang Cang, the Han Chancellor, happened upon a minor technique: by suckling a woman’s milk, he lived to one hundred and eight. This was but a shallow practice of the Dao, yet Cang thereby tripled the lifespan of an ordinary man. How much more, then, could one achieve long life by fully practicing the secret methods?

This account appears in the *Book of Han* and is no empty claim. As the *Baopuzi* states: "Ingesting elixirs is the foundation of long life, but if one can also practice *qi*-circulation, the benefits will be swift. Even without elixirs, if one fully masters *qi*-circulation, one may live for several centuries. However, one must also know the arts of the bedchamber. Without understanding the techniques of yin and yang, frequent exhaustion will weaken the efficacy of *qi*-circulation."

Man dwells within *qi*, and *qi* dwells within man. From heaven and earth to the myriad things, nothing exists without *qi* to sustain it. Those skilled in *qi*-circulation use it inwardly to nourish the body and outwardly to repel evil, yet the common people employ it daily without awareness.

In Wu and Yue, there are methods of incantation and *qi*-restraint that yield remarkable results. Those who master them can enter a great epidemic, share a bed with the sick, and remain uninfected. They can also protect dozens of their kin, leaving them unafraid—this is *qi* warding off heavenly disasters. If malevolent spirits or mountain demons invade a household, hurling tiles and stones, setting fires, or manifesting in visible or invisible forms, a master of *qi*-restraint can silence them instantly—this is *qi* subduing demonic spirits.

In mountain forests, where poisonous vapors and vipers abound, ordinary travelers are invariably harmed, yet those skilled in *qi*-restraint can clear a radius of dozens of *li*, ensuring their companions remain unharmed. They can also subdue tigers, leopards, and venomous snakes, rendering them motionless. With *qi*, they can staunch the bleeding of wounds, mend bones and sinews, and even tread upon blades or endure stabbings unscathed. If someone is bitten by a viper, a master of *qi* can cure them instantly.

In recent times, Zuo Ci, Zhao Ming, and others could reverse the flow of water with *qi*, making it surge upward for one or two *zhang*. They could cook food over a fire atop a thatched roof without burning it, or drive a nail seven or eight *cun* into a pillar and then expel it with a breath. They could boil water, toss in a hundred coins, and have someone retrieve them unscathed. They could expose water to the open air in freezing weather without it icing over, or prevent all cooking fires within a *li* from burning. They could silence dogs, rendering them unable to bark.

Once, when the Wu kingdom sent General He to suppress mountain bandits, the bandits had a master of *qi*-restraint who, during battle, rendered the official troops’ swords and blades immovable and caused their arrows to reverse and strike their own ranks. General He, resourceful and quick-witted, reasoned: "I have heard that edged weapons can be restrained, and poisonous creatures can be restrained, but objects without edges or venom cannot be restrained. Their restraint must be limited to weapons." He then had sturdy wooden clubs made and selected five thousand elite soldiers to lead the charge. When they attacked, the bandits, relying on their *qi*-restraint, were unprepared for unarmed weapons. The official troops crushed them with clubs, killing tens of thousands. The bandits’ restraint indeed no longer worked.

If *qi*, issuing from the body, can achieve such effects, why doubt that it can enable grain avoidance, cure illness, prolong life, and nurture one’s nature?

Zhongchang Tongli, a man of profound talent, wrote in his *Changyan* that *qi*-circulation could eliminate hunger and illness. At first, he did not believe it, but upon witnessing its practitioners, he found it to be true. The methods of nurturing life are so simple, yet he had not mastered them. Was this not because his mind was distracted by worldly affairs and his thoughts consumed by mundane concerns? Others who fail to practice must share this same affliction.

Long ago, a renowned master who knew the Way of immortality was sought by the Lord of Yan. Before the envoy could learn the method, the master died. Enraged, the lord intended to punish the envoy. A remonstrator said: "What we fear most is death, and what we value most is life. If even he lost his life, how could he have granted you immortality?" The lord relented, and this was indeed sound advice. Yet it was not entirely correct. If the master possessed the method of immortality, and if what I have heard of *qi*-circulation is true, then the master’s death did not necessarily mean he was ignorant of the Way. He simply could not abandon worldly affairs to practice it, so though he knew the method, it was of no benefit. It was not that the method of immortality did not exist.

It is also said that in Mixian County of Henan, a man named Bu Cheng studied the Dao for many years. One day, he bid farewell to his family, and as he walked, his steps gradually lifted him into the clouds until he vanished—this is what is meant by "lightening the body, ascending in broad daylight, and becoming an immortal of the highest order."

Chen Yuanfang and Han Yuanchang, both eminent scholars of Yingchuan, lived near Mixian. Their belief in the existence of immortals stemmed from their fathers and grandfathers having personally witnessed Bu Cheng’s ascension. This is yet another proof of immortality.

**Subtle Proofs, Part Six**
The *Baopuzi* states: "Those who share the same path and purpose need no words to trust one another, while those on divergent paths, even with earnest advice, are met with doubt. The Way is as near as a foot or a span, yet people debate trivial matters of worldly gain and loss. They distinguish black from white as clearly as gold from feathers or ink from powder, yet many remain confused and unable to discern the truth."

How much more so when one speaks of matters beyond the ways of the world and reveals the subtlest of proofs—yet mockery has long been the response. Is this unique to our time? What the enlightened eye perceives, even in the darkest depths of the netherworld or the faintest glimmer of a hair’s tip, presents no difficulty in discerning. Yet where understanding fails to reach, even the radiance of the sun and moon illuminating the heavens or the towering majesty of Mount Song piercing the clouds remain unseen.

The Divine Son and the Illustrious Sage, with profound insight and singular vision, uncovered secret texts in the sacred mountains and received the scriptures of immortality from divine beings. They cast off the dust of the world to shed its burdens, soared to the farthest reaches in lofty ascent—neither gold nor stone could match their durability, nor could cranes or tortoises rival their longevity. Moved by those who aspire to what lies ahead and pitying those who, though faithful, lack guidance, they bequeathed methods clear and luminous. Small efforts yield small gains; great endeavors bring great verification. Yet the shallow-minded cling stubbornly to their narrow views, content with bitterness like the sow-thistle and smartweed, unaware of the sweetness of maltose; drunk on thin wine, they fail to savor rich, unstrained liquor. They know the value of life yet remain ignorant of the Way of nurturing it; they fear death yet do not believe in the methods of transcending it. They recognize that excess in food and drink hastens illness yet cannot restrain their craving for rich flavors; they understand that indulgence in passion and desire leads to depletion and decay yet refuse to curb their desires.

Though I speak of the attainability of immortality, how can I make them believe? Some may object: "Your body lacks the auspicious signs, your understanding falls short of profound insight, your bones bear no extraordinary marks, and your years do not rival those of Anqi or Pengzu. You have never laid eyes on an immortal—how, then, can you claim to know of the possibility of long life or the evidence of cultivating one’s nature? If you have glimpsed the mysterious and wondrous within your heart and discerned the extraordinary through your own vision, I dare not affirm it." Such is the reasoning of those who, lacking even the means to cover their skin or provisions for the evening, yet boast of the arts of Tao Zhu or the methods of Yi Dun, inviting ridicule. Those burdened by chronic illness speak of the skills of Bian Que, or those who have repeatedly fled in defeat claim mastery of Sun and Wu’s strategies—people disbelieve because they see no results.

I reply: If a minnow swims in a puddle, it may claim the world holds no vast seas; if an ant writhes within a fruit pit, it may assert the eight directions end there. Though told of boundless oceans or the expanse of the cosmos, it would dismiss such words as empty talk and refuse to believe. If my eyes were square pupils and my ears extended beyond my crown, if I were to rein in flying dragons and ride auspicious clouds, outracing lightning to reach the inverted shadows—how then could you question me? Even if you saw me, you would call me a celestial spirit, an earthly deity, or some strange creature, never acknowledging that my attainments arose from study.

I merely seek to guide those of like mind as a precursor—how could I compel all your followers to believe? If immortals filled every household, visible as clearly as brows and lashes, even the blind would not doubt. But when such beings achieve the Dao, they tread the azure heavens and draw near the Purple Pole—unless one possesses spiritual communion, they remain unseen and unheard. You, however, dismiss this as nonexistent. The world trusts its hasty judgments, bows to its limited vision, and claims infallibility in its assessments. Is it any wonder, then, that they find the extraordinary so strange?

Those who dismiss what they rarely hear, pointing to their palms, will never awaken to its origins—this has long been the case. How is it only so today? Some may say: "Having repeatedly received your fine discourse, I no longer doubt the existence of immortals. Yet I still regret my inability to become one. Dare I ask if there is a more essential Way that can be practiced alone?"

I, Baopuzi, reply: In all Daoist cultivation, one must ascend from the shallow to the profound, proceed from the easy to the difficult. With steadfast sincerity and resolute determination, nothing will not be achieved. Doubt, however, yields no success—this is no single matter. To seek branches and leaves reaching the clouds without roots penetrating the earth, or to desire a river’s flow spanning ten thousand li without a deep and distant source—such things have never existed.

Without accumulating hidden virtue and secret goodness, one cannot move the divine spirits. Without wholehearted sincerity and mutual affinity, one cannot bind teacher and friend. Without meritorious deeds, one cannot undertake great trials. And without encountering an enlightened teacher, one cannot obtain the essential Way.

The Nine Elixirs and Golden Liquor are the foremost methods for immortality, yet their affairs are grand and their costs heavy—one cannot complete them hastily. Preserving essence and cherishing qi are most urgent. One should first ingest minor medicines to extend life and study basic arts to repel evil and misfortune, thereby gradually ascending to subtler realms.

Some may ask: "The methods and arts are so numerous—truly difficult to master in full. Setting aside the Golden Elixir, which of the remaining practices is best to cultivate?"

I reply: If one has not yet attained the greatest essentials, then the lesser ones cannot be neglected. For the various arts together accomplish long life. To illustrate with a grand analogy: it is like a ruler governing a state—civil and martial arts, rites and laws, none can be dispensed with. To illustrate with a small analogy: it is like a craftsman building a carriage—spokes, shafts, hubs, and linchpins, none may be lacking. The arts serve to inwardly cultivate form and spirit, extending years and curing illness, and outwardly to ward off evil, preventing calamity and harm. Compared to a zither or lute, one cannot seek the five notes with a single string. Compared to armor, one cannot withstand blades with a single plate. Why? The five notes must be used together, none can be missing; blades converge from all directions, none can be absent.

In nurturing life, one must desire extensive hearing yet grasp the essentials, see broadly yet choose wisely. Relying solely on one practice is insufficient. Moreover, those fond of life often cling to their own strengths: those who know the arts of the bedchamber say only those arts can transcend the world; those versed in exhalation and inhalation say only circulating qi can extend life; those who know the secrets of flexion and extension say only daoyin can forestall old age; those who know herbal formulas say only medicinal diets can lead to endless life. The failure of Daoist cultivation arises precisely from such one-sidedness.

Those of shallow vision, having learned but one thing, claim it is enough and do not recognize the truth. Even if they obtain an excellent method, they still seek endlessly, wasting effort and time, their intentions shifting without constancy—both sides err in this.

Some, dull-witted by nature and knowing only the most superficial, rashly enter famous mountains, braving poisonous stings and bites, repeatedly suffering harm. Ashamed to seek return, some are eaten by tigers and wolves, some killed by demons and sprites. Lacking methods to fast or warm themselves, they die in ravines and valleys—is this not foolish?

In pursuing learning, choosing a teacher is more important than the pursuit itself. If the teacher’s knowledge is narrow and they do not teach all they know, they may say: "The Way does not lie in multiplicity. Once you possess the Golden Elixir’s essentials, you need not rely on the rest." Yet those who know this are exceedingly rare—how can one idly await unknown great matters and neglect the small arts that bring mutual benefit?

It is like building a household without using other goods—this only applies if one has gold, silver, pearls, and jade in hand, sufficient to supply generations of expenses. If one lacks these, how can one not broadly sow grains and abundantly store fruits and vegetables?

Thus, grain abstention, avoiding weapons, suppressing demons, warding off evil spirits, restraining poisons, curing illnesses, entering mountains to make beasts not attack, crossing waters to make dragons not harm, passing through epidemics without fear, and facing crises by concealing one’s form—these are all minor matters one cannot be ignorant of. How much more so for matters beyond these?

Some may ask: "Dare I ask what prohibitions and taboos exist for cultivating long life?"

I reply: The most urgent prohibitions lie in not harming and not damaging. According to the *Yi’s* inner admonitions, the *Scripture of Red Pine*, and the *River Chart’s* records of life talismans, heaven and earth have spirits that oversee transgressions, reducing one’s allotted years according to the severity of the offense. When years are reduced, one suffers poverty, depletion, frequent illness, and repeated misfortune. When years are exhausted, one dies.

There are hundreds of ways to lose years—too many to enumerate. It is also said that within the body dwell the Three Corpses. Though formless, they are truly spiritual demons. Desiring a person’s early death, these Corpses, when able, act as demons, freely roaming and partaking in human sacrifices. Thus, on every *gengshen* day, they ascend to heaven and report a person’s transgressions to the Controller of Destinies. On the night of the month’s end, the Stove God also ascends to report one’s sins. For grave offenses, one’s *ji* is taken—*ji* being three hundred days. For lesser offenses, one’s *suan* is taken—*suan* being three days (or one day). I cannot verify the existence of these matters.

Yet the Way of Heaven is distant, and spirits are hard to fathom. Zhao Jianzi and Duke Mu of Qin both personally received golden defenses from the Supreme Emperor, with clear evidence in the land. Mountains, rivers, grasses, trees, wells, stoves, ponds, and pools all possess essence and qi—how much more so heaven and earth, the greatest of all things? By principle, they must have spirits. With spirits, they should reward good and punish evil. Yet their bodies are vast and their responses slow—they need not react instantly like an echo.

However, perusing all Daoist admonitions, none do not say: Those who seek long life must accumulate goodness and establish merit, be compassionate toward all things, extend forbearance to others, bring kindness to insects, rejoice in others’ fortune, pity others’ suffering, relieve others’ crises, and rescue others’ distress. Do not harm life with your hands or incite calamity with your mouth. See others’ gains as your own gains, others’ losses as your own losses. Do not exalt or praise yourself, do not envy those who surpass you, do not flatter or secretly harm others—only thus can one possess virtue and receive heaven’s blessings. What one does will surely succeed, and seeking immortality may be hoped for.

If, however, one hates good and loves killing, speaks falsely while harboring deceit, contradicts oneself, perverts justice, oppresses subordinates, deceives superiors, betrays those one serves, fails to appreciate kindness, manipulates the law for bribes, bends the straight and twists the upright, abandons the public for the private, punishes the innocent, destroys families, seizes treasures, harms others’ bodies, usurps positions, encroaches upon and suppresses the worthy, executes the submissive, slanders sages and worthies, injures Daoist practitioners, shoots flying birds, disembowels fetuses, breaks eggs, hunts in spring and summer, curses spirits, teaches others to do evil, conceals others’ goodness, endangers others for one’s own safety, takes credit for others’ work, ruins others’ fine deeds, seizes what others love, separates others’ kin, humiliates others to prevail, takes others’ long coins and returns short ones, diverts water and fire to harm others, coerces the weak, exchanges bad for good, forcibly takes and plunders, causes unjust harm, acts unfairly, indulges in debauchery, overthrows the upright, bullies orphans and widows, picks up lost items and takes offerings, deceives and swindles, loves to gossip about others’ private matters, holds others’ faults, invokes heaven and earth in curses, borrows without returning, exchanges loans without repayment, seeks desires endlessly, resents and rejects loyalty and trust, disobeys superiors’ commands, disrespects teachers, mocks others’ good deeds, ruins others’ crops, damages others’ belongings, impoverishes others, troubles others with uncleanness, uses light scales and small measures, short cloth and narrow gauges, mixes false with true, seeks illicit profit, lures others to take things, steps over wells and stoves, sings on the last day of the month and wails on the first—every such act is a sin. According to its severity, the Controller of Destinies reduces one’s *suan* and *ji*. When *suan* and *ji* are exhausted, one dies.

If one harbors evil thoughts without evil deeds, *suan* is reduced. If one commits evil that harms others, *ji* is reduced. If *suan* and *ji* are not exhausted yet one dies prematurely, the misfortune extends to one’s descendants.

Those who forcibly seize others’ property—calculating that their wives, children, and household will eventually repay the debt with death—bring ruin not immediately, but in time. If their evil deeds are insufficient to kill the victim’s family, they will nonetheless, over time, suffer from floods, fires, banditry, or the loss of valuables; or they may encounter official punishment, illness, and the expenses of medical treatment, animal sacrifice, and ritual offerings—all of which will inevitably exhaust the wealth they wrongfully took.

Daoist teachings say that those who wrongfully kill others do so with blades, slaying one another in turn. Those who seize wealth without justice, heedless of resentment, are like one who eats spoiled meat to stave off hunger or drinks poisoned wine to quench thirst: though they may briefly satisfy their craving, death will soon follow. If, however, one who has committed such evils later repents and reforms—having wrongfully killed, one should seek to save those facing death to atone; having unjustly taken others’ property, one should give alms to the poor to make amends. [Text incomplete.]

Some ask: “If one’s virtue is not yet perfected and one has not yet retreated to the remote mountains, how can one avoid the dangers of this age, where thieves and bandits abound, and guard against unforeseen calamities?”

The *Master Embracing Simplicity* replies: On a day governed by the *Trunk*, take earth from the position of the six *Gui* and mix it with the leaves of a hundred plants and fragrant herbs to plaster your door, one foot square—thieves will not come. Alternatively, take earth from the south gate of the market, or from the *Broken Year* and *Established Month* positions, shape it into a human figure, and place it in the Vermilion Bird direction—this will also suppress bandits. In an emergency, enter *Shengdi* (Living Earth) and remain there, and you will be safe.

The world has its *Shengdi*, a province has its *Shengdi*, a commandery has its *Shengdi*, a county has its *Shengdi*, a village has its *Shengdi*, a hamlet has its *Shengdi*, a house has its *Shengdi*, a room has its *Shengdi*.

Someone asks: “If even a single room has its *Shengdi*, is this not too restrictive?”

The *Master Embracing Simplicity* replies: The scriptures say that in the utmost extremity, one may even take refuge beneath a cart’s axle. Even within a single cart, there is *Shengdi* and *Sidi* (Dead Earth)—how much more so in a room?

Another asks: “I have heard that to seek the Way of life, one must know the Two Mountains. I do not know where these mountains are—would you deign to enlighten me and dispel my confusion?”

The *Master Embracing Simplicity* replies: They do exist, but they are neither Mount Hua nor Mount Heng, nor Mount Song nor Mount Tai. The Great Yuan Mountain is difficult to know yet easy to seek: neither in heaven nor on earth, neither submerged nor afloat, utterly remote and perilous, towering and rugged. Its harmonious *qi* swirls in abundance, and spirits and sages wander there together. Jade springs flow deep and unceasing, irrigating the hundred and twenty palaces and courts that stand in array. The *Li* and *Kan* trigrams hold their positions, and ten thousand stalks of dark mushrooms grow alongside crimson trees. Its treasures surpass gold and jade, and sweet springs flow from its corners. Those who drink from its pure streams become companions of Peng and Wang—this is the first mountain.

The Long Valley Mountain is vast and towering, its dark *qi* drifting like mist, its jade nectar scattering like dew. Golden pools and purple chambers lie hidden in its recesses. Fools who recklessly approach perish, but those of the Dao who ascend do not age. They pluck and consume yellow essence to ascend to heaven—this is the second mountain. Both are secrets guarded by the sages of old; ponder them deeply.

Another asks: “I wish to hear of the Realized One’s art of guarding the body and refining the form.”

The *Master Embracing Simplicity* replies: Profound is your question! At the beginning of the *Qing* cycle, when the moon and sun are half-risen and conjoined, they emerge as one. That which enters the jade pool and the golden chamber is the size of a pellet, yellow as a tangerine, containing a fine flavor sweet as a jujube. If you obtain it, guard it carefully and do not lose it. Once it has passed, it cannot be retrieved. When the body is about to perish, a pure white *qi*, subtle and dense, ascends through the secluded pass, winding thrice before reaching the radiant cinnabar of the *Mingmen* (Gate of Life), where form is not yet complete. How deep and wondrous! Difficult to attain, this is the oral transmission of the master. Those who know it fear neither the myriad demons nor the five weapons.

One asks: Can those who fully master the arts of the bedchamber through single practice attain transcendence, avert disasters, absolve sins, transform misfortune into blessing, secure high office, or multiply merchants’ profits? Is this true?

Embracing Simplicity replies: These are all exaggerations from the books of shamans—wild and deceptive claims, embellished by meddlers to the point of falsehood. Some are even fabricated by charlatans to deceive the world, weaving tangled threads to solicit offerings, gathering disciples to pursue worldly gain.

The arts of yin and yang, at best, may cure minor ailments or prevent depletion—nothing more. Their principles have limits; how could they lead to transcendence or dispel misfortune and summon fortune? Yet one cannot remain entirely without yin-yang union, lest illness arise. If, however, one indulges without restraint, exhausting desire without moderation, it will cut short one’s years. Those skilled in these arts can repel the "galloping horse" to replenish the brain, return the cinnabar elixir to the vermilion intestines, gather jade nectar from the golden pool, and draw the Three and Five to the resplendent beam, preserving youthful beauty to the end of one’s allotted lifespan.

Common folk hear that the Yellow Emperor ascended to heaven with one thousand two hundred women and assume he attained immortality solely through this practice. They do not know that at Mount Jing beneath the Tripod Lake, the Yellow Emperor refined the Ninefold Elixir before riding the dragon to heaven. He may well have had one thousand two hundred women, but this was not the sole path to his longevity.

Those who consume a thousand medicines and nourish themselves with the three sacrificial beasts yet remain ignorant of the bedchamber arts gain no benefit. Thus, the ancients, fearing people would indulge their passions recklessly, spoke of these arts in glowing terms—but such claims should not be taken entirely on faith.

Consider the analogy of water and fire: they can kill or give life, depending on whether one knows how to use them. In general, the essential method is to engage with many women, for the more the better. Yet without understanding the Way, even one or two partners may hasten death.

Among Peng Zu’s methods, the most crucial are the rest—other texts are overly cumbersome, difficult to practice, and their benefits do not match their claims. Few can master them. The oral instructions alone number several thousand words. Without this knowledge, even consuming a hundred medicines will not grant longevity.
